\section{PREFACE}

This series of physics problems and solutions, which consists of seven volumes - Mechanics, Electromagnetism, Optics, Atomic, Nuclear and Particle Physics, Thermodynamics and Statistical Physics, Quantum Mechanics, Solid State Physics and Relativity, contains a selection of 2550 problems from the graduate school entrance and qualifying examination papers of seven U.S. universities - California University Berkeley Campus, Columbia University, Chicago University, Massachusetts Institute of Technology, New York State University Buffalo Campus, Princeton University, Wisconsin University - as well as the CUSPEA and C. C. Ting's papers for selection of Chinese students for further studies in U.S.A. and their solutions which represent the effort of more than 70 Chinese physicists plus some 20 more who checked the solutions.

The series is remarkable for its comprehensive coverage. In each area the problems span a wide spectrum of topics while many problems overlap several areas. The problems themselves are remarkable for their versatility in applying the physical laws and principles, their uptodate realistic situations, and their scanty demand on mathematical skills. Many of the problems involve order of magnitude calculations which one often requires in an experimental situation for estimating a quantity from a simple model. In short, the exercises blend together the objectives of enhancement of one's understanding of the physical principles and ability of practical application.

The solutions as presented generally just provide a guidance to solving the problems, rather than step by step manipulation, and leave much to the students to work out for themselves, of whom much is demanded of the basic knowledge in physics. Thus the series would provide an invaluable complement to the textbooks.

The present volume, under the title "Solid State Physics, Relativity and Miscellaneous Topics" consists of 165 problems. Both special and general relativity problems are included, although some other special relativity problems have been solved elsewhere, particularly in the volumes on Mechanics and on Electromagnetism. Problems that are not appropriate for the other six volumes, such as mathematical techniques, are here grouped in the section Miscellaneous Topics.

In editing, no attempt has been made to unify the physical terms, units and symbols. Rather, they are left to the setters' and solvers' own preference so as to reflect the realistic situation of the usage today. Great pains has been taken to trace the logical steps from the first principles to the final solution, frequently even to the extent of rewriting the entire solution. In addition, a subject index to problems has been included to facilitate the location of topics. These editorial efforts hopefully will enhance the value of the volume to the students and teachers alike.

\author{
Yung-Kuo Lim \\ Editor
}



\section{INTRODUCTION}

Solving problems in school work is exercise of mental faculties, and examination problems are usually picked out from problems in school work. Working out problems is thus an essential and important aspect of the study of Physics.

Major American University Ph.D. Qualifying Questions and Solutions is a series of seven volumes. The subjects of each volume and the respective referees (in parentheses) are as follows:

1. Mechanics (Qing Yuan-qi, Gu En-pu, Cheng Jia-fu, Li Ze-hua, Yang De-tian)

2. Electromagnetism (Zhoo Shu-ping, You Jun-han, Zhu Jun-jie)

3. Optics (Bai Gui-ru, Guo Guang-can)

4. Atomic, Nuclear and Particle Physics (Tin Huai-cheng, Yang Baozhong, Fan Yang-mei)

5. Thermodynamics and Statistical Physics (Zheng Jiu-ren)

6. Quantum Mechanics (Zhang Yong-de, Zhu Dong-pei, Fan Hong-yi)

7. Solid State Physics, Relativity and Miscellaneous Topics (Zhang Jia-lu, Zhou You-yuan, Zhang Shi-ling)

This series covers almost all topics of University Physics and contains 2550 problems, most of which are solved in detail.

The problems have been carefully chosen from a collection of 3100 problems, of which some came from the China-U.S.A. Physics Examination and Application Program, some were selected from the Ph.D. Qualifying Examination on Experimental High Energy Physics sponsored by Chao Chong Ting, and the rest came from the graduate preliminary or qualifying examinations of the following seven world-renowned American universities: Columbia University, University of California at Berkeley, Massachusetts Institute of Technology, University of Wisconsin, University of Chicago, Princeton University, State University of New York at Buffalo.

Generally speaking, examination problems of physics in American universities do not involve too much mathematics. Rather, they are to a large extent characterized by the following three aspects. Some problems involving various frontier subjects and overlapping domains of science had been selected by professors directly from their own research work and have an "up-to-date flavor"; some problems involve broad fields and require a quick mind to analyze, while the methods needed for solving the other problems are simple and practical but require a full "touch of physics". Indeed, we venture to opine that the problems, as a whole, embody to some extent the characteristics of American science and culture, as well as the philosophy underlying American education.

Therefore, we consider it worthwhile to collect and solve these problems and introduce them to students and teachers in general, even though the effort involved was formidable. As many as a hundred teachers and graduate students took part in this time-consuming task.

A total of 165 problems make up this volume of three parts: solid state physics (81), relativity (28) and miscellaneous topics (56). In scope and depth, most of the problems conform to the undergraduate physics syllabi for solid state physics and relativity in most universities. The miscellaneous part tests the student's comprehension of general principles, familiarity with magnitudes and scales, knowledge of the history of physics, ability to estimate experimental errors, as well as his/her mathematical skills. Some questions are on the latest development in physics, purporting, no doubt, to test the scope of the student's scope of general reading. Yet a few others relate to the student's university and its research; these are not solved but are included for reference only.

The problems in this volume were solved by: Guo Zhi-chun, Chen Bing, Wang Ping, Zhang Shi-ling, Zhu Bing, Wang Yong, Zhou Dong-fang, Ning Bo, Wang Shan-xia, Si Qi-miao, Lu Jian-xin, Qiu Xiu, Wang An-min, Sun Yi, Jing Yi-peng, Liu Yu-zhen, Liu Fang-xin and Zhung Zhen-quan, and the initial translation from Chinese to English was carried out by Zhang Shu-zhen, refereed by Zhu Xing-fen and Ding Ze-jun.

\section{PART I}

\section{SOLID STATE PHYSICS}



![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_b8e2b1af07958c90efe7g-11.jpg?height=347&width=411&top_left_y=1190&top_left_x=848)



\subsection{CRYSTAL STRUCTURES AND PROPERTIES (1001-1027)}

\section{1}

Figure $1.1$ shows a hypothetical two-dimensional crystal consisting of atoms arranged on a square grid.
(a) Show an example of a primitive unit cell.
(b) Define "the reciprocal lattice" and explain its relation to Bragg reflection.

(c) Show the reciprocal lattice and the first Brillouin zone. How is this zone related to Bragg reflection?

(d) State and explain the theorem due to Bloch that says an electron moving in the potential of this lattice has traveling-wave functions. What boundary conditions must be used with this theorem?

(SUNY, Buffalo)

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_b8e2b1af07958c90efe7g-12.jpg?height=382&width=526&top_left_y=1365&top_left_x=797)

Fig. $1.1$

\section{Solution:}

(a) A primitive unit cell is a unit cell that contains lattice points at corners only, such as shown in Fig. 1.2. The basis vectors of the unit cell are

$$
\begin{aligned}
&\mathbf{a}_{1}=a(\mathbf{i}-\mathbf{j}), \\
&\mathbf{a}_{2}=a(\mathbf{i}+\mathbf{j}),
\end{aligned}
$$

where $a$ is the edge of the square lattice. 

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_b8e2b1af07958c90efe7g-13.jpg?height=428&width=548&top_left_y=344&top_left_x=794)

Fig. $1.2$

(b) If $\mathbf{a}_{i}(i=1,2)$ are the basis vectors of the direct lattice, vectors $\mathbf{b}_{j}(j=1,2)$ satisfying the relation

$$
\mathbf{a}_{i} \cdot \mathbf{b}_{j}=2 \pi \delta_{i j}= \begin{cases}2 \pi, & i=j \\ 0, & i \neq j\end{cases}
$$

are the basis vectors of the reciprocal lattice. In the reciprocal space the condition for Bragg reflection is that the difference between the reflected wave vector $\mathbf{k}$ and the incident wave vector $\mathbf{k}_{0}$ is an integer multiple $n$ of a reciprocal lattice vector $\mathbf{k}^{*}$

$$
\mathbf{k}-\mathbf{k}_{0}=n \mathbf{k}^{*}
$$

(c) From the direct basis vectors

$$
\begin{aligned}
&\mathbf{a}_{1}=a(\mathbf{i}-\mathbf{j}), \\
&\mathbf{a}_{2}=a(\mathbf{i}+\mathbf{j})
\end{aligned}
$$

the reciprocal basis vectors are obtained as

$$
\begin{aligned}
&\mathbf{b}_{1}=\frac{\pi}{a}(\mathbf{i}-\mathbf{j}), \\
&\mathbf{b}_{2}=\frac{\pi}{a}(\mathbf{i}+\mathbf{j}) .
\end{aligned}
$$

The reciprocal lattice and the first Brillouin zone are shown in Fig. 1.3. Bragg reflection takes place at the boundaries of the Brillouin zone.

(d) The wave representing an electron moving in the periodic potential field $V(\mathbf{r}+\mathbf{R})=V(\mathbf{r}), \mathbf{R}$ being a lattice vector, of the lattice has the form of a Bloch function

$$
\psi_{\mathbf{k}}(\mathbf{r})=e^{i \mathbf{k} \cdot \mathbf{r}} u_{\mathbf{k}}(\mathbf{r}),
$$



![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_b8e2b1af07958c90efe7g-14.jpg?height=458&width=566&top_left_y=362&top_left_x=801)

Fig. $1.3$

where the function $u_{\mathbf{k}}(\mathbf{r})$ has the same translational symmetry as the lattice:

$$
u_{\mathbf{k}}(\mathbf{r})=u_{\mathbf{k}}(\mathbf{r}+\mathbf{R}) .
$$

It is a plane wave modulated by the periodic potential field. This is Bloch's theorem. The exponential part of the Bloch wave is a plane wave which describes the global behavior of electrons in a crystal lattice, while the periodic function describes the local motion of those electrons around the nuclei. Therefore, Bloch waves characterize the motion of the electrons in a crystal.

The Born-von Kármán periodic boundary condition must be employed with Bloch's theorem.

\section{2}

A beam of electrons with kinetic energy $1 \mathrm{keV}$ is diffracted as it passes through a polycrystalline metal foil. The metal has a cubic crystal structure with a spacing of $1 \AA$. Given $m, q, h, c$,

(a) calculate the wavelength of the electrons,

(b) calculate the Bragg angle for the first order diffraction maximum.

(Wisconsin)

\section{Solution:}

(a) The electron wavelength is

$$
\lambda=\frac{h}{p}
$$

with $p$ given by 

$$
\frac{p^{2}}{2 m}=e V,
$$

$V$ being the accelerating voltage of the electrons. Thus

$$
\lambda=\frac{h}{(2 m e V)^{1 / 2}} \approx \frac{12.25}{V^{1 / 2}}=\frac{12.25}{\sqrt{1000}}=0.39 \AA .
$$

(b) The condition for Bragg reflection

$$
2 d \sin \theta=n \lambda
$$

gives for the first order diffraction maximum

$$
\sin \theta=\frac{\lambda}{2 d}=\frac{0.39}{2 \times 1}=0.195
$$

as $n=1, d=1 \AA$. Hence

$$
\theta=11.18^{\circ} \text {. }
$$

\section{3}

While sitting in front of a color TV with a $25 \mathrm{kV}$ picture tube potential, you have an excellent chance of being irradiated with $\mathrm{X}$-rays.

(a) What process produces most of the X-ray flux?

(b) For the resulting continuous distribution, calculate the shortest wavelength (maximum energy) X-ray. ( $h=6.6 \times 10^{-34} \mathrm{Js}, c=3 \times 10^{8} \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{s}$, $\left.1 \mathrm{eV}=1.6 \times 10^{-19} \mathrm{~J}\right)$

(c) For a rock salt $(\mathrm{NaCl})$ crystal placed in front of the tube, calculate the Bragg angle for a first order reflection maximum at $\lambda=0.5 \AA$. $\left(\rho_{\mathrm{NaCl}}=\right.$ $\left.2.165 \mathrm{~g} / \mathrm{cm}^{3}\right)$

(Wisconsin)

\section{Solution:}

(a) When a high voltage is applied to the picture tube, electrons emitted from the negative electrode will be accelerated by the electric field to strike the screen target. If the energy of the electrons exceeds a certain value, they can knock off inner-shell electrons in the target atoms and make holes in the inner shells. Then as the outer-shell electrons fall in to fill up these holes, X-rays are emitted. (b) The maximum energy of the X-ray photons produced, $h \nu_{\max }$, is equal to the energy $\mathrm{eV}$ of the incident electrons. Hence the minimum wavelength of the X-rays is

$$
\lambda_{\min }=\frac{h c}{e V}=\frac{12000}{V}=\frac{12000}{25 \times 10^{3}}=0.48 \AA .
$$

(c) Bragg's law

$$
2 d \sin \theta=n \lambda
$$

gives the angle $\theta$ for the first order diffraction $(n=1)$ maximum:

$$
\sin \theta=\frac{\lambda}{2 d},
$$

where $d$ is the distance between two neighboring ions in the $\mathrm{NaCl}$ crystal.

As $\mathrm{NaCl}$ crystal has a simple cubic structure with $\mathrm{Na}^{+}$and $\mathrm{Cl}^{-}$ions arranged alternately, there are $N_{0} \mathrm{Na}^{+}$ions and $N_{0} \mathrm{Cl}^{-}$ions in a mole of $\mathrm{NaCl}$, where $N_{0}$ is Avogadro's number. As $\mathrm{NaCl}$ has a molar weight $M=58.45 \mathrm{~g} / \mathrm{mol}$ and a density $\rho=2.165 \mathrm{~g} / \mathrm{cm}^{3}$, its crystal has

$$
d=\left(\frac{\frac{M}{\rho}}{2 N_{0}}\right)^{1 / 3}=\left(\frac{\frac{58.45}{2.165 \times 10^{6}}}{2 \times 6.02 \times 10^{23}}\right)^{1 / 3}=2.82 \AA .
$$

This gives

$$
\sin \theta=\frac{0.5}{2 \times 2.82}=0.088
$$

and hence

$$
\theta=5^{\circ} \text {. }
$$

\section{4}

Estimate to within $10 \%$ the wavelength of the most energy in the characteristic spectrum of $\mathrm{X}$-rays from copper $(\mathrm{Z}=29)$.

(Columbia)

Solution:

The ground state of the electron configuration of copper $(\mathrm{Z}=29)$ is $1 s^{2} 2 s^{2} 2 p^{6} 3 s^{2} 3 p^{6} 3 d^{10} 4 s^{1}$. The wavelength of the most energy in the characteristic spectrum of $\mathrm{X}$-rays corresponds to the transition of an electron from the $\mathrm{N}$ shell $(n=4)$ to a hole in the $\mathrm{K}$ shell $(n=1)$. Within $10 \%$ error the wavelength can be determined by the formula

$$
\frac{1}{\lambda}=R(Z-1)^{2}\left(\frac{1}{1^{2}}-\frac{1}{4^{2}}\right),
$$

where $R=1.09678 \times 10^{7} \mathrm{~m}^{-1}$ is the Rydberg constant. This yields

$$
\lambda=1.24 \times 10^{-10} \mathrm{~m}=1.24 \AA .
$$

\section{5}

$\mathrm{NaCl}$ crystallizes in a face-centered cubic lattice with a basis of $\mathrm{Na}$ and $\mathrm{Cl}$ ions separated by half the body diagonal of the cube. The atomic numbers of $\mathrm{Na}$ and $\mathrm{Cl}$ are 11 and 17 respectively.

(a) Determine which X-ray reflections will be observed (indexed for the conventional cubic unit cell).

(b) Of these which group will be strong and which group weak?

(Wisconsin)

\section{Solution:}

(a) The unit cell of $\mathrm{NaCl}$ includes eight atoms occupying the following positions: $\mathrm{Na}^{+}$at $\left(\begin{array}{lll}0 & 0 & 0\end{array}\right),\left(\frac{1}{2} \frac{1}{2} 0\right),\left(\frac{1}{2} 0 \frac{1}{2}\right),\left(0 \frac{1}{2} \frac{1}{2}\right)$, as indicated by solid circles in Fig. 1.4; $\mathrm{Cl}^{-}$at $\left(\frac{1}{2} 00\right),\left(0 \frac{1}{2} 0\right),\left(00 \frac{1}{2}\right),\left(\frac{1}{2} \frac{1}{2} \frac{1}{2}\right)$, as indicated by open circles in the figures.

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_b8e2b1af07958c90efe7g-17.jpg?height=675&width=824&top_left_y=1722&top_left_x=648)

Fig. $1.4$ The diffraction intensities are given by

$$
\begin{aligned}
I_{h k l} & \propto\left|F_{h k l}\right|^{2}=F_{h k l} \cdot F_{h k l}^{*} \\
&=\left[\sum_{j} f_{j} \cos 2 \pi\left(h u_{j}+k v_{j}+l w_{j}\right)\right]^{2} \\
&+\left[\sum_{j} f_{j} \sin 2 \pi\left(h u_{j}+k v_{j}+l w_{j}\right)\right]^{2},
\end{aligned}
$$

where $h, k, l$ are integers. Substitution of the ions' coordinates leads to

$$
\begin{aligned}
I_{h k l}=& f_{\mathrm{Na}^{+}}^{2}\{[1+\cos \pi(h+k)+\cos \pi(k+l)+\cos \pi(l+h)]\\
&+\alpha[\cos \pi h+\cos \pi k+\cos \pi l+\cos \pi(h+k+l)]\}^{2} \\
&+f_{\mathrm{Na}}^{2}\{[\sin \pi(h+k)+\sin \pi(k+l)+\sin \pi(l+h)]\\
&+\alpha[\sin \pi h+\sin \pi k+\sin \pi l+\sin \pi(h+k+l)]\}^{2}
\end{aligned}
$$

where $\alpha=f_{\mathrm{Cl}^{-}} / f_{\mathrm{Na}^{+}}=17 / 11$.

It is noted that the intensities $I_{h k l} \neq 0$ only when $h, k$ and $l$ are all odd numbers or all even numbers. Thus two different groups of diffracted beams can be observed.

(b) When $h, k$ and $l$ are all odd numbers,

$$
I \propto 16(1-\alpha)^{2},
$$

giving rise to weak reflection. When $h, k$ and $l$ are all even numbers,

$$
I \propto 16(1+\alpha)^{2},
$$

giving rise to strong reflection.

\section{6}

Give an approximate incident energy for which a crystal lattice would make a good diffraction grating for (a) photons, (b) neutrons. $\left(m_{n} c^{2}=\right.$ $939 \mathrm{MeV})$.

(Wisconsin) 

\section{Solution:}

The periodicity of a crystal lattice enables it to act as a diffraction grating to waves. Since the typical lattice constant in a crystal is about $10^{-10} \mathrm{~m}(1 \AA)$, diffraction can take place on a crystal when the wavelength of the incident particles is less than $10^{-10} \mathrm{~m}$.

(a) If the incident photons can just be diffracted by a crystal, their wavelength must be nearly $10^{-10} \mathrm{~m}$. Hence the energy of the incident photons is

$$
\begin{aligned}
E &=h \nu=\frac{h c}{\lambda}=\frac{6.624 \times 10^{-34} \times 3 \times 10^{8}}{10^{-10}} \\
&=1.98 \times 10^{-15} \mathrm{~J}=12420 \mathrm{eV} .
\end{aligned}
$$

(b) If the incident neutrons can just be diffracted, then their wavelength must be about $10^{-10} \mathrm{~m}$, corresponding to a kinetic energy of

$$
\begin{aligned}
E &=\frac{p^{2}}{2 m_{n}}=\frac{\left(\frac{h}{\lambda}\right)^{2}}{2 m_{n}}=\frac{h^{2} c^{2}}{2 \lambda^{2}} \cdot \frac{1}{m_{n} c^{2}} \\
&=\left(\frac{4.135 \times 10^{-15} \times 3 \times 10^{8}}{10^{-10}}\right)^{2} \cdot \frac{1}{2 \times 939 \times 10^{6}} \\
&=8.2 \times 10^{-2} \mathrm{eV} .
\end{aligned}
$$

\section{7}

Diffraction studies involving X-rays, electrons or neutrons give information about the crystallographic properties of solids. Compare these three techniques with reference to particle energies and types of information that can be obtained. Which technique is most appropriate for studying surface crystallography? Which technique is used to determine magnetic structure?

(Wisconsin)

\section{Solution:}

The typical energy of $\mathrm{X}$-rays is several thousand $\mathrm{eV}$, corresponding to a wavelength of about $10^{-10} \mathrm{~m}$, which is of the same order of magnitude as the interplanar distance in a crystal. Hence X-rays are suitable for determining crystal lattice structures. For low energy diffraction studies, energies $20-50 \mathrm{eV}$ are usually employed. Because of the large cross section of crystal atoms for scattering of low energy electrons, the incident electrons cannot penetrate deeply into a crystal. Thus low energy electron diffraction is an important technique for studying the surface structures of solids. A neutron sees two aspects of a crystal: distribution of nuclei and distribution of electronic magnetization. Hence the diffraction of neutrons by a magnetic crystal allows the determination of the distribution, orientation and order of the magnetic moments.

In brief, low energy electron diffraction is most suitable for studying surface crystallography, and neutron diffraction for determining magnetic structures of crystals.

\section{8}

X-rays are reflected from a crystal by Bragg reflection. If the density of the crystal which is of an accurately known structure is measured with an rms error of 3 parts in $10^{4}$, and if the angle the incident and reflected rays make with the crystal plane is $6^{\circ}$ and is measured with an rms error of $3.4$ minutes of arc, then what is the rms error in the determination of the X-ray wavelength?

(Wisconsin)

\section{Solution:}

For simplicity consider a crystal whose primitive cell is simple cubic with edge $d$ (to be multiplied by a factor of about one for the primitive cells of other crystal structures). For first order reflection, $n=1$ and Bragg's law gives

$$
2 d \sin \theta=\lambda .
$$

Differentiating, we have

$$
\left|\frac{\Delta \lambda}{\lambda}\right|=\left|\frac{\Delta d}{d}\right|+\cot \theta|\Delta \theta| .
$$

The volume of a unit cell is

$$
d^{3}=\frac{M}{\rho N_{0}},
$$

where $M$ is the molar weight and $\rho$ the mass density of the crystal, and $N_{0}$ is Avogadro's number. This differentiates to give 

$$
\left|\frac{\Delta d}{d}\right|=\frac{1}{3}\left|\frac{\Delta \rho}{\rho}\right| .
$$

Thus

$$
\left|\frac{\Delta \lambda}{\lambda}\right|=\frac{1}{3}\left|\frac{\Delta \rho}{\rho}\right|+\cot \theta \cdot|\Delta \theta|,
$$

and, in terms of rms errors,

$$
\frac{\sigma_{\lambda}}{\lambda}=\left[\left(\frac{1}{3} \frac{\sigma_{\rho}}{\rho}\right)^{2}+\left(\sigma_{\theta} \cot \theta\right)^{2}\right]^{1 / 2} \text {. }
$$

As $\frac{\sigma_{\rho}}{\rho}=3 \times 10^{-4}, \sigma_{\theta} \cot \theta=\frac{\sigma_{\theta} \cos \theta}{\sin \theta} \approx \frac{\sigma_{\theta}}{\theta}=\frac{3.4}{60 \times 6}=9.4 \times 10^{-3}$,

$$
\frac{\sigma_{\lambda}}{\lambda}=\sqrt{10^{-8}+\left(9.4 \times 10^{-3}\right)^{2}}=9.4 \times 10^{-3} .
$$

\section{9}

Estimate the pressure needed to compress a solid to several times its normal density.

(Columbia $)$

\section{Solution:}

To compress isothermally an elastic solid of volume $v$ by $d v$, the pressure increment $d p$ required is given by

$$
K=-v\left(\frac{\partial p}{\partial v}\right)_{T},
$$

where $K$ is a constant, the compressibility of the material.of the solid. Thus

$$
p=-\int_{v_{0}}^{v} \frac{K d v}{v}=K \ln \left(\frac{v_{0}}{v}\right) .
$$

To compress the solid to $\alpha$ times its normal density, i.e.,

$$
\frac{v_{0}}{v}=\frac{\rho}{\rho_{0}}=\alpha,
$$

the pressure required is $p=K \ln \alpha$.

For solids, $K \sim 10 \mathrm{~Pa}=10^{8} \mathrm{mb}$. For $\alpha=10$ say, we require

$$
p=10^{8} \ln 10=2.3 \times 10^{8} \mathrm{mb}=2.3 \times 10^{5} \text { atmospheres. }
$$



\section{0}

Consider a line of $2 N$ ions of alternating charges $\pm q$ with a repulsive potential $A / R^{n}$ between nearest neighbors in addition to the usual Coulomb potential.

(a) Find the equilibrium separation $R_{0}$ for such a system and evaluate the equilibrium energy $U\left(R_{0}\right)$.

(b) Let the crystal be compressed so that $R_{0} \rightarrow R_{0}(1-\delta)$. Calculate the work done in compressing a unit length of the crystal to order $\delta^{2}$.

(Princeton)

\section{Solution:}

(a) Neglecting surface effects, the lattice energy of the system is

$$
U(R)=N\left(-\frac{a q^{2}}{R}+\frac{A}{R^{n}}\right),
$$

where $a$ is the Madelung constant. $U(R)$ is a minimum at equilibrium. So the equilibrium separation $R_{0}$ is given by

$$
\left(\frac{d U}{d R}\right)_{R=R_{0}}=0,
$$

whence the equilibrium separation

$$
R_{0}=\left(\frac{n A}{a q^{2}}\right)^{\frac{1}{n-1}}
$$

and hence the equilibrium energy

$$
\begin{aligned}
U\left(R_{0}\right) &=-\frac{N}{R_{0}}\left(a q^{2}-\frac{A}{R_{0}^{n-1}}\right) \\
&=-\frac{N a q^{2}}{R_{0}}\left(1-\frac{1}{n}\right)
\end{aligned}
$$

with $a=2 \ln 2$ for a one-dimensional chain.

(b) When the crystal is compressed so that $R_{0}$ becomes $R$, the increase in the lattice energy is 

$$
\begin{aligned}
U(R)-U\left(R_{0}\right) &=N\left[-a q^{2}\left(\frac{1}{R}-\frac{1}{R_{0}}\right)+A\left(\frac{1}{R^{2}}-\frac{1}{R_{0}^{n}}\right)\right] \\
&=-\frac{N a q^{2}}{R_{0}}\left[\frac{R_{0}}{R}-1-\frac{1}{n}\left(\frac{R_{0}^{n}}{R^{n}}-1\right)\right]
\end{aligned}
$$

and is equal to the work done $W$ by the applied forces.

As $R=R_{0}(1-\delta)$, retaining terms up to $\delta^{2}$ we have

$$
\begin{gathered}
\frac{R_{0}}{R}-1=(1-\delta)^{-1}-1 \approx \delta+\delta^{2}, \\
\left(\frac{R_{0}}{R}\right)^{n}-1=(1-\delta)^{-n}-1 \approx n \delta+\frac{n(n+1)}{2} \delta^{2},
\end{gathered}
$$

and thus

$$
W \approx-\frac{N a q^{2}}{R_{0}}\left(1-\frac{n+1}{2}\right) \delta^{2}=\frac{N a q^{2}}{2 R_{0}}(n-1) \delta^{2} .
$$

The total length of the crystal is approximately $2 N R_{0}$. Hence the work done in compressing a unit length of the crystal is

$$
\frac{W}{2 N R_{0}}=\frac{q^{2}}{R_{0}^{2}} \frac{\ln 2}{2}(n-1) \delta^{2} .
$$

\section{1}

(a) What is the cohesive energy with respect to separated ions for crystalline $\mathrm{NaCl}$ ? Give the approximate value and a derivation using a very simple model. Lattice constant $a=5.6 \AA$.

(b) What experimental quantities must be added to or subtracted from the above to give you the cohesive energy with respect to separated sodium metal and chlorine gas? Omit small $(<10 \%)$ effects.

(Wisconsin)

\section{Solution:}

(a) For a crystal consisting of $N$ ions, each of charge $\pm e$, the cohesive energy is

$$
U(r)=-\frac{N}{2} \sum_{j}^{\prime}\left[\pm \frac{e^{2}}{r_{l j}}-\frac{b}{r_{l j}^{n}}\right]
$$

where $b$ is the Madelung constant and the prime indicates that $j=l$ is excluded from the summation. With $r_{l j}=\alpha_{j} R$ the above becomes

$$
U(R)=-\frac{N}{2}\left(\frac{\alpha e^{2}}{R}-\frac{\beta}{R^{n}}\right),
$$

where

$$
\alpha=\sum_{j}^{\prime}(\pm) \alpha_{j}^{-1}, \quad B=b \sum_{j}^{\prime} \alpha_{j}^{-n}
$$

At equilibrium $U(R)$ is a minimum and $R=R_{0}$ is given by

$$
\left(\frac{\partial U}{\partial R}\right)_{R=R_{0}}=0
$$

which yields

$$
R_{0}=\left(\frac{\alpha e^{2}}{n B}\right)^{\frac{1}{n-1}} .
$$

and thus

$$
U\left(R_{0}\right)=-\frac{N \alpha e^{2}}{2 R_{0}}\left(1-\frac{1}{n}\right) .
$$

The two terms in $U(R)$ are a Coulomb potential and a repulsive potential. By comparing the calculated Coulomb potential and the observed total binding energy, $n$ can be estimated to be about 10 . So to a $10 \%$ accuracy we have

$$
U\left(R_{0}\right) \approx-\frac{N \alpha e^{2}}{2 R_{0}} .
$$

Applying Ewald's method to the known $\mathrm{NaCl}$ structure, we can calculate $\alpha$ and obtain $\alpha=1.7476$. Then with $R_{0}=\frac{a}{2}=2.8 \AA$, we find

$$
U\left(R_{0}\right)=178 \mathrm{kcal} / \mathrm{mol},
$$

as for crystalline $\mathrm{NaCl}, n=8$.

(b) With respect to separated sodium metal and chlorine gas, to obtain the cohesive energy one would have to add to the above expression the energies for evaporation of sodium metal, separation of the chlorine molecules into atoms and ionization of the sodium and chlorine atoms. 

\section{2}

An ideal two-dimensional crystal consists of only one kind of atom (of mass $m$ ), and each atom has an equilibrium location at a point of a square lattice $\mathbf{R}=(r a, s a)$, where $r, s=1,2, \ldots, N$. The displacements from equilibrium are denoted by $\left(x_{r s}, y_{r s}\right)$, i.e.,

$$
\mathbf{R}_{r a}=\left(r a+x_{r s}, s a+y_{r s}\right),
$$

and in the harmonic approximation the potential is given by

$$
\begin{aligned}
V\left(x_{r s}, y_{r s}\right)=& \sum_{r, s}\left\{k_{1}\left[\left(x_{(r+1) s}-x_{r s}\right)^{2}\left(y_{r(s+1)}-y_{r s}\right)^{2}\right]\right.\\
&\left.+k_{2}\left[\left(x_{r(s+1)}-x_{r s}\right)^{2}\left(y_{(r+1) s}-y_{r s}\right)^{2}\right]\right\}
\end{aligned}
$$

For the case $k_{2}=0.1 k_{1}$,

(a) determine the general phonon dispersion relation $\omega_{q \lambda}$ throughout the Brillouin zone,

(b) sketch $\omega_{q \lambda}$ as a function of $q$ for

$$
q=(\xi, 0), \quad 0 \leq \xi \leq \frac{\pi}{a} .
$$

(Princeton)

\section{Solution:}

(a) The vibration of an atom at $\mathbf{R}_{r s}=(r a, s a)$ is given by Newton's second law

$$
m \ddot{\mathbf{R}}_{r s}=-\nabla_{r s} V,
$$

where

$$
\nabla_{r s} \equiv \mathbf{e}_{x} \frac{\partial}{\partial x_{r s}}+\mathbf{e}_{y} \frac{\partial}{\partial y_{r s}} .
$$

Consider a solution representing a wave traveling in the crystal:

$$
\mathbf{R}_{r s}=\mathbf{A} e^{i(\mathbf{q} \cdot \mathbf{R}-\omega t)},
$$

where $\mathbf{A}$ is a constant vector. As $\ddot{\mathbf{R}}_{r s}=-\omega^{2} \mathbf{R}_{r s}$ and $x_{r s}$ appears in the sum for $V$ only in the terms

$$
\begin{aligned}
&k_{1}\left[\left(x_{(r+1) s}-x_{r s}\right)^{2}+\left(x_{r s}-x_{(r-1) s}\right)^{2}\right], \\
&k_{2}\left[\left(x_{r(s+1)}-x_{r s}\right)^{2}+\left(x_{r s}-x_{r(s-1)}\right)^{2}\right],
\end{aligned}
$$

substitution of $\mathbf{R}_{r s}$ in the equation of motion gives

$$
-m \omega^{2} x_{r s}=2 k_{1}\left(x_{(r+1) s}+x_{(r-1) s}-2 x_{r s}\right)+2 k_{2}\left(x_{r(s+1)}+x_{r(s-1)}-2 x_{r s}\right)
$$

and, similarly,

$$
-m \omega^{2} y_{r s}=2 k_{1}\left(y_{r(s+1)}+y_{r(s-1)}-2 y_{r s}\right)+2 k_{2}\left(y_{(r+1) s}+y_{(r-1) s}-2 y_{r s}\right) .
$$

With $\mathbf{q}=\left(q_{x}, q_{y}\right), \mathbf{R}=(r a, s a)$, we have

$$
\begin{gathered}
x_{r s}=A_{x} e^{i\left[\left(q_{x} r+q_{y} s\right) a-\omega t\right]}, \\
y_{r s}=A_{y} e^{i\left[\left(q_{x} r+q_{y} s\right) a-\omega t\right]} .
\end{gathered}
$$

Substitution gives

$$
-m \omega^{2} x_{r s}=2 k_{1}\left(e^{i q_{x} a}+e^{-i q_{x} a}-2\right) x_{r s}+2 k_{2}\left(e^{i q_{y} a}+e^{-i q_{y} a}-2\right) x_{r s},
$$

or

$$
\begin{aligned}
\omega^{2} &=\frac{4 k_{1}}{m}\left[1-\cos \left(q_{x} a\right)\right]+\frac{4 k_{2}}{m}\left[1-\cos \left(q_{y} a\right)\right] \\
&=\omega_{0}^{2}\left[\sin ^{2}\left(\frac{q_{x} a}{2}\right)+\left(\frac{k_{2}}{k_{1}}\right) \sin ^{2}\left(\frac{q_{y} a}{2}\right)\right],
\end{aligned}
$$

where $\omega_{0}=\sqrt{\frac{8 k_{1}}{m}}$. Actually $q_{x}, q_{y}$ in the solution $x_{r s}, y_{r s}$ can be interchanged. Hence there are two phono dispersion relations for $k_{2}=0.1 k_{1}$ :

$$
\begin{aligned}
&\omega_{q_{1}}=\omega_{0}\left[\sin ^{2}\left(\frac{q_{x} a}{2}\right)+0.1 \sin ^{2}\left(\frac{q_{y} a}{2}\right)\right]^{1 / 2} \\
&\omega_{q_{2}}=\omega_{0}\left[\sin ^{2}\left(\frac{q_{y} a}{2}\right)+0.1 \sin ^{2}\left(\frac{q_{x} a}{2}\right)\right]^{1 / 2} .
\end{aligned}
$$

(b) Since $q_{x}=\xi, q_{y}=0$,

$$
\begin{aligned}
&\omega_{q_{1}}=\omega_{0}\left|\sin \left(\frac{\xi a}{2}\right)\right| \\
&\omega_{q_{2}}=\omega_{0} \sqrt{0.1}\left|\sin \left(\frac{\xi a}{2}\right)\right|=0.316 \omega_{0}\left|\sin \left(\frac{\xi a}{2}\right)\right| .
\end{aligned}
$$

As $\xi$ has values between 0 and $\frac{\pi}{a}$ inclusively, $\omega q$ varies with $\xi$ as shown in Fig. 1.5. 

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_b8e2b1af07958c90efe7g-27.jpg?height=440&width=512&top_left_y=379&top_left_x=774)

Fig. 1.5

\section{3}

Consider a two-dimensional square array of atoms with lattice constant a. The atoms interact so that when the atom at $\left(x_{0}, y_{0}\right)$ is displaced to $\left(x_{0}+\Delta x, y_{0}\right)$, it is subject to a restoring force $-c_{1} \Delta x$ due to its two nearest neighbors at $\left(x_{0} \pm a, y_{0}\right)$ and a restoring force $-c_{2} \Delta x$ due to its two nearest neighbors at $\left(x_{0}, y_{0} \pm a\right)$. Assume $c_{2}<c_{1}$.

(a) Find the dispersion relation for sound waves propagating in the $x$ direction, and draw the dispersion diagram.

(b) What is the speed of sound in this two-dimensional crystal?

If a few atoms in the lattice are removed from their sites, creating vacancies, the average restoring force for a plane wave of sound is reduced by an amount proportional to the concentration of vacancies. Furthermore, each vacancy will scatter any incident sound wave, and the amplitude of the scattered wave is $A / \lambda$ for $\lambda \gg a$, where $\lambda$ is the wavelength and $A$ is a constant.

(c) For a concentration of vacancies, find the attenuation length for sound as a function of wavelength (to within a multiplicative constant). Assume the vacancies scatter independently.

(d) Assume that an energy $\varepsilon$ is needed to remove an atom from the crystal and create a vacancy. What is the temperature dependence of the sound attenuation length?

(e) Under the same assumption as in (d), what is the temperature dependence of the sound velocity?

(Princeton) 

\section{Solution:}

(a) Take the origin at $\left(x_{0}, y_{0}\right)$, then $\left(x_{n}, y_{m}\right)=(n a, m a)$. Let the $x$ displacement of the atom at $\left(x_{n}, y_{m}\right)$ be $u_{n, m}$. The atom's $x$ displacement relative to the nearest neighbors situated parallel to the $x$-axis is

$$
-\left(u_{n+1, m}-u_{n, m}\right)+\left(u_{n, m}-u_{n-1, m}\right)=2 u_{n, m}-u_{n+1, m}-u_{n-1, m},
$$

and that relative to the nearest neighbors situated parallel to the $y$-axis is

$$
2 u_{n, m}-u_{n, m+1}-u_{n, m-1} .
$$

Hence the equation of motion of the atom at $\left(x_{n}, y_{m}\right)$ is

$$
\begin{aligned}
m \frac{d^{2} u_{n, m}}{d t^{2}}=&-C_{1}\left(2 u_{m, n}-u_{n+1, m}-u_{n+1, m}\right) \\
&-C_{2}\left(2 u_{m, n}-u_{n, m+1}-u_{n, m-1}\right) .
\end{aligned}
$$

Try a solution of the form

$$
u_{n, m}=A e^{i\left(q_{1} n a+q_{2} m a-\omega t\right)} .
$$

\section{Substitution gives}

$$
\begin{aligned}
m \omega^{2} &=C_{1}\left(2-e^{i q_{1} a}-e^{-i q_{1} a}\right)+C_{2}\left(2-e^{i q_{2} a}-e^{-i q_{2} a}\right) \\
&=2 C_{1}\left[1-\cos \left(q_{1} a\right)\right]+2 C_{2}\left[1-\cos \left(q_{2} a\right)\right]
\end{aligned}
$$

and hence

$$
\omega=\left\{\frac{4 C_{1}}{m} \sin ^{2}\left(\frac{q_{1} a}{2}\right)+\frac{4 C_{2}}{m} \sin ^{2}\left(\frac{q_{2} a}{2}\right)\right\}^{1 / 2} .
$$

For sound waves propagating in the $x$ direction, $q_{2}=0$ and the dispersion relation is as plotted in Fig. 1.6, where $\omega_{m}=\sqrt{\frac{4 C_{1}}{m}}$.

(b) The velocity of sound in the crystal is

$$
\begin{aligned}
\mathbf{v}=\nabla_{q} \omega &=\frac{\partial \omega}{\partial q_{1}} \mathbf{e}_{x}+\frac{\partial \omega}{\partial q_{2}} \mathbf{e}_{y} \\
&=\frac{a}{m \omega}\left[C_{1} \sin \left(q_{1} a\right) \mathbf{e}_{x}+C_{2} \sin \left(q_{2} a\right) \mathbf{e}_{y}\right]
\end{aligned}
$$

and has magnitude

$$
v=\frac{a}{m \omega}\left[C_{1}^{2} \sin ^{2}\left(q_{1} a\right)+C_{2}^{2} \sin ^{2}\left(q_{2} a\right)\right]^{1 / 2} .
$$



![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_b8e2b1af07958c90efe7g-29.jpg?height=450&width=637&top_left_y=423&top_left_x=793)

Fig. $1.6$

(c) Consider sound waves of intensity $I$ and cross sectional area $S$ traveling in the lattice. In a small distance $d x$ it will encounter $n S d x$ vacancies, where $n$ is the concentration of vacancies. Each scattering by a vacancy will cause an energy loss proportional to $\left(\frac{A}{\lambda}\right)^{2}$, and, since each vacancy will scatter any incident sound wave, the total energy loss by scattering will also be proportional to $I$. Thus

$$
-S d I=C I\left(\frac{A}{\lambda}\right)^{2} n S d x
$$

where $C$ is the proportionality constant. Hence

$$
I=I_{0} \exp \left(-\frac{C n A^{2}}{\lambda^{2}}\right) .
$$

The attenuation length $l$, defined as the distance over which the intensity decreases by a factor $e^{-1}$, is then

$$
l=\frac{\lambda^{2}}{C n A^{2}} .
$$

(d) The vacancy density is related to the absolute temperature $T$ by

$$
n \propto e^{-\varepsilon / k_{\mathrm{B}} T},
$$

where $k_{B}$ is Boltzmann's constant. Hence

$$
l \propto \frac{1}{n} \propto e^{\frac{\varepsilon}{k_{\mathrm{B}} T}} .
$$

(e) As the reduction in the restoring force is proportional to the vacancy concentration, the force constants are reduced from their original values $C_{1}^{0}$, $C_{2}^{0}$ to

$$
\begin{aligned}
&C_{1}=C_{1}^{0}(1-B n), \\
&C_{2}=C_{2}^{0}(1-B n),
\end{aligned}
$$

where $B$ is the proportionality constant. The speed of sound is therefore

$$
v \propto\left[1-B \exp \left(-\frac{\varepsilon}{k_{\mathrm{B}} T}\right)\right]^{1 / 2} .
$$

\section{4}

Consider a $d$-dimensional vibrating lattice at zero temperature. The number density is $\eta$ and the atoms have mass $m$. Using the Debye approximation and assuming all sound modes to have the same velocity $v$
(a) Evaluate the mean square displacement $\left\langle R^{2}\right\rangle$ for $d=3$.
(b) Evaluate $\left\langle R^{2}\right\rangle$ for $d=1$, and discuss its relevance to experiment.
(c) Evaluate the mean square strain $\left\langle\left(\frac{\partial R}{\partial x}\right)^{2}\right\rangle$ for $d=1$.

(Princeton)

\section{Solution:}

Consider an atom oscillating with angular frequency $\omega_{j}$ and amplitude $y_{0 j}$. Its displacement from the equilibrium position is

$$
y_{j}=y_{0 j} \cos \left(q_{j} x-\omega_{j} t\right)
$$

and its kinetic energy is

$$
\frac{1}{2} m \dot{y}_{j}^{2}=\frac{1}{2} m \omega_{j}^{2} y_{j}^{2} .
$$

Its kinetic energy averaged over time is

$$
\left\langle\frac{1}{2} m \dot{y}_{j}^{2}\right\rangle=\frac{1}{2} m \omega_{j}^{2}\left\langle y_{j}^{2}\right\rangle=\frac{1}{4} m \omega_{j}^{2} y_{0 j}^{2} .
$$

The oscillating atom is equivalent to a quantum harmonic oscillator of the same frequency whose total energy is $\left(n+\frac{1}{2}\right) \hbar \omega_{j}$. As the average kinetic energy of an oscillator is equal to half its total energy,

$$
\frac{1}{4} m \omega_{j}^{2} y_{0 j}^{2}=\frac{1}{2}\left(n+\frac{1}{2}\right) \hbar \omega_{j},
$$

we have

$$
y_{0 j}^{2}=\frac{(2 n+1) \hbar}{m \omega_{j}} .
$$

At zero absolute temperature, all oscillators are in the ground state for which the quantum number $n=0$. The mean square displacement is then

$$
\left\langle y_{j}^{2}\right\rangle=\frac{1}{2} y_{0 j}^{2}=\frac{\hbar}{2 m \omega_{j}} .
$$

Hence, averaged over all the atoms in the lattice, the mean square displacement at zero temperature is

$$
\left\langle R^{2}\right\rangle=\frac{1}{N} \sum_{j}^{N}\left\langle y_{j}^{2}\right\rangle=\frac{\hbar}{2 N m} \sum \omega_{j}^{-1}=\frac{\hbar}{2 \rho V} \sum_{j} \omega_{j}^{-1},
$$

where $\rho, V$ are respectively the mass density and volume, and $N$ is the total number of atoms of the lattice.

(a) $d=3$. In the Debye approximation, the number of modes of oscillation with wave numbers less than $q$ is given by the volume, measured in units of $\left(\frac{2 \pi}{L}\right)^{3}, L$ being the length of the lattice, assumed cubic, of a sphere of radius $q$ with center at the origin of the q-space. As there are three possible polarizations, the number of modes of vibration with wave numbers less than $q$ is

$$
\mathcal{N}=3 \cdot \frac{4}{3} \pi q^{3}\left(\frac{2 \pi}{L}\right)^{-3}=\frac{L^{3} q^{3}}{2 \pi^{2}} .
$$

Assuming all the modes have the same sound velocity $v$, we have $q=\frac{\omega}{v}$ and the density of state

$$
D(\omega)=\frac{d \mathcal{N}}{d \omega}=\frac{3 L^{3} q^{2}}{2 \pi^{2}} \frac{d q}{d \omega}=\frac{3 V \omega^{2}}{2 \pi^{2} v^{3}} .
$$

Furthermore as $\mathcal{N}=3 N$, the Debye cutoff angular frequency is

$$
\omega_{\mathrm{D}}=\left(\frac{6 \pi^{2} N}{V}\right)^{\frac{1}{3}} v=\left(6 \pi^{2} \eta\right)^{\frac{1}{3}} v .
$$

Hence

$$
\begin{aligned}
\left\langle R^{2}\right\rangle &=\frac{\hbar}{2 \rho v} \int_{0}^{\omega_{\mathrm{D}}} \omega^{-1} D(\omega) d \omega \\
&=\frac{3 \hbar \omega_{\mathrm{D}}^{2}}{8 \pi^{2} m \eta v^{3}} .
\end{aligned}
$$

(b) $d=1$. The Debye approximation gives

$$
\mathcal{N}=N=2 q\left(\frac{2 \pi}{L}\right)^{-1}=\frac{L q}{\pi}=\frac{L}{\pi} \frac{\omega}{v},
$$

and hence

$$
D(\omega)=\frac{L}{\pi v}
$$

and

$$
\omega_{\mathrm{D}}=\frac{\pi N v}{L}=\pi \eta v,
$$

where $\eta$ is the number of atoms per unit length of the one-dimensional lattice. Hence

$$
\begin{aligned}
\left\langle R^{2}\right\rangle &=\frac{\hbar}{2 \rho L} \sum_{j} \omega_{j}^{-1}=\frac{\hbar}{2 \pi \rho v} \int_{0}^{\omega_{\mathrm{D}}} \frac{d \omega}{\omega} \\
&=\left.\frac{\hbar}{2 \pi m \eta v} \ln \omega\right|_{0} ^{\omega_{\mathrm{D}}}=\infty .
\end{aligned}
$$

$\left\langle R^{2}\right\rangle$ is divergent for the one-dimensional case. It is seen that the divergence arises from the lower integration limit being zero. Physically, $\omega \approx 0$ corresponds to the lattice atoms move together as a rigid body, for which $\left\langle y^{2}\right\rangle$ is zero, not $\sim \omega^{-1}$. Thus an experimental determination of $\left\langle R^{2}\right\rangle$ would not yield infinity but same finite value.

(c) For the $j$ th atom,

$$
\left(\frac{\partial y_{j}}{\partial x}\right)^{2}=q_{j}^{2} y_{0 j}^{2} \sin ^{2}\left(q_{j} x-\omega_{j} t\right),
$$

which, when averaged over time, becomes

$$
\left\langle\left(\frac{\partial y_{i}}{\partial x}\right)^{2}\right\rangle=\frac{1}{2} q_{j}^{2} y_{0 j}^{2}=\frac{\hbar \omega_{j}}{2 m v^{2}} .
$$

Thus for the one-dimensional lattice,

$$
\begin{aligned}
\left\langle\left(\frac{\partial R}{\partial x}\right)^{2}\right\rangle &=\frac{1}{2 N} \sum_{j}^{N} \frac{\hbar \omega_{j}}{2 m v^{2}} \\
&=\frac{\hbar}{4 N m v^{2}} \int_{0}^{\omega_{\mathrm{D}}} \frac{L \omega}{\pi v} d \omega \\
&=\frac{\hbar \omega_{\mathrm{D}}^{2}}{4 \pi m \eta v^{3}},
\end{aligned}
$$

where $\omega_{\mathrm{D}}=\pi \eta v$, as $\frac{N}{L}=\eta$.

\section{5}

Consider a two-dimensional square lattice with one atom of mass $m$ per lattice point interacting with only nearest neighbors with force constant $K$. [Take the phonon dispersion curve to be $\omega_{q}=\sqrt{\frac{4 K}{m}} \sin (q a / 2)$.]

(a) In the long-wavelength limit, obtain the density of phonon states $D(\omega)=d N / d \omega$, i.e., the number of lattice-vibration modes per frequency interval $d \omega$.

(b) At high temperature $\left(k_{\mathrm{B}} T \gg \hbar \omega\right)$, find the mean square displacement of an atom from its equilibrium position, and comment on the stability of two-dimensional crystals.

(Wisconsin)

\section{Solution:}

(a) In the long-wavelength limit, $q \rightarrow 0$ and

$$
\omega_{q}=\sqrt{\frac{4 K}{m}} \sin \left(\frac{q a}{2}\right) \approx \sqrt{\frac{K}{m}} q a .
$$

The velocity of sound is then

$$
v=\frac{\omega_{q}}{q}=\sqrt{\frac{K}{m}} a,
$$

independent of $q$. In the Debye model, the number of modes with wave numbers less than $q$ is given by the area, measured in units of $\left(\frac{2 \pi}{L}\right)^{2}, L$ being the length of the square lattice, of a circle of radius $q$ with center at the origin in the q-space. As there are two possible polarizations, the number of normal modes with wave numbers less than $q$ is

$$
\mathcal{N}=2 \pi q^{2}\left(\frac{2 \pi}{L}\right)^{-2}=\frac{L^{2} q^{2}}{2 \pi}=\frac{S}{2 \pi} \frac{\omega^{2}}{v^{2}},
$$

where $S$ is the area of the lattice. Then the density of phonon states is

$$
D(\omega)=\frac{d \mathcal{N}}{d \omega}=\frac{S \omega}{\pi v^{2}}=\frac{S m \omega}{\pi a^{2} K} .
$$

(b) The total internal energy of the lattice is

$$
U=\int_{0}^{\omega_{m}} \frac{\hbar \omega}{e^{\hbar \omega / k_{\mathrm{B}} T}} \cdot D(\omega) d \omega .
$$

At high temperature, $\hbar \omega \ll k_{\mathrm{B}} T$ and

$$
e^{\hbar \omega / k_{\mathrm{B}} T} \approx 1+\frac{\hbar \omega}{k_{\mathrm{B}} T} .
$$

Then

$$
\begin{aligned}
U & \approx \int_{0}^{\omega_{\mathrm{D}}} k_{\mathrm{B}} T D(\omega) d \omega \\
&=\frac{S k_{\mathrm{B}} T}{2 \pi v^{2}} \omega_{\mathrm{D}}^{2} .
\end{aligned}
$$

If there are $N$ atoms in the lattice, then

$$
\mathcal{N}=2 N=\frac{S}{2 \pi} \frac{\omega_{\mathrm{D}}^{2}}{v^{2}},
$$

giving

$$
\omega_{\mathrm{D}}^{2}=\frac{4 \pi N v^{2}}{S},
$$

and so

$$
U=2 N k_{\mathrm{B}} T .
$$

At the high temperature limit, the average potential energy is equal to the average kinetic energy, and hence half the total energy. If $\left\langle r^{2}\right\rangle$ is the mean square displacement of an atom, then

$$
\frac{N}{2} K\left\langle r^{2}\right\rangle=N k_{\mathrm{B}} T,
$$

giving

$$
\left\langle r^{2}\right\rangle=\frac{2 k_{\mathrm{B}} T}{K},
$$

where $K$ is the interatomic force constant. Thus $\left\langle r^{2}\right\rangle \propto T$. If $T$ increases from a low value, $\sqrt{\left\langle r^{2}\right\rangle}$ will became greater than the lattice constant and the lattice breaks up. Hence a two-dimensional lattice is not stable at high temperatures.

\section{6}

(a) What is the specific heat (per mole) of a monatomic gas at constant volume?

(b) What is its specific heat (per mole) at constant volume for a diatomic gas? Explain.

(c) What is the specific heat of a monatomic crystalline solid?

(Wisconsin)

\section{Solution:}

Considered as ideal, a gas has internal energy per mole at absolute temperature $T$ of

$$
E=\frac{f}{2} N k_{\mathrm{B}} T=\frac{f}{2} R T,
$$

where $f$ is the number of degrees of freedom of a molecule and $R=N k_{\mathrm{B}}$ is the gas constant. The molar specific heat at constant volume is thus

$$
C_{v}=\left(\frac{\partial E}{\partial T}\right)_{v}=\frac{f}{2} R .
$$

(a) For a monatomic gas, $f=3$ and

$$
C_{v}=\frac{3}{2} R .
$$

(b) For a diatomic gas, $f=5$ and

$$
C_{v}=\frac{5}{2} R .
$$

It is noted that the specific heat per mole is independent of temperature for both monatomic and diatomic gases if they can be approximated as ideal gases.

(c) Consider one mole of a monatomic crystalline solid. It has a volume $V$ and constains $N$ (the Avogadro number) atoms. As there are $3 N$ normal modes of vibration, the number of modes in the angular frequency range $\omega$ to $\omega+d \omega$ is (Problem 1014)

$$
D(\omega) d \omega=\frac{3 V \omega^{2} d \omega}{2 \pi^{2} v_{0}^{3}} .
$$

where $v_{0}$ is the speed of sound in the crystal, subject to a maximum frequency

$$
\omega_{\mathrm{m}}=\left(\frac{6 \pi^{2} N}{V}\right)^{1 / 3} v_{0} .
$$

In a solid the average energy of an oscillator of frequency $\omega$ is

$$
\frac{\hbar \omega}{e^{\hbar \omega / k_{\mathrm{B}} T}-1} \text {. }
$$

Hence the total internal energy of the mole of crystal is

$$
\begin{aligned}
U &=\int_{0}^{\omega_{\mathrm{m}}} \frac{\hbar \omega}{e^{\hbar \omega / k_{\mathrm{B}} T}-1} \cdot D(\omega) d \omega \\
&=\frac{3 V \hbar}{2 \pi^{2} v_{0}^{3}} \int_{0}^{\omega_{\mathrm{m}}} \frac{\omega^{3}}{e^{\hbar \omega / k_{\mathrm{B}} T}-1},
\end{aligned}
$$

and the specific heat per mole at constant volume is

$$
C_{v}=\left(\frac{\partial U}{\partial T}\right)_{v}=\frac{3 V k_{\mathrm{B}}}{2 \pi^{2}}\left(\frac{T k_{\mathrm{B}}}{\hbar v_{0}}\right)^{3} \int_{0}^{x_{\mathrm{m}}} \frac{e^{x} x^{4} d x}{\left(e^{x}-1\right)^{2}},
$$

where $x_{m}=\frac{\hbar \omega_{\mathrm{m}}}{k_{\mathrm{B}} T}$. Introducing the Debye temperature

$$
\Theta=\frac{\hbar \omega_{\mathrm{m}}}{k_{\mathrm{B}}}
$$

we can write this as

$$
C_{v}=9 N k_{\mathrm{B}}\left(\frac{T}{\Theta}\right)^{3} \int_{0}^{\frac{\Theta}{T}} \frac{e^{x} x^{4} d x}{\left(e^{x}-1\right)^{2}}=9 N k_{\mathrm{B}} F\left(\frac{T}{\Theta}\right)
$$

When $T \gg \Theta, x \ll \frac{\Theta}{T}$ is small so that

$$
\frac{e^{x} x^{4}}{\left(e^{x}-1\right)^{2}} \approx \frac{x^{4}}{x^{2}}=x^{2}
$$

and

$$
F\left(\frac{T}{\Theta}\right) \simeq \frac{1}{3}\left(\frac{T}{\Theta}\right)^{3}\left(\frac{\Theta}{T}\right)^{3}=\frac{1}{3}
$$

giving

$$
C_{v} \approx 3 N k_{\mathrm{B}}=3 R .
$$

Thus $C_{v}$ is independent of temperature at high temperatures.

When $T \ll \Theta, \frac{\Theta}{T}$ is large and can be taken as $\infty$. Then

$$
C_{v} \approx 9 N k_{\mathrm{B}}\left(\frac{T}{\Theta}\right)^{3} \int_{0}^{\infty} \frac{e^{x} x^{4} d x}{\left(e^{x}-1\right)^{2}}
$$

showing that $C_{v} \propto T^{3}$ at low temperatures. For a quantitative estimate, we note that

$$
\int_{0}^{\infty} \frac{x^{3} d x}{e^{x}-1}=6 \sum_{1}^{\infty} \frac{1}{n^{4}}=\frac{\pi^{4}}{15}
$$

This gives

$$
U=\frac{3}{5} \pi^{4} N k_{\mathrm{B}} \frac{T^{4}}{\Theta^{3}}
$$

and hence

$$
C_{v}=\left(\frac{\partial U}{\partial T}\right)_{v}=\frac{12}{5} \pi^{4} N k_{\mathrm{B}}\left(\frac{T}{\Theta}\right)^{3}=234 N k_{\mathrm{B}}\left(\frac{T}{\Theta}\right)^{3}
$$

\section{7}

Use the Debye model to calculate the heat capacity of a monatomic lattice in one dimension at temperatures small compared with the Debye temperature $\Theta_{\mathrm{D}}=\frac{\hbar \pi v}{k_{\mathrm{B}} a}$, where $v$ is the sound velocity, $a$ is the lattice spacing and $k_{\mathrm{B}}$ is Boltzmann's constant. Numerical constants in the form of integrals need not be evaluated.

(Columbia $)$

\section{Solution:}

In the Debye model, the density of states for a one-dimensional monatomic lattice is (Problem 1014)

$$
\rho(\omega)=\frac{L}{\pi v} .
$$

As a mode of frequency $\omega$ has average energy

$$
\frac{\hbar \omega}{e^{\hbar \omega / k_{\mathrm{B}} T}-1},
$$

the internal energy is

$$
U=\int_{0}^{\omega \mathrm{D}} \frac{L}{\pi v} \frac{\hbar \omega}{e^{\hbar \omega / k_{\mathrm{B}} T}-1} d \omega,
$$

where $\omega_{\mathrm{D}}$ is given by

$$
\int_{0}^{\omega_{\mathrm{D}}} \frac{L}{\pi v} d \omega=N,
$$

i.e.,

$$
\omega_{\mathrm{D}}=\frac{\pi N v}{L}=\frac{\pi v}{a}=\frac{k_{\mathrm{B}} \Theta_{\mathrm{D}}}{\hbar} .
$$

Hence

$$
C_{v}=\frac{\partial U}{\partial T}=N k_{\mathrm{B}}\left(\frac{T}{\Theta_{\mathrm{D}}}\right) \int_{0}^{\Theta_{\mathrm{D}}} \frac{x^{2} e^{x}}{\left(e^{x}-1\right)^{2}} d x
$$

When $T \ll \Theta_{\mathrm{D}}$, we $\operatorname{can}$ take $\frac{\Theta_{\mathrm{D}}}{T} \approx \infty$, so that

$$
\int_{0}^{\Theta_{\mathrm{D}}} \frac{x^{2} e^{2}}{\left(e^{x}-1\right)^{2}} d x \approx \int_{0}^{\infty} \frac{x^{2} e^{2}}{\left(e^{x}-1\right)^{2}} d x=\frac{\pi^{2}}{3},
$$

giving

$$
C_{v}=\frac{\pi^{2}}{3} N k_{\mathrm{B}}\left(\frac{T}{\Theta_{\mathrm{D}}}\right)=\frac{\pi k_{\mathrm{B}}^{2} N a}{3 \hbar v} T .
$$



\section{8}

Graphite has a layered crystal structure in which the coupling between the carbon atoms in different layers is much weaker than that between the atoms in the same layer. Experimentally it is found that the specific heat is proportional to $T^{2}$ at low temperatures. How can the Debye theory be adapted to provide an explanation?

(SUNY, Buffalo)

\section{Solution:}

Debye's model gives that the density of states $\rho(\omega)$ of a two-dimensional lattice is proportional to $\omega$ (Problem 1015). If the interaction between atoms of different layers in a layered crystal structure is very weak, then $\rho(\omega)$ can be considered as approximately given by the sum of the densities of states of the various layers and so will still be proportional to $\omega$. Hence for a layered crystal

$$
\rho(\omega)=A \omega,
$$

$A$ being a constant. As

$$
\int_{0}^{\omega_{\mathrm{D}}} \rho(\omega) d \omega=3 N,
$$

where $N$ is the number of atoms in the crystal, $\omega_{\mathrm{D}}$ is the Debye frequency and the factor 3 is for the three possible polarizations, we have

$$
A=\frac{6 N}{\omega_{\mathrm{D}}^{2}} .
$$

Hence the total internal energy $E$ of the lattice is

$$
\begin{aligned}
E &=\int_{0}^{\omega_{\mathrm{D}}} \frac{\hbar \omega}{e^{\hbar \omega / k_{\mathrm{B}} T}-1} \rho(\omega) d \omega \\
&=A \int_{0}^{\omega_{\mathrm{D}}} \frac{\hbar \omega^{2}}{e^{\hbar \omega / k_{\mathrm{B}} T}-1} d \omega,
\end{aligned}
$$

and the specific heat $C_{v}$ is 

$$
C_{v}=\left(\frac{\partial E}{\partial T}\right)_{v}=A k_{\mathrm{B}}\left(\frac{k_{\mathrm{B}} T}{\hbar}\right)^{2} \int_{0}^{\frac{\theta_{\mathrm{D}}}{T}} \frac{x^{3} e^{x}}{\left(e^{x}-1\right)^{2}} d x
$$

where $x=\frac{\hbar \omega}{k_{\mathrm{B}} T}, \Theta_{\mathrm{D}}=\hbar \omega_{\mathrm{D}} / k_{\mathrm{B}}$.

When $T \ll \Theta_{\mathrm{D}}, \frac{\theta_{\mathrm{D}}}{T} \approx \infty$ and

$$
\int_{0}^{\frac{\theta_{\mathrm{O}}}{T}} \frac{x^{3} e^{x}}{\left(e^{x}-1\right)^{2}} d x \approx \int_{0}^{\infty} \frac{x^{3} e^{x}}{\left(e^{x}-1\right)^{2}} d x
$$

which is a constant. Thus $C_{v} \propto T^{2}$ at low temperatures for a 3 -dimensional layered crystal with weak coupling between layers according to the Debye theory, in agreement with experimental observations.

\section{9}

Acoustic properties of dielectric solids dominate their thermodynamic behavior and other properties such as photoconducting resistance. Diamond is a monatomic dielectric solid of carbon having $10^{21}$ atoms $\mathrm{cm}^{-3}$.

(a) In Fig. 1.7, sketch, roughly, its specific heat (per atom) as a function of absolute temperature.

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_b8e2b1af07958c90efe7g-40.jpg?height=439&width=898&top_left_y=1705&top_left_x=600)

Fig. $1.7$

(b) How is $T_{\text {Debye }}$ related to the Debye frequency $\omega_{\mathrm{D}}$ ?

(c) If the acoustic velocity at low frequencies is $5 \times 10^{5} \mathrm{~cm} / \mathrm{sec}$, what is approximately the value of $\omega_{\mathrm{D}}$ ?

(Wisconsin) 

\section{Solution:}

(a) The specific heat curve (Problem 1016) is sketched in Fig. 1.8.

(b) The Debye temperature $T_{\mathrm{D}}$ is defined in terms of the Debye frequency $\omega_{\mathrm{D}}$ by

$$
k_{\mathrm{B}} T_{\mathrm{D}}=\hbar \omega_{\mathrm{D}} .
$$

(c) For a 3-dimensional lattice, the density of states is (Problem 1014)

$$
\rho(\omega)=\frac{3 V \omega^{2}}{2 \pi^{3} v^{3}} d \omega .
$$

As

$$
\begin{gathered}
\int_{0}^{\omega_{\mathrm{D}}} \rho(\omega) d \omega=3 N, \\
\omega_{\mathrm{D}}=\left(6 \pi^{2} \frac{N}{V}\right)^{1 / 3} v=\left(6 \pi^{2} n\right)^{1 / 3} v .
\end{gathered}
$$

With $n=10^{27} \mathrm{~m}^{-3}, v=5 \times 10^{3} \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{s}$, we find

$$
\omega_{\mathrm{D}}=1.95 \times 10^{13} \mathrm{~s}^{-1} .
$$

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_b8e2b1af07958c90efe7g-41.jpg?height=540&width=987&top_left_y=1752&top_left_x=577)

Fig. $1.8$ 

\section{0}

The conduction of heat by a non-metallic, crystalline solid requires a mechanism by which the phonon distribution may be brought into thermal equilibrium.

(a) Show that a three-phonon collision process of the type $\mathbf{q}_{1}+\mathbf{q}_{2}=\mathbf{q}_{3}$ will not establish equilibrium.

(b) Describe the form of the processes which establish equilibrium. (Wisconsin)

\section{Solution:}

Suppose that two phonons of wave vectors $\mathbf{q}_{1}$ and $\mathbf{q}_{2}$ collide and produce a third phonon of wave vector $\mathbf{q}_{3}$. Conservation of quasimomentum allows the following two processes:

$\mathbf{q}_{1}+\mathbf{q}_{2}=\left\{\begin{array}{l}\mathbf{q}_{3}, \\ \mathbf{q}_{3}+\mathbf{k},\end{array} \quad \mathbf{k}\right.$ being an appropriate reciprocal lattice vector.

These two types of three-phonon collision process are illustrated in Fig. $1.9$ (a) and (b). The first is the normal process. The second, known as an umklapp process, can take place in a discrete lattice. In this case, $\mathbf{q}_{1}, \mathbf{q}_{2}$ produce a $\mathbf{q}_{3}$ that goes outside the limits $\pm \mathbf{q}_{m}$ which define the boundaries of the first Brillouin zone. It must be brought back to the latter by adding to it a reciprocal lattice vector $\mathbf{k}$, since all wave vectors are physically meaningful only in the first Brillouin zone, according to our convention.

First Brillouin zone

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_b8e2b1af07958c90efe7g-42.jpg?height=338&width=330&top_left_y=1815&top_left_x=621)

(a) normal process First Brillouin zone

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_b8e2b1af07958c90efe7g-42.jpg?height=333&width=437&top_left_y=1823&top_left_x=1191)

(b) umklapp process

Fig. $1.9$

(a) In the process $\mathbf{q}_{1}+\mathbf{q}_{2}=\mathbf{q}_{3}$, the total momentum of the phonon system remains the same before and after collision, which means that the heat current density is not affected by the collision. Hence a thermal equilibrium cannot be established, and there is no thermal resistivity, or, in other words, the thermal conductivity is infinite.

(b) In the umklapp process $\mathbf{q}_{1}+\mathbf{q}_{2}=\mathbf{q}_{3}+\mathbf{k}$, the total momentum of the phonon system will change because of the inclusion of $\mathbf{k}$. The effective phonon $\mathbf{q}_{4}=\mathbf{q}_{3}+\mathbf{k}$ produced by the collision travels in a direction almost opposite to either of the original phonons $\mathbf{q}_{1}, \mathbf{q}_{2}$. Then, within a certain relaxation time the system will reach a state of thermal equilibrium. It is this process that gives rise to a finite thermal resistivity.

\section{1}

Briefly describe the mechanism for thermal conductivity of crystalline insulating solids. The thermal conductivity of a solid electrical insulator (and gases, etc.) can be written as $k=\frac{1}{3} c v l$ where $c$ is a heat capacity per unit volume, $v$ a velocity, and $l$ a mean free path. Provide estimates of these parameters with physical justifications for a typical crystal at room temperature.

(Wisconsin)

\section{Solution:}

If there is a temperature gradient in a crystalline, insulating solid, the lattice vibrations excited at the hot end will have more vibrational modes and larger amplitudes, or, in other words, more phonons. As these lattice waves propagate toward the cold end, the latter's lattice vibrations will be enhanced and approach the same number of vibrational modes and the same larger amplitudes. This means that the phonons propagating in the lattice transfer from the hot to the cold end. On account of the nonlinearity of lattice vibrations, there are interactions among the phonons as they propagate. They collide with one another and bump against the imperfections in the crystal. The phonon collision processes may be the normal process or the umklapp process. The latter process plays the dominant role in thermal conduction and causes the distribution of phonons to approach an equilibrium. Hence the mean free path in the expression for thermal conductivity should be that of the umklapp process.

Take as example the $\mathrm{NaCl}$ crystal at room temperature. The typical values are molar specific heat $C_{v} \approx 25 \mathrm{~J} / \mathrm{mol} \cdot \mathrm{K}$, speed of sound $v=$ $6 \times 10^{4} \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{s}$, phonon mean free path $l=2.3 \times 10^{-9} \mathrm{~m}$, molar volume $V=27 \times 10^{-6} \mathrm{~m}^{3}$. These data give the thermal conductivity as

$$
k=\frac{1}{3}\left(\frac{C_{v}}{V}\right) v l=43 \mathrm{Wm}^{-1} \mathrm{~K}^{-1} .
$$

\section{2}

(a) Write down definitions and formulas which describe the thermal conductivity of a solid.

(b) Identify the excitations which carry the heat current.

(c) Sketch a graph of the thermal conductivity of a metal as a function of temperature.

(d) Identify the characteristic temperature dependence at high and low temperatures and describe the dominant physical effects in these two regions.

(Wisconsin)

\section{Solution:}

(a) The thermal conductivity of a solid is defined as the heat current density $Q$ per unit temperature gradient, i.e., the constant coefficient $k$ in the equation $Q=-k \frac{\partial J}{\partial x}$. For a nonmetallic solid, heat is conducted by phonons and the conductivity is given by

$$
k=\frac{1}{3} C_{v} \bar{v} l,
$$

where $C_{v}$ is the specific heat per unit volume at constant volume of the solid, $\bar{v}$ and $l$ are respectively the average speed and mean free path of the phonons.

(b) For nonmetallic solids the excitations which carry the heat current are phonons.

(c) The thermal conductivity of a metal consists of two parts, the lattice thermal conductivity $k_{a}$ contributed by phonons and the electronic thermal conductivity $k_{e}$ contributed by the free electrons:

$$
k=k_{a}+k_{e}
$$

with 

$$
\begin{aligned}
k_{a} &=\frac{1}{3} C_{a} \bar{v}_{a} l_{a}, \\
k_{e} &=\frac{1}{3} C_{e} \bar{v}_{e} l_{e}
\end{aligned}
$$

where $C$ is the specific heat at constant volume per unit volume, $\bar{v}$ and $l$ are the average speed and mean free path of the particles (phonons or free electrons as the case may be) respectively. As for normal pure metals

$$
\frac{k_{a}}{k_{e}}=\frac{C_{a} \bar{v}_{a} l_{a}}{C_{e} \bar{v}_{e} l_{e}} \approx 10^{-2},
$$

the conductivity of a typical metal is principally that due to free electrons. Thus

$$
k \approx k_{e}=\frac{1}{3} C_{e} \bar{v}_{e} l_{e}=\frac{\pi^{2} N k_{\mathrm{B}}^{2}}{3 m \bar{v}_{e}} l_{e} T,
$$

where we have used the result from Fermi-Dirac statistics

$$
C_{e}=\frac{\pi^{2} N k_{\mathrm{B}}^{2} T}{m \bar{v}_{e}^{2}},
$$

$N$ being the number of free electrons per unit volume. As $l_{e}$ is due mainly to electron-phonon scattering,

$$
l_{e} \propto \frac{1}{n_{\mathrm{ph}}},
$$

where $n_{\mathrm{ph}}$ is the mean number of phonons per unit volume at temperature $T$

(1) At high temperatures,

$$
n_{\mathrm{ph}} \propto T,
$$

or $l_{e} \propto T^{-1}$, giving

$$
k=\text { constant }
$$

independent of temperature.

(2) At low temperatures,

$$
n_{\mathrm{ph}} \propto T^{3},
$$

or $l_{e} \propto T^{-3}$, giving

$$
k=T^{-2} .
$$

(3) At very low temperatures, the number of phonons is very small and scattering of the free electrons is due mainly to impurities. Thus $l_{e} \propto \frac{1}{N_{I}}$, $N_{I}$ being the number density of the impurity atoms, which is independent of $T$. Thus

$$
k \propto T .
$$

The above may be summarized in a plot of the metallic solid thermal conductivity against temperature as shown in Fig. 1.10.

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_b8e2b1af07958c90efe7g-46.jpg?height=431&width=661&top_left_y=814&top_left_x=751)

Fig. $1.10$

\section{3}

A material of density $\rho$ has a face-centered cubic lattice with cube edge length $a_{0}$ and an Einstein temperature $\theta_{\mathrm{E}}$. For the high temperature limit, i.e., $T>\theta_{\mathrm{E}}$, express the atomic mean square displacement in the $x$ direction, $\overline{M_{x}^{2}}$, in terms of $\rho, a_{0}$ and $\theta_{\mathrm{E}}$ plus the necessary physical constants.

\section{(Wisconsin)}

\section{Solution:}

For a face-centered cubic lattice, the volume of a primitive cell per atom is $a_{0}^{3} / 4$ so that

$$
\rho=\frac{4 m}{a_{0}^{3}},
$$

where $m$ is the mass of an atom.

In the Einstein model a lattice of $N$ atoms is considered as a set of $3 N$ independent harmonic oscillators in one dimension, each oscillator having an independent angular frequency $\omega_{\mathrm{E}}$ related to the Einstein temperature $\theta_{\mathrm{E}}$ by

$$
\hbar \omega_{\mathrm{E}}=k_{\mathrm{B}} \theta_{\mathrm{E}}
$$

For a harmonic oscillator the average potential energy $\frac{k}{2} \overline{\mu_{x}^{2}}$ and the average kinetic energy $\frac{m}{2} \overline{\dot{x}^{2}}=\frac{m}{2} \omega_{\mathrm{E}}^{2} \overline{\mu_{x}^{2}}$ are equal, each being equal to half of the total energy $\left(n+\frac{1}{2}\right) \hbar \omega_{E}$ given by quantum mechanics. Hence the mean square displacement is

$$
\overline{\mu_{x}^{2}}=\left(n+\frac{1}{2}\right) \hbar \omega_{\mathrm{E}} / k=\left(n+\frac{1}{2}\right) \hbar / m \omega_{\mathrm{E}} .
$$

At temperature $T$, the oscillators are distributed over the eigenstates according to the Boltzmann distribution law $\exp \left[-\left(n+\frac{1}{2}\right) \frac{\hbar \omega_{\mathrm{E}}}{k_{\mathrm{B}} T}\right]$. Thus

$$
\begin{aligned}
\overline{\mu_{x}^{2}} &=\frac{\sum_{n=0}^{\infty}\left(n+\frac{1}{2}\right) \frac{\hbar}{m \omega_{\mathrm{E}}} e^{-\left(n+\frac{1}{2}\right) \hbar \omega_{\mathrm{E}} / k_{\mathrm{B}} T}}{\sum_{n=0}^{\infty} e^{-\left(n+\frac{1}{2}\right) \hbar \omega_{\mathrm{E}} / k_{\mathrm{B}} T}} \\
&=\frac{\hbar}{m \omega_{\mathrm{E}}}\left(\frac{\sum_{n=0}^{\infty} n e^{-n \hbar \omega / k_{\mathrm{B}} T}}{\sum_{n=0}^{\infty} e^{-n \hbar \omega / k_{\mathrm{B}} T}}+\frac{1}{2}\right) .
\end{aligned}
$$

The first term in the brackets can be written as

$$
\begin{aligned}
\frac{\sum n e^{n x}}{\sum e^{n x}} &=\frac{\sum \frac{d}{d x} e^{n x}}{\sum e^{n x}}=\frac{\frac{d}{d x} \sum e^{n x}}{\sum e^{n x}}=\frac{d}{d x} \ln \sum e^{n x} \\
&=\frac{d}{d x} \ln \left(\frac{1}{1-e^{x}}\right)=\frac{e^{x}}{1-e^{x}}=\frac{1}{e^{-x}-1}
\end{aligned}
$$

by putting $x=-\frac{\hbar \omega_{E}}{k_{\mathrm{B}} T}$. Hence

$$
\overline{\mu_{x}^{2}}=\frac{\hbar}{m \omega_{\mathrm{E}}}\left(\frac{1}{e^{\hbar \omega_{\mathrm{E}} / k_{\mathrm{B}} T}-1}+\frac{1}{2}\right) .
$$

With $m=\frac{a_{0}^{3} \rho}{4}, \omega_{\mathrm{E}}=\frac{k_{\mathrm{B}} \theta_{\mathrm{E}}}{\hbar}$, we have for the high temperature limit

$$
\begin{aligned}
\overline{\mu_{x}^{2}} &=\frac{4 \hbar^{2}}{k_{\mathrm{B}} \theta_{\mathrm{E}} a_{0}^{3} \rho}\left(\frac{1}{e^{\theta_{\mathrm{E}} / T}-1}+\frac{1}{2}\right) \\
& \approx \frac{4 \hbar^{2}}{k_{\mathrm{B}} \theta_{\mathrm{E}} a_{0}^{3} \rho} \cdot \frac{T}{\theta_{\mathrm{E}}} \\
&=\frac{4 \hbar^{2} T}{k_{\mathrm{B}} \theta_{\mathrm{E}}^{2} a_{0}^{3} \rho} .
\end{aligned}
$$



\section{4}

Consider a vibrating solid.

(a) Evaluate the Helmholtz free energy $F$ of a phonon mode of frequency $\omega$ at temperature $T$.

(b) Assume that the solid is harmonic with a bulk modulus $B$ and that $\Delta$ is the fractional volume change. Ignoring any dispersion of the phonon modes, i.e. taking $\omega_{k}=\omega$, write down the free energy of the crystal.

(c) If the volume dependence of $\omega$ is $\delta \omega / \omega=-\gamma \Delta$, where $\gamma$ is known as the Grüneisen constant, how much contraction exists at temperature $T$ ?

(d) Discuss the physical significance of the Grüneisen constant.

(Princeton)

\section{Solution:}

(a) For a phonon mode of frequency $\omega$, the partition function is

$$
Z=\sum_{n=0}^{\infty} e^{-\left(n+\frac{1}{2}\right) \hbar \omega / k_{\mathrm{B}} T}=\frac{e^{-\hbar \omega / 2 k_{\mathrm{B}} T}}{1-e^{-\hbar \omega / k_{\mathrm{B}} T}} .
$$

The Helmholtz free energy of a crystal is therefore

$$
\begin{aligned}
F &=-k_{\mathrm{B}} T \ln Z \\
&=k_{\mathrm{B}} T\left[\frac{\hbar \omega}{2 k_{\mathrm{B}} T}+\ln \left(1-e^{-\hbar \omega / k_{\mathrm{B}} T}\right)\right] .
\end{aligned}
$$

(b) The free energy of a crystal is

$$
F(V, T)=U(V)+k_{\mathrm{B}} T \sum_{k}\left[\frac{\hbar \omega}{2 k_{\mathrm{B}} T}+\ln \left(1-e^{-\hbar \omega / k_{\mathrm{B}} T}\right)\right]
$$

where $V$ is the volume of the crystal. If $\omega_{k}=\omega$ and $N$ is the total number of degrees of freedom of the crystal, we have

$$
F(V, T)=U(V)+N k_{\mathrm{B}} T\left[\frac{\hbar \omega}{2 k_{\mathrm{B}} T}+\ln \left(1-e^{-\hbar \omega / k_{\mathrm{B}} T}\right)\right] \text {, }
$$

where $U(V)$ is the internal energy of the crystal at $T=0 \mathrm{~K}$.

(c) Because of the nonlinearity of the vibration, $\omega$ changes with volume. The equation of state of the crystal is therefore

$$
p=-\left(\frac{\partial F}{\partial V}\right)_{T}=-\left(\frac{\partial U}{\partial V}\right)_{T}-N\left(\frac{1}{2} \hbar+\frac{\hbar e^{-\hbar \omega / k_{\mathrm{B}} T}}{1-e^{-\hbar \omega / k_{\mathrm{B}} T}}\right) \frac{\partial \omega}{\partial V} .
$$

As $\frac{\delta \omega}{\omega}=-\gamma \Delta=-\gamma \frac{\delta V}{V}$, we have

$$
\frac{\partial \omega}{\partial V}=-\gamma \frac{\omega}{V},
$$

and

$$
p=-\left(\frac{\partial U}{\partial V}\right)_{T}+\gamma \frac{\bar{E}}{V},
$$

where

$$
\bar{E}=N\left(\frac{1}{2} \hbar \omega+\frac{\hbar \omega}{e^{\hbar \omega / k_{\mathrm{B}} T}-1}\right)
$$

is the vibrational energy of the lattice at angular frequency $\omega$.

Since the thermal expansion occurs as a result of the vibration, in the absence of applied pressure, we have $p=0$ and so

$$
\frac{\partial U}{\partial V}=\gamma \frac{\bar{E}}{V} .
$$

Expanding $\frac{\partial U}{\partial V}$ around the static lattice volume $V_{0}$ by Taylor's expansion, we have

$$
\begin{aligned}
\frac{\partial U}{\partial V} &=\left(\frac{\partial U}{\partial V}\right)_{V_{0}}+\left(\frac{\partial^{2} U}{\partial V^{2}}\right)_{V_{0}} \delta V+\cdots \\
& \approx\left(\frac{\partial^{2} U}{\partial V^{2}}\right)_{V_{0}} \delta V
\end{aligned}
$$

Using the definition of the volume elastic modulus of a static lattice,

$$
B=V_{0}\left(\frac{\partial^{2} U}{\partial V^{2}}\right)_{V_{0}},
$$

we have

$$
\frac{\partial U}{\partial V}=B \frac{\delta V}{V_{0}}=\gamma \frac{\bar{E}}{V},
$$

or

$$
\frac{\delta V}{V_{0}}=\gamma \frac{\bar{E}}{B V}
$$

at temperature $T$

(d) Differentiating the two sides of the last equation with respect to $T$, we have as $\frac{\partial \bar{E}}{\partial T} \gg \frac{\partial V}{\partial T}$,

$$
\frac{1}{V_{0}} \frac{\partial \delta V}{\partial T}=\frac{\gamma}{B V} \frac{\partial \bar{E}}{\partial T}=\frac{\gamma}{B V} C_{v} .
$$

Then from the definition of the coefficient of volume thermal expansion

$$
\alpha=\frac{1}{V_{0}}\left(\frac{\partial \delta V}{\partial T}\right)_{p},
$$

we find the Grüneisen relation

$$
\alpha=\gamma \frac{C_{v}}{B V} .
$$

It can be seen that if the vibration of a crystal were strictly linear, $\gamma=0$ and hence $\alpha=0$, i.e., there would be no thermal expansion. Since thermal expansion does occur, the coefficient of thermal expansion gives a measure of the nonlinearity of crystal lattice vibration.

\section{5}

Neutron diffraction may be used to measure $\omega$ vs. $k$ for an excitation in a crystalline solid. To describe this, assume the crystal symmetry is known, write down the energy and momentum conservation laws for the diffraction, and then indicate what parameters must be measured in order to obtain $\omega$ vs. $k$.

(Wisconsin)

\section{Solution:}

Let $M$ be the mass of a neutron, $\mathbf{p}$ and $\mathbf{p}^{\prime}$ be the momenta and $\mathbf{k}$ and $\mathbf{k}^{\prime}$ be the wave vectors of the incident and scattered neutrons respectively. Then $\mathbf{p}=\hbar \mathbf{k}, \mathbf{p}^{\prime}=\hbar \mathbf{k}^{\prime}$ and the corresponding energies are $\frac{p^{2}}{2 M}, \frac{p^{\prime 2}}{2 M}$. Energy conservation gives

$$
\frac{\hbar^{2} k^{2}}{2 M}=\frac{\hbar^{2} k^{2}}{2 M} \pm \hbar \omega(\mathbf{q})
$$

and momentum conservation gives

$$
\hbar \mathbf{k}=\hbar \mathbf{k}^{\prime} \pm \hbar \mathbf{q}-\hbar \mathbf{G},
$$

where $\mathbf{G}$ is an appropriate reciprocal lattice vector, and $\mathbf{q}$ is the phonon wave vector generated $(+)$ or absorbed $(-)$ in the process.

To obtain $\omega$ vs. $k$ from the above equation it is necessary to measure the energy difference between the incident and scattered neutrons as a function of the scattering direction $\mathbf{k}-\mathbf{k}^{\prime}$. 

\section{6}

Consider the presence of the point defects in the form of vacancies in thermal equilibrium in a monatomic simple cubic crystal of $N$ sites.

(a) Write down or evaluate the number $n$ of such defects in a crystal held at temperature $T$ if it costs an energy $E$ to create one vacancy. (Assume $n \ll N$.)

We now consider the effect of lattice vibrations (i.e., phonons) on the result. Mark a simple Einstein theory of normal modes of the crystal with vacancies, i.e., treat each ion as an independent oscillator, choose two different frequencies, say $\omega$ and $\omega^{\prime}$, depending on whether a given ion has one of its six nearest-neighbor sites vacant.

(b) Which of the two frequencies do you expect to be larger?

(c) How is the number $n$ modified in the presence of the phonons?

(Princeton)

\section{Solution:}

(a) Neglecting the effect of phonons, the number of vacancies at thermal equilibrium at temperature $T$ is

$$
n=N e^{-E / k_{\mathrm{B}} T},
$$

where $E$ is the energy required to create one vacancy.

(b) When there are vacancies surrounding an atom, the restoring force it suffers when displaced from the equilibrium position becomes smaller. Consequently $\omega^{\prime}$ is smaller than $\omega$.

(c) Consider now the effect of phonons. As $n \ll N$, surrounding each vacancy there are six nearest-neighbor atoms whose vibrational frequency has changed from $\omega$ to $\omega^{\prime}$. Since each site represents three modes of oscillation, there are $3(N-6 n)$ phonons of frequency $\omega$ and $3 \times 6 n$ phonons of frequency $\omega^{\prime}$. The contribution of a phonon of frequency $\omega$ to the free energy of the crystal is (Problem 1024)

$$
k_{\mathrm{B}} T\left[\frac{\hbar \omega}{2 k_{\mathrm{B}} T}+\ln \left(1-e^{\hbar \omega / k_{\mathrm{B}} T}\right)\right] \approx k_{\mathrm{B}} T \ln \frac{\hbar \omega}{k_{\mathrm{B}} T}
$$

in the classical limit $\hbar \omega \ll k_{\mathrm{B}} T$. Hence the free energy of the crystal is

$$
F=U_{0}+n E+3(N-6 n) k_{\mathrm{B}} T \ln \frac{\hbar \omega}{k_{\mathrm{B}} T}+18 n k_{\mathrm{B}} T \ln \frac{\hbar \omega^{\prime}}{k_{\mathrm{B}} T}-k_{\mathrm{B}} T \ln \frac{N !}{(N-n) ! n !},
$$

where $U_{0}$ is the internal energy of the crystal at $0 \mathrm{~K}$, and the last term is contributed by the mixing entropy.

At equilibrium,

$$
\left(\frac{\partial F}{\partial n}\right)_{T}=0
$$

or

$$
\frac{\partial}{\partial n}\left[\frac{n E}{k_{\mathrm{B}} T}+18 n \ln \frac{\omega^{\prime}}{\omega}+\ln (N-n) !+\ln n !\right]=0 .
$$

Using Stirling's formula

$$
\ln x ! \approx x \ln x
$$

for $x \rightarrow \infty$, as $N, n$ are both large even though $n \ll N$ we have

$$
\frac{E}{k_{\mathrm{B}} T}+18 \ln \frac{\omega^{\prime}}{\omega}+\ln \frac{n}{N}=0,
$$

or

$$
n=N\left(\frac{\omega}{\omega^{\prime}}\right)^{18} e^{-\frac{E}{k_{\mathrm{B}} T}},
$$

in the presence of phonons.

\section{7}

A binary alloy consists of $N_{\mathrm{A}}$ metal atoms A and $N_{\mathrm{B}}$ metal atoms B. The atoms form a simple cubic lattice in which each atom interacts only with the six nearest-neighbor atoms. The interaction energies are $-J(J>0)$ for neighboring combination $\mathrm{A}-\mathrm{A}$ or $\mathrm{B}-\mathrm{B}$, and $+J$ for $\mathrm{A}-\mathrm{B}$ combination.

(a) Calculate the mixing entropy of the alloy. Assume that $N=N_{\mathrm{A}}+$ $N_{\mathrm{B}} \gg 1$

(b) Compute the total interaction energy assuming that the atoms are randomly distributed among the sites.

(c) Compute the free energy of the system, $F$, as a function of $x$, where $N_{\mathrm{A}}=N(1+x) / 2$ and $N_{\mathrm{B}}=N(1-x) / 2$. Expand $F(x)$ up to the 4th order in $x$. Show that there is a critical temperature $T_{\mathrm{c}}$ above and below which the curve $F(x)$ has different characteristic shapes. Determine $T_{\mathrm{c}}$ and sketch $F$ as a function of $x$ for $T>T_{\mathrm{c}}, T=T_{\mathrm{c}}$ and $T<T_{\mathrm{c}}$.

(d) For $T<T_{\mathrm{c}}$, the system with a composition $|x|<x_{p}(T)$ becomes unstable and separates into two phases having different compositions. Using the result of (c) find $x_{p}(T)$ and the compositions of the two separated phases.

$(M I T)$

\section{Solution:}

(a) The mixing entropy is

$$
S=k_{\mathrm{B}} \ln \Omega,
$$

where

$$
\Omega=\frac{N !}{N_{\mathrm{A}} ! N_{\mathrm{B}} !} .
$$

Using Stirling's formula

$$
\ln N ! \approx N \ln N,
$$

we have

$$
\ln \Omega \approx N_{\mathrm{A}} \ln \left(1+\frac{N_{\mathrm{B}}}{N_{\mathrm{A}}}\right)+N_{\mathrm{B}} \ln \left(1+\frac{N_{\mathrm{A}}}{N_{\mathrm{B}}}\right)
$$

and hence

$$
\begin{aligned}
S &=k_{\mathrm{B}} N_{\mathrm{A}} \ln \left(1+\frac{N_{\mathrm{B}}}{N_{\mathrm{A}}}\right)+k_{\mathrm{B}} N_{\mathrm{B}} \ln \left(1+\frac{N_{\mathrm{A}}}{N_{\mathrm{B}}}\right) \\
&=N k_{\mathrm{B}} \ln 2-\frac{N}{2} k_{\mathrm{B}}[(1+x) \ln (1+x)+(1-x) \ln (1-x)] .
\end{aligned}
$$

(b) Since on average each atom will have as its nearest neighbors $\frac{N_{\mathrm{A}}}{N}$ A-atoms and $\frac{N_{\mathrm{B}}}{N}$ B-atoms and the proportions of $\mathrm{A}, \mathrm{B}$ atom in the lattice are $\frac{N_{\mathrm{A}}}{N}, \frac{N_{\mathrm{B}}}{N}$ respectively, the mean interaction energy $U$ of an atom with its nearest-neighbor atoms is then

$$
\begin{aligned}
U &=6\left[(-J) \frac{N_{\mathrm{A}}}{N}+J \frac{N_{\mathrm{B}}}{N}\right] \frac{N_{\mathrm{B}}}{N}+6\left[(-J) \frac{N_{\mathrm{B}}}{N}+J \frac{N_{\mathrm{A}}}{N}\right] \frac{N_{\mathrm{B}}}{N} \\
&=6\left[(-J) \frac{N_{\mathrm{A}}^{2}+N_{\mathrm{B}}^{2}}{N^{2}}+J \frac{2 N_{\mathrm{A}} N_{\mathrm{B}}}{N^{2}}\right] \\
&=-\frac{6 J}{N^{2}}\left(N_{\mathrm{A}}-N_{\mathrm{B}}\right)^{2} .
\end{aligned}
$$

Hence the total interaction energy $E$ is

$$
\begin{aligned}
E &=\frac{N}{2} U=-\frac{3 J}{N}\left(N_{\mathrm{A}}-N_{\mathrm{B}}\right)^{2} \\
&=-3 N J x^{2} .
\end{aligned}
$$

(c) The free energy of the system $F$ is

$$
\begin{aligned}
F &=E-T S \\
&=-3 N J x^{2}-N k_{\mathrm{B}} T \ln 2+\frac{N k_{\mathrm{B}} T x}{2} \ln \frac{1+x}{1-x}+\frac{N k_{\mathrm{B}} T}{2} \ln \left(1-x^{2}\right) \\
&=-N k_{\mathrm{B}} T \ln 2+\left(\frac{1}{2} N k_{\mathrm{B}} T-3 N J\right) x^{2}+\frac{1}{12} N k_{\mathrm{B}} T x^{4}+\cdots,
\end{aligned}
$$

where we have used

$$
\begin{aligned}
&\ln (1+x)=x-\frac{1}{2} x^{2}+\frac{1}{3} x^{3}-\frac{1}{4} x^{4}+\cdots, \\
&\ln \frac{1+x}{1-x}=2\left(x+\frac{x^{3}}{3}+\frac{x^{5}}{5}+\cdots\right)
\end{aligned}
$$

To find the critical temperature $T_{\mathrm{c}}$ at which $F$ changes shape, consider $\frac{\partial F}{\partial x}$ and $\frac{\partial^{2} F}{\partial x^{2}}$ at a point, say $x=0$. These show that $T_{\mathrm{c}}$ is given by

$$
\frac{N}{2} k_{\mathrm{B}} T_{\mathrm{c}}-3 N J=0,
$$

i.e.,

$$
T_{\mathrm{c}}=\frac{6 J}{k_{\mathrm{B}}} .
$$

In fact

$$
\left(\frac{\partial^{2} F}{\partial x^{2}}\right)_{x=0}\left\{\begin{array}{lll}
=0, & T=T_{\mathrm{c}} \\
>0, & T>T_{\mathrm{c}}, & F(0)=F_{\min }, \\
<0, & T<T_{\mathrm{c}}, & F(0)=F_{\max },
\end{array}\right.
$$

showing that $T_{\mathrm{c}}$ is the critical transition temperature. $F(x)$ is plotted for $T>T_{\mathrm{c}}$ and $T<T_{\mathrm{c}}$ in Fig. $1.11$.

(d) As shown in Fig. 1.11, when $T<T_{\mathrm{c}}$ the free energy has a minimum at $x=x_{p}(T)$. The free energy, being greater than the minimum for $|x|<x_{p}(T)$, the system is unstable. In order to find $x_{p}(T)$, let $\frac{\partial F}{\partial x}=0$, i.e.

$$
N k_{\mathrm{B}} T-6 N J+\frac{1}{3} N k_{\mathrm{B}} T x^{2}=0,
$$

and obtain

$$
x_{p}(T)=\pm \sqrt{\frac{3\left(T_{\mathrm{c}}-T\right)}{T}} .
$$



![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_b8e2b1af07958c90efe7g-55.jpg?height=436&width=1065&top_left_y=381&top_left_x=538)

Fig. $1.11$

The compositions of the two separated phases are thus given by

$$
x_{1}=\sqrt{\frac{3\left(T_{\mathrm{c}}-T\right)}{T}}, \quad x_{2}=-\sqrt{\frac{3\left(T_{\mathrm{c}}-T\right)}{T}} .
$$

\subsection{ELECTRON THEORY, ENERGY BANDS AND SEMICONDUCTORS (1028-1051)}

\section{8}

The free electron model for a metal assumes that the conduction electrons can be approximated by a gas of free electrons where the only important parameters for the gas are $n$, the number density of electrons, and $\tau$, the time between collisions. Show that in this model the electrical conductivity of a metal can be expressed as

$$
\sigma=\frac{n e^{2} \tau}{m} .
$$

Estimate the collision time $\tau$ for an electron in copper. The resistivity of copper metal is $1.7 \times 10^{-6} \mathrm{ohm} \mathrm{cm}$ and the atomic density of copper is $8.5 \times 10^{22}$ atoms $/ \mathrm{cm}^{3}$.

(Wisconsin)

\section{Solution:}

For a free electron the momentum $\mathbf{p}$ and wave vector $\mathbf{k}$ are related by

$$
\mathbf{p}=\hbar \mathbf{k} .
$$

The equation of its motion in an applied force field $\mathbf{F}$ is given by Newton's second law as

$$
\mathbf{F}=\frac{d \mathbf{p}}{d t}=\hbar \frac{d \mathbf{k}}{d t} .
$$

While the wave vectors of all the electrons increase parallel to the applied force $\mathbf{F}$, an equilibrium state will be reached with the electrons reverting to their original states by collision with the lattice ions in a "relaxation time" for collision $\tau$, given by

$$
\delta \mathbf{k}=\frac{\tau}{\hbar} \mathbf{F},
$$

where $\delta \mathbf{k}$ is the change of the wave vector $\mathbf{k}$ from the equilibrium value. The corresponding change in velocity is

$$
\delta \mathbf{v}=\frac{\delta \mathbf{p}}{m}=\frac{\hbar}{m} \delta \mathbf{k}=\frac{\tau \mathbf{F}}{m} .
$$
