If the applied electric field is $\mathcal{E}$, the force acting on each electron is

$$
\mathbf{F}=-e \mathcal{E} .
$$

Hence the induced current density is

$$
\mathbf{j}=-n e \delta \mathbf{v}=\frac{n e^{2} \tau}{m} \mathcal{E}=\sigma \mathcal{E},
$$

where

$$
\sigma=\frac{n e^{2} \tau}{m}
$$

is the conductivity of the metal, Ohm's law being assumed. If each copper atom contributes one free electron, then $n=8.5 \times 10^{28} \mathrm{~m}^{-3}$ and

$$
\begin{aligned}
\tau &=\frac{m \sigma}{n e^{2}}=\frac{m}{n e^{2} \rho} \\
&=\frac{9.11 \times 10^{-31}}{8.5 \times 10^{28} \times\left(1.6 \times 10^{-19}\right)^{2} \times 1.7 \times 10^{-8}}=2.5 \times 10^{-14} \mathrm{~s} .
\end{aligned}
$$

\section{9}

Metallic sodium crystalizes in body-centered cubic form, the length of the cube being $4.25 \times 10^{-8} \mathrm{~cm}$. Find the concentration of conduction electrons. Assume one conduction electron per atom. Adopting the freeelectron Fermi gas model for the conduction electrons, derive an expression for the Fermi energy (at $0 \mathrm{~K}$ ) and show that it depends only on the concentration of conduction electrons, but not on the mass of the crystal.

(Wisconsin )

\section{Solution:}

A bcc lattice contains $\frac{2}{a^{3}}$ atoms per unit volume, so the concentration of conduction electrons is

$$
n=\frac{2}{\left(4.25 \times 10^{-8}\right)^{3}}=2.6 \times 10^{22} \mathrm{~cm}^{-3} .
$$

According to Fermi's free-electron gas model, the number of electrons in a volume $V$ with energies in the range $E$ to $E+d E$ is

$$
d N=C E^{1 / 2} f(E) d E
$$

with $c=4 \pi V(2 m)^{3 / 2} / \hbar^{3}, m$ being the electron mass. At $T=0 \mathrm{~K}$, the distribution function $f(E)$ is

$$
f(E)= \begin{cases}1 & \text { for } E \leq E_{F}, \\ 0 & \text { for } E>E_{F} .\end{cases}
$$

Hence the total number of electrons in $V$ is

$$
N=\int_{0}^{E_{\mathrm{F} 0}} C E^{1 / 2} d E=\frac{2}{3} C E_{\mathrm{F} 0}^{\frac{3}{2}} .
$$

where $E_{\mathrm{F} 0}$ is the Fermi energy at $T=0 \mathrm{~K}$. As $n=\frac{N}{V}$, the Fermi energy at $0 \mathrm{~K}$ is

$$
E_{\mathrm{F} 0}=\frac{h^{2}}{2 m}\left(\frac{3 n}{8 \pi}\right)^{\frac{2}{3}}=\frac{\hbar^{2}}{2 m}\left(3 \pi^{2} n\right)^{\frac{2}{3}} .
$$

For metallic sodium we have

$$
E_{\mathrm{F} 0}=\frac{\left(1.05 \times 10^{-27}\right)^{2}}{2 \times 9.1 \times 10^{-28}} \times \frac{\left(3 \pi^{2} \times 26 \times 10^{21}\right)^{2 / 3}}{1.6 \times 10^{-12}}=3.18 \mathrm{eV}
$$

The expression of $E_{\mathrm{F} 0}$ shows that it depends only on the concentration of conduction electrons, but not on the mass of the crystal. 

\section{0}

Figure $1.12$ is a rough plot of the electrical resistance of a solid $\left(\operatorname{ErRhB}_{4}\right.$, impure, but not purposely damaged).

(a) From the graph, is this material a metal or an insulator?

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_b8e2b1af07958c90efe7g-58.jpg?height=526&width=784&top_left_y=637&top_left_x=711)

Fig. $1.12$

(b) Describe the principal physical processes that account for the resistivity, and explain the temperature dependence of the resistivity in the following three temperature regions:

(1) $T$ very near $0 \mathrm{~K}$,

(2) $T$ near $25 \mathrm{~K}$,

(3) $T$ near $300 \mathrm{~K}$.

(c) Estimate the mean free path and mean free time at $T=0 \mathrm{~K}$ and $T=300 \mathrm{~K}$. Is the material a good metal?

(Useful numbers: $n=10^{23} \mathrm{~cm}^{-3}, e=5 \times 10^{-10} \mathrm{esu}, m=10^{-27} \mathrm{~g}$, $v_{\mathrm{F}}=10^{8} \mathrm{~cm} / \mathrm{s}, 1(\Omega \mathrm{cm})^{-1}=9 \times 10^{11}$ esu. $)$

(Princeton)

\section{Solution:}

(a) As seen from Fig. $1.12$ the electrical resistivity of this material generally increases with increasing temperature and is still small at $T=$ $300 \mathrm{~K},\left(\rho \sim 10^{-4} \Omega \mathrm{cm}\right)$. Hence the material is a metal.

(b) If the lattice of a metal is perfect and there are no lattice vibrations, electron waves would pass through the lattice unscattered, without resistance. In an actual metal the free electrons suffer scattering by the distortions of the periodic potential field due to thermal vibrations of the lattice atoms and by such structural irregularities as impurities and defects in the crystal, giving rise to an electrical resistivity. Thus the resistivity of a metal can be considered as the sum of two parts:

$$
\rho=\rho_{\text {phonon }}+\rho_{\text {impurity }} .
$$

(1) At $T$ very near $0 \mathrm{~K}$, the resistivity of a metal is dominated by the residual resistivity $\rho_{\text {impurity }}$, which is independent of temperature. Thus $\rho \approx \rho_{\text {impurity }}=$ constant. The curve in Fig. $1.12$ gives $\rho \approx 27 \times 10^{-6} \Omega \mathrm{cm}$, essentially constant near $0 \mathrm{~K}$.

(2) At $T$ near $25 \mathrm{~K}$, the resistance is caused mainly by scattering of the electrons by the lattice phonons although scattering by the impurities still occurs. At such low temperatures only long-wave acoustic phonons can be excited whose momenta $\hbar q$ are very small. The change of the momenta of the electrons along the direction of the electric field due to scattering by the phonons is also very small, resulting in small scattering angles.

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_b8e2b1af07958c90efe7g-59.jpg?height=312&width=678&top_left_y=1259&top_left_x=707)

Fig. $1.13$

Consider such a scattering. The change of the electron momentum $\hbar k_{\mathrm{F}}$ in the direction of the electric field is (Fig. 1.13)

$$
\hbar \delta k_{\mathrm{F}}=\hbar k_{\mathrm{F}}(1-\cos \theta) \approx \hbar k_{\mathrm{F}} \cdot \frac{\theta^{2}}{2},
$$

i.e., the momentum decreases by a fraction $\frac{\theta^{2}}{2}$ after each scattering. For $\hbar k_{\mathrm{F}}$ to vanish, a number $r$ of collisions are needed which is given by

$$
\hbar k_{\mathrm{F}}\left(1-\frac{\theta^{2}}{2}\right)^{r} \approx \hbar k_{\mathrm{F}}\left(1-\frac{r \theta^{2}}{2}\right) \approx 0,
$$

or

$$
r \approx \frac{2}{\theta^{2}} \approx \frac{2 k_{\mathrm{F}}^{2}}{q^{2}},
$$

as $\theta \approx \frac{q}{k_{F}}$ (see Fig. 1.13). These $r$ collisions are to be counted as one effective collision which deflects the electron from its original path. The phonon energy is $\hbar \omega=\hbar v_{\mathrm{s}} q \approx k_{\mathrm{B}} T$, where $v_{\mathrm{s}}$ is the speed of sound. Thus $q \propto T$ and $r \propto T^{-2}$. The number of collisions with the phonons is proportional to the number density $n_{\mathrm{s}}$ of the latter. Debye's theory gives

$$
n_{\mathrm{s}}=\int_{0}^{\omega_{\mathrm{D}}} \frac{B \omega^{2}}{e^{\hbar \omega / k_{\mathrm{B}} T}-1} d \omega \propto \frac{1}{x^{3}} \int_{0}^{x} \frac{y^{2} d y}{e^{y}-1},
$$

where $x=\frac{\Theta_{\mathrm{D}}}{T}, \Theta_{\mathrm{D}}$ being the Debye temperature. For low temperatures $(T \sim 25 \mathrm{~K}) T \ll \Theta_{\mathrm{D}}, x \rightarrow \infty$ and

$$
n_{\mathrm{s}} \propto \frac{1}{x^{3}} \propto T^{3} .
$$

Hence the probability for effective scattering for electron is

$$
\frac{1}{\tau} \propto \frac{\text { phonon density }}{r} \propto T^{5},
$$

so that the electrical resistivity is

$$
\rho \propto \frac{1}{\tau} \propto T^{5} .
$$

(3) For $T$ near $300 \mathrm{~K}, T \gg \Theta_{\mathrm{D}}$ and Debye's theory gives

$$
n_{\mathrm{s}} \propto \frac{1}{x^{3}} \int_{0}^{x} \frac{y^{2} d y}{y} \propto T .
$$

Furthermore, as the phonon momentum is high, each scattering is effective in deflecting the electron. Hence

$$
\rho \propto \frac{1}{\tau} \propto n_{\mathrm{s}} \propto T .
$$

(c) The time between collisions or the mean free time $\tau$ is related to the conductivity $\sigma$ by (Problem 1028)

$$
\sigma=\frac{n e^{2} \tau}{m} .
$$

Hence

$$
\tau=\frac{m}{n e^{2} \rho},
$$

where $n$ is the electron number density and $\rho=\frac{1}{\sigma}$ is the resistivity of the metal. The mean free path is accordingly

$$
l=v_{\mathrm{F}} \tau .
$$

(1) For $T=0 \mathrm{~K}$, Fig. $1.12$ gives $\rho \approx 27 \times 10^{-6} \Omega \mathrm{cm}$. Hence

$$
\begin{aligned}
\tau &=\frac{m}{n e^{2} \rho}=\frac{10^{-27} \times 9 \times 10^{11}}{10^{23} \times\left(5 \times 10^{-10}\right)^{2} \times 27 \times 10^{-6}}=1.3 \times 10^{-15} \mathrm{~s} \\
l &=10^{8} \times 1.3 \times 10^{-15}=1.3 \times 10^{-7} \mathrm{~cm} .
\end{aligned}
$$

(2) For $T=300 \mathrm{~K}$, Fig. $1.12$ gives $\rho \approx 120 \times 10^{-6} \Omega \mathrm{cm}$. Hence

$$
\begin{aligned}
\tau &=\frac{27}{120} \times 1.3 \times 10^{-15}=3 \times 10^{-16} \mathrm{~s}, \\
l &=10^{8} \times 3 \times 10^{-16}=3 \times 10^{-8} \mathrm{~cm}=3 \AA .
\end{aligned}
$$

At room temperature $T \approx 300 \mathrm{~K}$, the mean free path of a good metal is $\approx 300-400 \AA$. The mean free path of this material is much smaller. Since $\sigma \propto l$, it is not a good metal.

\section{1}

Graphs of the temperature dependence of the electrical resistivity of $\mathrm{Cu}, \mathrm{Ge}$, and $\mathrm{NaCl}$ are given in Fig. 1.14. They describe "real" high-purity samples: purity $>99.9 \%$, but not $100 \%$. In each case describe briefly the essential mechanism of conduction and explain the temperature dependence illustrated. (Note that the axes of the three graphs are different.)

(Columbia $)$

\section{Solution:}

(1) $\mathrm{Cu}$ is a metal. The graph of its electric resistivity vs. temperature shows: at $T \approx 0 \mathrm{~K}$, the resistivity is small and essentially constant; for $0<T \ll \Theta_{\mathrm{D}}, \rho \propto T^{5} ;$ for $T>\Theta_{\mathrm{D}}, \rho \propto T$. Such behavior is typical of a metal and arises from the relation between the resistivity $\rho$ and the probability $P$ of scattering of the passing electron:

$$
\rho=\frac{m}{n e^{2}} P,
$$



![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_b8e2b1af07958c90efe7g-62.jpg?height=1057&width=919&top_left_y=342&top_left_x=668)

Fig. $1.14$

where $m$ is the electron mass and $P=P_{\text {phonon }}+P_{\text {impurity }}$ (Problem 1030 (b))

(a) $T \approx 0 \mathrm{~K}$

At this temperature, $P_{\text {phonon }} \rightarrow 0$ and $\rho$ is contributed almost entirely by $P_{\text {impurity }}$, which is independent of temperature. Thus $\rho$ is at its minimum and essentially constant.

(b) $0<T \ll \Theta_{D}$, the Debye temperature of $\mathrm{Cu}$.

At such low temperatures, $\rho$ is mainly determined by $P_{\text {phonon }}$, which is proportional to (Problem 1030)

$\frac{\text { density of phonons }}{\text { number of collision making up one effective scattering }} \propto \frac{T^{3}}{T^{-2}}=T^{5}$.

Hence $\rho \propto T^{5}$.

(c) $T>\Theta_{\mathrm{D}}$

$\rho$ is almost entirely contributed by $P_{\text {phonon }}$, which at such high temperatures is proportional to the density of phonons which is $\propto T$. Hence $\rho \propto T$. Note that at such temperatures, the phonons have large momenta and every collision causes some deflection of the passing electrons.

(2) Ge is a semiconductor. The graph of its electric resistivity vs. temperature shows: at low temperatures, $\rho$ decreases with increasing temperature; for intermediate temperatures, $\rho$ increases with increasing temperature; at high temperatures, $\rho$ decreases rapidly with increasing temperature. This behavior is explained as follows.

(a) At low temperatures, impurity excitation plays the dominant role. The number of excited charge carriers from the impurities increases with temperature, so that $\rho$ decreases with rising temperature.

(b) In the middle temperature range, all the impurity atoms are ionized and so the number of charge carriers does not change with temperature. However, scattering of the passing electrons by phonons becomes more frequent as the temperature increases, leading to a lowering of mobility and hence increasing $\rho$.

(c) At high temperatures, intrinsic excitation starts and the concentration of charge carriers rapidly increases with rising temperature, causing $\rho$ to drop quickly.

(3) $\mathrm{NaCl}$ is an ionic crystal. The semi-log $\rho-T$ curve consists of linear segments with different slopes for low and high temperatures. The reason is as follows.

In an ionic crystal electrical conduction by the holes is the dominant process, the number of holes being proportional to $e^{-u_{0} / k_{\mathrm{B}} T}$, where $u_{0}$ is the activation energy. At low temperatures, conduction by impurity holes is the dominant mechanism; while at high temperatures, the conduction is due mainly to holes resulting from thermal motion of the ions. As their activation energies are different, the semi-log graph shows two lines of different slopes.

\section{2}

A piece of metal with $\mu=1$ is placed in a static uniform magnetic field $\mathbf{B}=B_{0} \hat{\mathbf{z}}$. The conduction electrons can be treated as a free electron gas with a scattering time $\tau$ and number density $n$.

(a) Derive an expression for the resistivity tensor of this metal.

(b) Using the result of (a) derive the low-frequency dispersion relation for waves propagation along $\hat{\mathbf{z}}$. Neglect $\frac{1}{\tau}$ and $\omega$ in comparison with the cyclotron frequency $\omega_{\mathrm{c}}$. (Derive a wave equation for $\mathbf{j}$, the current density.)

(Princeton)

\section{Solution:}

(a) In the scattering of a conduction electron of momentum $m \mathbf{v}$, the average change of momentum is $\frac{1}{2}(0+2 m \mathbf{v})=m \mathbf{v}$. If on average it suffers $\frac{1}{\tau}$ scatterings per unit time, it is subjected to a damping force $-\frac{m \mathbf{v}}{\tau}$. Hence the equation of motion of a conduction electron is

$$
m \frac{d \mathbf{v}}{d t}=-e \mathbf{E}-e \mathbf{v} \times \mathbf{B}-m \frac{\mathbf{v}}{\tau} .
$$

As

$$
\mathbf{j}=-n e \mathbf{v}
$$

the above becomes

$$
-\frac{m}{n e} \frac{d \mathbf{j}}{d t}=-e \mathbf{E}+\frac{\mathbf{j} \times \mathbf{B}}{n}+\frac{m \mathbf{j}}{\tau n e} .
$$

Consider a solution of the form

$$
\mathbf{j}=\mathbf{j}_{0} e^{-i \omega t} .
$$

Substitution gives

$$
\mathbf{E}=\frac{m}{n e^{2}}\left(\frac{1}{\tau}-i \omega\right) \mathbf{j}+\frac{\mathbf{j} \times \mathbf{B}}{n e}
$$

and in particular

$$
\begin{aligned}
E_{k} &=\frac{m}{n e^{2}}\left(\frac{1}{\tau}-i \omega\right) j_{k}+\frac{\varepsilon_{k l q} j_{l} B_{q}}{n e} \\
&=\left[\frac{m}{n e^{2}}\left(\frac{1}{\tau}-i \omega\right) \delta_{k l}+\frac{\varepsilon_{k l q} B_{q}}{n e}\right] j_{l}=\rho_{k l} j_{l}, \quad \text { say }
\end{aligned}
$$

where 

$$
\begin{aligned}
&\delta_{k l}= \begin{cases}1 & \text { if } k=l, \\
0 & \text { if } k \neq l,\end{cases} \\
&\varepsilon_{k l q}= \begin{cases}1 & \text { if } k \neq l \neq q \text { and } k, l, q \text { are in an even permutation } \\
-1 & \text { of } 123, \\
& \text { if } k \neq l \neq q \text { and } k, l, q \text { are in an odd permutation } \\
0 & \text { of } 123,\end{cases}
\end{aligned}
$$

and Einstein's summation rule is used.

If Ohm's law

$$
\mathbf{j}=\sigma \mathbf{E},
$$

or

$$
\mathbf{E}=\rho \mathbf{j},
$$

holds, a resistivity $\rho$ can be defined. The above shows that $\rho$ has the from of a matrix

$$
\rho=\left(\begin{array}{ccc}
\frac{m}{n e^{2}}\left(\frac{1}{\tau}-i \omega\right) & \frac{B_{0}}{n e} & 0 \\
-\frac{B_{0}}{n e} & \frac{m}{n e^{2}}\left(\frac{1}{\tau}-i \omega\right) & 0 \\
0 & 0 & \frac{m}{n e^{2}}\left(\frac{1}{\tau}-i \omega\right)
\end{array}\right)
$$

(b) In the metal,

$$
\mathbf{D}=\epsilon_{0} \mathbf{E}+\mathbf{P}, \quad \nabla \cdot \mathbf{D}=0, \quad \mathbf{B}=\mu_{0} \mathbf{H}
$$

and Maxwell's equations

$$
\nabla \times \mathbf{E}=-\dot{\mathbf{B}}, \quad \nabla \times \mathbf{H}=\dot{\mathbf{D}}
$$

give

$$
\mu_{0} \ddot{\mathbf{D}}=\nabla^{2} \mathbf{E}-\nabla(\nabla \cdot \mathbf{E}) .
$$

An electromagnetic wave propagating along $\hat{\mathbf{z}}$ can be represented by

$$
\mathbf{E}=\mathbf{E}_{0} e^{i(k z-\omega t)},
$$

from which

$$
\frac{\partial}{\partial t}=-i \omega, \quad \frac{\partial}{\partial z}=i k, \quad \frac{\partial}{\partial x}=\frac{\partial}{\partial y}=0 .
$$

Thus we have

$$
-\omega^{2} \mu_{0}\left(\epsilon_{0} \mathbf{E}+\mathbf{P}\right)=-k^{2} \mathbf{E}+k^{2} E_{z} \hat{\mathbf{z}}
$$

As

$$
\nabla \cdot \mathbf{D}=\frac{\partial D_{z}}{\partial z}=i k\left(\epsilon_{0} E_{z}+P_{z}\right)=0
$$

or

$$
E_{z}=-\frac{P_{z}}{\epsilon_{0}},
$$

the above can be written as

$$
\left(k^{2}-\frac{\omega^{2}}{c^{2}}\right) \mathbf{E}=\mu_{0} \omega^{2} \mathbf{P}-\frac{k^{2}}{\epsilon_{0}} P_{z} \hat{\mathbf{z}}
$$

or

$$
\mathbf{E}=\frac{1}{\epsilon_{0} \chi}\left[\mathbf{P}-(\chi+1) P_{z} \hat{\mathbf{z}}\right],
$$

where $\chi=\left(\frac{c k}{\omega}\right)^{2}-1$. Hence

$$
\left(\begin{array}{l}
E_{x} \\
E_{y} \\
E_{z}
\end{array}\right)=\frac{1}{\epsilon_{0} \chi}\left(\begin{array}{ccc}
1 & 0 & 0 \\
0 & 1 & 0 \\
0 & 0 & -\chi
\end{array}\right)\left(\begin{array}{l}
P_{x} \\
P_{y} \\
P_{z}
\end{array}\right) \text {. }
$$

The polarization current density $\mathbf{j}$ is given by

$$
\mathbf{j}=\dot{\mathbf{P}}=-i \omega \mathbf{P} \text {. }
$$

Writing Eqs. (1) and (2) as matrix equations

$$
E=R J, \quad E=A P=B J,
$$

where $E, P, J$, are column matrices, $R, A, B$ are square matrices. Equation (3) then gives

$$
B=\frac{i}{\omega} A .
$$

As

$$
(R-B) J=0 \text {, }
$$

for $J$ not identically zero, we require the determinant

$$
|R-B|=0,
$$

i.e.,

$$
\left[\frac{m}{n e^{2}}\left(\frac{1}{\tau}-i \omega\right)+\frac{i}{\epsilon_{0} \omega}\right]\left\{\left[\frac{m}{n e^{2}}\left(\frac{1}{\tau}-i \omega\right)-\frac{i}{\epsilon_{0} \chi \omega}\right]^{2}+\left(\frac{B_{0}}{n e}\right)^{2}\right\}=0 .
$$

The first factor of the left-hand side, which involves only the parameters $n, \tau$ of the electron gas and the angular frequency $\omega$ of the propagating wave, is generally not zero. Hence

$$
\left[\frac{m}{n e^{2}}\left(\frac{1}{\tau}-i \omega\right)-\frac{i}{\epsilon_{0} \chi \omega}\right]^{2}=-\left(\frac{B_{0}}{n e}\right)^{2} .
$$

Defining the plasma frequency

$$
\omega_{\mathrm{p}}=\sqrt{\frac{n e^{2}}{\epsilon_{0} m}}
$$

and the cyclotron frequency

$$
\omega_{\mathrm{c}}=\frac{e B_{0}}{m},
$$

we can write the above as

$$
\left[\left(\frac{1}{\tau}-i \omega\right)-\frac{i \omega_{\mathrm{p}}^{2}}{\chi \omega}\right]^{2}=-\omega_{\mathrm{c}}^{2} .
$$

As $\frac{1}{\tau}$ and $\omega$ can be neglected in comparison with $\omega_{\mathrm{c}}$ this gives

$$
-\frac{i \omega_{\mathrm{p}}^{2}}{\chi \omega}=\pm i \omega_{\mathrm{c}} .
$$

Hence the low-frequency dispersion relations are

$$
-\omega_{\mathrm{p}}^{2}=\pm \omega \omega_{\mathrm{c}} \chi=\pm \omega \omega_{\mathrm{c}}\left(\frac{c^{2} k^{2}}{\omega^{2}}-1\right)
$$

or

$$
\frac{c^{2} k^{2}}{\omega^{2}}=1 \mp \frac{w_{\mathrm{p}}^{2}}{\omega \omega_{\mathrm{c}}} .
$$



\section{3}

The Drude-Lorentz formula for the dielectric constant of a solid is

$$
\varepsilon(\omega)=1+\frac{\omega_{\mathrm{p}}^{2}}{\left(\omega_{0}^{2}-\omega^{2}\right)-i \omega \tau^{-1}} .
$$

Here $\omega_{p}$ is the plasma frequency, $\omega_{0}$ is the energy gap for interband transitions and $\tau$ is the scattering time of the electron.

(a) At room temperature a reasonable value for $\mathrm{Cu}$ is $\tau=10^{-14} \mathrm{sec}$. Give order of magnitude estimates of $\omega_{p}$ and $\omega_{0}$ for this metal. You may want to make use of the characteristic "color" of the metal in determining $\omega_{0}$. Plot the real and imaginary parts of $\varepsilon(\omega)$ as a function of $\omega$ (in $\mathrm{eV}$ ).

(b) At room temperature, calculate $\sigma(\omega)$, the complex frequencydependent conduction of $\mathrm{Cu}$.

(c) What is $\sigma(\omega)$ for perfectly pure, defect-free $\mathrm{Cu}$ at zero temperature?

(Chicago )

\section{Solution:}

(a) Suppose each copper atom contributes one free electron. The concentration of free electrons is then

$$
n=\frac{6.02 \times 10^{23} \times 8.9}{63.5}=8.4 \times 10^{22} \mathrm{~cm}^{-3}=8.4 \times 10^{28} \mathrm{~m}^{-3} \text {. }
$$

For the electron, $\frac{e^{2}}{\epsilon_{0} m}=3 \times 10^{3} \mathrm{~m}^{2} \mathrm{~s}^{-2}$, so that the plasma frequency is (Problem 1032)

$$
\omega_{\mathrm{p}}=\sqrt{\frac{n e^{2}}{\epsilon_{0} m}}=1.6 \times 10^{16} \mathrm{~s}^{-1} .
$$

Hence

$$
\hbar \omega_{\mathrm{p}}=6.6 \times 10^{-14} \times 1.6 \times 10^{16}=10.5 \mathrm{eV} \approx 10 \mathrm{eV} \text {. }
$$

The color of copper is redish, corresponding to a wavelength $\lambda_{0} \approx 6 \times$ $10^{-7} \mathrm{~m}$, or $\omega_{0} \approx 3 \times 10^{15} \mathrm{~s}^{-1}$. Hence

$$
\hbar \omega_{0} \approx 2 \mathrm{eV}
$$

At room temperature, $\tau=10^{-14} \mathrm{~s}$, or $\hbar \tau^{-1}=6.6 \times 10^{-2} \mathrm{eV} \approx 10^{-1} \mathrm{eV}$, and the real and imaginary parts of $\varepsilon(\omega)$ are 

$$
\begin{aligned}
&\operatorname{Re} \varepsilon(\omega)=1+\frac{\omega_{\mathrm{p}}^{2}\left(\omega_{0}^{2}-\omega^{2}\right)}{\left(\omega_{0}^{2}-\omega^{2}\right)^{2}+\omega^{2} \tau^{-2}} \approx 1+\frac{10^{2} \cdot\left(4-\omega^{2}\right)}{\left(4-\omega^{2}\right)^{2}+\omega^{2} \cdot 10^{-2}}, \\
&\operatorname{Im} \varepsilon(\omega)=\frac{\omega_{\mathrm{p}}^{2} \omega \tau^{-1}}{\left(\omega_{0}^{2}-\omega^{2}\right)^{2}+\omega^{2} \tau^{-2}} \approx \frac{20}{\left(4-\omega^{2}\right)^{2}+\omega^{2} \cdot 10^{-2}} .
\end{aligned}
$$

These are plotted in Fig. 1.15.

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_b8e2b1af07958c90efe7g-69.jpg?height=295&width=588&top_left_y=758&top_left_x=823)

Fig. $1.15$

(b) Applying Ohm's law $\mathbf{J}=\sigma \mathbf{E}$ and the expression for the polarization current density $\mathbf{J}=\dot{\mathbf{P}}=-i \omega \mathbf{P}$, we find the electric susceptibility

$$
\chi \equiv \frac{P}{\epsilon_{0} E}=\frac{i \sigma}{\epsilon_{0} \omega} .
$$

Then

$$
\mathbf{D}=\epsilon \mathbf{E}=\epsilon_{0} \mathbf{E}+\mathbf{P}
$$

gives the complex dielectric constant of $\mathrm{Cu}$

$$
\varepsilon(\omega)=1+\chi=1+\frac{i \sigma(\omega)}{\epsilon_{0} \omega} .
$$

The Drude-Lorentz formula then gives

$$
\frac{i \sigma}{\epsilon_{0} \omega}=\frac{\omega_{\mathrm{p}}^{2}}{\left(\omega_{0}^{2}-\omega^{2}\right)^{2}-i \omega \tau^{-1}},
$$

or

$$
\sigma(\omega)=\frac{\epsilon_{0} \omega \omega_{\mathrm{p}}^{2}\left[\omega \tau^{-1}-i\left(\omega_{0}^{2}-\omega^{2}\right)\right]}{\left(\omega_{0}^{2}-\omega^{2}\right)^{2}+\omega^{2} \tau^{-2}} .
$$

(c) At zero temperature, $\tau \rightarrow \infty$ and the complex conductivity is

$$
\sigma(\omega)=-\frac{i \epsilon_{0} \omega \omega_{\mathrm{p}}^{2}}{\omega_{0}^{2}-\omega^{2}} .
$$



\section{4}

At time $t=0$ a charge distribution $\rho(\mathbf{r})$ exists within an idealized homogeneous conductor whose permittivity $\epsilon$ and conductivity $\sigma$ are constants. Obtain $\rho(\mathbf{r}, t)$ for subsequent times.

(Wisconsin)

\section{Solution:}

Use of Maxwell's equation

$$
\nabla \cdot \mathbf{D}=\epsilon \nabla \cdot \mathbf{E}=\rho
$$

and Ohm's law

$$
\mathbf{j}=\sigma \mathbf{E}
$$

in the continuity equation which expresses conservation of charge,

$$
\frac{\partial \rho}{\partial t}+\nabla \cdot \mathbf{j}=0,
$$

gives

$$
\frac{\partial \rho}{\partial t}+\frac{\sigma}{\epsilon} \rho=0 .
$$

With the initial condition $\rho(\mathbf{r}, 0)=\rho(\mathbf{r})$, integration gives

$$
\rho(\mathbf{r}, t)=\rho(\mathbf{r}) e^{-\sigma t / \epsilon} .
$$

\section{5}

Consider a two-dimensional square lattice.

(a) The kinetic energy of a free electron at a corner of the first Brillouin zone is larger than that of an electron at the midpoint of a side face of the zone by a factor $b$. Calculate $b$.

(b) The crystal potential of the corresponding material is

$$
V(x, y)=-2 V_{0}\left(\cos \frac{2 \pi x}{a}+\cos \frac{2 \pi y}{a}\right),
$$

where $V_{0}$ is a constant and $a$ is the lattice spacing. Find approximately the energy gap at the midpoint of the zone face. (c) Assuming that your result in (b) is exact and that the material is divalent, write down a condition for the system to be metallic.

(Princeton)

\section{Solution:}

(a) The kinetic energy of a free electron is

$$
E=\frac{-p^{2}}{2 m}=\frac{\hbar^{2} k^{2}}{2 m} \propto k^{2} .
$$

The first Brillouin zone for a two-dimensional square lattice of lattice constant $a$ is a square in the $\mathbf{k}$-space of edge $\frac{2 \pi}{a}$, as shown in Fig. 1.16. As

$$
\begin{aligned}
\overline{\Gamma M} &=\sqrt{2} \overline{\Gamma X}, \\
b &=\frac{E_{M}}{E_{X}}=\frac{\overline{\Gamma M}^{2}}{\overline{\Gamma X}^{2}}=2 .
\end{aligned}
$$

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_b8e2b1af07958c90efe7g-71.jpg?height=396&width=483&top_left_y=1290&top_left_x=886)

Fig. $1.16$

(b) We can write

$$
V(x, y)=-V_{0}\left(e^{i 2 \pi x / a}+e^{-i 2 \pi x / a}+e^{i 2 \pi y / a}+e^{-i 2 \pi y / a}\right) .
$$

Let the basis vectors of the lattice be $\mathbf{b}_{1}, \mathbf{b}_{2}$. The midpoint of a side face of the zone in the reciprocal lattice has vector $\mathbf{k}=\frac{2 \pi}{a} \mathbf{b}_{1}$. The corresponding Fourier coefficient in $V(x, y)$ is $-V_{0}$, so the energy gap is

$$
\Delta_{x}=2\left|V_{x}\right|=2\left|V_{0}\right| .
$$

(c) For a divalent material, the condition for it to be metallic is that the energy bands overlap. Using the above result, this means that

$$
E_{X}+\Delta_{x} \leq E_{M},
$$

i.e.,

$$
\frac{1}{2 m} \hbar^{2}\left(\frac{\pi}{a}\right)^{2}+2\left|V_{0}\right| \leq 2 \cdot \frac{\hbar^{2}}{2 m}\left(\frac{\pi}{a}\right)^{2}
$$

giving

$$
\left|V_{0}\right| \leq \frac{\pi^{2} \hbar^{2}}{4 m a^{2}}
$$

\section{6}

Consider an electron of charge $e$ in a one-dimensional lattice with energy levels

$$
\varepsilon(k)=-2 T \cos (k a)
$$

where $a$ is the lattice constant. A small uniform electric field $\mathbf{E}$ is imposed parallel to the lattice. Describe qualitatively the motion of the electron in k-space and in real space in both the absence and presence of scattering. What is meant by small $\mathbf{E}$ and what else can happen in a real (multiband) crystal when $\mathbf{E}$ is no longer small?

(Chicago)

\section{Solution:}

In the absence of scattering, the equation of motion of an electron represented by a Bloch wave in an applied electric field $\mathbf{E}$ is

$$
\frac{d \mathbf{p}}{d t}=\hbar \frac{d \mathbf{k}}{d t}=\mathbf{F}=-e \mathbf{E}
$$

\section{Integration gives}

$$
\mathbf{k}=\mathbf{k}(0)-\frac{e \mathbf{E} t}{\hbar},
$$

showing that $\mathbf{k}$ varies linearly with $t$. In $\mathbf{k}$-space all the electrons move with the same velocity opposite to the direction of the electric field, as shown in Fig. $1.17$ (a). When an electron reaches the boundary of the first Brillouin zone, say at point $A$, it is reflected and reappears at $A^{\prime}$ on the other side of the origin. The states at $A$ and $A^{\prime}$ are perfectly identical. In this way the electron moves periodically in k-space.

Under the action of the applied electric field $\mathbf{E}$, the electron state changes continually and so does its velocity (identical with the group velocity) $v=\frac{\partial \omega}{\partial k}=\frac{1}{\hbar} \frac{\partial \varepsilon(k)}{\partial k}, \varepsilon$ being the electron energy, as shown in 

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_b8e2b1af07958c90efe7g-73.jpg?height=339&width=553&top_left_y=340&top_left_x=512)

(a)

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_b8e2b1af07958c90efe7g-73.jpg?height=315&width=548&top_left_y=363&top_left_x=1119)

(b)

Fig. 1.17

Fig. $1.17$ (b). The electron velocity thus alternates between positive and negative values. The motion of the electron in real space is also periodic.

In the presence of scattering, such oscillation as mentioned above is not observed. This is because in a crystal there are various mechanisms of scattering and the equation of motion given above only applies between scatterings. As the time between two scatterings is very short, the electron wave vector only moves along a small portion of the reciprocal lattice vector before the electron is scattered away, making it impossible to execute the oscillation described.

Small $\mathbf{E}$ means that it does not allow an electron to gain sufficient energy to transit to a higher energy band. A real crystal has a multitude of bands and if the electric field is large enough transitions between bands will take place. The condition for such transitions not to take place is

$$
e E a \ll\left[\varepsilon_{\text {gap }}(k)\right]^{2} / \varepsilon_{\mathrm{F}} .
$$

\section{7}

Consider a one-dimensional metal with atoms regularly spaced a distance $b$ apart in the tight-binding approximation, with one electron per atom. The atomic wave functions are of the form $\psi\left(r-R_{i}\right)$ where $R_{i}$ is the position of the nucleus of the $i$ th atom. $\left(R_{i}=i b\right.$, the nuclei will be considered to be fixed in space.) Assume further 

$$
\begin{aligned}
\int \psi^{*}\left(r-R_{i}\right) H \psi\left(r-R_{i}\right) d^{3} r &=-E_{0}, \\
\int \psi^{*}\left(r-R_{i}\right) H \psi\left(r-R_{i+1}\right) d^{3} r &=-V, \\
\int \psi^{*}\left(r-R_{i}\right) H \psi\left(r-R_{i+j}\right) d^{3} r &=0 \quad \text { for } j \geq 2,
\end{aligned}
$$

where $H$ is the Hamiltonian. Calculate the following:

(a) The electronic band structure $\left(\varepsilon_{k}\right.$ vs. $\left.k\right)$.

(b) The electronic density of states.

(c) The electronic contribution to the cohesive energy (relative to $-E_{0}$ ).

(d) The electronic contribution to the specific heat at temperature $T$. (Assume $k_{\mathrm{B}} T \ll V$ ).

\section{(Princeton)}

\section{Solution:}

(a) According to the Bloch theorem the motion of an electron in a lattice is described by a traveling wave $e^{i \mathbf{k} \cdot \mathbf{r}}$ with an amplitude oscillating periodically from cell to cell. In the tight-binding method the modulation function is the atomic orbital $\psi\left(\mathbf{r}-\mathbf{R}_{l}\right)$ which is large in the neighborhood of an atom at $\mathbf{R}_{l}$ but decays rapidly away from it. For a one-dimensional lattice the electron wave function is thus

$$
\phi_{k}(\mathbf{r})=\frac{1}{\sqrt{N}} \sum_{l} e^{i k R_{l}} \psi\left(r-R_{l}\right)
$$

where $N$ is the number of atoms in the lattice. Then the energy of the electron is

$$
\begin{aligned}
E(k) &=\left\langle\phi_{k}|H| \phi_{k}\right\rangle \\
&=\frac{1}{N} \sum_{l, l^{\prime}} e^{i k\left(R_{l}-R_{l^{\prime}}\right)}\left\langle\psi\left(r-R_{l}\right)|H| \psi\left(r-R_{l^{\prime}}\right)\right\rangle,
\end{aligned}
$$

with the summation taken over all the atoms in the lattice. Note that for each particular choice of $l$ the sum over $l^{\prime}$ yields the same result. As only nearest-neighbor interactions are important, we have 

$$
\begin{aligned}
E(k)=&\left\langle\psi\left(r-R_{l}\right)|H| \psi\left(r-R_{l}\right)\right\rangle \\
&+e^{-i k b}\left\langle\psi\left(r-R_{l}\right)|H| \psi\left(r-R_{l+1}\right)\right\rangle \\
&+e^{i k b}\left\langle\psi\left(r-R_{l}\right)|H| \psi\left(r-R_{l-1}\right)\right\rangle \\
=&-E_{0}-V\left(e^{-\imath k b}+e^{i k b}\right) \\
=&-E_{0}-2 V \cos (k b) .
\end{aligned}
$$

(b) The number of states with wave vectors having magnitude smaller than or equal to $k$ for a one-dimensional lattice of length $L$ is (Problem 1014)

$$
N=\frac{L k}{\pi},
$$

whence the density of state per unit length of the lattice is

$$
\rho(k)=\frac{1}{L} \frac{d N}{d k}=\frac{1}{\pi} .
$$

For electron states, as there are two spin directions the density of state per unit length is

$$
\rho(k)=\frac{2}{\pi} .
$$

As

$$
\rho(E) d E=\rho(k) d k,
$$

we have

$$
\begin{aligned}
\rho(E)=\rho(k)\left(\frac{d E}{d k}\right)^{-1} &=[\pi b V \sin (k b)]^{-1} \\
&=\left[\pi b V \sqrt{1-\left(\frac{E_{0}+E}{2 V}\right)^{2}}\right]^{-1} .
\end{aligned}
$$

(c) As each atom contributes one electron, there are $N$ electrons in the lattice and $L=N b, L$ being the length of the lattice. Then

$$
N=L \int_{0}^{E_{m}} f(E) \rho(E) d E,
$$

where $f(E)$ is the Fermi-Dirac distribution function

$$
f(E)=\frac{1}{e^{\beta\left(E-E_{F}\right)}+1},
$$

with $\beta=\left(k_{\mathrm{B}} T\right)^{-1}$ and $E_{\mathrm{F}}$ being the Fermi energy, which applies to particles of spin $\frac{1}{2}$. At $T=0 \mathrm{~K}, f(E)=1$ for $E \ll E_{\mathrm{F}}$ and $f(E)=0$ for $E>E_{\mathrm{F}}$. For $k_{\mathrm{B}} T \ll E_{\mathrm{F}}, E_{\mathrm{F}}(T) \approx E_{\mathrm{F}}(0)$ and only electrons with energies within about $k_{\mathrm{B}} T$ of $E_{\mathrm{F}}$ are effected by the distribution function, some electrons transiting from below $E_{\mathrm{F}}$ to above $E_{\mathrm{F}}$. Thus, unless the $T$-dependence is specifically required, we may approximate by taking $f(E) \approx 1$ for low temperatures. Hence

$$
\begin{aligned}
N &=L \int_{0}^{E_{m}} \rho(E) d E=L \int_{0}^{k_{m}} \rho(k) d k \\
&=N b \int_{0}^{k_{m}} \frac{2}{\pi} d k=\frac{2 N b}{\pi} k_{m},
\end{aligned}
$$

giving $k_{m}=\frac{\pi}{2 b}$, and

$$
E_{\mathrm{F}} \approx E_{m}=-E_{0}-2 V \cos \left(k_{m} b\right)=-E_{0}-2 V .
$$

The total energy of the electrons is

$$
\begin{aligned}
E_{\mathrm{t}} &=L \int_{0}^{k_{m}} E(k) \rho(k) d k \\
&=L \int_{0}^{k_{m}}\left[-E_{0}-2 V \cos (k b)\right] \frac{2}{\pi} d k \\
&=\frac{2 N b}{\pi}\left[-E_{0}-\frac{2 V}{b} \sin (k b)\right]_{0}^{\pi / 2 b} \\
&=N\left(-E_{0}-\frac{4 V}{\pi}\right)
\end{aligned}
$$

Thus each electron on average contributes $-E_{0}-\frac{4 V}{\pi}$ to the total energy, and the cohesive energy per electron relative to $-E_{0}$ is therefore $\frac{4 V}{\pi}$.

(d) As only electrons lying below and above but not near the Fermi level $E_{\mathrm{F}}=-E_{0}$ contribute to the specific heat, we just have to calculate

$$
\varepsilon_{\mathrm{t}}(T)=\frac{L}{\pi b V} \int_{-2 V}^{2 V} \frac{\varepsilon d \varepsilon}{\left(e^{\beta \varepsilon}+1\right) \sqrt{1-\left(\frac{\varepsilon}{2 V}\right)^{2}}},
$$

where $\varepsilon=E-E_{\mathrm{F}}=E+E_{0}$.

Let

$$
f(\varepsilon)=\frac{1}{e^{\beta \varepsilon}+1}, \quad F(\varepsilon)=\sqrt{1-\left(\frac{\varepsilon}{2 V}\right)^{2}}
$$

and consider

$$
\begin{aligned}
I=\int_{-2 V}^{2 V} f(\varepsilon) F^{\prime}(\varepsilon) d \varepsilon &=[f(\varepsilon) F(\varepsilon)]_{-2 V}^{2 V}-\int_{-2 V}^{2 V} f^{\prime}(\varepsilon) F(\varepsilon) d \varepsilon \\
&=-\int_{-2 V}^{2 V} f^{\prime}(\varepsilon) F(\varepsilon) d \varepsilon .
\end{aligned}
$$

Expand $F(\varepsilon)$ around $\varepsilon=0$ according to Taylor's theorem:

$$
F(\varepsilon)=F(0)+\varepsilon F^{\prime}(0)+\frac{1}{2} \varepsilon^{2} F^{\prime \prime}(0)+\cdots .
$$

As $F(0)=1, F^{\prime}(0)=0, F^{\prime \prime}(0)=-\frac{1}{4 V^{2}}$, we have, to second order in $\varepsilon$,

$$
I \approx-\int_{-2 V}^{2 V} f^{\prime}(\varepsilon) d \varepsilon+\frac{1}{8 V^{2}} \int_{-2 V}^{2 V} \varepsilon^{2} f^{\prime}(\varepsilon) d \varepsilon .
$$

The first integral gives

$$
[f(\varepsilon)]_{-2 V}^{2 V}=\frac{1}{e^{2 \beta V}+1}-\frac{1}{e^{-2 \beta V}+1} \approx-1
$$

as it is assumed that $k_{\mathrm{B}} T \ll V$, or $\beta V \gg 1$. Setting $\beta \varepsilon=x$, the second integral becomes

$$
-\frac{1}{8 V^{2} \beta^{2}} \int_{-\infty}^{\infty} \frac{x^{2} e^{x} d x}{\left(e^{x}+1\right)^{2}}=-\frac{\pi^{2}}{24 V^{2} \beta^{2}} .
$$

Thus

$$
I \approx 1-\frac{\pi^{2}}{24 V^{2}}\left(k_{\mathrm{B}} T\right)^{2}
$$

and

$$
\begin{aligned}
\varepsilon_{\mathrm{t}}(T) &=\frac{L}{\pi b V}\left(-4 V^{2}\right) I \\
& \approx N\left[-\frac{4 V}{\pi}+\frac{\pi}{6} \frac{\left(k_{\mathrm{B}} T\right)^{2}}{V}\right] .
\end{aligned}
$$

Hence the specific heat per electron is

$$
C_{\mathrm{e}}=\frac{d \varepsilon_{\mathrm{t}}}{d T} \approx \frac{\pi}{3} \frac{k_{\mathrm{B}}^{2} T}{V} .
$$

\section{8}

The method of orthogonalized plane waves (OPW, closely associated with the method of pseudo-potential) is very often used to make band structure calculations for electron states in metals. It explains successfully why the nearly free electron approximation can be used although the actual lattice potential acting on the electrons is not weak at all.

Discuss the essential physical ideas and mathematical steps in this method by including, among others, descriptions of a

(a) construction of Bloch function out of core states and its properties,

(b) construction of the orthogonalized plane wave states and their properties,

(c) construction of the pseudopotential and its non-uniqueness,

(d) physical explanation why the pseudopotential can be treated as small.

(SUNY, Buffalo )

\section{Solution:}

In a metal the valence electrons are nearly free, but inside a core the wave function oscillates very rapidly. In regions outside the cores, the electron wave is essentially a plane wave

$$
\begin{aligned}
|\mathbf{k}\rangle &=\frac{1}{\sqrt{N \Omega}} e^{i \mathbf{k} \cdot \mathbf{r}}, \\
\left|\mathbf{k}+\mathbf{k}_{i}\right\rangle &=\frac{1}{\sqrt{N \Omega}} e^{i\left(\mathbf{k}+\mathbf{k}_{i}\right) \cdot \mathbf{r}},
\end{aligned}
$$

where $N$ is the number of unit cells in the crystal, $\Omega$ is the volume of a unit cell, $\mathbf{k}_{i}$ is a reciprocal lattice vector.

(a) In the core zone the electrons are tightly bound. The tight-binding approximation gives the wave function as

$$
\left|\Phi_{j \mathbf{k}}(\mathbf{r})\right\rangle=\frac{1}{\sqrt{N}} \sum_{\iota} e^{i \mathbf{k} \cdot \mathbf{R}_{\iota}} \varphi_{j}\left(\mathbf{r}-\mathbf{R}_{\iota}\right),
$$

where $\varphi_{j}\left(\mathbf{r}-\mathbf{R}_{\iota}\right)$ is the $j$ th state orbital of the atom located at $\mathbf{R}_{\iota}$ and the summation extends over all the atoms in the lattice.

(b) Taking a plane wave with wave vector $\mathbf{k}$ as basis, construct a function $\chi_{i}(\mathbf{k}, \mathbf{r})$ orthogonal to all the electron wave functions with wave vector $\mathbf{k}$ in the core zone:

$$
\chi_{i}(\mathbf{k}, \mathbf{r})=\left|\mathbf{k}+\mathbf{k}_{i}\right\rangle-\sum_{j=1}^{N} \mu_{i j}\left|\Phi_{j \mathbf{k}}\right\rangle,
$$

where the coefficient $\mu_{i j}$ is determined by the orthogonal condition

$$
\int \Phi_{j \mathbf{k}} \chi_{i}(\mathbf{k}, \mathbf{r}) d^{3} \mathbf{r}=0 .
$$

The wave function $\chi_{i}(\mathbf{k}, \mathbf{r})$, called an orthogonalized plane wave function, is orthogonal to the electron wave function in the core zone. It behaves as a plane wave at large distances from an atom, but oscillates rapidly near a core. This makes such functions suitable for construction of the wave function of a valence electron in the crystal, in the form a linear combination of several orthogonalized plane waves:

$$
\psi_{\mathbf{k}}=\sum_{i} C_{i} \chi_{i} .
$$

(c) As

$$
\chi_{i}=\left|\mathbf{k}+\mathbf{k}_{i}\right\rangle-\sum_{j}\left|\Phi_{j \mathbf{k}}\right\rangle\left\langle\Phi_{j \mathbf{k}} \mid \mathbf{k}+\mathbf{k}_{i}\right\rangle,
$$

denoting

$$
\hat{p}=\sum_{j}\left|\Phi_{j \mathbf{k}}\right\rangle\left\langle\Phi_{j \mathbf{k}}\right|
$$

we have

$$
\psi_{\mathbf{k}}=(1-\hat{p})\left|\mathbf{k}+\mathbf{k}_{i}\right\rangle .
$$

Introducing the pseudo wave function

$$
\varphi=\sum_{i} C_{i}\left|\mathbf{k}+\mathbf{k}_{i}\right\rangle
$$

and substituting it in the Schrödinger equation, we have

$$
-\frac{\hbar^{2}}{2 m} \nabla^{2} \varphi+W \varphi=E \varphi,
$$

where

$$
\begin{aligned}
W &=V(\mathbf{r})-\left[-\frac{\hbar^{2}}{2 m} \nabla^{2}+V(\mathbf{r})\right] \hat{p}+E \hat{p} \\
&=V(\mathbf{r})+\sum_{j}\left(E-E_{j}\right)\left|\Phi_{j \mathbf{k}}\right\rangle\left\langle\Phi_{j \mathbf{k}}\right|
\end{aligned}
$$

is known as a pseudopotential.

(d) The pseudopotential is not unique. For if we substitute

$$
W^{\prime}=V(\mathbf{r})+\sum_{j} f\left(E, E_{j}\right)\left|\Phi_{j \mathbf{k}}\right\rangle\left\langle\Phi_{j \mathbf{k}}\right|,
$$

where $f\left(E, E_{j}\right)$ is an arbitrary function, in the Schrödinger equation

$$
-\frac{\hbar^{2}}{2 m} \nabla^{2} \varphi+W^{\prime} \varphi=E^{\prime} \varphi,
$$

and take the inner product of its both sides with $\psi_{\mathbf{k}}^{*}$, we can show that $E^{\prime}=E$. Thus $W^{\prime}$ is also a pseudopotential.

(e) Now write

$$
W=V(\mathbf{r})+\sum_{j}\left(E-E_{j}\right)\left|\Phi_{j \mathbf{k}}\right\rangle\left\langle\Phi_{j \mathbf{k}}\right|=V(\mathbf{r})+V_{B},
$$

where $V(\mathbf{r})$, the real crystal potential, is an attractive potential, and

$$
V_{B}=\sum_{j}\left(E-E_{j}\right)\left|\Phi_{j \mathbf{k}}\right\rangle\left\langle\Phi_{j \mathbf{k}}\right| .
$$

In a crystal the energy $E$ of a valence electron is higher than that of an inner electron, $E_{j}$, so that $V_{B}$ has the property of repulsing electrons and is a repulsive potential. Thus $V_{B}$ tends to cancel $V(\mathbf{r})$. This cancellation of the crystal potential by atomic functions is usually appreciable, leading to a very weak and quite smooth pseudopotential $W$.

\section{9}

Consider a one-dimensional solid of length $L=N a$ made up of $N$ diatomic molecules, the interatomic spacing within a molecule is $b\left(b<\frac{a}{2}\right)$. The centers of adjacent molecules are a distance $a$ apart. We represent the potential energy as a sum of delta functions centered on each atom: 

$$
V=-A \sum_{n=0}^{N-1}\left[\delta\left(x-n a+\frac{b}{2}\right)+\delta\left(x-n a-\frac{b}{2}\right)\right]
$$

with $A$ a positive quantity and $n=0,1,2, \ldots, N-1$. The potential is shown in Fig. 1.18.

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_b8e2b1af07958c90efe7g-81.jpg?height=222&width=1116&top_left_y=721&top_left_x=510)

Fig. $1.18$

(a) Consider free electrons in this solid (i.e., neglect $V$ for the moment) and periodic boundary conditions. Derive the allowed values of the electron wave vectors $k$, and normalize the wave function.

(b) Expressing the potential as a Fourier series

$$
V=\sum_{q} V_{q} e^{i q x},
$$

find the allowed values of $q$ and the coefficients $V_{q}$.

(c) Assuming $A$ to be small, show that for certain values of $k$ there are energy gaps. Derive a general formula for the gaps and show in particular that the gap in energy at the top of the first zone is proportional to $\cos \left(\frac{\pi b}{a}\right)$.

(d) Derive an expression for the number of states there are in the first zone. If each atom has one electron, will the substance be a conductor or an insulator?

(e) Suppose $b=a / 2$. Show what happens to the results of the previous sections and give a brief explanation.

(Princeton)

\section{Solution:}

(a) For a one-dimensional solid the Hamiltonian of a free electron, mass $m$, is

$$
H=\frac{p^{2}}{2 m}=-\frac{\hbar^{2}}{2 m} \nabla^{2}=-\frac{\hbar^{2}}{2 m} \frac{d^{2}}{d x^{2}} .
$$

The Schrödinger equation

$$
H \psi=E \psi
$$

then becomes

$$
\frac{d^{2} \psi}{d x^{2}}+\frac{2 m E}{\hbar^{2}} \psi=0 .
$$

Consider a plane wave solution

$$
\psi=A_{0} e^{i k x}
$$

Substitution gives

$$
k=\sqrt{\frac{2 m E}{\hbar^{2}}} .
$$

Normalization

$$
\int_{0}^{L} \psi^{*} \psi d x=1,
$$

$L$ being the length of the crystal, then gives

$$
A_{0}=\frac{1}{\sqrt{L}} .
$$

The periodic boundary condition $\psi(0)=\psi(L)$ requires

$$
e^{i k L}=1,
$$

yielding

$$
k=\frac{2 n \pi}{L}, \quad n=0, \pm 1, \pm 2, \ldots .
$$

This gives the allowed electron wave vectors $k$, for which the normalized wave function is

$$
\psi=\frac{1}{\sqrt{L}} e^{i k x} .
$$

(b) As $V(x)$ has period $a$, in the Fourier series

$$
V(x)=\sum_{q} V_{q} e^{i q x},
$$

we require

$$
e^{i q x}=e^{i q(x+a)},
$$

or

$$
q=\frac{2 n \pi}{a}, \quad n=0, \pm 1, \pm 2, \ldots
$$

which are the allowed values of $q$. Consider

$$
\begin{aligned}
\int_{0}^{a} V(x) e^{-i q^{\prime} x} d x &=\sum_{q} V_{q} \int_{0}^{a} e^{i \frac{2 \pi}{a}\left(n-n^{\prime}\right) x} d x \\
&=\sum_{q} V_{q} a \delta_{q q^{\prime}}=V_{q^{\prime}} a
\end{aligned}
$$

where $q^{\prime}=\frac{2 n^{\prime} \pi}{a}$ is a particular $q$. Thus the coefficients are given by

$$
V_{q}=\frac{1}{a} \int_{0}^{a} V(x) e^{-i q x} d x
$$

In the neighborhood of $x=n a$,

$$
V(x)=-A\left[\delta\left(x-n a+\frac{b}{2}\right)+\delta\left(x-n a-\frac{b}{2}\right)\right]
$$

so that

$$
\begin{aligned}
V_{q} &=-\frac{A}{a}\left[\int_{0}^{a} \delta\left(x-n a+\frac{b}{2}\right) e^{-i q x} d x+\int_{0}^{a} \delta\left(x-n a-\frac{b}{2}\right) e^{-i q x} d x\right] \\
&=-\frac{A}{a}\left[e^{-i q\left(n a-\frac{b}{2}\right)}+e^{-i q\left(n a+\frac{b}{2}\right)}\right] \\
&=-\frac{A}{a} e^{i q n a} \cdot 2 \cos \left(\frac{b q}{2}\right) \\
&=-\frac{2 A}{a} \cos \left(\frac{b q}{2}\right)
\end{aligned}
$$

as $e^{i q n a}=e^{i 2 n^{2} \pi}=1$.

(c) For the one-dimensional periodic potential

$$
V(x)=\sum_{q} V_{q} e^{i q x}
$$

with $q=\frac{2 n \pi}{a}, n=0, \pm 1, \pm 2, \ldots$, the Schrödinger equation for an electron moving in a one-dimensional crystal

$$
\left[H_{0}+V(x)\right] \psi=E \psi,
$$

where $H_{0}=-\frac{\hbar^{2}}{2 m} \frac{d^{2}}{d x^{2}}$, can be solved by expressing the solution as a series

$$
\psi=\sum_{k} C_{k} \varphi_{k},
$$

where $\varphi_{k}$ are the solutions of the free-electron Schrödinger equation

$$
H_{0} \varphi_{k}=E_{k}^{0} \varphi_{k},
$$

i.e.,

$$
\varphi_{k}=\frac{1}{\sqrt{L}} e^{i k x},
$$

with energy

$$
E_{k}^{0}=\frac{\hbar^{2} k^{2}}{2 m},
$$

where $k=\frac{2 \pi n}{L}, n=0, \pm 1, \pm 2, \ldots$

Substitution in the Schrödinger equation gives

$$
\sum_{k} C_{k} E_{k}^{0} \varphi_{k}+\sum_{k} \sum_{q} C_{k} V_{q} e^{i q x} \varphi_{k}=\sum_{k} C_{k} E \varphi_{k} .
$$

As

$$
e^{i q x} \varphi_{k}=\frac{1}{\sqrt{L}} e^{i(q+k) x}=\varphi_{q+k},
$$

the wave vectors that make up $\psi(x)$ differ from one another by a reciprocal lattice vector $q$ and the above can be written as

$$
\sum_{k} C_{k}\left(E-E_{k}^{0}\right) \varphi_{k}=\sum_{k} \sum_{q} C_{k} V_{q} \varphi_{q+k} .
$$

Then as the summation over $q$ and $k$ go from $-\infty$ to $\infty$, the above can be rewritten as

$$
\sum_{k} C_{k}\left(E-E_{k}^{0}\right) \varphi_{k}=\sum_{k} \sum_{q} C_{k-q} V_{q} \varphi_{k} .
$$

Equating the coefficients of $\varphi_{k^{\prime}}$ gives

$$
C_{k^{\prime}}\left(E-E_{k^{\prime}}^{0}\right)=\sum_{q} C_{k^{\prime}-q} V_{q} .
$$

Letting $k^{\prime}=k-K, q=K^{\prime}-K$, where $K$ is an arbitrary reciprocal lattice vector, in the above, we have

$$
C_{k-K}\left(E-E_{k-K}^{0}\right)=\sum_{K^{\prime}} C_{k-K^{\prime}} V_{K^{\prime}-K}
$$

For a free electron, $V(x)=0$ and the right-hand side vanishes. This shows that

$$
\begin{array}{ll}
C_{k-K}=0 & \text { for } E \neq E_{k-K}^{0}, \\
C_{k-K} \neq 0 & \text { only if } E=E_{k-K}^{0},
\end{array}
$$

for the free-electron states. For $A$ small the periodic potential can be taken as small perturbation. These coefficients are then used to express the wave function of wave vector $k$ for an electron in the potential field:

$$
\psi_{k}=\sum_{k^{\prime}} C_{k^{\prime}} \varphi_{k^{\prime}} .
$$

Suppose for certain $k$ degeneracy occurs: $E_{k-K_{1}}^{0}=E_{k-K_{2}}^{0}=\ldots$ For a one-dimensional lattice, there is only two-fold degeneracy. Suppose $E_{k}^{0}=$ $E_{k-K_{1}}^{0}$. Then only $C_{k}, C_{k-K_{1}}$ are nonvanishing and Eq. (1) gives

$$
\begin{aligned}
C_{k}\left(E_{k}-E_{k}^{0}\right) &=C_{k} V_{0}+C_{k-K_{1}} V_{K_{1}}, \\
C_{k-K_{1}}\left(E_{k}-E_{k}^{0}\right) &=C_{k} V_{K_{1}}+C_{k-K_{1}} V_{0} .
\end{aligned}
$$

As $V(x)$ is real

$$
V(x)=V^{*}(x)=\sum_{q} V_{q} e^{i q x}=\sum_{q} V_{q}^{*} e^{-i q x}=\sum_{q} V_{q}^{*} e^{i q x}
$$

since $q$ goes from $-\infty$ to $\infty$. Hence $V_{q}=V_{-q}^{*}=V_{-q}$ as $V_{q}$ is real. Equations (2) and (3) now become

$$
\begin{aligned}
&\left(E_{k}-E_{k}^{0}-V_{0}\right) C_{k}-V_{K_{1}} C_{k-K_{1}}=0 \\
&V_{K_{1}} C_{k}-\left(E_{k}-E_{k}^{0}-V_{0}\right) C_{k-K_{1}}=0 .
\end{aligned}
$$

For $C_{k}, C_{k-K_{1}}$ to be nonvanishing we require

$$
\left(E_{k}-E_{k}^{0}-V_{0}\right)^{2}-V_{K_{1}}^{2}=0
$$

or

$$
E_{k}=E_{k}^{0}+V_{0} \pm\left|V_{K_{1}}\right| .
$$

This equation means that an energy gap

$$
E_{\mathrm{g}}=\Delta E_{k}=2\left|V_{K_{1}}\right|
$$

exists at

$$
k=\frac{1}{2}\left(k+K_{1}-k\right)=\frac{K_{1}}{2} .
$$

For a linear crystal the first Brillouin zone is given by $-\frac{\pi}{a} \leq k \leq \frac{\pi}{a}$. At the top of the zone $k=\frac{\pi}{a}=\frac{K_{1}}{2}$, yielding $K_{1}=\frac{2 \pi}{a}$ and so $q=-K_{1}=-\frac{2 \pi}{a}$. Then we have from (a)

$$
\left|V_{K_{1}}\right|=\left|V_{\mathrm{g}}\right|=\frac{2 A}{a} \cos \left(\frac{\pi b}{2}\right) .
$$

Thus

$$
E_{\mathrm{g}}(k) \propto \cos \left(\frac{\pi b}{2}\right) .
$$

(d) The first zone consists of states with wave vectors in

$$
-\frac{\pi}{a} \leq k \leq \frac{\pi}{a}
$$

with $k=\frac{2 n \pi}{L}, n=0, \pm 1, \pm 2 \ldots$ The number of states in the first zone is thus

$$
\frac{2 \pi}{a}\left(\frac{2 \pi}{L}\right)^{-1}=\frac{L}{a}=N .
$$

Since each state corresponds to two spin orientations, there are $2 N$ states.

The solid has $N$ diatomic molecules, or $2 N$ atoms, and each atom contributes one electron. Thus there are $2 N$ electrons which completely fill up the $2 N$ states in the first zone. The solid is therefore an insulator.

(e) It can be seen from Fig. $1.18$ that if $b=\frac{a}{2}$, the period of the potential becomes $\frac{a}{2}$. The Brillouin zones will expand and the above results will change as follows:

(a) Remains the same.

(b) $q=\frac{4 \pi}{a}, V_{0}=\frac{2 A}{a}, V_{n}=(-1)^{n+1} \frac{2 A}{a}$.

(c) $E_{\mathrm{g}} \propto\left|V_{n}\right|$.

(d) The first zone of the $k$-space will have $2 N$ states, or $4 N$ states taking spin into account. The $2 N$ electrons available are not sufficient to fill up all the states. The material is therefore a conductor.

\section{0}

Consider a one-dimensional periodic potential $U(x)$ viewed as an array of identical potential barriers $V(x)$ of width $a$, centered at the points $x=\pm n a$ where $n$ is an integer. The barrier $V(x)$, schematically drawn in Fig. 1.19, may be characterized by a transmission coefficient $t(k)$ and a 

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_b8e2b1af07958c90efe7g-87.jpg?height=382&width=751&top_left_y=389&top_left_x=730)

Fig. $1.19$

reflection coefficient $r(k)$ for an electron incident on the barrier with energy $E=\frac{\hbar^{2} k^{2}}{2 m}$

(a) For the single barrier case, write down the most general solution for the wave function of an electron with energy $E=\frac{\hbar^{2} k^{2}}{2 m}$.

(b) How is the energy of the Bloch electron related to its wave vector $k$ for the crystal Hamiltonian case, i.e., $U(x)=\sum_{n=-\infty}^{n=+\infty} V(x+n a)$. Verify that this gives the right answer in the free-electron case $V=0$.

(c) By recalling some simple properties of $t$ and $r$, demonstrate that Bloch waves occur only in bands of energies.

(d) Suppose the barriers are weak, i.e., $|t|=1$ and $|r|=0$, find a simple expression for the width of the energy gaps.

(Princeton)

\section{Solution:}

(a) An electron of energy $E$ coming from the left of the single barrier can be represented by a wave

$$
\psi_{1}(x)= \begin{cases}e^{i k x}+r_{1} e^{-i k x}, & x \leq-\frac{a}{2} \\ t_{1} e^{i k x} & x \geq \frac{a}{2}\end{cases}
$$

$k$ being given by the relation $E=\frac{\hbar^{2} k^{2}}{2 m}$. An electron coming from the right can be represented by a wave

$$
\psi_{\mathrm{r}}(x)= \begin{cases}e^{-i k x}+r_{\mathrm{r}} e^{i k x}, & x \geq \frac{a}{2}, \\ t_{\mathrm{r}} e^{-i k x} & x \leq-\frac{a}{2}\end{cases}
$$

If $V(x)$ is not symmetrical, the reflection and transmission coefficients $r, t$ for the two cases are generally different. The general wave function for these regions is a linear combination of $\psi_{1}(x)$ and $\psi_{\mathrm{r}}(x)$ : 

$$
\psi(x)=A \psi_{1}(x)+B \psi_{\mathbf{r}}(x),
$$

$A, B$ being real constants.

Within the barrier $-\frac{a}{2} \leq x \leq \frac{a}{2}$, the wave function satisfies the onedimensional Schrödinger equation

$$
\left[-\frac{\hbar}{2 m} \frac{d^{2}}{d x^{2}}+V(x)\right] \psi=E \psi .
$$

(b) For a periodic crystal potential

$$
U(x)=\sum_{n=-\infty}^{\infty} V(x+n a),
$$

the Hamiltonian within the region $-\frac{a}{2} \leq x \leq \frac{a}{2}$ is identical with that for a single barrier and we can make use of the Bloch theorem to find the general solution of the Schrödinger equation:

$$
\psi(x)=A \psi_{\mathrm{l}}(x)+B \psi_{\mathbf{r}}(x),
$$

with

$$
\psi(x+a)=e^{i K a} \psi(x),
$$

where $K$ is a positive real constant, and hence, on differentiation with respect to $x$,

$$
\psi^{\prime}(x+a)=e^{i K a} \psi^{\prime}(x) .
$$

Thus at $x=-\frac{a}{2}$ we have

$$
\begin{aligned}
&A \psi_{1}\left(\frac{a}{2}\right)+B \psi_{\mathrm{r}}\left(\frac{a}{2}\right)=e^{i K a}\left[A \psi_{1}\left(-\frac{a}{2}\right)+B \psi_{\mathrm{r}}\left(-\frac{a}{2}\right)\right] \\
&A \psi_{1}^{\prime}\left(\frac{a}{2}\right)+B \psi_{\mathrm{r}}^{\prime}\left(\frac{a}{2}\right)=e^{i K a}\left[A \psi_{1}^{\prime}\left(-\frac{a}{2}\right)+B \psi_{\mathrm{r}}^{\prime}\left(-\frac{a}{2}\right)\right]
\end{aligned}
$$

or

$$
\begin{aligned}
A\left[t_{1} \beta-\alpha\left(1+r_{1} \beta\right)\right]+B\left(1+r_{\mathrm{r}} \beta-t_{\mathrm{r}} \alpha \beta\right) &=0 \\
A\left[t_{1} \beta-\alpha\left(1-r_{1} \beta\right)\right]+B\left(-1+r_{\mathrm{r}} \beta+t_{\mathrm{r}} \alpha \beta\right) &=0
\end{aligned}
$$

with

$$
\alpha=e^{i K a}, \quad \beta=e^{i k a} .
$$

Taking the sum and difference of the last two equations we have

$$
\begin{aligned}
&\left(t_{1} \beta-\alpha\right) A+r_{\mathrm{r}} \beta B=0, \\
&-r_{1} \alpha \beta A+\left(1-t_{\mathrm{r}} \alpha \beta\right) B=0 .
\end{aligned}
$$

For nontrivial solutions of $A, B$, the determinant of their coefficients must vanish, giving

$$
\left(t_{1} t_{\mathrm{r}}-r_{1} r_{\mathrm{r}}\right) e^{i k a}+e^{-i k a}=t_{1} e^{-i K a}+t_{\mathrm{r}} e^{i K a} .
$$

This gives the relation between the kinetic energy of the electron $E-V=$ $\frac{\hbar^{2} K^{2}}{2 m}$, and the wave vector $k$.

A crystal is usually symmetric so that $V(x)=V(-x)$ and hence $r_{1}=$ $r_{\mathrm{r}}=r, t_{1}=t_{\mathrm{r}}=t$. The above relation then becomes

$$
\left(t^{2}-r^{2}\right) e^{i k a}+e^{-i k a}=2 t \cos (K a) .
$$

If the electron is completely free, $r=0, t=1$, and the above gives

$$
\cos (k a)=\cos (K a),
$$

or

$$
k=K .
$$

This is the right answer since for $V=0$ we have $E=\frac{\hbar^{2} K^{2}}{2 m}$.

(c) Consider an electron traveling from left to right through a single barrier, assumed symmetric. The continuity of the probability current density $-\frac{i \hbar}{2 m}\left(\psi^{*} \psi^{\prime}-\psi \psi^{\prime *}\right)$ applied to the regions $x \leq-\frac{a}{2}$ and $x \geq \frac{a}{2}$ gives

$$
\begin{aligned}
\psi_{1}^{*} \psi_{1}^{\prime}-\psi_{1} \psi_{1}^{\prime *} &=\left(\alpha^{-1}+r^{*} \alpha\right) \frac{\partial}{\partial x}\left(\alpha+r \alpha^{-1}\right)-\left(\alpha+r \alpha^{-1}\right) \frac{\partial}{\partial x}\left(\alpha^{-1}+r^{*} \alpha\right) \\
&=t^{*} \alpha^{-1} \frac{\partial}{\partial x}(t \alpha)-t \alpha \frac{\partial}{\partial x}\left(t^{*} \alpha^{-1}\right),
\end{aligned}
$$

where $\alpha=e^{i k x}$, and hence

$$
1-r r^{*}=t t^{*},
$$

or

$$
|r|^{2}+|t|^{2}=1
$$

as expected from energy conservation. Consider next two simultaneously traveling electrons of the same energy, one from left to right and the other from right to left, crossing the barrier. The probability current density is then zero everywhere, in particular at $x \leq-\frac{a}{2}$. Thus

$$
\begin{aligned}
&\left(\psi_{1}+\psi_{\mathrm{r}}\right)^{*} \frac{\partial}{\partial x}\left(\psi_{1}+\psi_{\mathbf{r}}\right)-\left(\psi_{1}+\psi_{\mathbf{r}}\right) \frac{\partial}{\partial x}\left(\psi_{1}+\psi_{\mathbf{r}}\right)^{*} \\
&=\left(\alpha^{-1}+r^{*} \alpha+t^{*} \alpha\right) \frac{\partial}{\partial x}\left(\alpha+r \alpha^{-1}+t \alpha^{-1}\right) \\
&\quad-\left(\alpha+r \alpha^{-1}+t \alpha^{-1}\right) \frac{\partial}{\partial x}\left(\alpha^{-1}+r^{*} \alpha+t^{*} \alpha\right) \\
&=0
\end{aligned}
$$

or

$$
1-r r^{*}-t t^{*}-r^{*} t-r t^{*}=0 .
$$

Hence

$$
r^{*} t+r t^{*}=0,
$$

which means that $r^{*} t$ is purely imaginary. Let

$$
t=|t| e^{i \delta}, \quad r=i|r| e^{i \delta}
$$

and substitute these in Eq. (1), which then gives

$$
\frac{\cos (k a+\delta)}{|t|}=\cos (K a) \text {. }
$$

With increasing electron energy, the transmission coefficient increases, i.e., $|t|$ increases toward unity as $k$ increases. Then the variation of the left-hand side of Eq. (2) with $k$ is as shown in Fig. 1.20. However, as $\cos (K a)$ is confined to the region between $-1$ and 1 , certain values of $k$ are forbidden by Eq. (2). These forbidden gaps are shown as shadowed areas in the figure. Hence the possible Bloch waves occurs only in bands of energies.

(d) Consider an energy gap in the $k$-space with boundaries $k_{1}, k_{2}$ and midpoint at $k_{0}$ and width $2 \epsilon$. It can be seen from Fig. $1.20$ and Eq. (2) that

$$
\begin{aligned}
\left|\cos \left(k_{0} a+\delta\right)\right| &=1, \\
\left|\cos \left(k_{0} a+\delta+\epsilon a\right)\right| &=|t|,
\end{aligned}
$$

or

$$
k_{0} a+\delta=n \pi,
$$![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_41580ef03e6631735034g-01.jpg?height=431&width=876&top_left_y=359&top_left_x=646)

Fig. $1.20$

where $n$ is zero or an integer, and

$$
|\sin (n \pi+\epsilon a)|=\sqrt{1-|t|^{2}}=|r| \text {. }
$$

For a weak barrier, $|r| \rightarrow 0,|t| \rightarrow 1,|\delta| \rightarrow 0$, and as can be seen from Fig. 1.20, $\epsilon a \rightarrow 0$, so that

$$
k_{0}=\frac{1}{2}\left(k_{2}+k_{1}\right) \approx \frac{n \pi}{2 a}
$$

and

$$
|\sin (n \pi) \cos (\epsilon a)+\sin (\epsilon a) \cos (n \pi)| \approx \epsilon a=|r|
$$

i.e.,

$$
k_{2}-k_{1}=\frac{|r|}{a} .
$$

Hence the energy gap has width

$$
\Delta E=\frac{\hbar^{2}}{2 m}\left(k_{2}^{2}-k_{1}^{2}\right)=\frac{\hbar^{2}}{m} k_{0}\left(k_{2}-k_{1}\right)=\frac{n \pi}{2} \frac{\hbar^{2}}{m a^{2}}|r|
$$

\title{
1041
}

(a) For a hexagonal close-packed structure with periodic potential $V(\mathbf{r})=\sum_{\mathbf{G}} V_{\mathbf{G}} e^{i \mathbf{G} \cdot \mathbf{r}}$ (G being a reciprocal lattice vector), show that $V_{\mathbf{G}}=0$ for the $G$ corresponding to the first Brillouin zone face in the $c$ direction (that is, the normal to the face is in the $\left(\begin{array}{lll}0 & 0 & 1\end{array}\right)$ direction). What does this imply for the first-order energy gap across this zone face?

(b) Explain why it is not possible to obtain an insulator made up of monovalent atoms in an hcp structure. (c) Na metal has bcc structure and has one valence electron per atom. Find a formula for and estimate the threshold wavelength (in $\AA$ ) for interband transitions (from the lowest to the next higher energy band) in Na metal. Make use of the fact that $k_{\mathrm{F}}$ is appreciably less than $k_{\mathrm{ZB}}$ (the $k$-vector to the zone boundary nearest the origin). The size of the cube edge $a$ in $\mathrm{Na}$ is $4.23 \AA$.

(Princeton $)$

Solution:

(a) The coefficients in the expansion

$$
V(\mathbf{r})=\sum_{\mathbf{G}} V_{\mathbf{G}} e^{i \mathbf{G} \cdot \mathbf{r}}
$$

are

$$
V_{\mathbf{G}}=\frac{1}{v} \int_{\text {cell }} V(\mathbf{r}) e^{-i \mathbf{G} \cdot \mathbf{r}} d \mathbf{r},
$$

where $v$ is the volume of a unit cell.

If there are $n$ identical atoms in the unit cell with positions $\mathbf{d}_{1}, \mathbf{d}_{2}, \ldots$, $\mathbf{d}_{j}, \ldots, \mathbf{d}_{n}$, the periodic potential can be expressed as

$$
V(\mathbf{r})=\sum_{\mathbf{R}} \sum_{j=1}^{n} \Phi\left(\mathbf{r}-\mathbf{R}-\mathbf{d}_{j}\right),
$$

where $\mathbf{R}$ is the position of a lattice point. Hence

$$
\begin{aligned}
V_{\mathbf{G}} &=\frac{1}{v} \int_{\text {cell }} e^{-i \mathbf{G} \cdot \mathbf{r}} \sum_{\mathbf{R}} \sum_{j=1}^{n} \Phi\left(\mathbf{r}-\mathbf{R}-\mathbf{d}_{j}\right) d \mathbf{r} \\
&=\frac{1}{v} \sum_{j=1}^{n} \sum_{\mathbf{R}} \int_{\text {cell }} e^{-i \mathbf{G} \cdot \mathbf{r}} \Phi\left(\mathbf{r}-\mathbf{R}-\mathbf{d}_{j}\right) d \mathbf{r} \\
&=\frac{1}{v} \sum_{j=1}^{n} \int_{\text {whole space }} e^{-i \mathbf{G} \cdot \mathbf{r}} \Phi\left(\mathbf{r}-\mathbf{d}_{j}\right) d \mathbf{r} \\
&=\frac{1}{v} \int_{\text {whole space }} e^{-i \mathbf{G} \cdot \mathbf{r}} \Phi(\mathbf{r})\left(\sum_{j=1}^{n} e^{i \mathbf{G} \cdot \mathbf{d}_{j}}\right) d \mathbf{r} .
\end{aligned}
$$

In the expansion the sum $\sum_{j=1}^{n} e^{i \mathbf{G} \cdot \mathbf{d}_{j}}$ is the geometric structure factor. A unit cell of the hexagonal close-packed (hcp) structure contains two atoms at $\left(\begin{array}{lll}0 & 0 & 0\end{array}\right)$ and $\left(\frac{2}{3} \frac{1}{3} \frac{1}{2}\right)$. Thus for $\mathbf{G}=\frac{2 \pi}{c} \mathbf{b}_{3}$,

$$
\sum_{j=1}^{n} e^{i \mathbf{G} \cdot \mathbf{d}_{j}}=1+e^{\frac{i 2 \pi}{c} \cdot \frac{c}{2}}=0,
$$

and hence

$$
V_{\mathbf{G}}=0 .
$$

The first-order energy gap across this zone face is therefore

$$
2\left|V_{\mathbf{G}}\right|=0 .
$$

(b) In the hcp structure, each energy band contains $2 \times 2 \times N=4 N$ states, where $N$ is the number of unit cells. However, for a solid made up of monovalent atoms, the total number of valence electrons in $2 N$, not enough to fill up the energy band completely. Hence it cannot be an insulator.

(c) Conservation of lattice momentum requires

$$
\mathbf{k}^{\prime}=\mathbf{k}+\mathbf{k}_{\mathbf{p h}}+\mathbf{G} .
$$

The wavelength of visible light is about $5000 \AA$, giving $k_{\mathrm{ph}}=\frac{2 \pi}{\lambda}=$ $10^{5} \mathrm{~cm}^{-1}$. The linear size of the Brillouin zone $\pi / a$ is of the order $10^{8} \mathrm{~cm}^{-1}$. Hence $\mathbf{k}_{\mathrm{ph}}$ can affect the wave vector in the above formula only to a very small extent. Because of translation symmetry, a change of the wave vector by $\mathbf{G}$ does not generate any effect. Thus interband transitions can be considered as transitions between two energy bands with identical wave vectors, as shown by Fig. 1.21.

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_41580ef03e6631735034g-03.jpg?height=621&width=794&top_left_y=1782&top_left_x=687)

Fig. $1.21$ Since $k_{\mathrm{F}}$ is much less than $k_{\mathrm{ZB}}$, one can make an estimation of the threshold wavelength for interband transitions using the free-electron approximation. From Fig. $1.21$ it can be seen that

$$
\hbar \omega_{\text {th }}=\hbar \frac{2 \pi c}{\lambda_{\text {th }}}=\frac{\hbar^{2}}{2 m}\left(2 k_{\mathrm{ZB}}-k_{\mathrm{F}}\right)^{2}-\frac{\hbar^{2}}{2 m} k_{\mathrm{F}}^{2}=\frac{2 \hbar^{2}}{m} k_{\mathrm{B}}\left(k_{\mathrm{B}}-k_{\mathrm{F}}\right),
$$

which gives the threshold wavelength

$$
\lambda_{\mathrm{th}}=\frac{\pi m c}{\hbar k_{\mathrm{ZB}}\left(k_{\mathrm{ZB}}-k_{\mathrm{F}}\right)},
$$

where

$$
k_{\mathrm{F}}=\left(3 \pi^{2} n\right)^{1 / 3}=\left(3 \pi^{2} \frac{2}{a^{3}}\right)^{1 / 3}=\frac{1}{a}\left(6 \pi^{2}\right)^{1 / 3} .
$$

The reciprocal lattice of a bcc lattice with edge $a$ is an fcc lattice with edge $\frac{4 \pi}{a}$. Thus $k_{\mathrm{ZB}}$ is a quarter of the diagonal of the fcc face,

$$
k_{\mathrm{ZB}}=\frac{1}{4} \sqrt{2} \frac{4 \pi}{a}=\frac{\sqrt{2} \pi}{a} .
$$

Hence

$$
\begin{aligned}
\lambda_{\text {th }} &=\frac{m c}{\hbar} \frac{a^{2}}{2 \pi-\sqrt{2}\left(6 \pi^{2}\right)^{1 / 3}} \\
&=\frac{m c^{2}}{\hbar c} \frac{a^{2}}{2 \pi-\sqrt{2}\left(6 \pi^{2}\right)^{1 / 3}} .
\end{aligned}
$$

With $\hbar c=1.97 \times 10^{3} \mathrm{eV} \cdot \AA, m c^{2}=0.511 \mathrm{MeV}, a=4.23 \AA$, we obtain

$$
\lambda_{\text {th }}=6034 \AA .
$$

\section{2}

Figure $1.22$ is an energy versus wave vector diagram for electrons in a one-dimensional solid.

(a) If $n$ is the number density for electrons and $p$ is that for holes, what can be inferred about $p / n$ ?

(b) Does this material have an even or odd number of conduction electrons per unit cell? Justify your answer. 

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_41580ef03e6631735034g-05.jpg?height=504&width=700&top_left_y=336&top_left_x=772)

Fig. $1.22$

(c) Which is greater, the effective mass of the electron or that of the hole? In terms of quantities in the diagram, derive approximate expressions for the effective masses.

(Princeton)

\section{Solution:}

(a) $p / n=1$.

(b) Figure $1.22$ shows that there is no overlapping of the energy bands so that the conduction band is completely filled. Since each unit cell can take two electrons, this material contains an even number of conduction electrons per unit cell.

(c) The region $\left(k_{1}, k_{2}\right)$ corresponds to conduction electrons and $\left(k_{3}, k_{4}\right)$ to holes. The figure indicates that the energy curve varies more smoothly in $\left(k_{3}, k_{4}\right)$ than in $\left(k_{1}, k_{2}\right)$. Hence the effective mass, which is given by

$$
m^{*}=\left|\hbar^{2}\left(\frac{d^{2} \varepsilon}{d k^{2}}\right)^{-1}\right|
$$

is greater for the holes than for the electrons.

Approximating the energy curve in the regions $\left(k_{1}, k_{2}\right),\left(k_{3}, k_{4}\right)$ by parabolas

$$
\begin{aligned}
&\varepsilon-\varepsilon_{\mathrm{C}}=a\left(k-\frac{k_{1}+k_{2}}{2}\right)^{2} \\
&\varepsilon_{\mathrm{V}}-\varepsilon=b\left(k-\frac{k_{3}+k_{4}}{2}\right)^{2}
\end{aligned}
$$

where 

$$
\begin{aligned}
a &=4\left(\varepsilon_{\mathrm{F}}-\varepsilon_{\mathrm{C}}\right)\left(k_{2}-k_{1}\right)^{-2} \\
b &=4\left(\varepsilon_{\mathrm{V}}-\varepsilon_{\mathrm{F}}\right)\left(k_{4}-k_{3}\right)^{-2},
\end{aligned}
$$

respectively, we find

$$
m_{\mathrm{e}}^{*}=\frac{\hbar^{2}}{2 a}=\frac{\hbar^{2}\left(k_{2}-k_{1}\right)^{2}}{8\left(\varepsilon_{\mathrm{F}}-\varepsilon_{\mathrm{C}}\right)}
$$

as the effective mass of an electron, and

$$
m_{\mathrm{h}}^{*}=\frac{\hbar^{2}}{2 b}=\frac{\hbar^{2}\left(k_{4}-k_{3}\right)^{2}}{8\left(\varepsilon_{\mathrm{V}}-\varepsilon_{\mathbf{F}}\right)}
$$

as the effective mass of a hole. As $\left|k_{2}-k_{1}\right|<\left|k_{4}-k_{3}\right|, 0<\varepsilon_{\mathrm{V}}-\varepsilon_{\mathrm{F}}<\varepsilon_{\mathrm{F}}-\varepsilon_{\mathrm{C}}$,

$$
m_{\mathrm{e}}^{*}<m_{\mathrm{h}}^{*} \text {. }
$$

\section{3}

Consider an intrinsic semiconductor whose electronic density of states function $N(E)$ is depicted in Fig. 1.23.

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_41580ef03e6631735034g-06.jpg?height=445&width=1106&top_left_y=1504&top_left_x=604)

Fig. $1.23$

(1) Where is the Fermi level with respect to the valence and conduction bands?

(2) Write the Fermi distribution function assuming that $N(E)$ already contains the spin degeneracy factor of 2 .

(3) Estimate the density of conduction band electrons at room temperature.

(Wisconsin) 

\section{Solution:}

(1) The number of electrons in the conduction band, whose bottom is $\varepsilon_{\mathrm{C}}$, and the number of holes in the valence band, whose top is $\varepsilon_{\mathrm{V}}$, are respectively

$$
\begin{aligned}
&n=\int_{\varepsilon_{\mathrm{C}}}^{\infty} N(E) f(E) d E, \\
&p=\int_{-\infty}^{\varepsilon_{\mathrm{V}}} N(E)(1-f(E)) d E,
\end{aligned}
$$

where

$$
f(E)=\frac{1}{e^{\left(E-E_{\mathbf{F}}\right) / k_{\mathbf{B}} T}+1}
$$

is the Fermi-Dirac distribution function giving the probability that an energy level $E$ is occupied. For an intrinsic semiconductor, $n=p$, from which the Fermi level $E_{\mathrm{F}}$ can be derived. For example consider the case where $E-E_{\mathrm{F}} \gg k_{\mathrm{B}} T$, then $f(E) \approx e^{-\left(E-E_{\mathrm{F}}\right) k_{\mathrm{B}} T}$, whence

$$
\begin{aligned}
n & \approx \int_{\varepsilon_{\mathrm{C}}}^{\infty} n_{0} e^{-\left(E-E_{\mathrm{F}}\right) / k_{\mathrm{B}} T} d E=n_{0} k_{\mathrm{B}} T e^{-\left(\varepsilon_{\mathrm{C}}-E_{\mathrm{F}}\right) / k_{\mathrm{B}} T}, \\
p & \approx n_{0} k_{\mathrm{B}} T e^{-\left(E_{\mathrm{F}}-\varepsilon_{\mathrm{V}}\right) / k_{\mathrm{B}} T},
\end{aligned}
$$

where $n_{0}$ is the density of electron states. From $n=p$ we have

$$
E_{\mathrm{F}}=\frac{\varepsilon_{\mathrm{C}}+\varepsilon_{\mathrm{V}}}{2} .
$$

(2) The function $f(E)$ above gives the distribution of the electrons among the energy states $E$ of the conduction band and is known as the Fermi-Dirac distribution function.

(3) As

$$
\varepsilon_{\mathrm{C}}-E_{\mathrm{F}} \approx \frac{E_{\mathrm{g}}}{2}=0.75 \mathrm{eV}
$$

and at room temperature $k_{\mathrm{B}} T=\frac{1}{40} \mathrm{eV}$, we have the density of conduction electrons

$$
n \approx n_{0} k_{\mathrm{B}} T \exp \left(-\frac{\varepsilon_{\mathrm{C}}-E_{\mathrm{F}}}{k_{\mathrm{B}} T}\right)=4.68 \times 10^{6} \mathrm{~cm}^{-3} .
$$



\section{4}

Consider an intrinsic semiconductor. Let $\varepsilon$ be the energy of an electron. Let $g_{\mathrm{C}}(\varepsilon)$ be the density of states in the conduction band, and $g_{\mathrm{V}}(\varepsilon)$ be the density of states in the valence band (see Fig. 1.24). Assume $\varepsilon_{\mathrm{C}}-\varepsilon_{\mathrm{F}} \gg k_{\mathrm{B}} T$, $\varepsilon_{\mathrm{F}}-\varepsilon_{\mathrm{V}} \gg k_{\mathrm{B}} T$, and

$$
\begin{aligned}
&g_{\mathrm{C}}(\varepsilon)=C_{1}\left(\varepsilon-\varepsilon_{\mathrm{C}}\right)^{1 / 2}, \\
&g_{\mathrm{V}}(\varepsilon)=C_{2}\left(\varepsilon_{\mathrm{V}}-\varepsilon\right)^{1 / 2},
\end{aligned}
$$

where $\varepsilon_{C}$ represents the energy of the bottom of the conduction band and $\varepsilon_{V}$ the top of the valence band. The Fermi energy is $\varepsilon_{\mathrm{F}}$.

(a) Find an expression for $n$, the number of electrons in the conduction band, in terms of $k_{\mathrm{B}}, T, C_{1}, \varepsilon_{\mathrm{C}}, \varepsilon_{\mathrm{F}}$ and a dimensionless definite integral.

(b) Find an expression for $p$, the number of holes in the valence band, in terms of $k_{\mathrm{B}}, T, C_{2}, \varepsilon_{\mathrm{V}}, \varepsilon_{\mathrm{F}}$ and a dimensionless definite integral.

(c) Find an explicit expression for $\varepsilon_{\mathbf{F}}(T)$.

(d) Which, if any, of the results of (a), (b) or (c) remain true if the material is doped with donor atoms? Explain

(Princeton)

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_41580ef03e6631735034g-08.jpg?height=528&width=501&top_left_y=1476&top_left_x=823)

Fig. 1.24

\section{Solution:}

(a) The concentration of electrons in the conduction band is given by

$$
n=\int_{\varepsilon_{\mathbf{C}}}^{\infty} f(\varepsilon) g_{\mathrm{C}}(\varepsilon) d \varepsilon
$$

with

$$
f(\varepsilon)=\frac{1}{e^{\beta\left(\varepsilon-\varepsilon_{F}\right)}+1} \text {, }
$$

where $\beta=\left(k_{\mathrm{B}} T\right)^{-1}$. Since $\varepsilon-\varepsilon_{\mathrm{F}} \geq \varepsilon_{\mathrm{C}}-\varepsilon_{\mathrm{F}} \gg k_{\mathrm{B}} T$

$$
f(\varepsilon) \approx e^{-\beta\left(\varepsilon-\varepsilon_{F}\right)}
$$

Setting $x=\beta\left(\varepsilon-\varepsilon_{C}\right)$, we have

$$
\begin{aligned}
n & \approx C_{1} \int_{\varepsilon_{\mathrm{C}}}^{\infty} e^{-\beta\left(\varepsilon-\varepsilon_{\mathrm{F}}\right)}\left(\varepsilon-\varepsilon_{\mathrm{C}}\right)^{\frac{1}{2}} d \varepsilon \\
&=C_{1} \beta^{-3 / 2} e^{-\beta\left(\varepsilon_{\mathrm{C}}-\varepsilon_{\mathrm{F}}\right)} \int_{0}^{\infty} e^{-x} x^{1 / 2} d x \\
&=C_{1}\left(k_{\mathrm{B}} T\right)^{3 / 2} e^{-\left(\varepsilon_{\mathrm{C}}-\varepsilon_{\mathrm{F}}\right) / k_{\mathrm{B}} T} \int_{0}^{\infty} e^{-x} x^{1 / 2} d x
\end{aligned}
$$

(b) The probability of hole occupation of a state is $1-f(\varepsilon)$. As $\varepsilon_{\mathrm{F}}-\varepsilon \geq \varepsilon_{\mathrm{F}}-\varepsilon_{\mathrm{V}} \gg k_{\mathrm{B}} T$

$$
1-f(\varepsilon) \approx \frac{e^{\beta\left(\varepsilon-\varepsilon_{F}\right)}}{e^{-\beta\left(\varepsilon_{F}-\varepsilon\right)}+1} \approx e^{\beta\left(\varepsilon-\varepsilon_{F}\right)} .
$$

Setting $x=-\beta\left(\varepsilon-\varepsilon_{V}\right)$, we have the concentration of holes in the valence band

$$
\begin{aligned}
p &=C_{2} \beta^{-3 / 2} e^{\beta\left(\varepsilon_{\mathrm{V}}-\varepsilon_{\mathrm{F}}\right)} \int_{0}^{\infty} e^{-x} x^{1 / 2} d x \\
&=C_{2}\left(k_{\mathrm{B}} T\right)^{3 / 2} e^{-\left(\varepsilon_{\mathrm{F}}-\varepsilon_{\mathrm{V}}\right) / k_{\mathrm{B}} T} \int_{0}^{\infty} e^{-x} x^{1 / 2} d x
\end{aligned}
$$

(c) For an intrinsic semiconductor, $n=p$. Hence

$$
C_{1} e^{-\left(\varepsilon_{\mathrm{C}}-\varepsilon_{\mathrm{F}}\right) / k_{\mathrm{B}} T}=C_{2} e^{-\left(\varepsilon_{\mathrm{F}}-\varepsilon_{\mathrm{V}}\right) / k_{\mathrm{B}} T}
$$

giving

$$
\varepsilon_{\mathrm{F}}=\frac{1}{2}\left(\varepsilon_{\mathrm{C}}+\varepsilon_{\mathrm{V}}-k_{\mathrm{B}} T \ln \frac{C_{1}}{C_{2}}\right)
$$

(d) If the material is doped, the results of (a) and (b) still hold true so long as it remains in the equilibrium state. However, because of doping the condition $n=p$ is no longer valid and the Fermi level $\varepsilon_{\mathrm{F}}$ as given in (c) will shift.

\section{5}

At room temperature, $k_{\mathrm{B}} T / e=26 \mathrm{mV}$. A sample of cadmium sulfide displays a mobile carrier density of $10^{16} \mathrm{~cm}^{-3}$ and a mobility coefficient $\mu=10^{2} \mathrm{~cm}^{2} /$ volt sec.

(a) Calculate the electrical conductivity of this sample.

(b) The carriers are continuously trapped into immobile sites and then being thermally reionized into mobile states. If the average free lifetime in a mobile state is $10^{-5}$ second, what is the rms distance a carrier diffuses between successive trappings?

(c) If the charge carriers have an effective mass equal to $0.1$ times the mass of a free electron, what is the average time between successive scatterings?

(Wisconsin )

\section{Solution:}

(a) The electrical conductivity is given by $\sigma=n e \mu$. With $n=10^{22} \mathrm{~m}^{-3}$, $e=1.6 \times 10^{-19} \mathrm{C}, \mu=10^{-2} \mathrm{~m}^{2} \mathrm{~V}^{-1} \mathrm{~s}^{-1}$, we have for the material $\sigma=$ $16 \Omega^{-1} \mathrm{~m}^{-1}$.

(b) The law of equipartition of energy

$$
\frac{1}{2} m \bar{v}_{x}^{2}=\frac{1}{2} m \bar{v}_{y}^{2}=\frac{1}{2} m \bar{v}_{z}^{2}=\frac{1}{2} k_{\mathrm{B}} T
$$

gives

$$
\frac{1}{2} m \bar{v}^{2}=\frac{3}{2} k_{\mathrm{B}} T,
$$

or

$$
\bar{v}^{2}=\sqrt{\frac{3 k_{\mathrm{B}} T}{m}} .
$$

The rms distance $l$ between successive trappings is given by

$$
l^{2}=\bar{v}^{2} t^{2} .
$$

Hence

$$
l=\sqrt{\frac{3 k_{\mathrm{B}} T}{m}} t=\sqrt{3\left(\frac{k_{\mathrm{B}} T}{e}\right) \frac{e}{m}} t .
$$

With $\frac{k_{\mathrm{B}} T}{e}=26 \times 10^{-3} \mathrm{~V}, \frac{e}{m}=1.76 \times 10^{11} \mathrm{C} \mathrm{kg}^{-1}, t=10^{-5} \mathrm{~s}$, we have $l=1.17 \mathrm{~m}$.

(c) The free electron model of metals gives

$$
\sigma=\frac{n e^{2}\langle\tau\rangle}{m^{*}},
$$

where $m^{*}$ is the effective mass of an electron. Then the average time between successive scatterings is

$$
\langle\tau\rangle=\frac{0.1 \sigma}{n e}\left(\frac{m}{e}\right)=5.7 \times 10^{-15} \mathrm{~s} .
$$

\section{6}

Determine the form of the temperature dependence of the electrical conductivity $\frac{n e^{2} \tau}{m}$ (where $\tau=$ effective collision time) of a semiconductor in a temperature domain where the density $n$ of free carriers is constant and the dominant scattering mechanism is Rutherford scattering from a constant and small number of charged impurities.

(Wisconsin)

\section{Solution:}

The differential cross section for Rutherford scattering of a particle of charge $e$, mass $m$, and velocity $v$ by an impurity nucleus of charge $Z e$ per unit solid angle is

$$
\frac{d \phi}{d \Omega}=\left(\frac{Z e^{2}}{8 \pi \epsilon m v^{2}}\right)^{2} \operatorname{cosec}^{4}\left(\frac{\theta}{2}\right)=R^{2} \operatorname{cosec}^{4}\left(\frac{\theta}{2}\right),
$$

where $\epsilon$ is the permittivity of the semiconductor material and $\theta$ is the scattering angle as shown in Fig. 1.25. The probability per unit time of scattering through a scattering angle between $\theta$ and $\theta+d \theta$ is 

$$
\begin{aligned}
d P=N_{\mathrm{I}} v d \phi &=2 \pi N_{\mathrm{I}} v R^{2} \frac{\sin \theta d \theta}{\sin ^{4}\left(\frac{\theta}{2}\right)} \\
&=8 \pi N_{\mathrm{I}} v R^{2} \frac{d(1-\cos \theta)}{(1-\cos \theta)^{2}},
\end{aligned}
$$

where $N_{\mathrm{I}}$ is the number of impurity atoms per unit volume.

In the expression for electrical conductivity,

$$
\sigma=\frac{n e^{2} \tau}{m},
$$

the effective collision time, or the relaxation time, $\tau$, is identical with the mean free time between collisions $\tau_{\mathrm{c}}$ only if the scattering is isotropic, which is not the case for Rutherford scattering. Instead,

$$
\tau=\tau_{\mathrm{c}}(1-\langle\cos \theta\rangle)^{-1},
$$

where $\langle\cos \theta\rangle$ is the average of the cosine of the scattering angle $\theta$. Thus

$$
\begin{aligned}
\frac{1}{\tau} &=\frac{\langle 1-\cos \theta\rangle}{\tau_{\mathrm{c}}} \approx \int(1-\cos \theta) d P \\
&=8 \pi N_{\mathrm{I}} v R^{2} \int_{\theta_{\min }}^{\theta_{\max }} \frac{d(1-\cos \theta)}{(1-\cos \theta)} \\
&=\frac{Z^{2} e^{4} N_{\mathrm{I}}}{8 \pi \epsilon^{2} m^{2} v^{3}} \ln \left(\frac{1-\cos \theta_{\max }}{1-\cos \theta_{\min }}\right) .
\end{aligned}
$$

Hence, treating the logarithmic function as approximately constant,

$$
\frac{1}{\tau} \propto N_{\mathrm{I}} v^{-3} .
$$

Since each degree of freedom of the carrier has an average energy $\frac{1}{2} k_{\mathrm{B}} T$,

$$
v=\sqrt{\frac{3 k_{\mathrm{B}} T}{m}} \propto T^{\frac{1}{2}}
$$

and

$$
\frac{1}{\tau} \propto T^{-\frac{3}{2}} .
$$

It follows that the electric conductivity

$$
\sigma=\frac{n e^{2} \tau}{m} \propto T^{\frac{3}{2}} .
$$



![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_41580ef03e6631735034g-13.jpg?height=417&width=721&top_left_y=377&top_left_x=707)

Fig. 1.25

\section{7}

Derive an expression for the Hall coefficient for an intrinsic semiconductor in terms of the electron and hole mobilities and the density of carriers.

(Wisconsin)

\section{Solution:}

Consider a rectangular sample of the intrinsic semiconductor parallel to the $x y$ plane, to which an electric field $E_{x}$ and a magnetic field $B_{z}$ are applied as shown in Fig. 1.26. The electric field causes the electrons to drift in the $-x$ direction, and the holes to drift in the $x$ direction, assuming the end surfaces perpendicular to $x$-axis are joined in a closed circuit. Because of the drift, the magnetic field exerts Lorentz forces $q \mathbf{v} \times \mathbf{B}$ on the carriers, causing both the electrons and holes to deflect in the $-y$ direction, toward the $D$ surface. While the electrons and holes tend to cancel each other, the cancellation is not complete with the result that a net charge accumulates on $D$ and an equal and opposite charge on C. This produces an electric field, the Hall field $\varepsilon_{y}$, in the $y$ direction.

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_41580ef03e6631735034g-13.jpg?height=460&width=794&top_left_y=1922&top_left_x=644)

Fig. $1.26$ The electric conductivity of a semiconductor is

$$
\sigma=n q \mu_{\mathrm{n}}+p q \mu_{\mathrm{p}}
$$

where $n, p$ are the electron and hole concentrations respectively and $\mu_{\mathrm{n}}$, $\mu_{\mathrm{p}}$ are their mobilities defined as velocity per unit field strength in the direction of the field. In the steady state there is no net current in the $y$ direction. However the hole and electron currents are not zero individually. Let their densities be $\left(J_{\mathrm{p}}\right)_{y},\left(J_{\mathrm{n}}\right)_{y}$ respectively. Then

$$
\begin{aligned}
\left(J_{\mathrm{p}}\right)_{y} &=p q \mu_{\mathrm{p}} \varepsilon_{y}-p q \mu_{\mathrm{p}}\left(v_{\mathrm{p}}\right)_{x} B_{z} \\
&=p q \mu_{\mathrm{p}} \varepsilon_{y}-p q \mu_{\mathrm{p}}^{2} E_{x} B_{z} \\
