\left(J_{\mathrm{n}}\right)_{y} &=n q \mu_{\mathrm{n}} \varepsilon_{y}-n q \mu_{\mathrm{n}}\left(v_{\mathrm{n}}\right)_{x} B_{z} \\
&=n q \mu_{\mathrm{n}} \varepsilon_{y}+n q \mu_{\mathrm{n}}^{2} E_{x} B_{z} .
\end{aligned}
$$

In the steady state,

$$
J_{y}=\left(J_{p}\right)_{y}+\left(J_{n}\right)_{y}=0,
$$

giving

$$
\varepsilon_{y}=\left(\frac{p \mu_{\mathrm{p}}^{2}-n \mu_{\mathrm{n}}^{2}}{p \mu_{\mathrm{p}}+n \mu_{\mathrm{n}}}\right) E_{x} B_{z} .
$$

The Hall coefficient, defined as $\frac{\varepsilon_{y}}{J_{x} B_{z}}$, is then

$$
R_{\mathrm{H}}=\frac{1}{q} \frac{\left(p \mu_{\mathrm{p}}^{2}-n \mu_{\mathrm{n}}^{2}\right)}{\left(p \mu_{\mathrm{p}}+n \mu_{\mathrm{n}}\right)^{2}},
$$

since $J_{x}=\sigma E_{x}$. For an intrinsic semiconductor, $n=p$ and

$$
R_{\mathrm{H}}=\frac{1}{q n} \frac{\left(\mu_{\mathrm{p}}-\mu_{\mathrm{n}}\right)}{\left(\mu_{\mathrm{p}}+\mu_{\mathrm{n}}\right)},
$$

\section{8}

Assume that the $E$ vs. $\mathbf{k}$ relationship for electrons in the conduction band of a hypothetical tetravalent $n$-type semiconductor can be approximated by

$$
E=a k^{2}+\text { constant } .
$$

The cyclotron resonance for electrons in a field $B=0.1$ Weber $/ \mathrm{m}^{2}$ occurs at an angular rotation frequency $\omega_{\mathrm{c}}=1.8 \times 10^{11} \mathrm{rad} \mathrm{s}^{-1}$.

(a) Find the value of $a$.

(b) Assume that the semiconductor is doped with pentavalent donors. Estimate the number of donors per $\mathrm{m}^{3}$, given that the Hall coefficient at room temperature is $R_{\mathrm{H}}=6.25 \times 10^{-6} \mathrm{~m}^{3}$ coul $^{-1}$ and the relative dielectric constant is $\varepsilon_{r}=15$.

(Wisconsin)

\section{Solution:}

(a) An electron in the conduction band behaves as if it had an effective mass $m^{*}$ given by

$$
\frac{1}{m^{*}}=\frac{1}{\hbar} \frac{\partial^{2} E}{\partial k^{2}}=\frac{2 a}{\hbar^{2}} .
$$

The cyclotron resonance frequency for the electron is

$$
\omega_{\mathrm{c}}=\frac{e B}{m^{*}} .
$$

Hence

$$
a=\frac{\hbar^{2}}{2} \frac{\omega_{\mathrm{c}}}{e B}=\frac{\left(1.05 \times 10^{-34}\right)^{2} \times 1.8 \times 10^{11}}{2 \times 1.6 \times 10^{-19} \times 0.1}=6.2 \times 10^{-38} \mathrm{~J} \mathrm{~m}^{2} .
$$

(b) For an $n$-type semiconductor, $n \gg p$ and (Problem 1047)

$$
R_{\mathrm{H}} \approx-\frac{1}{n e} .
$$

At room temperature, the impurity atoms are almost entirely ionized. Hence the impurity number density is

$$
N_{1} \approx n=-\frac{1}{R_{\mathrm{H}} e}=\frac{1}{6.25 \times 10^{-6} \times 1.6 \times 10^{-19}}=10^{24} \mathrm{~m}^{-3} .
$$

\section{9}

Optical excitation of intrinsic germanium creates an average density of $10^{12}$ conduction electrons per $\mathrm{cm}^{3}$ in the material at liquid nitrogen temperature. At this temperature, the electron and hole mobilities are equal, $\mu=0.5 \times 10^{4} \mathrm{~cm}^{2} /$ volt sec. The germanium dielectric constant is 20. (a) If 100 volts is applied across a $1 \mathrm{~cm}$ cube of crystal under these conditions, about how much current is observed?

(b) What is the approximate value of the diffusion coefficient for the electrons and holes? (At room temperature, $\frac{k_{\mathrm{B}} T}{e}=26 \mathrm{mV}$ )

(Wisconsin)

\section{Solution:}

(a) For an intrinsic semiconductor, $n=p$ and the current density is

$$
j=\sigma E=\left(n \mu_{\mathrm{n}}+p \mu_{\mathrm{p}}\right) e E=2 n \mu_{\mathrm{n}} e E,
$$

with $n=10^{18} \mathrm{~m}^{-3}, \mu_{\mathrm{n}}=0.5 \mathrm{~m}^{2} \mathrm{~V}^{-1} \mathrm{~s}^{-1}, E=\frac{100}{10^{-2}}=10^{4} \mathrm{Vm}^{-1}, V=$ $100 \mathrm{~V}, e=1.6 \times 10^{-19} \mathrm{C}$, the current density is

$$
j=1.6 \times 10^{3} \mathrm{Am}^{-2}
$$

and the current is

$$
I=j S=1.6 \times 10^{3} \times 10^{4}=0.16 \mathrm{~A}=160 \mathrm{~mA} .
$$

(b) The diffusion coefficient $D$ is given by the Einstein relation

$$
\frac{D}{\mu}=\frac{k_{\mathrm{B}} T}{e} .
$$

As at liquid nitrogen temperature $T_{\mathrm{D}}=77 \mathrm{~K}, \mu_{\mathrm{n}}=\mu_{\mathrm{p}}=\mu$, we have

$$
D_{n}=D_{p}=D .
$$

The diffusion coefficient for the electrons and holes is then

$$
D=\frac{k_{\mathrm{B}} T_{\mathrm{D}}}{e} \mu=\frac{1.38 \times 10^{-23} \times 77 \times 0.5}{1.6 \times 10^{-19}}=3.3 \times 10^{-3} \mathrm{~m}^{2} \mathrm{~s}^{-1} .
$$

\section{0}

The low temperature $(T \sim 4 \mathrm{~K})$ optical absorption spectrum of very pure InP, a direct gap semiconductor, is shown in Fig. 1.27, where intensity of absorption is plotted as a function of phonon energy (full lines). There 

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_41580ef03e6631735034g-17.jpg?height=442&width=954&top_left_y=348&top_left_x=648)

Fig. $1.27$

are a few lower intensity peaks between $\mathrm{C}$ and $\mathrm{A}$; these have not been shown for simplicity.

(a) What is the physical origin of the absorption continuum with threshold at $A$, and of the peak at $C$ ?

The energies $E_{\mathrm{A}}$ and $E_{\mathrm{C}}$ are $1.400 \mathrm{eV}$ and $1.386 \mathrm{eV}$ respectively. The static dielectric constant of $\operatorname{InP}$ is 9.6, and the hole in $\operatorname{InP}$ is much lighter than the electron $\left(m_{\mathrm{h}}^{*} \ll m_{\mathrm{e}}^{*}\right)$. From the above information calculate

(b) the direct band gap in InP,

(c) the hole mass $m_{\mathrm{h}}^{*}$.

At room temperature the sharp line $\mathrm{C}$ is broadened out and is not clearly seen (dotted lines).

(d) Explain this effect.

(Princeton)

\section{Solution:}

(a) The absorption continuum with threshold at $A$ is caused by the absorption of a photon from the incident beam by a valence electron, which then jumps into the conduction band. The process can take place if $h \nu \geq E_{\mathrm{g}}$, the energy gap. The lower photon energy limit corresponds to the threshold. The excited electron in the conduction band and the hole left in the valence band both behave as free particles.

If the incident photon energy $h \nu<E_{\mathrm{g}}$, it is not sufficient to excite a valence electron into the conduction band. However, it may still be sufficient to excite an electrons from some atom of the crystal leaving behind a hole, the electron-hole pair forming a bound state, called an exciton, in which the two particles revolve around their center of mass. The photon involved in exciton absorption has energy $h \nu=E_{\mathrm{g}}-E_{\text {ex }}$, where $E_{\text {ex }}$ is the exciton binding energy, usually about $0.01 \mathrm{eV}$. The exciton spectrum consists of a sharp line, slightly below the threshold of the absorption continuum, shown as the sharp peak at $\mathrm{C}$ in Fig. 1.27. The line may often be broadened by interaction of the exciton with impurities and other effects.

(b) $E_{\mathrm{g}}=E_{\mathrm{A}}=1.400 \mathrm{eV}$.

(c) Consider the exciton as analogous to the hydrogen atom. Then the energy of the $n$th state is

$$
E_{\mathrm{n}}=E_{\mathrm{g}}-\frac{\mu e^{4}}{2\left(4 \pi \epsilon_{0}\right)^{2} \epsilon^{2} \hbar^{2} n^{2}},
$$

where

$$
\mu=\frac{m_{\mathrm{e}}^{*} m_{\mathrm{h}}^{*}}{m_{\mathrm{e}}^{*}+m_{\mathrm{h}}^{*}} \approx m_{\mathrm{h}}^{*},
$$

as $m_{\mathrm{h}}^{*} \ll m_{\mathrm{e}}^{*}$, is the reduced mass of the exciton system. For the ground state, $n=1, E_{1}=E_{\mathrm{C}}=1.386 \mathrm{eV}, E_{\mathrm{g}}=1.400 \mathrm{eV}$, we have

$$
m_{\mathrm{h}}^{*} c^{2}=2\left(E_{\mathrm{g}}-E_{\mathrm{c}}\right) \epsilon^{2}\left(\frac{e^{2}}{4 \pi \epsilon_{0} \hbar c}\right)^{-2}
$$

With

$$
E_{\mathrm{g}}-E_{\mathrm{c}}=0.014 \mathrm{eV}, \quad \epsilon=9.6, \quad \frac{e^{2}}{4 \pi \epsilon_{0} \hbar c}=\frac{1}{137},
$$

we find

$$
m_{\mathrm{h}}^{*} c^{2}=4.85 \times 10^{4} \mathrm{eV}=9.5 \times 10^{-2} \mathrm{~m}_{\mathrm{e}} c^{2},
$$

or

$$
m_{\mathrm{h}}^{*}=9.5 \times 10^{-2} \mathrm{~m}_{\mathrm{e}} .
$$

(d) At room temperature, lattice vibration is appreciable and the electron in an exciton system interacts with the optical branch of the lattice vibration, causing broadening of the exciton line, which may merge with the characteristic absorption band although the peak of the exciton line may still be discernable. 

\section{1}

In crystalline silicon, the energy gap is $1.14 \mathrm{eV}$, the hole effective mass is roughly $m_{\mathrm{h}}=0.3 \mathrm{~m}$, and the electron effective mass is $m_{\mathrm{e}}=0.2 \mathrm{~m}$.

(a) Making (and justifying) appropriate approximations, find an expression for the function $f(T)$ in the law of mass action:

$$
n p=f(T),
$$

where $n$ and $p$ are the electron and hole concentrations and $T$ is the temperature. (You may need to know $\int_{0}^{\infty} x^{1 / 2} e^{-x} d x=\left(\frac{\pi}{4}\right)^{1 / 2}$ ).

(b) What concentration $N_{\mathrm{d}}$ of pentavalent As donors must be added to make the extrinsic conductivity $10^{4}$ times greater than the intrinsic conductivity at room temperature? (Neglect acceptor impurities and take the static dielectric constant to be $\varepsilon=11.8)$

(c) The work function $\phi_{\mathrm{s}}$ of a semiconductor is the difference in energy between an electron at rest in vacuum and the Fermi energy in the semiconductor. If a metal with work function $\phi_{\mathrm{m}}$ is used to make contact with the semiconductor, use band diagrams of the materials at the junction to show that the junction can be rectifying or ohmic, depending on the relative magnitudes of $\phi_{\mathrm{m}}$ and $\phi_{\mathrm{s}}$.

(Princeton)

\section{Solution:}

(a) Near the bottom of the conduction band of a semiconductor an electron of wave vector $k$ has energy

$$
E(k)=E_{\mathrm{g}}+\frac{\hbar^{2} k^{2}}{2 m_{\mathrm{e}}},
$$

where $E_{\mathrm{g}}$ is the energy gap and the top of the valence band is taken to be the zero-energy level, as shown in Fig. 1.28.

The number of states per unit volume of the semiconductor in the range $k, k+d k$ is

$$
\frac{4 \pi k^{2} d k}{(2 \pi)^{3}}=\frac{1}{4 \pi^{2}}\left(\frac{2 m_{\mathrm{e}}}{\hbar^{2}}\right)^{\frac{3}{2}}\left(E-E_{\mathrm{g}}\right)^{\frac{1}{2}} d E .
$$

As each $k$ corresponds to two spin states, the density of states per unit interval is

$$
g(E)=\frac{1}{2 \pi^{2}}\left(\frac{2 m_{\mathrm{e}}}{\hbar^{2}}\right)^{\frac{3}{2}}\left(E-E_{\mathrm{g}}\right)^{\frac{1}{2}} .
$$



![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_41580ef03e6631735034g-20.jpg?height=517&width=656&top_left_y=365&top_left_x=778)

Fig. $1.28$

When the system is in thermal equilibrium at temperature $T$, the electrons follow the Fermi distribution function

$$
f(E)=\frac{1}{1+e^{\left(E-E_{\mathrm{F}}\right) / k_{\mathrm{B}} T}} .
$$

In a semiconductor in Fermi level $E_{F}$ usually lies within the forbidden band between the valence and conduction bands, and its distance from either is far greater than $k_{\mathrm{B}} T$. Thus $E-E_{\mathrm{F}} \gg k_{\mathrm{B}} T$ and we can take

$$
f(E) \approx e^{\left(E_{\mathrm{F}}-E\right) / k_{\mathrm{B}} T} .
$$

The electron concentration of the conduction band is therefore

$$
\begin{aligned}
n &=\int_{E_{\mathrm{g}}}^{\infty} f(E) g(E) d E \\
& \approx \frac{1}{2 \pi^{2}}\left(\frac{2 m_{\mathrm{e}}}{\hbar^{2}}\right)^{\frac{3}{2}} \int_{E_{\mathrm{g}}}^{\infty}\left(E-E_{\mathrm{g}}\right)^{\frac{1}{2}} e^{\left(E_{\mathrm{F}}-E\right) / k_{\mathrm{B}} T} d E \\
&=\frac{1}{2 \pi^{2}}\left(\frac{2 m_{\mathrm{e}} k_{\mathrm{B}} T}{\hbar^{2}}\right)^{\frac{3}{2}} e^{-\left(E_{\mathrm{g}}-E_{\mathrm{F}}\right) / k_{\mathrm{B}} T} \int_{0}^{\infty} x^{\frac{1}{2}} e^{-x} d x
\end{aligned}
$$

with $x=\frac{E-E_{\mathrm{g}}}{k_{\mathrm{B}} T}$. Hence

$$
n \approx N_{\mathrm{e}} e^{-\left(E_{\mathrm{g}}-E_{\mathrm{F}}\right) / k_{\mathrm{B}} T}
$$

with 

$$
N_{\mathrm{e}}=2\left(\frac{m_{\mathrm{e}} k_{\mathrm{B}} T}{2 \pi \hbar^{2}}\right)^{\frac{3}{2}}
$$

Similarly, the hole concentration in the valence band is

$$
p=\int_{-\infty}^{0} f_{\mathrm{h}}(E) g_{\mathrm{h}}(E) d E
$$

where

$$
\begin{aligned}
&f_{\mathrm{h}}(E)=1-f(E) \approx e^{-\left(E_{\mathrm{F}}-E\right) / k_{\mathrm{B}} T} \\
&g_{\mathrm{h}}(E)=\frac{1}{2 \pi^{2}}\left(\frac{2 m_{\mathrm{h}}}{\hbar^{2}}\right)^{\frac{3}{2}}(-E)^{\frac{1}{2}}
\end{aligned}
$$

Hence

$$
p \approx N_{\mathrm{h}} e^{-E_{\mathrm{F}} / k_{\mathrm{B}} T}
$$

with

$$
N_{\mathrm{h}}=2\left(\frac{m_{\mathrm{h}} k_{\mathrm{B}} T}{2 \pi \hbar^{2}}\right)^{\frac{3}{2}}
$$

Therefore

$$
\begin{aligned}
f(T) &=n p \approx N_{\mathrm{e}} N_{\mathrm{h}} e^{-E_{\mathrm{g}} / k_{\mathrm{B}} T} \\
&=4\left(m_{\mathrm{e}} m_{\mathrm{h}}\right)^{\frac{3}{2}}\left(\frac{k_{\mathrm{B}} T}{2 \pi \hbar^{2}}\right)^{3} e^{-E_{\mathrm{g}} / k_{\mathrm{B}} T}
\end{aligned}
$$

(b) The expression of $n p=f(T)$ shows that, at a definite temperature and for a nondegenerate semiconductor, the product of the carrier concentrations at thermal equilibrium is independent of the impurity present. Hence it is also true for an intrinsic semiconductor for which $n=p=n_{\mathbf{i}}$. Thus for an intrinsic semiconductor, the conductivity is

$$
\sigma_{\mathrm{i}}=e n_{\mathrm{i}}\left(\mu_{\mathrm{e}}+\mu_{\mathrm{h}}\right) \approx 2 e n_{\mathrm{i}} \mu_{\mathrm{e}}
$$

The donor As is pentavalent while the acceptor $\mathrm{Si}$ is tetravalent so each donor atom contributes one free electron at room temperature. Thus $n \approx N_{\mathbf{d}}$. Neglecting the effect of the acceptor impurities, the conductivity of the material is

$$
\sigma=e \mu_{\mathrm{e}} N_{\mathrm{d}}
$$

For

$$
\frac{\sigma}{\sigma_{i}}=\frac{N_{\mathrm{d}}}{2 n_{\mathrm{i}}}=10^{4},
$$

we require

$$
N_{\mathrm{d}}=2 \times 10^{4} n_{\mathrm{i}}
$$

\section{From (a)}

$$
\begin{aligned}
n_{\mathrm{i}} &=\sqrt{f(T)}=2\left(m_{\mathrm{e}} m_{\mathrm{h}}\right)^{\frac{3}{4}}\left(\frac{k_{\mathrm{B}} T}{2 \pi \hbar^{2}}\right)^{\frac{3}{2}} e^{-E_{\mathrm{g}} / k_{\mathrm{B}} T} \\
&=2(0.2 \times 0.3)^{0.75}\left(\frac{k_{\mathrm{B}} T m c^{2}}{2 \pi \hbar^{2} c^{2}}\right)^{1.5} e^{-E_{\mathrm{g}} / k_{\mathrm{B}} T} .
\end{aligned}
$$

\section{With}

$$
\begin{aligned}
k_{\mathrm{B}} T & \approx \frac{1}{40} \mathrm{eV} \text { at room temperature, } \\
E_{\mathrm{g}} &=1.14 \mathrm{eV} \text { for } \mathrm{Si}, \\
m c^{2} &=0.511 \mathrm{MeV}, \quad c=3 \times 10^{8} \mathrm{~ms}^{-1}, \\
\hbar &=6.582 \times 10^{-16} \mathrm{eVs},
\end{aligned}
$$

we have

$$
\begin{aligned}
n_{\mathrm{i}} &=2(0.2 \times 0.3)^{0.75}\left(\frac{0.511 \times 10^{6}}{80 \pi}\right)^{1.5}\left(\frac{1}{6.58 \times 10^{-16} \times 3 \times 10^{8}}\right)^{3} e^{-22.8} \\
&=3.62 \times 10^{14} \mathrm{~m}^{-3}
\end{aligned}
$$

and hence

$$
N_{\mathrm{d}}=7.2 \times 10^{18} \mathrm{~m}^{-3} .
$$

(c) If $\Phi_{\mathrm{m}}<\Phi_{\mathrm{s}}$, the energy band diagrams before and after the metal makes contact with the $n$-type semiconductor are as shown in Fig. $1.29$ (a) and (b) respectively. 

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_41580ef03e6631735034g-23.jpg?height=461&width=629&top_left_y=412&top_left_x=455)

(a)

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_41580ef03e6631735034g-23.jpg?height=626&width=520&top_left_y=324&top_left_x=1147)

(b)

Fig. $1.29$

After contact, because $\Phi_{m}<\Phi_{s}$, the electrons in the metal flow into the semiconductor. The energy band near the surface of the semiconductor bends down to form a high-conductivity antiblocking layer whose effect on the contact resistance between the semiconductor and the metal is very small. Hence such a junction is ohmic.

If $\Phi_{m}>\Phi_{s}$, the energy band diagrams before and after the metal makes contact with the $n$-type semiconductor are as shown in Fig. $1.30$ (a) and (b) respectively.

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_41580ef03e6631735034g-23.jpg?height=467&width=725&top_left_y=1745&top_left_x=364)

(a)

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_41580ef03e6631735034g-23.jpg?height=534&width=574&top_left_y=1674&top_left_x=1147)

(b)

Fig. $1.30$ After contact, as $\Phi_{\mathrm{m}}>\Phi_{\mathrm{s}}$, the electrons in the semiconductor flow into the metal, and the energy band near the surface of the semiconductor bends up to form a high-resistance zone, i.e., a blocking layer or surface potential barrier. When a forward bias is applied, the metal becomes positive and the semiconductor negative, the surface potential barrier decreases, and the electron current flowing from the semiconductor to the metal increases significantly, forming a large forward current. Conversely when a reverse bias is applied, the surface potential barrier will increases and the electron current flowing from the semiconductor to the metal will decrease appreciably, approaching zero. Thus, only a few electrons flow from the metal to the semiconductor, forming only a small reverse current. Hence such a junction acts as a rectifier.

\subsection{ELECTROMAGNETIC PROPERTIES, OPTICAL PROPERTIES AND SUPERCONDUCTIVITY (1052-1076)}

\section{2}

The orienting tendency of an electric field $E$ on a permanent dipole is opposed by thermal agitation.

(a) Using classical statistical mechanics, calculate the total polarization of a gas of $N$ independent permanent dipoles of moment $P$.

(b) Show that for small fields, the orientational polarization per dipole is inversely proportional to the temperature.

(c) Liscuss the effect of this phenomenon on the dielectric constant of water, a polarizable molecule, at high frequencies and at low frequencies.

(Princeton)

\section{Solution:}

(a) In a gas of independent dipoles, the latter are able to rotate freely. The potential energy of a dipole of moment $\mathbf{p}$ in an applied electric field $\mathbf{E}$ is

$$
V=-\mathbf{p} \cdot \mathbf{E}=-p E \cos \theta,
$$

where $\theta$ is the angle it makes with the direction of the field. Classical statistical mechanics gives that the probability of finding the dipole along the $\theta$ direction is given by the Boltzmann distribution function 

$$
f(\theta)=e^{-V / k_{\mathrm{B}} T}=\exp \left(\frac{p E \cos \theta}{k_{\mathrm{B}} T}\right)
$$

The average value of $p$ along the direction of the field is then

$$
\langle p \cos \theta\rangle=\frac{\int p \cos \theta f(\theta) d \Omega}{\int f(\theta) d \Omega}
$$

with $d \Omega=2 \pi \sin \theta d \theta$. Thus

$$
\begin{aligned}
\langle p \cos \theta\rangle &=\frac{\int_{0}^{\pi} p \cos \theta \exp \left(\frac{p E \cos \theta}{k_{\mathrm{B}} T}\right) 2 \pi \sin \theta d \theta}{\int_{0}^{\pi} \exp \left(\frac{p E \cos \theta}{k_{\mathrm{B}} T}\right) 2 \pi \sin \theta d \theta} \\
&=\frac{p \int_{-1}^{1} e^{\beta x} x d x}{\int_{-1}^{1} e^{\beta x} d x} \\
&=\frac{p}{\beta}(\beta \operatorname{coth} \beta-1)
\end{aligned}
$$

where $x=\cos \theta, \beta=\frac{p E}{k_{\mathrm{B}} T}$.

As there are $N$ independent dipoles in the gas the total polarization is

$$
P=N\langle p \cos \theta\rangle=\frac{N k_{\mathrm{B}} T}{E}\left(\frac{p E}{k_{\mathrm{B}} T} \operatorname{coth} \frac{p E}{k_{\mathrm{B}} T}-1\right)
$$

(b) For small fields, $\beta=\frac{p E}{k_{\mathrm{B}} T} \ll 1$ and

$$
\operatorname{coth} \beta=\frac{1}{\beta}+\frac{\beta}{3}-\frac{\beta^{3}}{45}+\ldots \approx \frac{k_{\mathrm{B}} T}{p E}+\frac{p E}{3 k_{\mathrm{B}} T} .
$$

By definition, the orientational polarizability per dipole is

$$
\alpha=\frac{P}{E N} \approx \frac{p^{2}}{3 k_{\mathrm{B}} T} \propto T^{-1} \text {. }
$$

(c) If the electric field oscillates, the dipoles, following the field, will flip back and forth as the field reverses its direction during each cycle. However, the dipole may experience friction due to its collision with other molecules in the gas, causing some loss of energy known as dielectric loss. This means that a relaxation time $\tau$ is involved, as well as a phase lag between the field and the polarization, and that the dielectric constant $\epsilon=\epsilon_{0}+\alpha n$, where $\epsilon_{0}$ is the vacuum permittivity and $n$ the number of dipoles per unit volume, is complex:

$$
\epsilon=\epsilon_{1}+i \epsilon_{2} .
$$

The variations of $\epsilon_{1}, \epsilon_{2}$ with $\omega \tau$ are shown in Fig. 1.31. The imaginary part, $\epsilon_{2}$, is proportional to the dielectric loss.

At low frequencies $(\omega \tau \ll 1)$, dielectric loss is small and $\epsilon \approx \epsilon_{1} \approx$ $\epsilon_{r}$, the static permittivity. As the frequency increases to the vicinity of $\omega \tau \approx 1$, dielectric loss becomes appreciable and the real part of the dielectric constant decreases with increasing frequency in that region. At high frequencies $(\omega \tau \gg 1)$, the flippings of the dipoles are unable to follow the rapid oscillations of the field and dielectric loss again becomes small. In this region the real part $\epsilon_{1}$ tends to a constant $\epsilon_{\infty}$, the dielectric constant for high frequencies.

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_41580ef03e6631735034g-26.jpg?height=589&width=715&top_left_y=1424&top_left_x=800)

Fig. 1.31

\section{3}

(a) Derive the expression $\varepsilon(\omega)=1-\frac{\omega_{\mathrm{p}}^{2}}{\omega^{2}}, \omega_{\mathbf{p}}^{2}=\frac{n e^{2}}{\epsilon_{0} m}$ for the dielectric constant as a function of $\omega$ for a free electron gas of number density $n$. (b) Show clearly that metals are opaque to light for which $\omega$ is less than $\omega_{\mathrm{p}}$

(c) Calculate the wavelength cutoff for $\mathrm{Na}$ metal if the volume of a primitive unit cell in $\mathrm{Na}$ is $35 \times 10^{-30} \mathrm{~m}^{3}$.

(Wisconsin)

\section{Solution:}

(a) Take the $x$-axis parallel to the applied electric field $\mathbf{E}=\mathbf{E}_{0} e^{i \omega t}$. The displacement of a free electron from its neutral position, taken as the origin, is given by

$$
m \frac{d^{2} x}{d t^{2}}=-e E_{0} e^{i \omega t} .
$$

Try a solution of the form $x=x_{0} e^{i \omega t}$. Substitution gives

$$
x_{0}=\frac{e E_{0}}{m \omega^{2}} .
$$

The dipole moment per unit volume, or the polarization, of the electron gas is by definition

$$
P=-n e x=-\frac{n e^{2}}{m \omega^{2}} E_{0} e^{i \omega t}=-\frac{n e^{2}}{m \omega^{2}} E
$$

The (relative) dielectric constant $\epsilon$ is then

$$
\epsilon=\frac{D}{\epsilon_{0} E}=\frac{\epsilon_{0} E+P}{\epsilon_{0} E}=1-\frac{n e^{2}}{\epsilon_{0} m} \frac{1}{\omega^{2}}=1-\frac{\omega_{\mathrm{p}}^{2}}{\omega^{2}}
$$

where $\omega_{\mathrm{p}}=\sqrt{\frac{n e^{2}}{\epsilon_{0} m}}$ is the plasma (angular) frequency of the gas.

(b) The refractive index of a substance is $n=\sqrt{\epsilon}$. For light of frequency $\omega<\omega_{\mathrm{p}}$ incident on a metal of plasma frequency $\omega_{\mathbf{p}}, n$ is imaginary. This means that such light cannot be transmitted through the metal but is totally reflected from its surface. Thus metals are opaque to light for which $\omega<\omega_{\mathrm{p}}$.

(c) The sodium atom has valency one and in the metallic state each atom contributes one conduction electron. As each primitive cell contains one atom, the number density of conduction electrons is

$$
n=\frac{1}{35 \times 10^{-30}}=2.86 \times 10^{28} \mathrm{~m}^{-3} .
$$

Then

$$
\begin{aligned}
\omega_{\mathbf{p}}^{2} &=4 \pi c^{2} n\left(\frac{e^{2}}{4 \pi \epsilon_{0} m c^{2}}\right) \\
&=4 \pi \times\left(3 \times 10^{8}\right)^{2} \times 2.86 \times 10^{28} \times 2.82 \times 10^{-15} \\
&=9.12 \times 10^{31} \mathrm{~s}^{-2},
\end{aligned}
$$

and the cutoff angular frequency is

$$
\omega=\omega_{\mathrm{p}}=9.55 \times 10^{15} \mathrm{~s}^{-1} .
$$

Hence the cutoff wavelength is

$$
\lambda=\frac{2 \pi c}{\omega}=2.0 \times 10^{-7} \mathrm{~m}=2000 \AA .
$$

\section{4}

A solid is composed of a collection of identical atoms, each of which can be modeled as an electron of charge $q$ and mass $m$ coupled by a spring of constant $\kappa$ to a fixed atomic site. Energy dissipation is modeled by a damping force in each atom equal to $-b \mathbf{v}$, where $\mathbf{v}$ is the electron velocity. The number density of these atoms in the solid is $N$. A plane polarized electromagnetic wave of frequency $\omega$ propagates in the solid. Treat the oscillators classically and nonrelativistically. For uniformity of notation, use $\omega_{0}^{2}=\kappa / m, \gamma=b / m$ and $\omega_{\mathrm{p}}^{2}=4 \pi N q^{2} / m$.

(a) Compute the index of refraction $n(\omega)$.

(b) Sketch the real and imaginary parts of $n(\omega)$ in the low density limit $\left(\omega_{\mathrm{p}} / \omega_{0} \ll 1\right.$ and $\left.\omega_{\mathrm{p}} / \gamma \ll 1\right)$.

(c) At resonance (i.e., $\omega=\omega_{0}$ ), compute the attenuation length in the solid in the low density limit.

In the vicinity of resonance, the group velocity $v_{\mathrm{g}}$, defined as $d \omega / d k$, can exceed the speed of light. Explain why this is not a violation of causality.

$(M I T)$

\section{Solution:}

(a) Let $\mathbf{r}$ be the displacement of an electron of charge $q$ from the fixed atomic site and $\mathbf{E}=\mathbf{E}_{0} e^{-i \omega t}$ be the electric field intensity of the traversing wave at the location of the atom. The equation of motion of the electron is 

$$
m \ddot{\mathbf{r}}+b \dot{\mathbf{r}}+\kappa \mathbf{r}=q \mathbf{E}
$$

Try a solution $\mathbf{r}=\mathbf{r}_{0} e^{-i \omega t}$. Substitution gives

$$
\mathbf{r}=\frac{q \mathbf{E}}{-m \omega^{2}-i \omega b+\kappa}=\frac{q}{m} \frac{\mathbf{E}}{\omega_{0}^{2}-\omega^{2}-i \omega \gamma} .
$$

The electric dipole moment per unit volume, or the polarization, of the solid is then

$$
\mathbf{P}=n q \mathbf{r}=\frac{N q^{2}}{m} \frac{E}{\omega_{0}^{2}-\omega^{2}-i \omega \gamma}
$$

As $\mathbf{P}$ and $\mathbf{E}$ are parallel, the relative dielectric constant is

$$
\epsilon=\frac{\epsilon_{0} E+P}{\epsilon_{0} E}=1+\frac{\omega_{p}^{2}}{\omega_{0}^{2}-\omega^{2}-i \omega \gamma}
$$

where $\omega_{\mathbf{p}}^{2}=\frac{N q^{2}}{\epsilon_{0} m}$ (in SI units). The index of refraction $n$ is now complex:

$$
n \equiv \sqrt{\epsilon}=\eta+i \kappa
$$

with $\eta, \kappa$ given by

$$
\begin{aligned}
\eta^{2}-\kappa^{2} &=1+\frac{\left(\omega_{0}^{2}-\omega^{2}\right) \omega_{\mathrm{p}}^{2}}{\left(\omega_{0}^{2}-\omega^{2}\right)^{2}+\omega^{2} \gamma^{2}} \\
2 \eta \kappa &=\frac{\omega \omega_{\mathrm{p}}^{2} \gamma}{\left(\omega_{0}^{2}-\omega^{2}\right)^{2}+\omega^{2} \gamma^{2}}
\end{aligned}
$$

(b) As $\omega_{p} \propto \sqrt{N}$, in the low density limit, $\eta^{2}-\kappa^{2} \sim 1,2 \eta \kappa \sim 0$. This means that the imaginary part of $n, \kappa$, is small and the real part, $\eta$, is close to unity at low densities. The variations $\eta(\omega), \kappa(\omega)$ for low densities are sketched in Fig. 1.32. Note that $\kappa$ is maximum at $\omega=\omega_{0}$. The corresponding maximum absorption is known as resonance absorption. Also $\eta>1$ for $\omega<\omega_{0}$ and $\eta<1$ for $\omega>\omega_{0}$.

(c) At resonance, $\omega \approx \omega_{0}$ and

$$
\eta^{2}-\kappa^{2}=1, \quad 2 \eta \kappa=\frac{\omega_{p}^{2}}{\omega_{0} \gamma} .
$$

In the low density limit, $\omega_{p} \ll \gamma$ and the above set of equations has solution

$$
\eta \approx 1, \quad \kappa \approx \frac{\omega_{\mathbf{p}}^{2}}{2 \omega_{0} \gamma} .
$$



![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_41580ef03e6631735034g-30.jpg?height=463&width=569&top_left_y=365&top_left_x=756)

Fig. $1.32$

The wave function has an exponential factor

$$
e^{i(k n r-\omega t)}=e^{-\kappa k r} e^{i(k \eta r-\omega t)},
$$

where $k=\omega_{0} / c$, showing that the intensity has an attenuation factor $e^{-2 \kappa \omega_{0} r / c}$. The attenuation length $l$ is then given by

$$
\frac{2 \kappa \omega_{0}}{c} l=1,
$$

or

$$
l=\frac{c}{2 \kappa \omega_{0}}=\frac{c \gamma}{\omega_{\mathrm{p}}^{2}} .
$$

(d) The index of refraction is by definition

$$
n=\frac{c}{v}=\frac{c k}{\omega},
$$

giving

$$
k=\frac{n(\omega) \omega}{c} .
$$

The group velocity $v_{\mathrm{g}}$ is then

$$
v_{\mathrm{g}}=\left(\frac{d k}{d \omega}\right)^{-1}=\left(\frac{\omega}{c} \frac{d n}{d \omega}+\frac{n}{c}\right)^{-1} .
$$

In the low density limit, near resonance $\omega \approx \omega_{0}, \kappa \ll 1$ so that

$$
\begin{aligned}
n &=\eta+i \kappa \approx \eta, \\
v_{\mathrm{g}} & \simeq \frac{c}{\omega_{0}\left(\frac{d \eta}{d \omega}\right)_{\omega_{0}}+\eta} .
\end{aligned}
$$

As $\left(\frac{d \eta}{d \omega}\right)_{\omega_{0}}=\frac{1}{2 \eta}\left(\frac{d \eta^{2}}{d \omega}\right)_{\omega_{0}}=-\frac{\omega_{\mathrm{p}}^{2}}{\omega_{0} \gamma^{2}}, \eta \approx 1$ for low densities,

$$
v_{\mathrm{g}} \approx \frac{c}{1-\frac{\omega_{\mathrm{p}}^{2}}{\gamma^{2}}}>c,
$$

which shows that in the vicinity of resonance the group velocity exceeds the space of light. However, this is not a violation of causality because when anomalous dispersion occurs the group velocity can no longer be identified with the velocity of energy propagation, which is always less than $c$.

\section{5}

Let a dielectric be described as $N$ valence electrons per unit volume, bound to ions harmonically with angular frequency $\omega_{0}$. It is in a uniform magnetic field B directed along the $z$-axis. Monochromatic light of frequency $\omega$ is sent through the dielectric along the $z$-direction.

(a) If the light is circularly polarized, find the indices of refraction.

(b) If the light is linearly polarized, calculate the angle through which the polarization rotates as the wave propagates a distance $z$ in the dielectric (you may assume that $\Delta n \ll n$, where $\Delta n=n_{\mathrm{R}}-n_{\mathrm{L}}$ and $\left.n=\left(n_{\mathrm{R}}+n_{\mathrm{L}}\right) / 2\right)$.

(Wisconsin)

\section{Solution:}

(a) The equation of motion for a valence electron is

$$
m \frac{d^{2} \mathbf{r}}{d t^{2}}=-e \mathbf{E}-e \mathbf{V} \times \mathbf{B}-m \omega_{0}^{2} \mathbf{r},
$$

where $\mathbf{r}$ is the displacement of the electron from its equilibrium position. The collective motion of the $N$ valence electrons generates a polarization

$$
\mathbf{P}=-N e \mathbf{r} .
$$

The equation of the polarization is accordingly

$$
-\frac{m}{N e} \frac{d^{2} \mathbf{P}}{d t^{2}}=-e \mathbf{E}-e\left(-\frac{1}{N e} \frac{d \mathbf{P}}{d t}\right) \times \mathbf{B}+\frac{m \omega_{0}^{2}}{N e} \mathbf{P} .
$$

As $\mathbf{E} \sim e^{i \omega t}$, we have $\mathbf{P} \sim e^{i \omega t}$ and $\frac{d}{d t}=i \omega$. Substitution in the above gives or

$$
\frac{m \omega^{2}}{N e} \mathbf{P}=-e \mathbf{E}-\frac{i \omega \mathbf{B} \times \mathbf{P}}{N}+\frac{m \omega_{0}^{2}}{N e} \mathbf{P},
$$

$$
\mathbf{E}=\frac{\omega_{0}^{2}-\omega^{2}}{\epsilon_{0} \omega_{\mathbf{p}}^{2}} \mathbf{P}+\frac{i \omega}{N e} \mathbf{B} \times \mathbf{P},
$$

where $\omega_{\mathrm{p}}^{2}=\frac{N e^{2}}{m \epsilon_{0}}$. As $\mathbf{B}=(0,0, B)$, the above can be written as

$$
\left(\begin{array}{l}
E_{x} \\
E_{y} \\
E_{z}
\end{array}\right)=\left(\begin{array}{ccc}
\frac{\omega_{0}^{2}-\omega^{2}}{\epsilon_{0} \omega_{\mathrm{p}}^{2}} & \frac{i \omega}{N e} B & 0 \\
-\frac{i \omega}{N e} B & \frac{\omega_{0}^{2}-\omega^{2}}{\epsilon_{0} \omega_{\mathrm{p}}^{2}} & 0 \\
0 & 0 & \frac{\omega_{0}^{2}-\omega^{2}}{\epsilon_{0} \omega_{\mathrm{p}}^{2}}
\end{array}\right)\left(\begin{array}{c}
P_{x} \\
P_{y} \\
P_{z}
\end{array}\right) .
$$

Maxwell's equations for free space

$$
\nabla \times \mathbf{E}=-\dot{\mathbf{B}}, \quad \nabla \times \mathbf{H}=\dot{\mathbf{D}}, \quad \nabla \cdot \mathbf{D}=0
$$

with $\mathbf{B}=\mu_{0} \mathbf{H}, \mathbf{D}=\epsilon_{0} \mathbf{E}+\mathbf{P}$ give

$$
\mu_{0} \epsilon_{0} \ddot{\mathbf{E}}-\nabla^{2} \mathbf{E}=\frac{1}{\epsilon_{0}} \nabla(\nabla \cdot \mathbf{P})-\mu_{0} \ddot{\mathbf{P}} .
$$

With $\mathbf{E}=E_{0} e^{i(\omega t-k \cdot \mathbf{r})}, \mathbf{P}=P_{0} e^{i(\omega t-k \cdot \mathbf{r})}, \mathbf{k}=k \mathbf{e}_{z}$, we have

$$
\begin{gathered}
\frac{\partial^{2}}{\partial t^{2}}=-\omega^{2}, \quad \nabla^{2} \mathbf{E}=-k^{2} \mathbf{E}, \\
\nabla \cdot \mathbf{P}=-i \mathbf{k} \cdot \mathbf{P}=-i k P_{z}, \quad \nabla(\nabla \cdot \mathbf{P})=-k^{2} P_{z} \mathbf{e}_{z},
\end{gathered}
$$

and thus

$$
\epsilon_{0}\left(k^{2} c^{2}-\omega^{2}\right) \mathbf{E}=-k^{2} c^{2} P_{z} \mathbf{e}_{z}+\omega^{2} \mathbf{P}
$$

since $\mu_{0} \epsilon_{0}=c^{-2}$. Letting $\chi=\frac{k^{2} c^{2}}{\omega^{2}}-1$, we can write the above as

$$
\epsilon_{0} \chi \mathbf{E}=P_{x} \mathbf{e}_{x}+P_{y} \mathbf{e}_{y}-\chi P_{z} \mathbf{e}_{z},
$$

or

$$
\left(\begin{array}{c}
E_{x} \\
E_{y} \\
E_{z}
\end{array}\right)=\frac{1}{\epsilon_{0} \chi}\left(\begin{array}{ccc}
1 & 0 & 0 \\
0 & 1 & 0 \\
0 & 0 & -\chi
\end{array}\right)\left(\begin{array}{l}
P_{x} \\
P_{y} \\
P_{z}
\end{array}\right) .
$$

The above two matrix equations combine to give

$$
\left(\begin{array}{ccc}
\frac{\omega_{0}^{2}-\omega^{2}}{\epsilon_{0} \omega_{\mathrm{p}}^{2}}-\frac{1}{\epsilon_{0} \chi} & \frac{i \omega}{N e} B & 0 \\
-\frac{i \omega}{N e} B & \frac{\omega_{0}^{2}-\omega^{2}}{\epsilon_{0} \omega_{\mathrm{p}}^{2}}-\frac{1}{\epsilon_{0} \chi} & 0 \\
0 & 0 & \frac{\omega_{0}^{2}-\omega^{2}}{\epsilon_{0} \omega_{\mathrm{p}}^{2}}+\frac{1}{\epsilon_{0}}
\end{array}\right)\left(\begin{array}{c}
P_{x} \\
P_{y} \\
P_{z}
\end{array}\right)=0 .
$$

In general $\frac{\omega_{0}^{2}-\omega^{2}}{\epsilon_{0} \omega_{\mathrm{p}}^{2}}+\frac{1}{\epsilon_{0}} \neq 0$, hence $P_{z}=0$ and so $E_{z}=0$. This means that an incident plane wave will remain a plane wave. The condition for nonvanishing solutions is that determinant of the square matrix in the equation for $\mathbf{P}$ must vanish, giving

$$
\frac{\omega_{0}^{2}-\omega^{2}}{\epsilon_{0} \omega_{\mathbf{p}}^{2}}-\frac{1}{\epsilon_{0} \chi_{\pm}}=\pm \frac{\omega B}{N e} .
$$

Thus there are two refractive indices

$$
n_{\pm}=\frac{c k_{\pm}}{\omega}=\sqrt{1+\chi_{\pm}}=\sqrt{1+\frac{N e \omega_{\mathbf{p}}^{2}}{N e\left(\omega_{0}^{2}-\omega^{2}\right) \mp \epsilon_{0} \omega_{\mathrm{p}}^{2} \omega B}} .
$$

Corresponding to $n_{\pm}$we have

$$
\frac{\omega B}{N e} P_{x}=\mp \frac{i \omega B}{N e} P_{y}, \quad \text { or } \quad P_{y}=\pm i P_{x}
$$

and hence the amplitudes

$$
P_{\pm}=C_{\pm}\left(\begin{array}{c}
1 \\
\pm i \\
0
\end{array}\right)
$$

where $C_{+}, C_{-}$are constants. Similarly,

$$
E_{\pm}=\frac{C_{\pm}}{\epsilon_{0} \chi_{\pm}}\left(\begin{array}{c}
1 \\
\pm i \\
0
\end{array}\right)
$$

Since the factors $\pm i$ correspond to additional phase angles $\pm \frac{\pi}{2}$, the above shows that $E_{+}, E_{-}$are right-hand and left-hand circularly polarized waves with indices of refraction $n_{+}, n_{-}$respectively. (Right-hand rotation means that looking against the direction of light, E rotates clockwise. For $E_{+}$, $E_{y}=i E_{x}$, or $E_{x}=\frac{E_{0}}{2} \cos \omega t=\frac{E_{0}}{2} \cos (-\omega t), E_{y}=\frac{E_{0}}{2} \cos \left(\omega t+\frac{\pi}{2}\right)=$ $\left.\frac{E_{0}}{2} \sin (-\omega t).\right)$

(b) The linearly polarized light can be decomposed into right- and left-hand circulating polarized components 

$$
\begin{aligned}
\mathbf{E} &=\mathbf{E}_{+}+\mathbf{E}_{-}=\frac{\mathbf{E}_{0}}{2}\left(\begin{array}{c}
1 \\
i
\end{array}\right) e^{i\left(\omega t-k_{+} z\right)}+\frac{\mathbf{E}_{0}}{2}\left(\begin{array}{c}
1 \\
-i
\end{array}\right) e^{i\left(\omega t-k_{-} z\right)} \\
&=\mathbf{E}_{0}\left(\begin{array}{c}
\cos \left(\frac{k_{+}-k_{-}}{2} z\right) \\
-\sin \left(\frac{k_{+}-k_{-}}{2} z\right)
\end{array}\right) e^{i\left(\omega t-\frac{k_{+}+k_{-}}{2} z\right)} .
\end{aligned}
$$

This shows that when looking against the direction of light propagation, the plane of polarization turns anticlockwise with an angle

$$
\theta=\frac{k_{+}-k_{-}}{2} z=\frac{\omega z}{2 c}\left(n_{+}-n_{-}\right)=\frac{\omega z}{2 c} \Delta n,
$$

after traveling the medium for a distance $z$. Assuming

$$
\frac{\epsilon_{0} \omega_{\mathrm{p}}^{2} \omega B}{N e\left(\omega_{0}^{2}-\omega^{2}\right)} \ll 1,
$$

we have

$$
\begin{aligned}
n_{\pm} &=\sqrt{1+\frac{\omega_{\mathrm{p}}^{2}}{\omega_{0}^{2}-\omega^{2}}\left(1 \pm \frac{\epsilon_{0} \omega_{\mathrm{p}}^{2} \omega B}{N e\left(\omega_{0}^{2}-\omega^{2}\right)}\right)} \\
& \approx \sqrt{1+\frac{\omega_{\mathrm{p}}^{2}}{\omega_{0}^{2}-\omega^{2}}} \pm \frac{\epsilon_{0} \omega_{\mathrm{p}}^{4} \omega B}{2 N e\left(\omega_{0}^{2}-\omega^{2}\right)^{2}}\left(1+\frac{\omega_{\mathrm{p}}^{2}}{\omega_{0}^{2}-\omega^{2}}\right)^{-\frac{1}{2}} .
\end{aligned}
$$

Note that this approximation agrees with the condition $\Delta n \ll \frac{1}{2}\left(n_{+}+n_{-}\right)$. As

$$
\Delta n \approx \frac{\epsilon_{0} \omega_{\mathrm{p}}^{4} \omega B}{N e\left(\omega_{0}^{2}-\omega^{2}\right) \sqrt{\left(\omega_{0}^{2}-\omega^{2}\right)^{2}+\omega_{\mathrm{p}}^{2}\left(\omega_{0}^{2}-\omega^{2}\right)}},
$$

we obtain

$$
\theta=\frac{\omega_{\mathrm{p}}^{2} \omega^{2} \omega_{\mathrm{c}} z}{2 \mathrm{c}\left(\omega_{0}^{2}-\omega^{2}\right) \sqrt{\left(\omega_{0}^{2}-\omega^{2}\right)^{2}+\omega_{\mathrm{p}}^{2}\left(\omega_{0}^{2}-\omega^{2}\right)}},
$$

where $\omega_{\mathrm{c}}=\frac{e B}{m}$.

\section{6}

Circularly polarized light propagates along the $z$-axis through a fully ionized medium in which a static magnetic field $B$ is also directed along the $z$-axis. The index of refraction for right-hand circularly polarized radiation of angular frequency $\omega$ is

$$
n_{\mathbf{R}}^{2}=1-\frac{4 \pi N e^{2} / m}{\omega^{2}+\frac{e B \omega}{m c}},
$$

where $N$ is the electron density and $e, m$ and $c$ have their usual meaning.

(a) What is the corresponding expression for left-hand circularly polarized light?

(b) If $B=0$, what is the phase velocity?

(c) if $B=0$, what is the group velocity? You may assume that $\omega \gg \sqrt{\frac{4 \pi N e^{2}}{m}}$ but not $\omega \rightarrow \infty$

(Wisconsin)

\section{Solution:}

(a) The equation of motion of an electron in the medium is

$$
m \frac{d^{2} \mathbf{r}}{d t^{2}}=-e(\mathbf{E}+\mathbf{v} \times \mathbf{B}),
$$

where $\mathbf{r}$ is the displacement of the electron from the original position. The force on the electron due to the magnetic field associated with the traversing light has been omitted as it is smaller than the force due to the associated electric field by a factor $v / c$. With $\mathbf{E}=E_{x} \mathbf{e}_{x}+E_{y} \mathbf{e}_{y}, \mathbf{B}=B \mathbf{e}_{z}$, $\mathbf{r}=x \mathbf{e}_{x}+y \mathbf{e}_{y}+z \mathbf{e}_{z}$, the above becomes

$$
\begin{aligned}
&m \ddot{x}=-e E_{x}-e B \dot{y}, \\
&m \ddot{y}=-e E_{y}+e B \dot{x}, \\
&m \ddot{z}=0 .
\end{aligned}
$$

The last equation shows that $\dot{z}=$ constant so that any velocity in the $z$ direction is not associated with the oscillation caused by the traversing light and can hence be neglected. We can also take $z=0$.

For right-hand circularly polarized light, we have

$$
E_{x}=E_{0} e^{i(\omega t-k z)}, \quad E_{y}=i E_{x},
$$

and so

$$
x=x_{0} e^{i(\omega t-k z)}, \quad y=y_{0} e^{i(\omega t-k z)} .
$$

The equation of motion can thus be written as

$$
\begin{aligned}
&-m \omega^{2} x=-e E_{x}-i \omega e B y, \\
&-m \omega^{2} y=-e E_{y}+i \omega e B x .
\end{aligned}
$$

In terms of the polarization vector $\mathbf{P}=-N e \mathbf{r}$, the above become

$$
\begin{aligned}
-\frac{m \omega^{2}}{N e} P_{x} &=e E_{x}-\frac{i \omega B}{N} P_{y}, \\
-\frac{m \omega^{2}}{N e} P_{y} &=e E_{y}+\frac{i \omega B}{N} P .
\end{aligned}
$$

Maxwell's equation for a source-free isotropic medium,

$$
\nabla+\mathbf{E}=-\dot{\mathbf{B}}, \quad \nabla \times \mathbf{H}=\dot{\mathbf{D}},
$$

with

$$
\mathbf{B}=\mu_{0} \mathbf{H}, \quad \mathbf{D}=\epsilon_{0} \mathbf{E}+\mathbf{P}
$$

give

$$
\mu_{0} \ddot{\mathbf{P}}-\frac{1}{\epsilon_{0}} \nabla(\nabla \cdot \mathbf{P})=\nabla^{2} \mathbf{E}-\frac{1}{c^{2}} \ddot{\mathbf{E}} .
$$

We also have $\nabla \cdot \mathbf{D}=0$, which gives

$$
\nabla \cdot \mathbf{P}=\frac{1}{\epsilon_{0}} \nabla \cdot \mathbf{E}=\frac{1}{\epsilon_{0}} \frac{\partial E_{z}}{\partial z}=0
$$

since $\mathbf{E}$ depends on $z$ only and $E_{z}=0$. This also implies that $P_{z}=0$. Noting that

$$
\nabla^{2} \mathbf{E}=-k^{2} \mathbf{E}, \quad \frac{\partial^{2}}{\partial t^{2}}=-\omega^{2},
$$

we finally have

$$
\mathbf{P}=\chi \mathbf{E},
$$

where

$$
\chi=\epsilon_{0}\left(\frac{c^{2} k^{2}}{\omega^{2}}-1\right) .
$$

The differential equations for $P_{x}, P_{y}$ can now be written as

$$
\begin{aligned}
&\left(\frac{\omega^{2}}{N e}+\frac{e}{m \chi}\right) E_{x}-\frac{i \omega B}{N m} E_{y}=0, \\
&\frac{i \omega B}{N m} E_{x}+\left(\frac{\omega^{2}}{N e}+\frac{e}{m \chi}\right) E_{y}=0 .
\end{aligned}
$$

For nonvanishing solutions, we require that the determinant of the coeffcients of $E_{x}, E_{y}$ be zero. This gives

$$
\left(\frac{\omega^{2}}{N e}+\frac{e}{m \chi}\right)^{2}=\left(\frac{\omega B}{N m}\right)^{2}
$$

which has two solutions

$$
\frac{\omega^{2}}{N e}+\frac{e}{m \chi_{\pm}}=\pm \frac{\omega B}{N m} .
$$

The top sign gives $E y=-i E_{x}$, which is the left-hand circularly polarized light. The refraction index $n_{R}$ for the right-hand circularly polarized light is then given by

$$
\begin{aligned}
n_{\mathrm{R}}^{2} &=\frac{\epsilon_{0} E+P}{\epsilon_{0} E}=1+\frac{\chi_{-}}{\epsilon_{0}} \\
&=1-\frac{\omega_{\mathrm{p}}^{2}}{\omega^{2}+\omega \omega_{\mathrm{g}}},
\end{aligned}
$$

where $\omega_{\mathrm{p}}^{2}=\frac{N e^{2}}{m \epsilon_{0}}, \omega_{\mathrm{g}}=\frac{e B}{m}$. Note that in Gaussian units,

$$
\omega_{\mathrm{p}}^{2}=\frac{4 \pi N e^{2}}{m}, \quad \omega_{\mathrm{g}}=\frac{e B}{m c},
$$

and the expression for $n_{\mathrm{R}}^{2}$ is identical with that given in the problem.

For the left-hand circularly polarized light,

$$
n_{\mathrm{L}}^{2}=1+\frac{\chi_{+}}{\epsilon_{0}}=1-\frac{\omega_{\mathrm{p}}^{2}}{\omega^{2}-\omega \omega_{\mathrm{g}}} .
$$

(b) If $B=0$,

$$
n^{2}=1-\frac{\omega_{\mathrm{p}}^{2}}{\omega^{2}}=1+\frac{\chi}{\epsilon_{0}}=\frac{c^{2} k^{2}}{\omega^{2}}
$$

giving

$$
c^{2} k^{2}=\omega^{2}-\omega_{\mathrm{p}}^{2} .
$$

The phase velocity is then

$$
v_{\mathbf{p}}=\frac{\omega}{k}=\frac{c}{\sqrt{1-\left(\frac{\omega_{p}}{\omega}\right)^{2}}} .
$$

(c) As $2 c^{2} k d k=2 \omega d \omega$, the group velocity is

$$
v_{\mathrm{g}}=\frac{d \omega}{d k}=\frac{c^{2} k}{\omega}=c \sqrt{1-\left(\frac{\omega_{\mathrm{p}}}{\omega}\right)^{2}} .
$$

\section{7}

Below are listed some measured properties of $\mathrm{NaCl}$ at room temperature: static dielectric constant $\varepsilon=5.90$, side of unit cell $a=5.6 \AA$, atomic mass of $\mathrm{Na}=23 \mathrm{amu}\left(1 \mathrm{amu}=1.66 \times 10^{-24} \mathrm{~g}\right)$, atomic mass of $\mathrm{Cl}$ $=35.5 \mathrm{amu}$.

(a) Polaritons results from the coupling between phonons and photons in ionic crystals. To see this, derive the dispersion relation $\omega(k)$ and draw the dispersion diagram for transverse electromagnetic modes propagating through an ionic crystal (e.g. $\mathrm{NaCl}$ ). Neglect atomic polarization.

(b) For what band of photon wavelengths would you expect $\mathrm{NaCl}$ to reflect incident radiation? (That is, obtain and evaluate expressions for the shortest and longest wavelengths of this band.)

\section{(Princeton)}

\section{Solution:}

(a) Figure $1.33$ (a) shows optical and acoustic branches of the dispersion relation for a one-dimensional diatomic lattice as shown in Fig. 1.33 (b) without any coupling between them. Near $k=0$, the acoustic branch is linear, $\omega=v k$. As $v \approx 10^{-5} c$, the corresponding wave for light, $\omega=c k$ will practically coincide with the $\omega$-axis. Thus only the coupling between the optical mode of $k=0$ and light is possible.

Neglecting electronic polarizability, we assume polarization to be caused entirely by ionic motion. Consider the motion of a pair of ions in an applied electric field $\mathbf{E}$ with $k \approx 0$. The relative displacement $\mathbf{W}$ of a positive from a negative ion has equation of motion

$$
\mu \ddot{\mathbf{W}}=-2 K \mathbf{W}+q \mathbf{E},
$$

where $\mu=\frac{M m}{M+m}$ is the reduced mass of the pair of ions and $K$ is the force constant between them, $q$ is the magnitude of the charge of each ion. When the ions are stationary, $\ddot{\mathbf{W}}=0$ and $\mathbf{W}=\frac{q}{2 K} \mathbf{E}$. If $V$ is the volume per unit pair, and $\rho_{0}=q / V$ is the charge density, the polarization is by definition 

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_41580ef03e6631735034g-39.jpg?height=528&width=414&top_left_y=327&top_left_x=606)

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_41580ef03e6631735034g-39.jpg?height=114&width=518&top_left_y=542&top_left_x=1129)

(b)

(a)

Fig. $1.33$

$$
\mathbf{P}=\rho_{0} \mathbf{W}=\frac{q^{2}}{2 K V} \mathbf{E}=\epsilon_{0} \chi \mathbf{E}
$$

where $\epsilon_{0}$ is the free-space permittivity and $\chi$ the electric susceptibility related to the relative dielectric constant $\varepsilon$ by $\chi=\varepsilon-1$. The force constant is then

$$
K=\frac{q^{2}}{2 \chi \epsilon_{0} V}=\frac{q^{2}}{2(\varepsilon-1) \epsilon_{0} V} \text {. }
$$

Setting $Q=\frac{\rho_{0}}{\epsilon_{0}} W$, we have from the equation of motion

$$
\ddot{\mathbf{Q}}+\omega_{0}^{2} \mathbf{Q}-\omega_{0}^{2} \chi \mathbf{E}=0
$$

where

$$
\omega_{0}=\sqrt{\frac{2 K}{\mu}}=\sqrt{\frac{q^{2}}{\mu(\varepsilon-1) \epsilon_{0} V}}
$$

is the angular frequency of the optical mode when $k=0$, which is characteristic of the optical mode. To find the interaction of the optical mode with the traversing electromagnetic waves and derive the coupled mode, we have to combine this characteristic equation with Maxwell's equations

$$
\begin{aligned}
&\nabla \times \mathbf{E}=-\dot{\mathbf{B}} \\
&\nabla \times \mathbf{H}=-\dot{\mathbf{D}}+\mathbf{J}
\end{aligned}
$$

with $\mathrm{B}=\mu_{0} \mathbf{H}, \mathrm{D}=\epsilon_{0} \mathbf{E}, J_{0}=\rho_{0} \dot{\mathbf{W}}=\epsilon_{0} \dot{Q}$ being the current density. Furthermore, as the medium is charge-free (apart from the ions), $\nabla \cdot \mathbf{E}=$ $\frac{1}{\epsilon_{0}} \nabla \cdot D=0$. Hence

$$
\nabla \times(\nabla \times \mathbf{E})=-\nabla^{2} \mathbf{E}=-\mu_{0} \nabla \times \dot{\mathbf{H}}=-\mu_{0} \epsilon_{0}(\ddot{\mathbf{E}}+\ddot{\mathbf{Q}}),
$$

or

$$
\nabla^{2} \mathbf{E}=\frac{1}{c^{2}} \frac{\partial^{2}}{\partial t^{2}}(\mathbf{E}+\dot{\mathbf{Q}}) .
$$

Taking the direction of propagation of the electromagnetic wave as the $x$ direction, we have

$$
\mathbf{E}=\mathbf{E}_{0} e^{i(k x-\omega t)}, \quad \mathbf{Q}=\mathbf{Q}_{0} e^{i(k x-\omega t)} .
$$

Substituting these in Eqs. (1) and (2) results in

$$
\left\{\begin{array}{l}
\left(\omega_{0}^{2}-\omega^{2}\right) \mathbf{Q}_{0}=\chi \omega_{0}^{2} \mathbf{E}_{0} \\
k^{2} c^{2} \mathbf{E}_{0}=\omega^{2}\left(\mathbf{E}_{0}+\mathbf{Q}_{0}\right)
\end{array}\right.
$$

which give the dispersion relation of the coupled mode

$$
\frac{c^{2} k^{2}}{\omega^{2}}=\frac{\varepsilon \omega_{0}^{2}-\omega^{2}}{\omega_{0}^{2}-\omega^{2}}
$$

It is seen that for $k \rightarrow 0, \omega \rightarrow 0$ or $\omega \rightarrow \sqrt{\varepsilon} \omega_{0}$; for $k \rightarrow \infty, \omega \rightarrow \omega_{0}$ or $\omega \rightarrow \infty$. The relation consists of two branches as shown in Fig. 1.34.

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_41580ef03e6631735034g-40.jpg?height=439&width=528&top_left_y=1420&top_left_x=842)

Fig. $1.34$

(b) As shown in Fig. 1.34, in the band between $\omega_{0}$ and $\sqrt{\varepsilon} \omega_{0}$, coupling between the phonons and photons is forbidden and the incident light will be completely reflected. The shortest and longest wavelengths of this band are

$$
\begin{aligned}
\lambda_{\min } &=\frac{2 \pi c}{\sqrt{\varepsilon} \omega_{0}}=\frac{2 \pi c}{q} \sqrt{\epsilon_{0} \mu V\left(1-\frac{1}{\varepsilon}\right)} \\
\lambda_{\max } &=\frac{2 \pi c}{\omega_{0}}=\frac{2 \pi c}{q} \sqrt{\epsilon_{0} \mu V(\varepsilon-1)}
\end{aligned}
$$

For $\mathrm{NaCl}, V=\frac{1}{4} a^{3}, \mu=\frac{M_{\mathrm{Na}} m_{\mathrm{Cl}}}{M_{\mathrm{Na}}+m_{\mathrm{Cl}}}, q=e$, with the data given we obtain

$$
\begin{aligned}
\lambda_{\min } &=32.2 \mu \mathrm{m}, \\
\lambda_{\max } &=78.3 \mu \mathrm{m} .
\end{aligned}
$$

\section{8}

Suppose you are given an alkali halide with $\varepsilon(0)=5.9, \varepsilon(\infty)=2.25$, reflectivity $=0$ at $\lambda=3.06 \mu \mathrm{m}$, and $\varepsilon(\omega)$ real.

(a) Find $\omega_{\mathrm{L}}, \omega_{\mathrm{T}}$. (Recall that $\omega_{\mathrm{T}}$ is the TO phonon frequency in the absence of any coupling to external fields and defines a pole of $\varepsilon(\omega)$, whereas $\omega_{\mathrm{L}}$ defines a zero of $\left.\varepsilon(\omega).\right)$

(b) Sketch the reflectivity vs. $\omega$.

(Princeton $)$

\section{Solution:}

(a) For an ionic crystal, if viscous damping can be neglected, the dielectric constant is real and the relative dielectric constant can be expressed as

$$
\varepsilon(\omega)=\varepsilon(\infty)+\frac{[\varepsilon(0)-\varepsilon(\infty)] \omega_{\mathbf{T}}^{2}}{\omega_{\mathrm{T}}^{2}-\omega^{2}} .
$$

The refractive index of a substance is $n=\sqrt{\varepsilon(\omega)}$ and for normal incidence the reflectivity is

$$
R=\left(\frac{n-1}{n+1}\right)^{2}=\left(\frac{\sqrt{\varepsilon(\omega)}-1}{\sqrt{\varepsilon(\omega)+1}}\right)^{2} .
$$

If $R=0$ at $\omega=\omega_{0}$, then $\varepsilon\left(\omega_{0}\right)=1$, or

$$
1=\varepsilon(\infty)+\frac{[\varepsilon(0)-\varepsilon(\infty)] \omega_{\mathbf{T}}^{2}}{\omega_{\mathrm{T}}^{2}-\omega_{0}^{2}},
$$

giving

$$
\omega_{\mathrm{T}}=\left[\frac{\varepsilon(\infty)-1}{\varepsilon(0)-1}\right]^{1 / 2} \omega_{0} .
$$

As $\omega_{0}=\frac{2 \pi c}{\lambda_{0}}=\frac{2 \pi \times 3 \times 10^{8}}{30.6 \times 10^{-6}}=6.16 \times 10^{13} \mathrm{~s}^{-1}, \varepsilon(0)=5.9, \varepsilon(\infty)=2.25$,

$$
\omega_{\mathrm{T}}=\sqrt{\frac{1.25}{4.9}} \times 6.16 \times 10^{13}=3.11 \times 10^{13} \mathrm{~s}^{-1} \text {. }
$$

At $\omega=\omega_{\mathrm{L}}, \varepsilon(\omega)=0$, i.e.,

$$
\varepsilon(\infty)+\frac{[\varepsilon(0)-\varepsilon(\infty)] \omega_{\mathrm{T}}^{2}}{\omega_{\mathrm{T}}^{2}-\omega_{\mathrm{L}}^{2}}=0,
$$

giving the Lyddane-Sachs-Teller relation

$$
\frac{\omega_{\mathrm{L}}^{2}}{\omega_{\mathrm{T}}^{2}}=\frac{\varepsilon(0)}{\varepsilon(\infty)} .
$$

Hence

$$
\omega_{\mathrm{L}}=\left[\frac{\varepsilon(0)}{\varepsilon(\infty)}\right]^{1 / 2} \omega_{\mathrm{T}}=5.04 \times 10^{13} \mathrm{~s}^{-1} .
$$

(b) We have

$$
\text { for } \quad \omega=0, \quad \varepsilon(\omega)=\varepsilon(0)=5.9, \quad R=\frac{(\sqrt{\varepsilon(0)}-1)^{2}}{(\sqrt{\varepsilon(0)}+1)^{2}}=0.17 \text {; }
$$

$$
\begin{aligned}
& \text { for } \quad \omega \rightarrow \infty, \quad \varepsilon(\omega) \rightarrow \varepsilon(\infty)=2.25, \quad R=\frac{(\sqrt{\varepsilon(\infty)}-1)^{2}}{(\sqrt{\varepsilon(\infty)}+1)^{2}}=0.04 \text {; } \\
& \text { for } \omega=\omega_{0}=6.16 \times 10^{13} \mathrm{~s}^{-1} \text {, }
\end{aligned}
$$

$$
\varepsilon(\omega)=1, \quad R=0 \text {; }
$$

for $\quad R=1, \quad \varepsilon(\omega)=0$ and $\omega=\sqrt{\frac{\varepsilon(0)}{\varepsilon(\infty)}} \omega_{\mathrm{T}}=\omega_{\mathrm{L}}=5.04 \times 10^{13} \mathrm{~s}^{-1}$.

Furthermore, for $\omega_{\mathrm{T}}<\omega<\omega_{\mathrm{L}}, \varepsilon(\omega)<0$, which means that $n$ is imaginary, total reflection occurs and $R=1$. Hence we can sketch the $R$ vs. $\omega$ curve, which is shown in the semi-log graph Fig. 1.35. 

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_41580ef03e6631735034g-43.jpg?height=753&width=743&top_left_y=388&top_left_x=707)

Fig. 1.35

\section{9}

A rough sketch of the absorption coefficient for electromagnetic radiation is given for a nonmagnetic pure solid substance. The substance absorbs light in a narrow range in the infrared $(0.15 \mathrm{eV})$ and then does not absorb light until the photon energy is $2.3 \mathrm{eV}$. Briefly explain this pattern of absorption and answer the following.

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_41580ef03e6631735034g-43.jpg?height=461&width=1369&top_left_y=1843&top_left_x=367)

Fig. $1.36$

(a) Is this material a metal or an insulator? Why? (b) Is there more than one atom per unit cell in the crystal structure of this material? Why?

(Wisconsin)

\section{Solution:}

The absorption of light in the infrared is caused by the interaction of photons with lattice vibration, or more specifically, by the interaction of long-wavelength optical phonons with photons. Locally the long wavelength optical vibration of the lattice causes the atoms or molecules to behave as oscillating electric dipoles. In their interaction with the electromagnetic field of the traversing light, absorption of the latter occurs. The strong absorption above $2.3 \mathrm{eV}$ is due to excitation of electrons from valence band into conduction band.

(a) This material is an insulator (probably a semiconductor as the energy gap $\Delta E$ is $2.3 \mathrm{eV}$ ).

(b) There are more than two atoms per unit cell, otherwise the optical branch would have been absent and there would be no absorption peak at $0.15 \mathrm{eV}$

\section{0}

Give a numerical estimate, with $60 \%$ accuracy, of the magnetic susceptibility $\chi=M / B$ (where $B=$ applied magnetic field) for

(a) a Fermi free electron gas with $N=4.7 \times 10^{22} \mathrm{~cm}^{-3}$ electrons at $T=300 \mathrm{~K}$ and at $T=350 \mathrm{~K}$;

(b) a type I superconductor at temperatures below the critical temperature.

(Wisconsin)

\section{Solution:}

(a) For a Fermi free electron gas the magnetic susceptibility $\chi=M / B$ is

$$
\chi=\frac{3 N \mu_{\mathrm{B}}^{2}}{2 E_{\mathrm{F}}},
$$

where $N$ is the number of free electrons per unit volume, $\mu_{\mathrm{B}}=\frac{e \hbar}{2 m c}$ is the Bohr magneton and $E_{\mathrm{F}}$ is the Fermi energy. With the accuracy required

$$
E_{\mathrm{F}} \approx E_{\mathrm{F}}^{0}=\frac{\hbar^{2}}{2 m}\left(3 N \pi^{2}\right)^{2 / 3}=\frac{\hbar^{2} c^{2}}{2 m c^{2}}\left(3 N \pi^{2}\right)^{\frac{2}{3}}
$$

With

$$
\begin{aligned}
\hbar &=6.58 \times 10^{-16} \mathrm{eV} \cdot \mathrm{s}, \\
m c^{2} &=0.51 \times 10^{6} \mathrm{eV}, \\
N &=4.7 \times 10^{22} \mathrm{~cm}^{-3},
\end{aligned}
$$

we find

$$
E_{\mathrm{F}}=4.76 \mathrm{eV} \text {, }
$$

and with $\mu_{\mathrm{B}}=9.27 \times 10^{-21} \mathrm{erg} \mathrm{G}^{-1}$ we obtain

$$
\chi=\frac{3 \times 4.7 \times 10^{22} \times\left(9.27 \times 10^{-21}\right)^{2}}{2 \times 4.76 \times 1.6 \times 10^{-12}}=8.0 \times 10^{-7} \mathrm{cgs} .
$$

As the paramagnetic susceptibility of a Fermi free electron gas does not depend on temperature,

$$
\chi(300 \mathrm{~K})=\chi(350 \mathrm{~K})=0.80 \times 10^{-6} \mathrm{cgs} .
$$

(b) For a type I superconductor below the critical temperature,

$$
\chi \leq-\frac{1}{4 \pi} \operatorname{cgs} \text {. }
$$

\section{1}

Consider a crystal containing a paramagnetic impurity whose energy levels are $\pm \varepsilon$ in a given magnetic field.

(a) Write the contribution of this impurity to the partition function of the crystal in the presence of the field.

(b) Write the corresponding expression of the impurity's contribution to the specific heat and plot it as a function of temperature.

(c) Compare the temperature dependence of this specific heat with that of the phonon specific heat (Debye theory) in the limit of very low temperatures and give a physical interpretation of their difference.

(d) Perform the comparison (c) for the limit of very high temperatures.

(Chicago)

\section{Solution:}

(a) The contribution of the impurity to the partition function of the crystal is 

$$
z=e^{-\varepsilon / k_{\mathrm{B}} T}+e^{\varepsilon / k_{\mathrm{B}} T}=2 \cosh \left(\frac{\varepsilon}{k_{\mathrm{B}} T}\right) .
$$

(b) The mean energy of the crystal due to the impurity is

$$
\bar{E}=-\frac{d \ln z}{d\left(\frac{1}{k_{\mathrm{B}} T}\right)}=-\varepsilon \tanh \left(\frac{\varepsilon}{k_{\mathrm{B}} T}\right),
$$

and hence the specific heat due to the impurity is

$$
c_{\mathrm{v}}=\frac{d \bar{E}}{d T}=k_{\mathrm{B}}\left(\frac{\varepsilon}{k_{\mathrm{B}} T}\right)^{2} \operatorname{sech}^{2}\left(\frac{\varepsilon}{k_{\mathrm{B}} T}\right) .
$$

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_41580ef03e6631735034g-46.jpg?height=564&width=871&top_left_y=1054&top_left_x=627)

Fig. $1.37$

(c) In the limit of very low temperatures, $\operatorname{sech}^{2}\left(\frac{\varepsilon}{k_{\mathrm{B}} T}\right) \sim 4 e^{-\frac{2 \varepsilon}{k_{\mathrm{B}} T}}$. Hence as $T \rightarrow 0, c_{v} \sim 4 k_{\mathrm{B}}\left(\frac{\varepsilon}{k_{\mathrm{B}} T}\right) \exp \left(\frac{-2 \varepsilon}{k_{\mathrm{B}} T}\right) \rightarrow 0$. The more accurate Debye's model gives $c_{v} \sim T^{3}$ for $T \ll \theta_{\mathbf{D}}$, the Deybe temperature. The reason is that at low temperatures, only those lattice vibration modes for which $\hbar \omega<k_{\mathrm{B}} T$ can be excited and the contribution of such modes to the specific heat is proportional to $T^{3}$.

(d) In the limit of very high temperatures $k_{\mathrm{B}} T \gg \varepsilon$, $\operatorname{sech}^{2}\left(\frac{\varepsilon}{k_{\mathrm{B}} T}\right) \rightarrow 1$ and hence $c_{v} \sim T^{-2}$. However, in Debye's model $c_{v}$ approaches a constant at high temperatures. This is because at a high temperatures $T \gg \theta_{\mathrm{D}}$ every mode is excited and has an energy $k_{\mathrm{B}} T$, giving rise to a constant $c_{v}$. Using the above information, we sketch the curve of $c_{v} / k_{\mathrm{B}}$ vs. $\frac{k_{\mathrm{B}} T}{\varepsilon}$ in Fig. $1.37$. 

\section{2}

(a) Show that the zero-temperature spin susceptibility of an independent electron gas is

$$
\chi=\mu_{\mathrm{B}}^{2} g\left(\varepsilon_{\mathrm{F}}\right),
$$

where $\mu_{\mathrm{B}}$ is the Bohr magneton and $g\left(\varepsilon_{\mathrm{F}}\right)$ is the density of states (per unit energy) at the Fermi energy. "Independent" is intended to imply that electron-electron interactions are also ignored.

(b) Evaluate $g\left(\varepsilon_{\mathrm{F}}\right)$ for a free electron gas in which electron-ion interactions are also ignored. (A derivation is required here; do not simply state the standard result.)

(c) The spin susceptibility has a weak temperature dependence of the form

$$
\chi(T)=\chi(0)\left(1+\alpha T^{2}\right) .
$$

Would you expect $\alpha$ to be positive or negative? (Qualitative arguments only are expected.)

(Princeton $)$

\section{Solution:}

(a) The energy of an electron in a magnetic field of flux density $\mathbf{B}$ is

$$
\varepsilon(k)-\boldsymbol{\mu} \cdot \mathbf{B}=\varepsilon(k) \mp \mu_{\mathrm{B}} B,
$$

where $\boldsymbol{\mu}$ is the magnetic dipole moment due to the spin of the electron, $\varepsilon(k)$ denotes electron energy in the absence of the magnetic field, and the $\pm$ signs correspond to dipole moments parallel and antiparallel to the field respectively. Let $g(\varepsilon)$ be density of states of independent electrons per unit volume taking spin into account. Setting $\varepsilon=\varepsilon(k)$, we have for the total number of electrons with parallel and antiparallel spins respectively

$$
\begin{aligned}
&N_{+}=V \int_{0}^{\varepsilon_{+}(k)_{\max }} \frac{1}{2} g(\varepsilon) d \varepsilon, \\
&N_{-}=V \int_{0}^{\varepsilon_{-}(k)_{\max }} \frac{1}{2} g(\varepsilon) d \varepsilon .
\end{aligned}
$$

At $T=0, \varepsilon_{\mathrm{F}}=\varepsilon_{+}(k)_{\max }-\mu_{\mathrm{B}} B=\varepsilon_{-}(k)_{\max }+\mu_{\mathrm{B}} B$, where $\varepsilon_{\mathrm{F}}$ is the Fermi energy, and thus 

$$
\begin{aligned}
&N_{+}=V \int_{0}^{\varepsilon_{F}+\mu_{B} B} \frac{1}{2} g(\varepsilon) d \varepsilon \\
&N_{-}=V \int_{0}^{\varepsilon_{F}-\mu_{B} B} \frac{1}{2} g(\varepsilon) d \varepsilon .
\end{aligned}
$$

If $\mu_{\mathrm{B}} B \gg \varepsilon_{\mathrm{F}}$, we can take $\epsilon \approx \epsilon_{\mathrm{F}}$ under the integral sign and find

$$
N_{+}-N_{-} \approx \frac{1}{2} V \cdot 2 \mu_{\mathrm{B}} B \cdot g\left(\varepsilon_{\mathrm{F}}\right)=V \mu_{\mathrm{B}} B g\left(\varepsilon_{\mathrm{F}}\right) .
$$

The magnetization $M$ is by definition

$$
M=\frac{1}{V}\left(N_{+}-N_{-}\right) \mu_{\mathrm{B}}=\mu_{\mathrm{B}}^{2} g\left(\varepsilon_{\mathrm{F}}\right) B .
$$

The spin susceptibility is therefore

$$
\chi=\frac{M}{H}=\frac{\mu_{0} M}{B}=\mu_{0} \mu_{\mathrm{B}}^{2} g\left(\varepsilon_{\mathrm{F}}\right),
$$

or, in Gaussian units where $\mu_{0}=1$,

$$
\chi=\mu_{\mathrm{B}}^{2} g\left(\varepsilon_{\mathrm{F}}\right),
$$

provided that $B$ is small.

(b) For a free electron

$$
\varepsilon=\frac{\hbar^{2} k^{2}}{2 m}, \quad \frac{d k}{d \varepsilon}=\frac{m}{\hbar^{2} k} .
$$

The number of electron states per unit volume in the energy range $\varepsilon, \varepsilon+d \varepsilon$ corresponds to the density of states in $k$-space in a spherical shell of radius $k$ and thickness $d k$ :

$$
\rho(\varepsilon) d \varepsilon=2 \times \frac{4 \pi k^{2} d k}{(2 \pi)^{3}}=\frac{k^{2}}{\pi^{2}} \frac{d k}{d \varepsilon} d \varepsilon,
$$

where the factor 2 is due to the fact that each energy state comprises two spin states. Hence

$$
\rho(\varepsilon)=\frac{m k}{\pi^{2} \hbar^{2}}=\frac{1}{2 \pi^{2}}\left(\frac{2 m}{\hbar^{2}}\right)^{\frac{3}{2}} \varepsilon^{\frac{1}{2}} .
$$

(c) When $k_{\mathrm{B}} T \ll \varepsilon_{\mathrm{F}}$, the excited electrons will lie within a range $k_{\mathrm{B}} T$ above the Fermi surface. For those electrons whose magnetic moments are exactly parallel to the applied magnetic field, $\varepsilon_{+}(k)_{\max }$ is greater and so the fraction of such excited electrons is relatively small and the reduction of their chemical potential is less. In order to maintain equilibrium, some of the electrons whose magnetic moments are almost parallel to the magnetic field will orientate so that their moments are more parallel to the magnetic field. Hence we expect $\alpha<0$.

\section{3}

Consider a conduction electron gas at absolute zero temperature in a weak magnetic field $B$. The concentration of spin-up, $N_{+}$, and spin-down, $N_{-}$, electrons may be parameterized by the quantity $x$

$$
N_{+}=\frac{1}{2} N(1+x), \quad N_{-}=\frac{1}{2} N(1-x),
$$

where $N$ is the total number of electrons.

(a) Calculate the total energy of the gas and evaluate the magnetization $M$

(b) We may approximate the effect of exchange interactions among the conduction electrons if we assume that only electrons with parallel spins interact with each other, with energy $-V$ where $V>0$. How is the magnetization modified?

(Princeton)

\section{Solution:}

(a) The energy of a conduction electron is

$$
\varepsilon=\frac{p^{2}}{2 m} \pm \mu B,
$$

where $E=\frac{p^{2}}{2 m}$ is the kinetic energy of the electron and $\mu$ is its magnetic moment. The signs $+,-$ are for spins parallel and antiparallel to $B$ respectively. The density of states is (Problem 1062)

$$
\rho(E)=C \sqrt{E} d E,
$$

where $C$ is a constant, and the densities of electrons with magnetic moments parallel and antiparallel to $B$ are respectively

$$
N_{+}=C \int_{0}^{E_{+}} \rho(E) d E, \quad N_{-}=C \int_{0}^{E_{-}} \rho(E) d E .
$$

At absolute-zero temperature, all the states below a certain energy $\varepsilon_{0}$ are filled by electrons of both spin directions. As

$$
\varepsilon_{0}=E_{+}-\mu B=E_{-}+\mu B,
$$

we have

$$
\begin{aligned}
&E_{+}=\varepsilon_{0}+\mu B, \quad E_{-}=\varepsilon_{0}-\mu B, \\
&N_{+} \equiv \frac{1}{2} N(1+x)=C \int_{0}^{\varepsilon_{0}+\mu B} E^{\frac{1}{2}} d E=\frac{2}{3} C\left(\varepsilon_{0}+\mu B\right)^{3 / 2}, \\
&N_{-} \equiv \frac{1}{2} N(1-x)=C \int_{0}^{\varepsilon_{0}-\mu B} E^{\frac{1}{2}} d E=\frac{2}{3} C\left(\varepsilon_{0}-\mu B\right)^{3 / 2} .
\end{aligned}
$$

Suppose $E_{\mathrm{F}}$ is the Fermi energy in the absence of any applied magnetic field, then

$$
N=2 C \int_{0}^{E_{\mathrm{F}}} E^{\frac{1}{2}} d E=\frac{4}{3} C E_{\mathrm{F}}^{3 / 2}
$$

giving $C=\frac{3}{4} N E_{\mathrm{F}}^{-\frac{3}{2}}$. Hence

$$
\begin{aligned}
&\varepsilon_{0}+\mu B=E_{\mathrm{F}}(1+x)^{2 / 3}, \\
&\varepsilon_{0}-\mu B=E_{\mathrm{F}}(1-x)^{2 / 3},
\end{aligned}
$$

whose difference gives for $x \ll 1$

$$
x \approx \frac{3 \mu B}{2 E_{\mathrm{F}}} .
$$

The total energies of the electrons with spin $+$ and spin $-$ are respectively 

$$
\begin{aligned}
E_{\text {+tot }} &=C \int_{0}^{\varepsilon_{0}+\mu B}(E-\mu B) E^{\frac{1}{2}} d E \\
&=\frac{2}{5} C\left(\varepsilon_{0}+\mu B\right)^{5 / 2}-\mu B N_{+}, \\
&=\frac{2}{5} C E_{\mathbf{F}}^{\frac{2}{5}}(1+x)^{\frac{5}{3}}-\frac{1}{2} \mu B N(1+x) \\
&=\frac{3}{10} E_{\mathrm{F}} N(1+x)^{\frac{5}{3}}-\frac{1}{2} \mu B N(1+x), \\
E_{-\text {tot }} &=C \int_{0}^{\varepsilon_{0}-\mu B}(E+\mu B) E^{\frac{1}{2}} d E \\
&=\frac{2}{5} C\left(\varepsilon_{0}-\mu B\right)^{5 / 2}+\mu B N_{-}, \\
&=\frac{3}{10} E_{\mathbf{F}} N(1-x)^{\frac{5}{3}}+\frac{1}{2} \mu B N(1-x) .
\end{aligned}
$$

Hence, the total energy of the gas is

$$
\begin{aligned}
E_{\mathrm{tot}} &=\frac{3}{10} N E_{\mathrm{F}}\left[(1+x)^{5 / 3}+(1-x)^{5 / 3}\right]-\mu B N x \\
& \approx \frac{3}{5} N E_{\mathrm{F}}-\mu B N x \\
& \approx \frac{3}{5} N E_{\mathrm{F}}-\frac{2 \mu^{2} B^{2}}{2 E_{\mathrm{F}}} N .
\end{aligned}
$$

Note that for a weak field it is justified to take $\mu B \ll E_{\mathrm{F}}$, or $x \ll 1$.

As the magnetic moment arises from electron spin, $\mu=\mu_{B}$, the Bohr magneton. The total magnetic moment $v M$, where $M$ is the magnetization and $v$ the volume of the electron gas, is then

$$
v M=\left(N_{+}-N_{-}\right) \mu_{B}=N x \mu_{B}
$$

or

$$
M=n x \mu_{B}=\frac{3 n \mu_{B}^{2} B}{2 E_{\mathrm{F}}},
$$

where $n=N / v$ is the number of electrons per unit volume of the gas.

(b) There are $N_{+}$electrons of spin $+$, making up $\frac{1}{2} N_{+}\left(N_{+}-1\right) \approx \frac{1}{2} N_{+}^{2}$ pairs, each of which contributes an interaction energy $-V$. Thus, taking into account the interaction energies we have 

$$
\begin{aligned}
E_{+\text {tot }} & \approx \frac{3}{10} E_{\mathrm{F}} N(1+x)^{5 / 3}-\frac{1}{2} \mu_{\mathrm{B}} B N(1+x)+\frac{1}{2} N_{+}^{2}(-V) \\
&=\frac{3}{10} E_{\mathrm{F}} N(1+x)^{5 / 3}-\frac{1}{2} \mu_{\mathrm{B}} B N(1+x)-\frac{1}{8} V N^{2}(1+x)^{2}
\end{aligned}
$$

and similarly

$$
E_{-\text {tot }} \approx \frac{3}{10} E_{\mathrm{F}} N(1-x)^{5 / 3}+\frac{1}{2} \mu_{\mathrm{B}} B N(1-x)-\frac{1}{8} V N^{2}(1-x)^{2} .
$$

The total energy of the electron gas is then

$$
\begin{aligned}
E_{\mathrm{tot}}=& \frac{3}{10} E_{\mathrm{F}} N\left[(1+x)^{\frac{5}{3}}+(1-x)^{\frac{5}{3}}\right]-\mu_{\mathrm{B}} B N x \\
&-\frac{V}{8} N^{2}\left[(1+x)^{2}+(1-x)^{2}\right] .
\end{aligned}
$$

At equilibrium, $\frac{\partial E_{\text {tot }}}{\partial x}=0$, giving

$$
\frac{1}{2} E_{\mathrm{F}}\left[(1+x)^{\frac{2}{3}}-(1-x)^{\frac{2}{3}}\right]-\mu_{\mathrm{B}} B-\frac{V}{4} N[(1+x)-(1-x)]=0,
$$

or

$$
x \approx \frac{6 \mu_{\mathrm{B}} B}{4 E_{\mathrm{F}}-3 V N}
$$

for $x \ll 1$.

Hence the magnetization is

$$
M=n x \mu_{\mathrm{B}}=\frac{6 n \mu_{\mathrm{B}}^{2} B}{4 E_{\mathrm{F}}-3 V N} .
$$

Note that for $V=0$ this reduces to the expression for non-interacting electrons.

\section{4}

Consider an ideal gas of stable vector mesons (massive particles with spin 1) with magnetic moment $\mu$.

(a) Compute the paramagnetic susceptibility per unit volume in a weak magnetic field.

(b) Perform the corresponding calculation with classical dipoles. 

\section{Solution:}

(a) For an ideal gas, in which the particles are non-interacting, the partition function of a single particle is

$$
z_{1}=e^{-\beta \varepsilon}+1+e^{\beta \varepsilon},
$$

where $\varepsilon=\mu B, \beta=\frac{1}{k_{\mathrm{B}} T}$. The probabilities for its magnetic moment to be parallel, perpendicular and antiparallel to the magnetic field $\mathbf{B}$ are respectively .

$$
P_{+}=\frac{e^{\beta \varepsilon}}{z_{1}}, \quad P_{0}=\frac{1}{z_{1}}, \quad P_{-}=\frac{e^{-\beta \varepsilon}}{z_{1}} .
$$

Hence the mean magnetic moment along the direction of the field per particle is

$$
\begin{aligned}
\bar{\mu} &=\mu\left(P_{+}-P_{-}\right)=\mu \cdot \frac{e^{\beta \varepsilon}-e^{-\beta \varepsilon}}{z_{1}} \\
&=\frac{2 \mu \sinh (\beta \varepsilon)}{2 \cosh (\beta \varepsilon)+1} .
\end{aligned}
$$

If $n$ is the number of particles per unit volume of the gas, the paramagnetic magnetization is

$$
M=n \bar{\mu}=\frac{2 n \mu \sinh (\beta \varepsilon)}{2 \cosh (\beta \varepsilon)+1} .
$$

In a weak magnetic field, $\mu B \ll k_{\mathrm{B}} T$, or $\beta \varepsilon \ll 1, \sinh (\beta \varepsilon) \approx \beta \varepsilon, \cosh (\beta \varepsilon) \approx$ 1 , and so

$$
M=\frac{2 n \mu \beta \varepsilon}{3}=\frac{2 n \mu^{2} B}{3 k_{\mathrm{B}} T} .
$$

Hence the paramagnetic susceptibility is

$$
\chi=\frac{M}{H}=\frac{\mu_{0} M}{B}=\frac{2 n \mu_{0} \mu^{2}}{3 k_{\mathrm{B}} T} .
$$

(b) For a classical magnetic dipole $\mu$ the potential energy in a magnetic field $B$ is

$$
\varepsilon=-\boldsymbol{\mu} \cdot \mathbf{B}=-\mu B \cos \theta,
$$

where $\theta$ is the angle between $\mu$ and $\mathbf{B}$. The probability of finding it along the $\theta$ direction is given by the distribution function

$$
e^{-\frac{\epsilon}{k_{\mathrm{B}} T}}=e^{\mu B \beta \cos \theta} .
$$

Hence the mean magnetic moment along $\mathbf{B}$ is

$$
\begin{aligned}
\bar{\mu} &=\frac{\int_{0}^{\pi} e^{\mu B \beta \cos \theta} \mu \cos \theta \cdot 2 \pi \sin \theta d \theta}{\int_{0}^{\pi} e^{\mu B \beta \cos \theta} \cdot 2 \pi \sin \theta d \theta} \\
&=\frac{1}{B \beta}[\mu B \beta \operatorname{coth}(\mu B \beta)-1] \\
&=\mu\left[\operatorname{coth}(\mu B \beta)-\frac{1}{\mu B \beta}\right] .
\end{aligned}
$$

For a weak field

$$
\mu B \ll \frac{1}{\beta}, \quad \text { or } \quad \mu B \beta \ll 1,
$$

and

$$
\operatorname{coth}(\mu B \beta) \approx \frac{1}{\mu B \beta}+\frac{\mu B \beta}{3} .
$$

Thus the paramagnetic magnetization for a weak field is

$$
M=n \bar{\mu} \approx \frac{n \mu^{2} B \beta}{3},
$$

and the paramagnetic susceptibility is

$$
\chi=\frac{M}{H}=\frac{\mu_{0} M}{B}=\frac{n \mu_{0} \mu^{2}}{3 k_{\mathrm{B}} T} .
$$

\section{5}

What is the typical saturation value for the density of magnetization of iron/steel (state units)? From this value and the values of other specified quantities, and stated assumptions about the nature of ferromagnetism, estimate the intrinsic-spin magnetic moment of the electron.

(Columbia)

\section{Solution:}

In an applied magnetic field, the magnetization of iron increases with the intensity of the field. When the field increases to a certain value, the magnetization will not increase further but reach a saturation value $M_{\mathbf{s}}$, at which time, according to the theory of ferromagnetism, all magnetic moments due to electron spin are aligned along the direction of the applied magnetic field. Let the number density of electrons in a ferromagnetic material be $n$ and the magnetic moment due to the spin of an electron be $\mu$, then

$$
M_{\mathrm{s}}=n \mu .
$$

The saturation magnetization of iron is typically $M_{\mathrm{s}}=10^{6} \mathrm{Am}^{-1}$, and as $n=3 \times 10^{28} \mathrm{~m}^{-3}$, the intrinsic-spin magnetic moment of an electron is $\mu=\frac{M_{\mathrm{s}}}{n} \approx 3 \times 10^{-23} \mathrm{Am}^{2}$.

\section{6}

If an iron crystal were totally magnetized spontaneously, estimate the resulting magnetization using plausible values for the parameters required.

(Wisconsin )

\section{Solution:}

The number of atoms per unit volume of iron crystal is

$$
n=\frac{\rho N_{\mathrm{A}}}{A}=\frac{7.9}{56} \times 6.02 \times 10^{23}=8.5 \times 10^{22} \mathrm{~cm}^{-3}=8.5 \times 10^{28} \mathrm{~m}^{-3} \text {, }
$$

where $\rho$ is the specific weight of iron, $A$ is its atomic weight and $N_{\mathrm{A}}$ is Avogadro's number. The magnetic moment per iron atom is $\mu_{0} \approx 2.2 \mu_{\mathrm{B}}$, $\mu_{\mathrm{B}}$ being the Bohr magneton. Hence the magnetic moment per unit volume, or the magnetization, is

$$
\begin{aligned}
M & \approx n \mu_{0}=2.2 n \mu_{\mathrm{B}}=2.2 \times 8.5 \times 10^{28} \times 9.3 \times 10^{-24} \\
&=1.74 \times 10^{6} \mathrm{Am}^{-1} .
\end{aligned}
$$

\section{7}

Figure $1.38$ displays neutron diffraction data for scattering from a crystal of $\mathrm{MnO}$, which is known to have the same structure as $\mathrm{NaCl}$. Although the neutron data changes dramatically when the temperature is reduced below $120 \mathrm{~K}$, X-ray diffraction patterns are nearly independent of temperature (exhibiting the same peaks as the $293 \mathrm{~K}$ neutron data) and indicate a lattice constant $a=4.43 \AA$. 

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_41580ef03e6631735034g-56.jpg?height=832&width=1027&top_left_y=370&top_left_x=495)

Fig. $1.38$

(a) Explain quantitatively why a crystal of the rock salt $(\mathrm{NaCl})$ structure should have no Bragg reflections in the (100) and (110) directions (for either neutrons or X-rays).

(b) From the figure, what is the approximate lattice constant indicated by the neutron data taken at $80 \mathrm{~K}$ ?

(c) How does the state of the $\mathrm{MnO}$ crystal at $80 \mathrm{~K}$ differ from its state at $293 \mathrm{~K}$ ?

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_41580ef03e6631735034g-56.jpg?height=407&width=436&top_left_y=1881&top_left_x=584)

(a)

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_41580ef03e6631735034g-56.jpg?height=404&width=390&top_left_y=1885&top_left_x=1128)

(b)

Fig. 1.39 (d) The magnetic susceptibility $\chi$ is schematically plotted for two different materials in Fig. 1.39. For each plot, describe the magnetic nature of the material in the low temperature range. Which plot could belong to $\mathrm{MnO} ?$

(Princeton)

\section{Solution:}

(a) $\mathrm{MnO}$ and $\mathrm{NaCl}$ have the same face-centered cubic (fcc) lattice structure, for which the geometrical structure factor vanishes if the indices of a diffraction plane are a mixture of odd and even numbers. Hence, crystals of such a structure have no Bragg reflection in directions such as (100) and (110).

(b) Figure $1.38$ shows that at $T=293 \mathrm{~K}$ the scattering angle is $2 \theta \approx 24^{\circ}$, and at $T=80 \mathrm{~K}$ the scattering angle is $2 \theta^{\prime} \approx 12^{\circ}$. For fcc structure, the interplanar distance is $d=\frac{n a}{3}$, where $a$ is the lattice constant. Bragg's law $2 d \sin \theta=n \lambda$ then gives

$$
a \sin \theta=a^{\prime} \sin \theta^{\prime} .
$$

Hence the lattice constant at $T=80 \mathrm{~K}$ is

$$
a^{\prime}=\frac{a \sin \theta}{\sin \theta^{\prime}}=4.43 \times \frac{\sin 12^{\circ}}{\sin 6^{\circ}}=8.81 \AA .
$$

(c) The MnO crystal, which has fcc structure, has lattice constant $4.43 \AA$ at $T=293 \mathrm{~K}$ and $8.81 \AA$ at $T=80 \mathrm{~K}$, and some new diffraction peaks at the lower temperature. The occurrence of these additional peaks is caused by the ordering of the magnetic moments at low temperatures. Specifically, the spins of $\mathrm{Mn}^{2+}$ have the same one direction on some (111) plane, but on an adjacent (111) plane the spins are in the opposite direction. Thus, considering one (111) plane the spins are in an ferromagnetic arrangement, but considering adjacent (111) planes the spins are in an antiferromagnetic arrangement. Because of this situation, only if the phase difference between adjacent (111) planes occupied by $\mathrm{Mn}^{2+}$ is $\pi$ can a diffraction maximum be generated. This gives rise to the reflection at scattering angle $2 \theta^{\prime}=12^{\circ}$.

By X-ray diffraction the lattice constant is determined to be $4.43 \AA$, which indicates that the lattice constant of a chemical cell is $4.43 \AA$. In order to get the same spin direction, it is necessary to enlarge each side of a chemical cell by a factor of 2 , so as to form a magnetic cell. (d) Figure 1.39(a) refers to a ferromagnetic substance. When $T<$ $T_{\mathrm{C}}$, the Curie temperature, the substance is in an ordered ferromagnetic phase in which the molecular spins are arranged in parallel alignment. Above $T_{\mathrm{C}}$ the substance is paramagnetic. Figure $1.39(\mathrm{~b})$ illustrates a case of antiferromagnetism. Below the Néel temperature, $T_{\mathrm{N}}$, neighboring spins favor an antiparallel arrangement. Above $T_{\mathrm{N}}$, the substance also becomes paramagnetic. Since at low temperatures the substance described by Fig. 1.39(b) is antiferromagnetic, it could be a plot of $\chi$ vs. $T$ for MnO.

\section{8}

(a) You are given two pieces of transparent material which to the eye appear identical in all respects. One is a crystal and the other is a glass. Describe two independent experiments for distinguishing between the crystal and the glass.

(b) You are given four identical looking objects:

(1) a permanent magnet,

(2) a diamagnetic material,

(3) a paramagnetic material,

(4) an unmagnetized ferromagnetic material.

Is it possible to identify all of the objects without the use of any externally produced electromagnetic field? If so, describe how it could be done. If not, give reasons.

(Chicago )

\section{Solution:}

(a) Experiment one: Heating the material. A crystal would melt at a definite melting temperature which remains constant during melting. Glass, on the other hand, has no definite melting point.

Experiment two: Taking X-ray diffraction patterns. A crystal will give regular spots in the pattern, while glass only gives a diffused pattern.

(b) It is possible to identify the objects by the following steps.

(1) Bring the objects in turn near some iron powder. The permanent magnet will strongly attract the powder.

(2) Bring the permanent magnet near each of the other three objects in turn. The unmagnetized ferromagnetic object will be strongly attracted to it. (3) Arrange the remaining two objects near the permanent magnet symmetrically as shown in Fig. 1.40. Measure the magnetic field at the points $A_{1}$ and $B_{1}$ between the two objects with a gaussmeter. The point nearer the paramagnetic material will show a greater magnetic intensity.

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_41580ef03e6631735034g-59.jpg?height=295&width=545&top_left_y=671&top_left_x=779)

Fig. $1.40$

\section{9}

A long thin uniformly magnetized needle of hard steel, length $12 \mathrm{~cm}$, is suspended by a torsionless suspension and makes free, small-angle oscillations about an axis through the center in a horizontal plane in the earth's magnetic field. The period of oscillation is $3-5$ seconds. Estimate the density of magnetization of the steel.

(Columbia )

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_41580ef03e6631735034g-59.jpg?height=434&width=461&top_left_y=1724&top_left_x=881)

Fig. $1.41$

\section{Solution:}

As only small-angle oscillations are involved, the earth's magnetic field can be considered uniform. Let $\boldsymbol{\mu}$ be the magnetic moment of the steel needle. Its potential energy in the earth's magnetic field is $V=-\boldsymbol{\mu} \cdot \mathbf{B}=-\mu B \cos \theta$, where $\theta$ is the angle between $\boldsymbol{\mu}$ and $\mathbf{B}$ as shown in Fig. 1.41. The equation of motion is

$$
I \ddot{\theta}=-\frac{d V}{d \theta}=-\mu B \sin \theta \approx-\mu B \theta,
$$

$I$ being the moment of inertia of the needle, for small angle oscillations. The angular frequency of oscillation is given by

$$
\omega^{2}=\frac{\mu B}{I} .
$$

Let $l, V, \rho, \mathrm{M}$ be respectively the length, volume, density and magnetization of the steel needle. Then

$$
I=\frac{\rho V l^{2}}{12}, \quad \boldsymbol{\mu}=V \mathbf{M}
$$

and thus

$$
\omega^{2}=\frac{12 B M}{\rho l^{2}},
$$

on

$$
M=\frac{\pi^{2} \rho l^{2}}{3 B T^{2}},
$$
