where $T$ is the period of oscillation. With $l=0.12 \mathrm{~m}, T=4 \mathrm{~s}, \rho=$ $78 \times 10^{3} \mathrm{kgm}^{-3}, B \sim 5 \times 10^{-5} \mathrm{Wm}-2$, we find

$$
M=4.6 \times 10^{5} \mathrm{Am}^{-1} \text {. }
$$

\section{0}

Magnetism in condensed matter manifests itself in various forms for different materials and at different temperatures. Four types of magnetic behavior are:
1. Ferromagnetism
3. Antiferromagnetism
2. Diamagnetism
4. Paramagnetism .

(a) For an ideal example of a material exhibiting each of the above magnetic behavior, plot the magnetic susceptibility as a function of temperature. Pay attention to the relative scales in your diagrams so that the relative strengths of the magnetic phenomena are presented. Give one example of a real material that approximates each ideal form of magnetism.

(b) Indicate any critical behavior involved in any of these phenomena.

(c) Explain the "exchange interaction" and the part it plays in the above magnetic behaviors. Give a brief quantum mechanical explanation of the exchange interactions, the Hamiltonian for the interaction, and the connection between the exchange interaction and the Weiss molecular field.

(d) Explain briefly the difference between the magnetic behaviors of sodium metal and $\mathrm{Cr}^{2+}$-doped (0.01\%) $\mathrm{Al}_{2} \mathrm{O}_{3}$ single crystal.

(SUNY, Buffalo)

\section{Solution:}

(a) Diamagnetism is a property of certain substances that in an external magnetic field the constituting atoms can generate induced currents whose magnetic field is in a direction opposite to that of the applied magnetic field, so that the magnetic susceptibility $\chi<0$. Generally $\chi$ is of the order $10^{-5} \mathrm{~cm}^{-3}$, and is hardly affected by temperature, as shown by curve $\mathrm{A}$ of Fig. 1.42. A substance whose atoms have null permanent magnetic moment exhibits diamagnetism, e.g. lead.

In a paramagnetic material Langevin paramagnetism dominates. It is caused by the lining up of the permanent magnetic moments, with the resultant magnetic susceptibility $\chi \propto \frac{1}{T}$, as shown by curve $B$ of Fig. 1.42. In addition, in a metal there is also Pauli paramagnetism due to conduction electrons, each of which carries a spin magnetic moment, with the magnetic susceptibility independent of temperature as shown by curve C. Generally speaking, the magnetic susceptibility of a paramagnetic substance is larger than that of a diamagnetic substance, but is of the same order of magnitude. It is necessary for a paramagnetic material to have atoms with nonzero permanent magnetic moments. Examples are metallic elements like sodium and aluminium, and chemical compounds of transition and rare-earth elements like $\mathrm{Cr}^{2+}$-doped $\mathrm{Al}_{2} \mathrm{O}_{3}$.

A ferromagnetic substance typically has a magnetic susceptibility that is $10^{3}$ times larger than that of a paramagnetic material and varies with temperature according to $\chi \propto \frac{1}{T-T_{\mathrm{C}}}$, as shown by curve $\mathrm{D}$. At $T$ below the Curie temperature $T_{\mathrm{C}}$, a ferromagnetic material is spontaneously magnetized; at $T$ above $T_{\mathrm{C}}$, it is paramagnetic. Typical examples are iron, cobalt and nickel. An antiferromagnetic material has a magnetic susceptibility $\chi$ which is of the same order of magnitude as that of a paramagnetic material but varies with temperature as follows: At $T$ higher than the Néel temperature $T_{\mathrm{N}}, \chi \propto \frac{1}{T+\Theta}$, where $\Theta$ is a positive constant; at $T<T_{\mathrm{N}}, \chi$ decreases with decreasing temperature, as shown by curve E. Antiferromagnetism is exhibited by many compounds involving transition metals, e.g. MnO.

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_41580ef03e6631735034g-62.jpg?height=623&width=718&top_left_y=735&top_left_x=690)

Fig. $1.42$

(b) For a ferromagnet, the phase transition ferromagnetism $\leftrightarrow$ paramagnetism occurs at the Curie temperature $T_{\mathrm{C}}$; for an antiferromagnetic substance, antiferromagnetism $\leftrightarrow$ paramagnetism occurs at the Néel temperature $T_{\mathrm{N}}$.

(c) In the ferromagnetic region atomic or molecular magnetic moments are magnetized spontaneously, implying the existence of an internal molecular field. This indicates that neighboring moments interact with each other and that the interaction is spin-dependent. If the spin angular momenta of atoms $i$ and $j$ are $\mathbf{S}_{i} \hbar$ and $\mathbf{S}_{j} \hbar$ respectively, their interaction gives rise to an exchange Hamiltonian

$$
-2 J_{\mathrm{e}} \mathrm{S}_{i} \cdot \mathbf{S}_{j},
$$

where $J_{\mathrm{e}}$ is the exchange integral. Suppose that the ferromagnetic substance has $N$ atoms per unit volume and that each atom contributes one electron to ferromagnetism. Then the exchange Hamiltonian for the crystal is

$$
H_{\mathrm{ex}}=-\sum_{\mathbf{R}_{i}} \sum_{\mathbf{R}_{j}}^{\prime} 2 J_{\mathbf{e}}\left(\mathbf{R}_{i}-\mathbf{R}_{j}\right) \mathbf{S}\left(\mathbf{R}_{i}\right) \cdot \mathbf{S}\left(\mathbf{R}_{j}\right)
$$

where $\mathbf{R}_{i}$ and $\mathbf{R}_{j}$ are two lattice vectors, and in the sum $\sum^{\prime}, \mathbf{R}_{i} \neq \mathbf{R}_{j}$. If we assume that only the exchange interactions of electrons of nearest-neighbor atoms are appreciable, then

$$
H_{\mathrm{ex}}=-\sum_{\mathbf{R}_{i}}\left[2 \sum_{\substack{\text { neighboring } \\ \mathbf{R}_{j}}} J_{\mathrm{e}}\left(\mathbf{R}_{i}-\mathbf{R}_{j}\right) \mathbf{S}\left(\mathbf{R}_{j}\right)\right] \cdot \mathbf{S}\left(\mathbf{R}_{i}\right) .
$$

This means that, if the exchange integral $J_{\mathrm{e}}>0$, parallel spins correspond to a state of lower energy. In a ferromagnetic domain the spin magnetic moments spontaneously tend to the parallel arrangement, indicating that the exchange integral is positive for interactions between neighboring atoms.

To find the connection between the exchange interaction and the Weiss molecular field, consider a crystal in which each atom has $z$ nearest-neighbor atoms. $H_{\mathrm{ex}}$ can then be written as

$$
-2 J_{\mathrm{e}} \mathbf{S}_{0} \cdot \sum_{j=1}^{z} \mathbf{S}_{j}
$$

where $\mathbf{S}_{0} \hbar$ is the spin angular momentum of the atom under consideration and $\mathbf{S}_{j} \hbar(j=1,2, \ldots, z)$ are those of its nearest neighbors. The corresponding magnetic moments $\boldsymbol{\mu}_{0}, \boldsymbol{\mu}_{j}$ are $g_{\mathrm{s}} \mu_{\mathrm{B}} \mathbf{S}_{0}, g_{\mathrm{s}} \mu_{\mathrm{B}} \mathbf{S}_{j}$, where $g_{\mathrm{s}}$ is a constant and $\mu_{\mathrm{B}}$ is the Bohr magneton. Thus

$$
H_{\mathrm{ex}}=-\mu_{0}\left[\frac{2 J_{\mathrm{e}}}{\left(g_{\mathrm{s}} \mu_{\mathrm{B}}\right)^{2}} \sum_{j=1}^{z} \boldsymbol{\mu}_{i}\right] .
$$

Comparing this with the expression for the energy of a magnetic dipole in a magnetic field, $-\boldsymbol{\mu} \cdot \mathbf{B}$, shows that the exchange interaction gives rise to an effective field

$$
\frac{2 J_{\mathrm{e}}}{\left(g_{\mathrm{s}} \mu_{\mathrm{B}}\right)^{2}} \sum_{j}^{z} \boldsymbol{\mu}_{j}=\frac{2 J_{\mathrm{e}} z}{N\left(g_{\mathrm{s}} \mu_{\mathrm{B}}\right)^{2}} \mathbf{M}
$$

where $N$ is the number of atoms per unit volume and $\mathbf{M}$ is the magnetization. This has the form of the Weiss molecular field $\gamma \mathbf{M}$ with the field constant

$$
\gamma=\frac{2 J_{\mathrm{e}} z}{N\left(g_{\mathrm{s}} \mu_{\mathrm{B}}\right)^{2}} .
$$

(d) In sodium metal, the sodium atom has a full-shell construction plus a valence electron which is free. Therefore its magnetism is due to the Pauli paramagnetism of free electrons and the diamagnetism of ions with complete shells. The sum of these two contributions results in a very weak paramagnetism. In a $\mathrm{Cr}^{2+}$-doped $\mathrm{Al}_{2} \mathrm{O}_{3}$ single crystal, on the other hand, there are nonvanishing permanent magnetic moments which line up to give a stronger paramagnetism.

\section{1}

When a metal enters the superconducting state it becomes more ordered and its free energy decreases.

What is the nature of the new ordering? Consider a superconducting ring carrying a circulating persistent current. Suppose that at some instant thermal fluctuations cause a small decrease in this current. In terms of your answer above, explain why the system will restore the current to its previous value.

Explain why there is an energy gap when one tries to add energy to a superconductor by shining light on it, but not when one adds energy by applying a dc current.

$(S U N Y, B u f f a l o)$

\section{Solution:}

When a metal enters the superconducting state, electrons lying deep inside the Fermi sphere remain normal electrons, but electrons very near the Fermi surface couple to form Cooper pairs. These are the superconducting electrons and are condensed into the lowest energy state, which does not contribute to entropy and is a highly ordered state. Thus when a metal enters the superconducting state it becomes ordered and its free energy decreases.

At some instant, some Cooper pairs may be destroyed by thermal fluctuations. The density of superconducting electrons then decreases, which causes a small decrease in the current. At that time, the ordering of the system becomes less and the free energy increases. But the energy of the system must be conserved, so some other electrons near the Fermi surface inevitably couple to form superconducting electrons, and their density increases. The system will thus restore the current to its previous value. There is an energy gap $\Delta$ between the superconducting ground state and the lowest excited state. When shining light on a superconductor, the latter absorbs light appreciably only if the energy of the photon is larger than or equal to $\Delta$. This means that there is an energy gap when one tries to add energy to a superconductor by shining light on it. On the other hand, when a dc current is applied, a Cooper pair moves as a whole and does not have to overcome the energy gap.

\section{2}

Briefly describe one of the Josephson tunneling effects.

(Wisconsin)

\section{Solution:}

A dc current can flow through a junction formed by two pieces of superconductors $S_{1}$ and $S_{2}$ separated by a thin insulating film $T$ of thickness about $10 \AA$, even though the potential difference across the junction may be zero. This indicates that the superconducting electrons can penetrate an insulating potential barrier between two superconductors resulting in a tunneling superconducting current. Such a current can be explained by a coupling between the wave functions in the two superconductors as briefly described below.

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_41580ef03e6631735034g-65.jpg?height=417&width=1005&top_left_y=1727&top_left_x=533)

Fig. 1.43

The motion of superconducting electrons (a Cooper pair) can be described by wave functions in $S_{1}$ and $S_{2}$ which attenuate exponentially in the insulating layer, as shown in Fig. 1.43. Let $\varphi_{1}$ and $\varphi_{2}$ be the phases of the waves in $S_{1}$ and $S_{2}$ respectively. The total wave function in the insulating layer is

$$
\begin{aligned}
\psi &=e^{i \varphi_{1}} e^{-k(x+a)}+e^{i \varphi_{2}} e^{-k(a-x)} \\
&=e^{-k a}\left(e^{i \varphi_{1}-k x}+e^{i \varphi_{2}+k x}\right) .
\end{aligned}
$$

The current density is

$$
j=q J,
$$

where $J$ is the probability current density

$$
J=-\frac{i \hbar}{2 m}\left(\psi^{*} \nabla \psi-\psi \nabla \psi^{*}\right) .
$$

Hence the current density crossing the junction is

$$
\begin{aligned}
j &=-\frac{i \hbar q}{2 m}\left(\psi^{*} \frac{d \psi}{d x}-\psi \frac{d \psi^{*}}{d x}\right) \\
&=\frac{2 \hbar q k}{m} e^{-2 k a} \sin \left(\phi_{2}-\phi_{1}\right) \\
&=j_{0} \sin \left(\phi_{2}-\phi_{1}\right)
\end{aligned}
$$

with $j_{0}=\frac{2 \hbar q k}{m} e^{-2 k a}$. Therefore, as long as the two coupling superconducting wave functions have a nonzero phase difference, a superconducting current exists although the potential difference across the junction may be absent.

\section{3}

Describe each of the following with a very few well-chosen words (and with no equations).

(a) A Josephson junction

(b) The dc Josephson effect

(c) The ac Josephson effect

$($ Columbia $)$

\section{Solution:}

(a) A Josephson junction consists of a very thin insulating film (about $10^{-9} \mathrm{~m}$ thick) between two superconducting slabs. As the coherent length of superconducting electrons is about $10^{-6} \mathrm{~m}$, there will be coupling between the wave functions of the superconducting electrons in the two sides of the insulating film, which gives rise to a tunneling superconducting current. This kind of sandwich structure, superconductor-insulating filmsuperconductor, is called a Josephson junction.

(b) In the absence of electric and magnetic fields, provided that a phase difference exists between the superconducting wave functions on the two sides of the insulating film of a Josephson junction, a superconducting current may flow through the Josephson junction without damping. This is the dc Josephson effect.

(c) If a steady voltage $V$ is applied to the two outer surfaces of the superconductors of a Josephson junction, an alternating superconducting current with frequency $\nu=2 e V / h$ will flow through the junction. This is the ac Josephson effect.

\section{4}

Give a simple-minded argument for how two electrons in a lattice might experience an attractive interaction which can lead to the formation of a Cooper pair.

Consider such a pair of electrons in a spin-singlet state with the total momentum equal to zero. The pair is in a metal with Fermi energy $\varepsilon_{F}$, density of states $N$. The Schrödinger equation for such a pair can be written as $\left(H_{0}+H_{1}\right) \psi(\mathbf{r})=E \psi(\mathbf{r})$, where $H_{0}$ is the kinetic energy operator of the pair, $H_{1}$ is the two-electron interaction, and $\mathbf{r}$ is the relative coordinate.

(SUNY, Buffalo)

\section{Solution:}

The electrons interact indirectly through an exchange of lattice wave phonons. For example, an electron with wave vector $\mathbf{k}_{1}$ emits a phonon with wave vector $\mathbf{q}$ and is scattered into a state of wave vector $\mathbf{k}_{1}-\mathbf{q}$; another electron whose original wave vector is $\mathbf{k}_{2}$ absorbs the phonon emitted by the first electron and enters into a state of wave vector $\mathbf{k}_{2}+\mathbf{q}$. An alternative process is that the first electron absorbs a phonon with wave vector $-\mathbf{q}$ emitted by the second electron and is scattered from a state $\mathbf{k}_{1}$ into $\mathbf{k}_{1}-\mathbf{q} ;$ the second electron is scattered from a state $\mathbf{k}_{2}$ into $\mathbf{k}_{2}+\mathbf{q}$. According to quantum theory, if the energy difference $\left|E\left(\mathbf{k}_{1}\right)-E\left(\mathbf{k}_{1}-\mathbf{q}\right)\right|<\hbar \omega_{\mathbf{q}}$, then the interaction energy is negative, which means that there is a net attractive interaction between the electrons. Obviously, only within the range $\pm \hbar \omega_{\mathrm{D}}$, where $\omega_{\mathrm{D}}$ is the Deyby frequency, near the Fermi surface can electrons satisfy this condition.

For a pair of independent electrons the resultant wave is

$$
\varphi\left(\mathbf{r}_{1}, \mathbf{r}_{2}\right)=\frac{1}{V_{\mathbf{c}}} e^{i\left(\mathbf{k}_{1} \cdot \mathbf{r}_{1}+\mathbf{k}_{2} \cdot \mathbf{r}_{2}\right)}=\frac{1}{V_{\mathrm{c}}} e^{i \mathbf{k} \cdot \mathbf{r}},
$$

where $V_{\mathrm{c}}$ is the volume of the crystal, $\mathbf{r}=\mathbf{r}_{1}-\mathbf{r}_{2}, \mathbf{k}=\mathbf{k}_{1}=-\mathbf{k}_{2}$ as the total momentum is zero.

If the two electrons interact the resultant wave can be expressed as a linear combination of the independent-electron waves:

$$
\psi(\mathbf{r})=\sum_{\mathbf{k}} g(\mathbf{k}) \varphi_{\mathbf{k}}(\mathbf{r}) .
$$

Substitution in the Schrödinger equation

$$
\left(H_{0}+H_{1}\right) \psi(\mathbf{r})=E \psi(\mathbf{r}),
$$

where

gives

$$
H_{0}=-\frac{\hbar^{2}}{2 m}\left(\nabla_{\mathbf{r}_{1}}^{2}+\nabla_{\mathbf{r}_{2}}^{2}\right)=-\frac{\hbar^{2}}{m} \nabla_{\mathbf{r}}^{2}, \quad H_{1}=V(\mathbf{r}),
$$

$$
\frac{\hbar^{2} k^{2}}{m} g(\mathbf{k})+\sum_{\mathbf{k}^{\prime}} g\left(\mathbf{k}^{\prime}\right) V_{\mathbf{k}, \mathbf{k}^{\prime}}=E g(\mathbf{k})
$$

with

$$
V_{\mathbf{k}, \mathbf{k}^{\prime}}=\frac{1}{V_{\mathrm{c}}} \int V(\mathbf{r}) e^{i\left(\mathbf{k}-\mathbf{k}^{\prime}\right) \cdot \mathbf{r}} d \mathbf{r}
$$

To first approximation we can take

$$
V_{\mathbf{k}, \mathbf{k}^{\prime}}= \begin{cases}-V, & \text { for }|\varepsilon(\mathbf{k})|,\left|\varepsilon\left(\mathbf{k}^{\prime}\right)\right| \leq \hbar \omega_{\mathrm{D}}, \\ 0, & \text { otherwise }\end{cases}
$$

where $\varepsilon(\mathbf{k})=\frac{\hbar^{2} \mathbf{k}^{2}}{2 m}-\varepsilon_{\mathrm{F}}$ and $V$ is a positive constant. Equation (1) now becomes

$$
g(\mathbf{k})=\frac{V \sum_{\mathbf{k}^{\prime}} g\left(\mathbf{k}^{\prime}\right)}{\frac{\hbar^{2} \mathbf{k}^{2}}{m}-E} .
$$

Summing up each side of the above over $\mathbf{k}$, we have

$$
\sum_{\mathbf{k}} g(\mathbf{k})=V \sum_{\mathbf{k}^{\prime}} g\left(\mathbf{k}^{\prime}\right) \sum_{\mathbf{k}} \frac{1}{\left(\frac{\hbar^{2} \mathbf{k}^{2}}{m}-E\right)},
$$

or

$$
1=V \sum_{k} \frac{1}{\frac{\hbar^{2} k^{2}}{m}-E} \approx V \int_{0}^{k_{\mathrm{D}}} \frac{N(k) d k}{\frac{\hbar^{2} k^{2}}{m}-E}=V \int_{0}^{\hbar \omega_{\mathrm{D}}} \frac{N(\varepsilon) d \varepsilon}{2\left(\varepsilon+\varepsilon_{\mathrm{F}}\right)-E}
$$

As $\hbar \omega_{\mathrm{D}} \ll E_{\mathrm{F}}$, we can take $N(\varepsilon) \approx N(0)$. Hence

$$
1 \approx N(0) V \int_{0}^{\hbar \omega_{D}} \frac{d \varepsilon}{2\left(\varepsilon+\varepsilon_{F}\right)-E}=\frac{N(0) V}{2} \ln \left(\frac{E-2 \varepsilon_{F}-2 \hbar \omega_{\mathrm{D}}}{E-2 \varepsilon_{\mathrm{F}}}\right)
$$

or

$$
E=2 \varepsilon_{\mathrm{F}}-\frac{2 \hbar \omega_{\mathrm{D}}}{\exp (2 / N(0) V)-1}<2 \varepsilon_{\mathrm{F}}
$$

as $N(0) V$ is positive.

Therefore two electrons near the Fermi surface will combine into a bound state forming a Cooper pair if a small net attractive interaction $-V$ exists between them.

\section{5}

(1) Below the superconducting transition temperature $T_{\mathbf{c}}$, a finite fraction $n_{\mathrm{s}} / n$ of the total number of conduction electrons can participate in a supercurrent; $n_{\mathrm{s}}$ is known as the number density of superconducting electrons. The London equation for simple superconductors is a phenomenological equation relating the supercurrent $\mathfrak{j}_{s}$ to the magnetic vector potential $A$ :

$$
\dot{j}_{\mathrm{s}}=\frac{-n_{\mathrm{s}} e^{2}}{m_{\mathrm{e} c}} \mathbf{A}
$$

where $m_{\mathrm{e}}$ is the electron mass. Using the appropriate Maxwell equation, show how Eq. (1) leads to the Meissner effect. What is the London penetration depth $\Lambda$ ?

(2) Another typical feature of most superconductors is the appearance of an energy gap of width $\Delta$ centered about the Fermi energy in the set of excited one-electron levels. Briefly discuss how any two of the following individual experimental measurements can discern the existence and magnitude of $\Delta$.

(a) Specific heat measurements at $T \ll T_{\text {c }}$. (b) Tunneling of single electrons across a thin insulator separating two superconductors.

(c) Microwave (where $\hbar \omega \leq \Delta$ ) absorption in thin films.

(d) Infrared $(\hbar \omega \leq \Delta)$ absorption in bulk samples.

(e) Acoustic attenuation at $T \ll T_{\mathrm{c}}$.

(Princeton)

\section{Solution:}

(1) The Meissner effect refers to the fact that in the superconducting state magnetic induction vanishes and materials become strongly diamagnetic. The London equation

$$
\mathbf{j}_{\mathrm{s}}=-\frac{n_{\mathrm{s}} e^{2}}{m_{\mathrm{e}} c} \mathbf{A}=-\frac{1}{c \lambda^{2}} \mathbf{A}
$$

where $\lambda^{2}=\frac{m_{\mathrm{e}}}{n_{\mathrm{s}} e^{2}}$, gives

$$
\nabla \times \mathbf{j}_{\mathrm{s}}=-\frac{1}{c \lambda^{2}} \nabla \times \mathbf{A}=-\frac{1}{c \lambda^{2}} \mathbf{B} .
$$

Inside a superconductor the electric field vanishes and we have the Maxwell equation

$$
\nabla \times \mathbf{B}=\frac{4 \pi}{c} \mathbf{j}_{\mathbf{s}} .
$$

Hence

$$
\mathbf{B}=-c \lambda^{2} \nabla \times \mathbf{j}_{s}=-\frac{c^{2} \lambda^{2}}{4 \pi}\left[\nabla(\nabla \cdot \mathbf{B})-\nabla^{2} \mathbf{B}\right],
$$

or, using Maxwell's equation $\nabla \cdot \mathbf{B}=0$,

$$
\nabla^{2} \mathbf{B}=\frac{1}{\Lambda^{2}} \mathbf{B},
$$

where

$$
\Lambda=\left(\frac{c^{2}}{4 \pi} \lambda^{2}\right)^{1 / 2}=\left(\frac{m_{\mathrm{e}} c^{2}}{4 \pi n_{\mathrm{s}} e^{2}}\right)^{1 / 2} .
$$

Let $x$ denote depth from the surface of the superconductor, then

$$
\frac{d^{2} \mathbf{B}}{d x^{2}}=\frac{\mathbf{B}}{\Lambda^{2}},
$$

giving

$$
\mathbf{B}=\mathbf{B}_{0} e^{-\frac{x}{\Lambda}} .
$$

This shows that $\mathbf{B}$ decays exponentially such that $\mathbf{B}=\frac{1}{e} \mathbf{B}_{0}$ at $x=\Lambda$. $\Lambda$ is known as the London penetration depth. For a great majority of metals showing superconductivity, the penetration depths are in the range $10^{-8}-10^{-7} \mathrm{~m}$. For $x \gg \Lambda, \mathbf{B} \rightarrow 0$, indicating that the magnetic field exists only in a thin layer of thickness $\approx \Lambda$ beneath the surface of the superconductor. Thus the magnetic field inside a superconductor is zero. This is the Meissner effect.

(2) The exponential decrease of specific heat with temperature for a superconductor at low temperatures implies that there is an energy gap $\Delta$, centered at about the Fermi energy, between the ground and lowest excited states in the energy spectrum of the electrons, as shown in Fig. 1.44. Two of the experiments mentioned may be used to determine the magnitude of $\Delta$. These are the following.

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_41580ef03e6631735034g-71.jpg?height=493&width=586&top_left_y=1160&top_left_x=672)

Fig. $1.44$

(a) Specific heat measurements at $T \ll T_{\mathrm{c}}$. For $T \ll T_{\mathrm{c}}$, the specific heat of the system is expressible as

$$
C_{\mathrm{S}} \approx \frac{A}{T} \exp \left(-\frac{\Delta}{k_{\mathrm{B}} T}\right),
$$

where $A$ is a constant. For $T \approx T_{\mathrm{c}}$ we find experimentally

$$
C_{\mathrm{S}}=a \exp \left(-\frac{b}{t}\right) \text {, }
$$

where $t=\frac{T}{T_{\mathrm{c}}}$ and $a$ is a constant. Comparing the above, we obtain

$$
\frac{b}{t}=\frac{\Delta}{k_{\mathrm{B}} T},
$$

or

$$
\Delta=b k_{\mathrm{B}} T_{\mathrm{c}}=1.52 k_{\mathrm{B}} T_{\mathrm{c}} \text {, }
$$

using the experimentally determined value of $b$.

(b) Acoustic attenuation of ultrasonic waves in the superconducting state is different from that in the normal state. Theory gives

$$
\frac{\alpha_{\mathrm{S}}}{\alpha_{\mathrm{N}}}=\frac{2}{\exp \left(\frac{\Delta}{k_{\mathrm{B}} T}\right)+1}
$$

where $\alpha_{\mathrm{S}}$ and $\alpha_{\mathrm{N}}$ are the attenuation coefficients for the superconducting state and the normal state respectively. Then using the experimentally obtained ratio of $\frac{\alpha_{\mathrm{S}}}{\alpha_{\mathrm{N}}}, \Delta \operatorname{can}$ be determined

\section{6}

A ring is made of $1 \mathrm{~mm}$ diameter lead wire formed into a circle of $10 \mathrm{~cm}$ diameter as shown in Fig. 1.45. The ring is in the superconducting state and has a 100 A current flowing in it. It is observed that there is no detectable change in current for a period of 1 year. If the detector is sensitive to a change in current of a little as $1 \mu \mathrm{A}$, calculate the experimental upper limit for the resistivity of lead in the superconducting state.

Note: An answer within a factor of three will suffice.

(Chicago )

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_41580ef03e6631735034g-72.jpg?height=282&width=588&top_left_y=1789&top_left_x=823)

Fig. $1.45$

\section{Solution:}

Let $\rho$ be the resistivity of lead. Then the resistance of the ring is $R=\rho \frac{l}{S}$, where $l=2 \pi r, S$ is the cross-sectional area of the lead wire. The ring may be considered as an RL circuit, so that 

$$
-L \frac{d I}{d t}=I R,
$$

or

$$
I(t)=I(0) e^{-R t / L},
$$

i.e.

$$
R=\frac{L}{t} \ln \frac{I(0)}{I(t)} .
$$

The self-inductance $L$ is given by the flux linkage $\Phi$ and the current $I$ by $L=\Phi / I$, thus

$$
I(0)=100 \mathrm{~A}, \quad I(t)=100-10^{-6} \mathrm{~A} .
$$

The magnetic field distribution in the ring is somewhat complicated, but for a rough estimate we can approximate the magnetic field inside the ring by a uniform field equal in magnitude to that at the center of the ring. Thus

$$
\Phi=\pi r^{2} B=\pi r^{2} \cdot \frac{\mu_{0} I}{2 r}=\frac{1}{2} \pi \mu_{0} r I
$$

and

$$
L=\frac{1}{2} \pi \mu_{0} r .
$$

As $I(t) \approx I(0), \frac{R t}{L} \ll 1$. Then

$$
\begin{aligned}
\Delta I &=I(0)-I(t) \approx \frac{I_{0} R t}{L}, \\
\ln \frac{I(0)}{I(t)} &=\ln \left[1+\frac{\Delta I}{I(t)}\right] \approx \frac{\Delta I}{I(0)} \approx 10^{-8}, \\
R & \approx \frac{\pi \mu_{0} r}{2 t}\left[\frac{\Delta I}{I(0)}\right]=\frac{\pi}{2} \times \frac{4 \pi \times 10^{-7} \times 5 \times 10^{-2}}{365 \times 24 \times 3600} \times 10^{-8} \\
&=3.13 \times 10^{-23} \Omega .
\end{aligned}
$$

This gives

$$
\rho=\frac{S R}{2 \pi r}=\frac{\pi \times\left(0.5 \times 10^{-3}\right)^{2} \times 3.13 \times 10^{-23}}{2 \pi \times 5 \times 10^{-2}}=7.8 \times 10^{-29} \Omega \mathrm{m} .
$$



\subsection{OTHER TOPICS (1077-1081)}

\section{7}

A currently important technique for precisely measuring the lifetimes of states of multiply ionized atoms consists of exciting a beam of the desired ions with a laser tuned to a resonance wavelength of the ion under study, and measuring the emission intensity from the ion beam as a function of down- stream distance. When a particular ion beam with a resonance wavelength at $4885 \AA$ is excited with a $4880 \AA$ argon-ion laser output, the intensity is found to drop by a factor of two, $10 \mathrm{~mm}$ down-stream from the excitation point. If ion beam velocity is $v / c=5 \times 10^{-3}$, calculate the angle the laser beam must make with the normal to the ion beam to achieve resonance and the lifetime of the excited state of the ion.

(Wisconsin)

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_41580ef03e6631735034g-74.jpg?height=301&width=417&top_left_y=1302&top_left_x=865)

Fig. $1.46$

\section{Solution:}

Take two coordinate frames $\Sigma, \Sigma^{\prime} . \Sigma$ is the laboratory frame in which the laser source $L$ is at rest and the ions move with velocity $\beta c$ along the $x$-axis as shown in Fig. 1.46. $\Sigma^{\prime}$ is the rest frame of an ion in the beam and moves with velocity $\beta c$ relative to $\Sigma$ along the $x$ direction. Consider the transformation of the 4 -vector $\left(\mathbf{k}, \frac{\omega}{c}\right)$. We have

$$
\frac{\omega^{\prime}}{c}=\gamma\left(\frac{\omega}{c}-\beta k \sin \theta\right),
$$

where $\gamma=\left(1-\beta^{2}\right)^{-\frac{1}{2}}, k=\frac{\omega}{c}$. Hence 

$$
\begin{aligned}
\sin \theta &=\frac{1}{\beta}\left(1-\frac{\lambda}{\lambda^{\prime}} \sqrt{1-\beta^{2}}\right) \\
& \approx \frac{1}{\beta}\left[1-\frac{\lambda}{\lambda^{\prime}}\left(1-\frac{\beta^{2}}{2}\right)\right]
\end{aligned}
$$

With $\lambda=4885 \AA, \lambda^{\prime}=4880 \AA, \beta=5 \times 10^{-3}$, we find

$$
\sin \theta=0.2074, \quad \text { or } \quad \theta=11.97^{\circ} \text {. }
$$

As the excited ions move with velocity $\beta c$, on account of time dilation the laboratory lifetime of an ion is $\gamma \tau$, where $\tau$ is the proper lifetime. Hence the excited ion beam will decay according to

$$
I=I_{0} \exp \left(-\frac{t}{\gamma \tau}\right)
$$

giving

$$
\begin{aligned}
\tau &=\frac{t}{\gamma \ln \left(\frac{I_{0}}{I}\right)}=\frac{x}{\gamma \beta c \ln \left(\frac{I_{0}}{I}\right)} \\
& \approx\left(1-\frac{\beta^{2}}{2}\right) \frac{x}{\beta c \ln \left(\frac{I_{0}}{I}\right)}=9.6 \times 10^{-9} \mathrm{~s} .
\end{aligned}
$$

\section{8}

Tiny balls of a reflecting material are floating on the surface of a transparent material. Begin by assuming that they form a perfect square lattice with lattice spacing of $10,000 \AA$ as shown in Fig. 1.47. A coherent beam of light going straight down hits the surface. The light has a wavelength of $5000 \AA$.

(a) At what angles $(\theta, \phi)$ will we observe light going out if we stand back from the surface?

(b) If the incoming beam were infinitely wide and infinitely well collimated and if the lattice were infinitely large, the outgoing beam would of course also move in perfectly precise directions. If we have a horizontal screen 10 meters above the surface, perfectly sharp spots would appear on it. If, however, the lattice were a square containing "only" 10,000 balls, 

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_41580ef03e6631735034g-76.jpg?height=721&width=941&top_left_y=358&top_left_x=641)

Fig. $1.47$

what spot width would you expect to see on the screen? (An order of magnitude estimate is good enough, but do give an argument for it.)

(c) Now assume that each ball were held in its place in the lattice by forces which behaved like tiny springs. Each ball independently jiggles up and back about its lattice position in a random pattern of motion with a mean squared displacement of about $2000 \AA$. In what way will this motion change the pattern of the outgoing light in comparison to the case in which the balls were at their lattice sites? A comparison of the "in motion" intensity pattern to the "at rest" pattern can tell us whether the motion of the balls is horizontal or vertical. How can one distinguish between these two cases?

(Chicago )

\section{Solution:}

(a) The direction of the outgoing beam is that of an interference maximum of the reflected light. Let the unit vector of the interference-maximum direction be

$$
\mathbf{k}_{0}=\sin \theta \cos \varphi \mathbf{e}_{x}+\sin \theta \sin \varphi \mathbf{e}_{y}+\cos \theta \mathbf{e}_{z}
$$

A plane through the origin with unit normal $\mathbf{k}_{0}$ is given by

$$
\mathbf{k}_{0} \cdot \mathbf{r}=0
$$

$\mathbf{r}$ being any vector lying on the plane. If $\mathbf{R}$ is the radius vector of a point on the $x y$ plane, its distance from this plane is $d_{\mathrm{A}}=\mathbf{k}_{0} \cdot \mathbf{R}$. Then for the lattice points $\mathrm{A}(d, 0,0), \mathrm{B}(0, d, 0), \mathrm{C}(d, d, 0)$, shown in Fig. 1.48, $d$ being the edge of a unit cell, we have

$$
\begin{aligned}
&d_{\mathrm{A}}=d \sin \theta \cos \varphi, \\
&d_{\mathrm{B}}=d \sin \theta \sin \varphi, \\
&d_{\mathrm{C}}=d \sin \theta(\cos \varphi+\sin \varphi) .
\end{aligned}
$$

The conditions for $\mathbf{k}_{0}$ to be a direction of interference maximum are

$$
d_{\mathrm{A}}=n_{1} \lambda_{0}, \quad d_{\mathrm{B}}=n_{2} \lambda_{0}, \quad d_{\mathrm{C}}=n_{3} \lambda_{0},
$$

where $n_{1}, n_{2}, n_{3}$ are integers, and $\lambda_{0}$ is the wavelength of the incident light. Note that as $d_{\mathrm{C}}=d_{\mathrm{A}}+d_{\mathrm{B}}$, the last condition is already included in the first two.

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_41580ef03e6631735034g-77.jpg?height=437&width=491&top_left_y=1283&top_left_x=817)

Fig. 1.48.

On account of translational symmetry of the lattice, the distances from the plane to other lattice points are also integral multiples of the wavelength. As

$$
\tan \varphi=\frac{n_{2}}{n_{1}}, \quad d^{2} \sin ^{2} \theta=\left(n_{1}^{2}+n_{2}^{2}\right) \lambda_{0}^{2}
$$

we have

$$
\begin{aligned}
\theta=\arcsin \left(\sqrt{n_{1}^{2}+n_{2}^{2}} \frac{\lambda_{0}}{d}\right), & \text { or } \quad \theta=\pi-\arcsin \left(\sqrt{n_{1}^{2}+n_{2}^{2}} \frac{\lambda_{0}}{d}\right), \\
\varphi=\arctan \frac{n_{2}}{n_{1}}, \quad \text { or } \quad \varphi=\pi+\arctan \frac{n_{2}}{n_{1}} .
\end{aligned}
$$

(b) The edge of a square containing 10,000 balls is $100 d$. Now the beam passing through the square will produce diffraction and the width of a spot on the screen is

$$
\Delta x=\Delta y \approx \frac{\lambda}{100 d} \times D=\frac{5000}{100 \times 10000} \times 10=0.05 \mathrm{~m},
$$

$D$ being the distance between the screen and the square lattice.

(c) The oscillatory motion of the balls will make the original sharp spots diffuse into larger spots as well as produce some background light where there are no spots.

If the motion of the balls is horizontal, the phase of the light along the $z$-direction is less affected. Then the interference pattern is such that central spots are brighter and smaller and are on a darker background, while spots near the edges are fainter and larger, and are on a brighter background. If the motion of the balls is vertical, the phase of the light about the $z$ direction will be affected more, with the result that the effects on the spots and background light will be the reverse of the above.

\section{9}

Describe briefly the basic parts and principle of operation of a hotcathode ionization gauge for measuring pressure in a vacuum system. State approximately the high and low pressure limits of the gauge and the factors which determine these limits.

(Wisconsin)

\section{Solution:}

A hot-cathode ionization gauge consists of a cathode F, an accelerator A and a collector C, as shown in Fig. $1.49$ together with a typical circuit for its operation.

The cathode filament, heated by an electric current, emits electrons which move toward the accelerator A maintained at a positive potential. Some of the electrons are absorbed by A while others pass through the gaps between the grids of the accelerator toward the collector $\mathrm{C}$, kept at a negative potential, which repulses the electrons as they approach C. Thus the electrons oscillate to and fro between $\mathrm{C}$ and $\mathrm{A}$ until they are absorbed by A. During such oscillations the electron collide frequently with the gas molecules in the tube, giving rise to positive ions and electrons. The positive 

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_41580ef03e6631735034g-79.jpg?height=621&width=575&top_left_y=435&top_left_x=794)

Fig. $1.49$

ions are collected by the collector $\mathrm{C}$ to give an ionic current $i_{+}$, and the electrons are collected by the accelerator to give an electric current $i_{\mathrm{e}}$. Theory shows that the pressure $p$ in the tube can be expressed as

$$
p=K \frac{i_{+}}{i_{\mathrm{e}}},
$$

where $K$ is a constant. If $i_{\mathrm{e}}$ is kept constant, then

$$
p=K_{1} i_{+},
$$

which enables one to determine the pressure in a vacuum system by measuring the ionic current $i_{+}$.

The high pressure limit of the ionization gauge is about $10^{-3}$ torr. When $p>10^{-3}$ torr, the filament is easily burnt out; furthermore, $i_{+}$ does not increase with $p$, as due to the chemical cleaning of the hot filament it behaves as an absorber of gas. The low pressure limit is about $10^{-7}$ torr, below which the current collected by the collector is mainly the photoemission current; only a small fraction of it is the ionic current with the result that $p$ does not vary linearly with $i_{+}$. 

\section{0}

Discuss briefly the following items:

(a) The umklapp process and its relation to the thermal conductivity of a crystal lattice.

(b) The Cooper pair and its relation to the smearing of the Fermi surface and the ground state wave function in the BCS theory of superconductivity.

(c) The Bloch theorem and its relation to the symmetry of the crystal lattice.

(d) The essentials of the Landau theory of Fermi liquids for electrons in metals.

(SUNY, Buffalo)

\section{Solution:}

(a) Suppose two phonons of wave vectors $\mathbf{q}_{1}, \mathbf{q}_{2}$ collide and produce a phonon of wave vector $\mathbf{q}_{3}$. Then $\mathbf{q}_{3}=\mathbf{q}_{1}+\mathbf{q}_{2}$ by momentum conservation. If $\mathbf{q}_{3}$ so obtained lies inside the Brillouin zone (BZ), the process is said to be normal. If $\mathbf{q}_{3}$ lies outside the BZ, then the third phonon has an effective vector $\mathbf{q}_{4}$ inside the $B Z$ given by $\mathbf{q}_{3}=\mathbf{q}_{4}+\mathbf{G}$, where $\mathbf{G}$ is an appropriate reciprocal lattice vector. $\mathbf{q}_{4}$ will be in a direction almost opposite to either of $\mathbf{q}_{1}, \mathbf{q}_{2}$, the difference in momentum being transferred to the center of mass of the lattice. Such a process is known as the umklapp process. It is effective only at high temperatures, where many phonons near the boundaries of the $\mathrm{BZ}$ are excited.

The umklapp process is highly effective in changing the momentum of a phonon and is responsible for its mean free path at high temperatures. It is the umklapp process, not the normal process, that gives rise to the thermal resistance of a crystal. For the normal process, if the initial momentum of the crystal is not zero, it is possible to generate a steady thermal conductive current even though a temperature gradient may not exist, leading to an infinite thermal conductivity. Therefore the finite thermal conductivity is caused by the umklapp process.

(b) For two electrons lying just inside the Fermi surface, the repulsive Coulomb force between them is substantially reduced because of the screening of the other electrons in the Fermi sphere. Then, by exchanging phonons they can attract each other and form a bound state of an electron pair known as the Cooper pair. The binding energy is strongest when the electrons forming the pair have opposite momenta and opposite spins, $\mathbf{k} \uparrow,-\mathbf{k} \downarrow$. The formation of Cooper pairs produces an energy gap in the spectrum of electrons which straddles the Fermi energy level. Thus the Fermi surface is smeared.

In the BCS theory the ground state is such that if the state $\mathbf{k} \uparrow$ is occupied, so is the state $-\mathbf{k} \downarrow$, and if the state $\mathbf{k} \uparrow$ is empty, so is the state $-\mathbf{k} \downarrow$. Thus if $\psi_{1 \mathbf{k}}$ denotes the state of $(\mathbf{k} \uparrow,-\mathbf{k} \downarrow)$ being occupied by two electrons, and $\psi_{2 \mathbf{k}}$ denotes the empty state of $(\mathbf{k} \uparrow,-\mathbf{k} \downarrow)$, with probabilities $u_{\mathbf{k}}^{2}, v_{\mathbf{k}}^{2}$ respectively, then the wave function of the BCS ground state is

$$
\psi=u_{\mathbf{k}} \psi_{1 \mathbf{k}}+v_{\mathbf{k}} \psi_{2 \mathbf{k}} .
$$

(c) The Bloch theorem applies to the wave function of an electron moving in a crystalline solid in which its potential is periodic:

$$
V(\mathbf{r}+\mathbf{R})=V(\mathbf{r}),
$$

$\mathbf{R}$ being a lattice vector, i.e., the potential has the same translational symmetry as the lattice. The motion of the electron is described by the Schrödinger equation

$$
\left[-\frac{\hbar^{2}}{2 m} \nabla^{2}+V(\mathbf{r})\right] \psi(\mathbf{r})=E \psi(\mathbf{r}) .
$$

The Bloch theorem states that the solution $\psi(\mathbf{r})$, the wave function of the electron, has the form

$$
\psi_{\mathbf{k}}(\mathbf{r})=e^{i \mathbf{k} \cdot \mathbf{r}} u_{\mathbf{k}}(\mathbf{r}),
$$

where the function $u_{\mathbf{k}}(\mathbf{r})$ has the same translational symmetry as the lattice:

$$
u_{\mathbf{k}}(\mathbf{r}+\mathbf{R})=u_{\mathbf{k}}(\mathbf{r}),
$$

and the vector $\mathbf{k}$ is related to the momentum $\mathbf{p}$ of the electron by $\mathbf{p}=\hbar \mathbf{k}$.

The Bloch theorem is a consequence of the translational symmetry of the lattice. The solution of the Schrödinger equation can always be written as

$$
\psi(\mathbf{r})=f(\mathbf{r}) u(\mathbf{r}),
$$

where $u(\mathbf{r})$ has the above-mentioned symmetry. Since the potential $V(\mathbf{r})$ is periodic, all observable quantities associated with the electron must be periodic also, in particular the probability density $|\psi(\mathbf{r})|^{2}$. Hence

$$
|f(\mathbf{r}+\mathbf{R})|^{2}=|f(\mathbf{r})|^{2} .
$$

The only function satisfying this relation for all $\mathbf{R}$ has the exponential form $e^{i \mathbf{k} \cdot \mathbf{r}}$, as required by the Bloch theorem.

(d) In a system of conduction electrons, the electrons interact with one another through the electrostatic potential, while a moving electron also exerts an inertial reaction on the surrounding electron gas, which has the effect of increasing the electron's effective mass. When this interaction is taken into account, the electron system is called a Fermi liquid. Essentially, the Landau theory gives a unified explanation for the effects of the interactions.

\section{1}

For each of the following describe in one sentence each the phenomenon observed (not the explanation).
(a) Raman effect
(b) Ramsauer effect
(c) Kerr effect
(d) Faraday effect
(e) Paschen-Back effect.

(Wisconsin)

\section{Solution:}

(a) A phonon is scattered inelastically by a crystal with the creation or annihilation of a phonon or magnon.

(b) The mean free path of low energy electrons is increased in an inert gas.

(c) Certain transparent substances become doubly refracting when an electric field is applied at right angles to the direction of the incident light.

(d) The plane of polarization of linearly polarized light rotates when it travels in ceratin transparent substances placed in a magnetic field which is parallel to the direction of the light.

(e) When a light source is placed in a strong magnetic field, a spectral line is seen to split into two lines when observed along the direction of the field, and into three lines when observed perpendicular to the field. 

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_41580ef03e6631735034g-83.jpg?height=347&width=411&top_left_y=1190&top_left_x=848)



\section{PART II}

\section{RELATIVITY}



![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_41580ef03e6631735034g-85.jpg?height=347&width=411&top_left_y=1190&top_left_x=848)



\subsection{SPECIAL RELATIVITY (2001-2007)}

\section{1}

In this problem you are asked to derive some aspects of Lorentz transformations, using some thought experiments devised by Einstein. Einstein's thought experiments involve an idealized clock (Fig. 2.1(a)) in which a light wave (or a massive particle) is bouncing back and forth between two mirrors. The clock "ticks" when the light wave makes a round trip from mirror A to mirror $\mathrm{B}$ and back.

(a) Derive the time dilation formula of special relativity as follows. Assuming that light has velocity $c$ in every frame, how much slower does the clock tick in a frame (Fig. 2.1(b)) in which it has velocity $V$ in a direction perpendicular to the separation between the mirrors than it ticks in its rest frame.

(b) According to special relativity, the time dilation factor is the same for all clocks of a given velocity, independent of how they operate. Suppose that in Einstein's clock a massive particle rather than a photon is bouncing between the mirrors. In the rest frame of the clock, the massive particle has velocity $v<c$. Derive the Lorentz transformation law for velocities as follows. What must be the velocity of the massive particle in the frame considered in exercise (a) if it is required that the clock, when operated with a massive particle, ticks more slowly in this frame than in its rest frame by the same time dilation factor found in (a)?

(c) Now derive the Lorentz contraction law for length. Suppose again that the clock is operated with a photon of velocity $c$, but suppose that it is rotated by $90^{\circ}$ before being set in motion, so that now (Fig. 2.1(c)) it has velocity $V$ parallel to the separation between the mirrors. How much shorter must the clock be in the moving frame than in its rest frame, so that the ticking is slowed down in the moving frame relative to the rest frame by the same time dilation factor found in (a)?

(Princeton $)$

\section{Solution:}

(a) Suppose the mirrors $\mathrm{A}$ and $\mathrm{B}$ are separated by a distance $L$ in the rest frame. In that frame a light wave will take a time $t_{0}=\frac{2 L}{c}$ for the round trip $\mathrm{A} \rightarrow \mathrm{B} \rightarrow \mathrm{A}$. This is the proper time interval between two consecutive ticks of the clock. Let $t_{0}^{\prime}$ be the time interval between two consecutive ticks (a)

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_41580ef03e6631735034g-87.jpg?height=330&width=204&top_left_y=426&top_left_x=928)

A

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_41580ef03e6631735034g-87.jpg?height=84&width=179&top_left_y=858&top_left_x=946)

(b)

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_41580ef03e6631735034g-87.jpg?height=499&width=1068&top_left_y=984&top_left_x=493)

(c)

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_41580ef03e6631735034g-87.jpg?height=225&width=987&top_left_y=1449&top_left_x=599)

Fig. $2.1$

of the clock in a frame in which the mirrors move with velocity $V$ as shown in Fig 2.1(b). It is noted that when the light wave is bounced back at the mirror $B$, the latter has already moved a distance $\frac{t_{0}^{\prime}}{2} V$. As shown in Fig. 2.2, we have, since light has velocity $c$ in all directions

$$
L^{2}+\left(\frac{t_{0}^{\prime} V}{2}\right)^{2}=\left(\frac{t_{0}^{\prime}}{2} c\right)^{2}
$$

or

$$
t_{0}^{\prime}=\frac{2 L}{\sqrt{c^{2}-V^{2}}}=\left(1-\frac{V^{2}}{c^{2}}\right)^{-\frac{1}{2}} t_{0}
$$

Hence the ticking of the clock in a frame in which it moves with velocity $V$ in a direction perpendicular to the separation of the mirrors is slower by a factor $\left(1-\frac{V^{2}}{c^{2}}\right)^{-\frac{1}{2}}$

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_41580ef03e6631735034g-88.jpg?height=572&width=426&top_left_y=576&top_left_x=836)

Fig. $2.2$

(b) The light wave is now replaced by a massive particle of velocity $v$ in the rest frame of the clock. Then the proper time interval between two consecutive ticks is $t=\frac{2 L}{v}$. Suppose the velocity of the massive particle in the frame in which the clock has velocity $V$, as in (a), is $v^{\prime}$ in the direction that it bounces at $B$. We have

$$
L^{2}+\left(\frac{t^{\prime} V}{2}\right)^{2}=\left(\frac{t^{\prime} v^{\prime}}{2}\right)^{2}
$$

or

$$
t^{\prime}=\frac{2 L}{\sqrt{v^{\prime 2}-V^{2}}}=\frac{t}{\sqrt{\left(\frac{v^{\prime}}{v}\right)^{2}-\left(\frac{V}{v}\right)^{2}}}
$$

where $t^{\prime}$ is the time interval between two consecutive ticks in the moving frame of the clock. We required that

$$
\frac{1}{\sqrt{\left(\frac{v^{\prime}}{v}\right)^{2}-\left(\frac{V}{v}\right)^{2}}}=\frac{1}{\sqrt{1-\left(\frac{V}{c}\right)^{2}}},
$$

which gives

$$
v^{\prime}=\sqrt{v^{2}+\left(1-\frac{v^{2}}{c^{2}}\right) V^{2}} .
$$

(c) Suppose the mirrors A and B are separated by $L^{\prime}$ in the moving frame of the clock. After leaving $A$, suppose the light wave reaches $B$ at time $t_{1}^{\prime}$. As B has moved a distance $V t_{1}^{\prime}$, we have

$$
L^{\prime}+V t_{1}^{\prime}=c t_{1}^{\prime},
$$

or

$$
t_{1}^{\prime}=\frac{L^{\prime}}{c-V} .
$$

Supposing the light wave, after bouncing at $\mathrm{B}$, takes time $t_{2}^{\prime}$ to reach $\mathrm{A}$ again, we have by the same reasoning

$$
L^{\prime}+V t_{1}^{\prime}-V\left(t_{1}^{\prime}+t_{2}^{\prime}\right)=c t_{2}^{\prime},
$$

or

$$
t_{2}^{\prime}=\frac{L^{\prime}}{c+V} .
$$

Hence the interval between two consecutive ticks in the moving frame is

$$
t_{0}^{\prime}=t_{1}^{\prime}+t_{2}^{\prime}=\frac{2 L^{\prime}}{c\left(1-\frac{V^{2}}{c^{2}}\right)}=\left(\frac{L^{\prime}}{L}\right) \frac{t_{0}}{1-\frac{V^{2}}{c^{2}}} .
$$

We require that

$$
\left(\frac{L^{\prime}}{L}\right) \frac{1}{1-\frac{V^{2}}{c^{2}}}=\frac{1}{\sqrt{1-\frac{V^{2}}{c^{2}}}},
$$

which gives

$$
L^{\prime}=\left(1-\frac{V^{2}}{c^{2}}\right)^{1 / 2} L .
$$

\section{2}

(a) A muon at rest lives $10^{-6} \mathrm{sec}$, and its mass is $100 \mathrm{MeV} / \mathrm{c}^{2}$. How energetic must a muon be to reach the Earth's surface if it is produced high in the atmosphere (say $\sim 10^{4} \mathrm{~m} \mathrm{up}$ )?

(b) Suppose to a zeroth approximation that the Earth has a 1 gauss magnetic field pointing in the direction of its axis, extending out to $10^{4} \mathrm{~cm}$. How much, and in what direction, is a muon of energy $E$ normally incident at the equator deflected by the field? (c) Very high energy protons in cosmic rays can lose energy through collision with $3 \mathrm{~K}$ radiation (cosmological background) in the process $p+$ $\lambda \rightarrow p+\pi$. How energetic need a proton be to be above threshold for this reaction?

(Princeton $)$

\section{Solution:}

(a) A clock moving at velocity $v$ appears to go slow by a factor $\gamma=$ $\left(1-\frac{v^{2}}{c^{2}}\right)^{-\frac{1}{2}}$. Thus a muon of velocity $v$ will have a lifetime $\gamma \tau_{0}$ in the laboratory frame, where $\tau_{0}$ is its rest lifetime. For a muon produced at a height $h$ with velocity $v$ to reach the Earth's surface, it must have a lifetime

$$
\gamma \tau_{0}=\frac{h}{v},
$$

which gives

$$
\gamma v=c \sqrt{\gamma^{2}-1}=\frac{h}{\tau_{0}} .
$$

Hence the muon of rest mass $m$ must have a total energy

$$
\begin{aligned}
m \gamma c^{2} &=m c^{2} \sqrt{\left(\frac{h}{\tau_{0} c}\right)^{2}+1} \approx \frac{m c^{2} h}{\tau_{0} c} \\
&=\frac{100 \times 10^{4}}{10^{-6} \times 3 \times 10^{8}}=3.3 \times 10^{3} \mathrm{MeV} .
\end{aligned}
$$

(b) Suppose the muon has rest mass $m$, charge $q$ and velocity $\mathbf{v}$. It suffers gravity, which is negligible, and magnetic force $\mathbf{F}=q \mathbf{v} \times \mathbf{B}$. As the magnetic force does no work on the muon, for $\mathbf{F} \cdot \mathbf{v}=0$, the muon energy $E=m \gamma c^{2}$ is constant. It follows that the magnitudes of its velocity $\mathbf{v}$ and momentum $\mathbf{p}=m \gamma \mathbf{v}$, where $\gamma$ is the Lorentz factor $\left(1-\frac{v^{2}}{c^{2}}\right)^{-\frac{1}{2}}$, are constant.

Take Cartesian coordinates as shown in Fig. $2.3$ with the origin on the Earth's surface, $y$-axis vertically upward, $z$-axis pointing north, and $x$-axis pointing west. With $\mathbf{v}=(\dot{x}, \dot{y}, \dot{z}), \mathbf{B}=(0,0, B)$, the equation of motion in SI units

$$
\frac{d \mathbf{p}}{d t}=m \gamma \frac{d \mathbf{v}}{d t}=q \mathbf{v} \times \mathbf{B}
$$

can be written as

$$
\ddot{x}=\omega \dot{y}, \quad \ddot{y}=-\omega \dot{x}, \quad \ddot{z}=0
$$![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_b94dc6d6f98c5187157dg-01.jpg?height=599&width=545&top_left_y=365&top_left_x=885)

Fig. $2.3$

with $\omega=\frac{q B}{m \gamma}$. With the initial condition that at $t=0$, when the muon is just within the range of the Earth's magnetic field, $x=z=0, y=y_{0}$, $\dot{x}=\dot{z}=0, \dot{y}=-v$, the solution is

$$
x=\frac{v}{\omega} \cos (\omega t)-\frac{v}{\omega}, \quad y=y_{0}-\frac{v}{\omega} \sin (\omega t), \quad z=0 .
$$

Hence

$$
x=\frac{v}{\omega} \sqrt{1-\frac{\left(y-y_{0}\right)^{2} \omega^{2}}{v^{2}}}-\frac{v}{\omega} .
$$

On the Earth's surface, $y=0, x=x_{\mathrm{e}}$ given by

$$
x_{\mathrm{e}}=\frac{v}{\omega} \sqrt{1-\left(\frac{y_{0} \omega}{v}\right)^{2}}-\frac{v}{\omega} .
$$

We have $B=1$ gauss $=10^{-4} \mathrm{~Wb} \mathrm{~m}^{-2},|q|=e=1.6 \times 10^{-19} \mathrm{C}$,

$$
\frac{v}{\omega}=\frac{m \gamma v}{q B}=\frac{p}{q B},
$$

so that if $p$ is $1 \mathrm{MeV} / \mathrm{c}$

$$
\left|\frac{v}{\omega}\right|=\frac{1.6 \times 10^{-19} \times 10^{6}}{1.6 \times 10^{-23} \times 3 \times 10^{8}}=\frac{1}{3} \times 10^{2} \mathrm{~m} .
$$

Thus if $p$ is greater than, say, $10 \mathrm{MeV} / \mathrm{c}$, we can take $\left|\frac{v}{\omega}\right| \gg y_{0}$ which is $\sim 10^{2} \mathrm{~m}$, and approximate 

$$
\begin{aligned}
x_{\mathrm{e}} & \approx \frac{v}{\omega}\left[1-\frac{1}{2}\left(\frac{y_{0} \omega}{v}\right)^{2}\right]-\frac{v}{\omega} \approx \frac{\omega y_{0}^{2}}{2 v} \\
&=\frac{q B y_{0}^{2}}{2 p}=\frac{c q B y_{0}^{2}}{2 \sqrt{E^{2}-m^{2} c^{4}}} .
\end{aligned}
$$

Hence the angle of deflection is

$$
\alpha=\frac{x_{\mathrm{e}}}{y_{0}}=\frac{c q B y_{0}}{2 \sqrt{E^{2}-m^{2} c^{4}}} .
$$

If $E$ is that given by (a), $3.3 \times 10^{3} \mathrm{MeV}$,

$$
\begin{aligned}
\alpha &=\frac{c q B y_{0}}{2 E}=\frac{3 \times 10^{8} \times 1.6 \times 10^{-19} \times 10^{-4} \times 10^{2}}{2 \times 3.3 \times 10^{9} \times 1.6 \times 10^{-19}} \\
&=5 \times 10^{-4} \mathrm{rad}=1.7^{\prime} .
\end{aligned}
$$

The deflection is to the west for positive muons and to the east for negative muons.

(c) The line element of the energy-momentum four-vector, $E^{2}-P^{2} c^{2}$ is invariant under Lorentz transformation and, if the total energy $E$ and total momentum $P$ are conserved, remains constant in a reaction. Let $E_{\mathbf{p}}$, $E_{\lambda}, \mathbf{p}_{\mathbf{p}}, \mathbf{p}_{\lambda}$ be respectively the energy and momentum of the proton and a background photon. Before the collision,

$$
\begin{aligned}
E^{2}-P^{2} c^{2} &=\left(E_{\mathbf{p}}+E_{\lambda}\right)^{2}-\left(\mathbf{p}_{\mathrm{p}}+\mathbf{p}_{\lambda}\right)^{2} c^{2} \\
&=\left(E_{\mathbf{p}}^{2}-p_{\mathrm{p}}^{2} c^{2}\right)+2 E_{\mathrm{p}} E_{\lambda}+\left(E_{\lambda}^{2}-p_{\lambda}^{2} c^{2}\right)-2 \mathbf{p}_{\mathrm{p}} \cdot \mathbf{p}_{\lambda} c^{2} \\
&=m_{\mathrm{p}}^{2} c^{4}+2 E_{\mathrm{p}} E_{\lambda}-2 p_{\mathrm{p}} p_{\lambda} c^{2} \cos \theta
\end{aligned}
$$

where $\theta$ is the angle between $p_{\mathrm{p}}$ and $p_{\lambda}$, as $E_{\mathrm{p}}^{2}=m^{2} c^{4}+p_{\mathrm{p}}^{2} c^{2}, E_{\lambda}=p_{\lambda} c$. At threshold, the proton and pion are produced with zero velocity in the center of mass frame of the system. Thus

$$
\bar{E}^{2}-\bar{P}^{2} c^{2}=\left(m_{\mathrm{p}} c^{2}+m_{\pi} c^{2}\right)^{2} .
$$

Equating the above two expressions and making the approximation $E_{\mathrm{p}} \approx$ $p_{\mathrm{p}} c$ for energetic protons, we find

$$
E_{\mathrm{p}} \approx \frac{m_{\pi}\left(m_{\pi}+2 m_{\mathrm{p}}\right) c^{4}}{2(1-\cos \theta) E_{\lambda}} .
$$

The lowest threshold proton energy is given by $\cos \theta=-1$, or $\theta=\pi$, and is 

$$
E_{\mathrm{p}} \approx \frac{m_{\pi}\left(m_{\pi}+2 m_{\mathrm{p}}\right) c^{4}}{4 E_{\lambda}} .
$$

The energy of a background photon can be taken to be $\sim k T$, where $k$ is the Boltzman constant and $T=3 \mathrm{~K}$. As $k=1.38 \times 10^{-23} \mathrm{~J} \mathrm{deg}^{-1}$ $=8.63 \times 10^{-11} \mathrm{MeV} \mathrm{deg}^{-1}, m_{\pi}=140 \mathrm{MeV}, m_{\mathrm{p}}=938 \mathrm{MeV}$, the lowest threshold is

$$
E_{\mathrm{p}} \approx \frac{140 \times(140+2 \times 938)}{4 \times 8.63 \times 10^{-11} \times 3}=2.7 \times 10^{14} \mathrm{MeV}
$$

\section{3}

A light beam is propagating through a block of glass with index of refraction $n$. If the glass is moving at constant velocity $v$ in the same direction as the beam, what is the velocity of light in the block as measured by an observer in the laboratory?

$(M I T)$

\section{Solution:}

In a reference frame fixed on the block of glass, the velocity of light in the glass is

$$
u^{\prime}=\frac{c}{n} .
$$

This frame moves with velocity $v$ relative to the laboratory frame. Hence the equation for transformation of velocity gives the velocity in the laboratory frame as

$$
u=\frac{u^{\prime}+v}{1+\frac{u^{\prime} v}{c^{2}}}=\frac{\frac{c}{n}+v}{1+\frac{(c / n) v}{c^{2}}}=\frac{c+n v}{n+\frac{v}{c}}
$$

\section{4}

This problem is to be analyzed according to the doctrines of special relativity.

An observer A at rest relative to fixed distant stars sees an isotropic distribution of stars in a finite galaxy: the number of stars seen within an element of solid angle $d \Omega$ is $P d \Omega=\frac{N}{4 \pi} d \Omega$, where $N$ is the total number of stars that A can see.

Another observer B moves uniformly along the $z$-axis relative to $\mathrm{A}$ with a large velocity $v=\beta c$. Let $\theta$ and $\varphi$ be respectively the polar (with respect to the $z$-axis) and azimuthal angles in the inertial frame of $\mathrm{B}$, and let $P^{\prime}\left(\theta^{\prime}, \varphi^{\prime}\right) d \Omega^{\prime}$ be the number of stars in the element $d \Omega^{\prime}=\sin \theta^{\prime} d \theta^{\prime} d \varphi^{\prime}$ seen by B.

(a) Compute $P^{\prime}$ as a function of $\theta^{\prime}, \varphi^{\prime}$, assuming that every star seen by $\mathrm{A}$ can be seen by $\mathrm{B}$.

(b) Also in particular discuss for the limiting case $\beta \rightarrow 1$ what $B$ sees in (1) the forward direction, (2) the backward direction.

(Princeton $)$

\section{Solution:}

(a) Consider transformation of the propagation four-vector $k^{\alpha}=\left(\mathbf{k}, \frac{\omega}{c}\right)$ of the light wave from a star, where $|\mathbf{k}| \equiv k=\frac{\omega}{c}$ :

$$
\begin{aligned}
& k^{\prime} \cos \theta^{\prime}=\gamma\left(k \cos \theta-\frac{\omega}{c} \beta\right)=\gamma k(\cos \theta-\beta) \text {, } \\
& k^{\prime}=\gamma\left(\frac{\omega}{c}-k \beta \cos \theta\right)=\gamma k(1-\beta \cos \theta) .
\end{aligned}
$$

These combine to give

$$
\cos \theta^{\prime}=\frac{\cos \theta-\beta}{1-\beta \cos \theta},
$$

or

$$
1+\beta \cos \theta^{\prime}=\frac{1-\beta^{2}}{1-\beta \cos \theta} .
$$

Differentiating we have

$$
\frac{d \cos \theta^{\prime}}{d \cos \theta}=\frac{1-\beta^{2}}{(1-\beta \cos \theta)^{2}} .
$$

In spherical coordinates

$$
d \Omega=\frac{r d \theta \cdot r \sin \theta d \varphi}{r^{2}}=-d \cos \theta \cdot d \varphi .
$$

Thus

$$
d \Omega^{\prime}=\frac{d \cos \theta^{\prime}}{d \cos \theta} d \Omega=\frac{\left(1-\beta^{2}\right)}{(1-\beta \cos \theta)^{2}} d \Omega,
$$

or, for the inverse transformation,

$$
d \Omega=\frac{\left(1-\beta^{2}\right)}{\left(1+\beta \cos \theta^{\prime}\right)^{2}} d \Omega^{\prime}
$$

As the same stars are seen by both $A$ and $B$,

$$
P^{\prime}\left(\theta^{\prime}, \varphi^{\prime}\right) d \Omega^{\prime}=P(\theta, \varphi) d \Omega,
$$

and so

$$
P^{\prime}\left(\theta^{\prime}, \varphi^{\prime}\right)=\frac{N}{4 \pi} \frac{d \Omega}{d \Omega^{\prime}}=\frac{N}{4 \pi} \frac{1-\beta^{2}}{\left(1+\beta \cos \theta^{\prime}\right)^{2}} .
$$

(b) (1) For the light from a star in the forward direction of the observer to reach him, it must propagate in the $-z$ direction. Then $\theta^{\prime}=\pi$, and

$$
P^{\prime}\left(\theta^{\prime}, \varphi^{\prime}\right)=\frac{N}{4 \pi} \frac{1-\beta^{2}}{(1-\beta)^{2}}=\frac{N}{4 \pi} \frac{1+\beta}{1-\beta} \rightarrow \infty
$$

as $\beta \rightarrow 1$

(2) For stars in the backward direction of the observer, $\theta^{\prime}=0$, and

$$
P^{\prime}\left(\theta^{\prime}, \varphi^{\prime}\right)=\frac{N}{4 \pi} \frac{1-\beta^{2}}{(1+\beta)^{2}}=\frac{N}{4 \pi} \frac{1-\beta}{1+\beta} \rightarrow 0
$$

as $\beta \rightarrow 1$

Hence the stars appear to be highly concentrated in the forward direction and none can be seen in the backward direction.

\section{5}

An observer at rest with respect to fixed distant stars observes an isotropic distribution of stars, $d N=\frac{N}{4 \pi} d \Omega$, in any solid angle $d \Omega$. Consider another observer who accelerates with a constant (relative to his instantaneous inertial frame) acceleration $a$. If he starts at rest at time $t=0$ with respect to the fixed observer, determine the distribution of stars, $d N^{\prime}=N^{\prime}\left(\theta^{\prime}, \varphi^{\prime}\right) d \Omega^{\prime}$, that he observes at time $t^{\prime}$, where $t^{\prime}$ is the time measured in his frame.

(Princeton)

\section{Solution:}

Let $\Sigma, \Sigma^{\prime}$ respectively be the frame of the observer at rest with respect to distant stars and the instantaneous rest frame of the accelerated observer, with the $x$-axis along the direction of relative motion. At $t=t^{\prime}=0$, the latter observer starts at rest with respect to the former. Consider the instant when $\Sigma^{\prime}$ moves with velocity $v$ relative to $\Sigma$.

The acceleration four-vector of a point in an inertial frame is defined as

$$
a^{\alpha}=\frac{\gamma_{0}}{c} \frac{d u^{\alpha}}{d t}
$$

with $u^{\alpha}$ being the velocity four-vector $\left(\frac{\gamma_{0}}{c} \mathbf{u}, \gamma_{0}\right)$, where $\mathbf{u}$ is the velocity of the point, $\gamma_{0}=\left(1-\frac{u^{2}}{c^{2}}\right)^{-\frac{1}{2}}$. For the accelerated observer, $\mathbf{u}^{\prime}=0$, $\gamma_{0}^{\prime}=1, \mathbf{a}^{\prime}=(a, 0,0), a^{\alpha^{\prime}}=\left(\frac{a}{c^{2}}, 0,0,0\right)$ in $\Sigma^{\prime}$, and $\mathbf{u}=(v, 0,0), \gamma_{0}=\gamma$, $a^{\alpha}=\left(\frac{\gamma}{c^{2}} \frac{d}{d t}(\gamma v), 0,0, \frac{\gamma}{c} \frac{d \gamma}{d t}\right)$ in $\Sigma$. Four-vector transformation then gives

$$
\frac{\gamma}{c^{2}} \frac{d}{d t}(\gamma v)=\gamma \frac{a}{c^{2}},
$$

or

$$
\frac{d}{d t}(\gamma v)=a
$$

giving

$$
\gamma v=a t
$$

since $a$ is constant and $v=0$ at $t=0$. It follows that

$$
v=\frac{a t}{\sqrt{1+\left(\frac{a t}{c}\right)^{2}}} \text {. }
$$

We still have to express $t$ in terms of $t^{\prime}$. Lorentz transformation gives

$$
d t=\gamma\left(d t^{\prime}+\frac{v}{c^{2}} d x^{\prime}\right)=\gamma d t^{\prime} .
$$

Hence

$$
t^{\prime}=\int_{0}^{t^{\prime}} d t^{\prime}=\int_{0}^{t} \sqrt{1-\left(\frac{v}{c}\right)^{2}} d t=\int_{0}^{t} \frac{d t}{\sqrt{1+\left(\frac{a t}{c}\right)^{2}}}=\frac{c}{a} \sinh ^{-1}\left(\frac{a t}{c}\right)
$$

or

$$
t=\frac{c}{a} \sinh \left(\frac{a t^{\prime}}{c}\right)
$$

and

$$
v=\frac{c \sinh \left(a t^{\prime} / c\right)}{\sqrt{1+\sinh ^{2}\left(a t^{\prime} / c\right)}}=\frac{c \sinh \left(a t^{\prime} / c\right)}{\cosh \left(a t^{\prime} / c\right)}=c \tanh \left(a t^{\prime} / c\right) .
$$

Using spherical coordinates with the polar axis along the $x^{\prime}$-axis and the solution of Problem 2004, we have

$$
N\left(\theta^{\prime}, \varphi^{\prime}\right)=\frac{N}{4 \pi} \frac{d \Omega}{d \Omega^{\prime}}=\frac{N}{4 \pi} \frac{1-\beta^{2}}{\left(1+\beta \cos \theta^{\prime}\right)^{2}}
$$

with $\beta=\tanh \left(\frac{a t^{\prime}}{c}\right)$, or

$$
d N^{\prime}=\frac{N}{4 \pi} \frac{\left[1-\tanh \left(\frac{a t^{\prime}}{c}\right)\right]}{\left[1+\tanh \left(\frac{a t^{\prime}}{c}\right) \cos \theta^{\prime}\right]^{2}} \sin \theta^{\prime} d \theta^{\prime} d \varphi^{\prime} .
$$

\section{6}

In flat space-time, a spherical star has total luminosity $L$. Thus, at a distance $r$ from its center, an observer at rest relative to the star sees an energy flux of $L / 4 \pi r^{2}$.

(a) Using spherical polar coordinates centered on the star, write down the energy-momentum tensor for the radiation.

(b) An observer at radius $r$ is in motion with velocity $\mathbf{v}$; $v_{\|}$is the component of $\mathbf{v}$ along the line joining the observer to the center of the star, and $v_{\perp}$ is the component perpendicular to that line. What will the observer measure the energy flux to be in terms of $L, r, v_{\|}$and $v_{\perp}$ ?

(c) A photon of frequency $\nu_{c}$ in the rest frame of the star is emitted from its surface. What frequency will the observer in part (b) measure for this photon?

(Princeton $)$

\section{Solution:}

(a) The total luminosity of the star is

$$
L=\oint \mathbf{N} \cdot d \mathbf{S}=\int \mathbf{N} \cdot \hat{\mathbf{r}} r^{2} d \Omega=4 \pi r^{2} N,
$$

where $\mathbf{N}=\mathbf{E} \times \mathbf{H}$ is the Poynting vector, which gives the energy flux, of the electromagnetic field of the star. The above relation suggests that

$$
\mathbf{N}=N(r) \hat{\mathbf{r}},
$$

i.e., $\mathbf{N}$ is radial and independent of the angles $\theta, \varphi$ of a spherical coordinate system with origin at the center. At large distances from the source the electromagnetic waves can be considered plane. Hence we can take

$$
E=E_{\theta}, \quad H=H_{\varphi}, \quad \sqrt{\epsilon_{0}} E=\sqrt{\mu_{0}} H .
$$

The contravariant energy-momentum tensor $T^{\alpha \beta}$ has components

$$
\begin{aligned}
T^{i j} &=T^{j i}=-\epsilon_{0} E_{i} E_{j}-\mu_{0} H_{i} H_{j}+U \delta_{i j}, \\
T^{4 j} &=T^{j 4}=\frac{N_{j}}{c} \\
T^{44} &=U
\end{aligned}
$$

where $i, j=1,2,3, \mathbf{N}=\mathbf{E} \times \mathbf{H}$ is the Poynting vector and $U=\frac{1}{2}\left(\epsilon_{0} E^{2}+\right.$ $\left.\mu_{0} H^{2}\right)$ is the energy density, the two being related by $N=U c$. Thus

$$
\begin{aligned}
T^{11} &=-\epsilon_{0} E_{r}^{2}-\mu_{0} H_{r}^{2}+U=U=\frac{L}{4 \pi r^{2} c}, \\
T^{22} &=-\epsilon_{0} E_{\theta}^{2}-\mu_{0} H_{\theta}^{2}+\frac{1}{2}\left(\epsilon_{0} E_{\theta}^{2}+\mu_{0} H_{\varphi}^{2}\right) \\
&=-\frac{1}{2} \epsilon_{0} E_{\theta}^{2}+\frac{1}{2} \mu_{0} H_{\varphi}^{2}=0, \\
T^{33} &=0, \\
T^{44} &=U=\frac{L}{4 \pi r^{2} c}, \\
T^{12} &=T^{21}=-\epsilon_{0} E_{r} E_{\theta}-\mu_{0} H_{r} H_{\theta}=0, \\
T^{13} &=T^{31}=T^{23}=T^{32}=0, \\
T^{41} &=T^{14}=\frac{N_{r}}{c}=\frac{L}{4 \pi r^{2} c}, \\
T^{42} &=T^{24}=\frac{N_{\theta}}{c}=0, \\
T^{43} &=T^{34}=\frac{N_{\varphi}}{c}=0,
\end{aligned}
$$

since $E_{r}=E_{\varphi}=H_{r}=H_{\theta}=0, N_{\theta}=N_{\varphi}=0$.

(b) Decompose $\mathbf{E}, \mathbf{B}$ into components parallel and perpendicular to the velocity $\mathbf{v}$ of $\Sigma^{\prime}$ relative to $\Sigma$ : 

$$
\mathbf{E}=\mathbf{E}_{\|}+\mathbf{E}_{\perp}, \quad \mathbf{B}=\mathbf{B}_{\|}+\mathbf{B}_{\perp} .
$$

Lorentz transformation gives

$$
\begin{gathered}
\mathbf{E}_{\|}^{\prime}=\mathbf{E}_{\|}, \quad \mathbf{B}_{\|}^{\prime}=\mathbf{B}_{\|}, \\
\mathbf{E}_{\perp}^{\prime}=\gamma\left(\mathbf{E}_{\perp}+\mathbf{v} \times \mathbf{B}_{\perp}\right), \quad \mathbf{B}_{\perp}^{\prime}=\gamma\left(\mathbf{B}_{\perp}-\frac{\mathbf{v} \times \mathbf{E}_{\perp}}{c^{2}}\right) .
\end{gathered}
$$

These can be combined as follows:

$$
\begin{aligned}
\mathbf{E}^{\prime} &=\gamma\left[\left(\mathbf{E}-\mathbf{E}_{\|}\right)+\mathbf{v} \times\left(\mathbf{B}-\mathbf{B}_{\|}\right)\right]+\mathbf{E}_{\|} \\
&=\gamma(\mathbf{E}+\mathbf{v} \times \mathbf{B})-(\gamma-1) \mathbf{E}_{\|}-\gamma \mathbf{v} \times \mathbf{B}_{\|} \\
&=\gamma(\mathbf{E}+\mathbf{v} \times \mathbf{B})-(\gamma-1)\left(\frac{\mathbf{E} \cdot \mathbf{v}}{v^{2}}\right) \mathbf{v}, \\
\mathbf{B}^{\prime} &=\gamma\left(\mathbf{B}-\frac{\mathbf{v} \times \mathbf{E}}{c^{2}}\right)-(\gamma-1)\left(\frac{\mathbf{B} \cdot \mathbf{v}}{v^{2}}\right) \mathbf{v},
\end{aligned}
$$

as $\mathbf{v} \times \mathbf{B}_{\|}=\mathbf{0}, \mathbf{v} \times \mathbf{E}_{\|}=0$.

The quantity $\mathbf{E} \cdot \mathbf{B}$ is invariant under Lorentz transformation. In $\Sigma$ the waves are plane so that $\mathbf{E} \cdot \mathbf{B}=0$. Then $\mathbf{E}^{\prime} \cdot \mathbf{B}^{\prime}=0$, i.e., the waves are plane in all other inertial frames. Hence

$$
\mu_{0}^{2} N^{2}=(\mathbf{E} \times \mathbf{B})^{2}=E^{2} B^{2}-(\mathbf{E} \cdot \mathbf{B})^{2}=E^{2} B^{2} .
$$

Consider

$$
\begin{aligned}
E^{2}=& \gamma^{2}\left[E^{2}+(\mathbf{v} \times \mathbf{B})^{2}+2 \mathbf{E} \cdot \mathbf{v} \times \mathbf{B}\right]+(\gamma-1)^{2}\left(\frac{\mathbf{E} \cdot \mathbf{v}}{v^{2}}\right)^{2} v^{2} \\
&-2 \gamma(\gamma-1)\left(\frac{\mathbf{E} \cdot \mathbf{v}}{v^{2}}\right) \mathbf{v} \cdot(\mathbf{E}+\mathbf{v} \times \mathbf{B}) \\
=& \gamma^{2}\left[E^{2}+v^{2} B^{2}-(\mathbf{v} \cdot \mathbf{B})^{2}-2 v_{\|} E B-\left(\frac{\mathbf{v} \cdot \mathbf{E}}{c}\right)^{2}\right] \\
=& \gamma^{2}\left[E^{2}-\frac{2 v_{\|} E^{2}}{c}+\frac{v_{\|}^{2} E^{2}}{c^{2}}+\left(\frac{v_{\perp} E}{c}\right)^{2}-\left(\frac{\mathbf{v}_{\perp} \cdot \mathbf{E}}{c}\right)^{2}-\left(\mathbf{v}_{\perp} \cdot \mathbf{B}\right)^{2}\right] \\
=& \gamma^{2}\left[E^{2}\left(1-\frac{v_{\|}}{c}\right)^{2}+\left(\frac{\mathbf{v}_{\perp} \times \mathbf{E}}{c}\right)^{2}-\left(v_{\perp} \cdot \mathbf{B}\right)^{2}\right] .
\end{aligned}
$$

Using 

$$
\begin{gathered}
(\mathbf{v} \times \mathbf{B})^{2}=v^{2} B^{2}-(\mathbf{v} \cdot \mathbf{B})^{2}, \\
\mathbf{E} \cdot \mathbf{v} \times \mathbf{B}=-\mathbf{v} \cdot \mathbf{E} \times \mathbf{B}=-v_{\|}|\mathbf{E} \times \mathbf{B}|=-v_{\|} E B \\
\mathbf{v} \cdot \mathbf{v} \times \mathbf{B}=0 \\
\mathbf{v} \cdot \mathbf{E}=\left(v_{\perp}+v_{\|}\right) \cdot \mathbf{E}=\mathbf{v}_{\perp} \cdot \mathbf{E} \\
\mathbf{v} \cdot \mathbf{B}=\mathbf{v}_{\perp} \cdot \mathbf{B} \\
v^{2}=v_{\|}^{2}+v_{\perp}^{2}, \quad \gamma^{2}-1=\frac{\gamma^{2} v^{2}}{c^{2}} .
\end{gathered}
$$

As $\mathbf{E}, \mathbf{B}, \hat{\mathbf{r}}$ are mutually perpendicular and $E=c B$, we have

$$
\left(\mathbf{v}_{\perp} \cdot \mathbf{B}\right)^{2}=\left(\frac{\mathbf{v}_{\perp} \cdot \mathbf{E} \times \hat{\mathbf{r}}}{c}\right)^{2}=\left(\frac{\mathbf{v}_{\perp} \times \mathbf{E} \cdot \hat{\mathbf{r}}}{c}\right)^{2}=\left(\frac{\mathbf{v}_{\perp} \times \mathbf{E}}{c}\right)^{2} .
$$

Hence

$$
E^{2}=\gamma^{2}\left(1-\frac{v_{\|}}{c}\right)^{2} E^{2},
$$

and similarly

$$
B^{\prime 2}=\gamma^{2}\left(1-\frac{v_{\|}}{c}\right)^{2} B^{2} .
$$

Therefore

$$
N^{\prime 2}=\frac{E^{2} B^{\prime 2}}{\mu_{0}^{2}}=\gamma^{4}\left(1-\frac{v_{\|}}{c}\right)^{4} \frac{E^{2} B^{2}}{\mu_{0}^{2}},
$$

or

$$
N^{\prime}=\left(1-\frac{v_{\|}^{2}+v_{\perp}^{2}}{c^{2}}\right)^{-1}\left(1-\frac{v_{\|}}{c}\right)^{2} \frac{L}{4 \pi r^{2}} .
$$

(c) The last component of the propagation four-vector $k^{\alpha}=\left(\mathbf{k}, \frac{\omega}{c}\right)$, where $k=\frac{\omega}{c}$, transforms according to

$$
\frac{\omega^{\prime}}{c}=\gamma\left(\frac{\omega}{c}-\frac{v}{c} k \cos \theta\right)
$$

or

$$
\omega^{\prime}=\omega \gamma\left(1-\frac{v_{\|}}{c}\right) .
$$

If a photon is emitted from the surface of the star with frequency $\nu_{0}$, the observed frequency is thus

$$
\nu^{\prime}=\nu_{0}\left(1-\frac{v_{\perp}^{2}+v_{\|}^{2}}{c^{2}}\right)^{-\frac{1}{2}}\left(1-\frac{v_{\|}}{c}\right) .
$$

However $\nu_{0}$ is not the true frequency of the photon because of gravitational shift given by

$$
\nu_{0}=\nu_{c}\left(1-\frac{2 G M}{r c^{2}}\right)^{\frac{1}{2}},
$$

where $M$ is the mass and $r$ the radius of the star and $G$ is the gravitational constant. Hence

$$
\nu^{\prime}=\nu_{c}\left(1-\frac{2 G M}{r c^{2}}\right)^{\frac{1}{2}}\left(1-\frac{v_{\perp}^{2}+v_{\|}^{2}}{c^{2}}\right)^{-\frac{1}{2}}\left(1-\frac{v_{\|}}{c}\right) .
$$

\section{7}

Consider the collision of $n$ bodies in a Lorentz frame $S$. The bodies are small enough to be adequately characterized by their rest masses and velocities, and they collide simultaneously at the same point. Assume the velocities and rest masses to be $\mathbf{v}_{\alpha}$ and $m_{\alpha}(\alpha=1, \ldots, n)$ before collision, and $\mathbf{v}_{\alpha}^{\prime}$ and $m_{\alpha}^{\prime}(\alpha=1, \ldots, n)$ after collision. (All $m_{\alpha}$ and $m_{\alpha}^{\prime}$ are positive.)

For given $\mathbf{v}_{\alpha}$ and $m_{\alpha}$ call a collision maximally inelastic in $S$ if after the collision the sum of kinetic energies of all bodies has the smallest value compatible with energy and momentum conservation. (The kinetic energy of a body is $m_{\alpha} c^{2}\left[\left(1-\frac{v_{\alpha}^{2}}{c^{2}}\right)^{-1 / 2}-1\right]$.)

(a) Find $v_{\alpha}^{\prime}$ and $m_{\alpha}^{\prime}$ - to the extent that they are determinate - for a maximally inelastic collision.

(b) Is the notion of a maximally inelastic collision Lorentz invariant? Explain.

(Princeton)

\section{Solution:}

(a) The total kinetic energy after the collision is

$$
T=\sum\left(m_{\alpha}^{\prime} \gamma_{\alpha}^{\prime} c^{2}-m_{\alpha}^{\prime} c^{2}\right)
$$

with $\gamma_{\alpha}^{\prime}=\left(1-\frac{v_{\alpha}^{\prime}}{c^{2}}\right)^{-\frac{1}{2}}$. As there is no external force acting on the system, energy is conserved:

$$
\sum m_{\alpha}^{\prime} \gamma_{\alpha}^{\prime} c^{2}=\sum m_{\alpha} \gamma_{\alpha} c^{2}
$$

Thus

$$
T=\sum\left(m_{\alpha} \gamma_{\alpha} c^{2}-m_{\alpha}^{\prime} c^{2}\right) .
$$

For $T$ to be minimum, we require $\sum m_{\alpha}^{\prime}$ to be a maximum compatible with conservation of energy and momentum.

Consider the collision in the center of mass frame $S_{\mathrm{cm}}$, which is given by its velocity relative to $S$ :

$$
\mathbf{u}=\frac{\sum m_{\alpha} \gamma_{\alpha} \mathbf{v}_{\alpha}}{\sum m_{\alpha} \gamma_{\alpha}} .
$$

Denoting all quantities in this frame by a bar we have

$$
\begin{aligned}
\bar{T} &=\sum\left(m_{\alpha} \bar{\gamma}_{\alpha}^{\prime} c^{2}-m_{\alpha}^{\prime} c^{2}\right) \\
&=\sum\left(m_{\alpha} \bar{\gamma}_{\alpha} c^{2}-m_{\alpha}^{\prime} c^{2}\right) .
\end{aligned}
$$

As $\bar{T} \geq 0, \sum m_{\alpha}^{\prime} \leq \sum m_{\alpha} \bar{\gamma}_{\alpha}$. Hence $\sum m_{\alpha}^{\prime}$ has the maximum value $\sum m_{\alpha} \bar{\gamma}_{\alpha}$

For a system the quantity $E^{2}-P^{2} c^{2}$ is Lorentz invariant. Thus

$$
\left(\sum m_{\alpha} \gamma_{\alpha} c^{2}\right)^{2}-\left(\sum m_{\alpha} \gamma_{\alpha} \mathbf{v}_{\alpha}\right)^{2} c^{2}=\left(\sum m_{\alpha} \bar{\gamma}_{\alpha} c^{2}\right)^{2}
$$

since $\bar{P}=0$ in $S_{\mathrm{cm}}$, i.e.,

$$
\left(\sum m_{\alpha} \gamma_{\alpha}\right)^{2}-\left(\sum m_{\alpha} \gamma_{\alpha}\right)^{2} \frac{u^{2}}{c^{2}}=\left(\sum m_{\alpha} \bar{\gamma}_{\alpha}\right)^{2}
$$

or

$$
\sum m_{\alpha} \bar{\gamma}_{\alpha}=\left(1-\frac{u^{2}}{c^{2}}\right)^{\frac{1}{2}} \sum m_{\alpha} \gamma_{\alpha} .
$$

Therefore $\sum m_{\alpha}^{\prime}$ has the maximum value

$$
\left(1-\frac{u^{2}}{c^{2}}\right)^{\frac{1}{2}} \sum m_{\alpha} \gamma_{\alpha}
$$

which determines $\sum m_{\alpha}^{\prime}$ for a maximally inelastic collision. Momentum conservation

$$
\sum m_{\alpha}^{\prime} \gamma_{\alpha}^{\prime} \mathbf{v}_{\alpha}^{\prime}=\sum m_{\alpha} \gamma_{\alpha} \mathbf{v}_{\alpha}=\mathbf{u} \sum m_{\alpha} \gamma_{\alpha}
$$

determines the collective velocity $\mathbf{u}$, which is the same before and after the collision.

(b) The notion of a maximally inelastic collision is Lorentz invariant as a Lorentz transformation has been used to transform to the center of mass frame and this does not affect the internal energy arising from the relative motion of the particles of the system.

\subsection{GENERAL RELATIVITY (2008-2023)}

\section{8}

(a) What is the form of the "global extension" of Newton's first law of motion in general relativity?

Identify all terms in the generalized equation of motion.

(b) Show the correspondence between the equation of motion in (a) and Newton's law of gravity in a linear approximation to first order in $v / c$.

The following expression may be useful in answering part (b):

$$
\Gamma_{\mu \nu}^{\rho}=\frac{1}{2} g^{\rho \lambda}\left(\partial_{\nu} g_{\lambda \mu}+\partial_{\mu} g_{\lambda \nu}-\partial_{\lambda} g_{\mu \nu}\right)
$$

(SUNY, Buffalo)

\section{Solution:}

(a) In special relativity, a particle subject to no force will have a constant four-velocity. That is, $\frac{d u^{\alpha}}{d \tau}=0$. This is exactly Newton's first law or law of inertia. In the general covariant form this becomes $\frac{D u^{\alpha}}{D \tau}=0$, or

$$
\frac{d u^{\alpha}}{d \tau}+\Gamma_{\mu \nu}^{\alpha} \frac{d x^{\mu}}{d \tau} \frac{d x^{\nu}}{d \tau}=0 .
$$

On the left-hand side, the first term denotes the four-acceleration of the particle, the second term represents the action of a gravitational field of intensity $\Gamma_{\mu \nu}^{\alpha}$.

(b) In the absence of gravitational field, the space is flat or Euclidean. Use rectangular Cartesian coordinates and let

$$
x^{0}=c t, \quad x^{1}=x, \quad x^{2}=y, \quad x^{3}=z .
$$

The space-time line element is

$$
d s^{2}=\eta_{\mu \nu} d x^{\mu} d x^{\nu}
$$

with $\eta_{\mu \nu}=-\delta_{\mu \nu}$, except $\eta_{00}=1$.

When gravitational field is present, the space is curved or Riemannian with line element

$$
d s^{2}=g_{\mu \nu} d x^{\mu} d x^{\nu},
$$

where $g_{\mu \nu}$ is symmetric and diagonal when rectangular Cartesian coordinates are used. If the field is weak,

$$
g_{\mu \nu}=\eta_{\mu \nu}+h_{\mu \nu},
$$

where $h_{\mu \nu} \ll 1$ are functions of the space coordinates; For small velocities $v \ll c$

$$
\begin{aligned}
\left(\frac{d s}{d \tau}\right)^{2} &=\left(\eta_{\mu \nu}+h_{\mu \nu}\right) \frac{d x^{\mu}}{d \tau} \frac{d x^{\nu}}{d \tau} \\
& \approx\left(\frac{d x^{0}}{d \tau}\right)^{2}-\left(\frac{d x^{j}}{d t}\right)^{2}\left(\frac{d t}{d \tau}\right)^{2} \\
&=\left(c^{2}-v^{2}\right)\left(\frac{d t}{d \tau}\right)^{2} \approx c^{2}\left(\frac{d t}{d \tau}\right)^{2}
\end{aligned}
$$

or

$$
d s \approx c d t,
$$

and

$$
\frac{d x^{j}}{d s} \simeq \frac{1}{c} \frac{d x^{j}}{d t} \approx 0, \quad \frac{d x^{0}}{d s} \approx 1, \quad(j=1,2,3) .
$$

The motion of the particle is given by the geodesic equation

$$
\frac{d^{2} x^{\alpha}}{d s^{2}}+\Gamma_{\mu \nu}^{\alpha} \frac{d x^{\mu}}{d s} \frac{d x^{\nu}}{d s}=0 .
$$

For a weak field and low velocities this gives approximately

$$
\begin{aligned}
\frac{d^{2} x^{\alpha}}{d s^{2}} & \approx-\Gamma_{00}^{\alpha}\left(\frac{d x^{0}}{d s}\right)^{2} \\
& \approx-\Gamma_{00}^{\alpha} \\
&=-\frac{1}{2} g^{\alpha \lambda}\left(\frac{\partial g_{\lambda 0}}{\partial x^{0}}+\frac{\partial g_{0 \lambda}}{\partial x^{0}}-\frac{\partial g_{00}}{\partial x^{\lambda}}\right) \\
&=\frac{1}{2} g^{\alpha \alpha}\left(2 \frac{\partial g_{\alpha 0}}{\partial x^{0}}-\frac{\partial g_{00}}{\partial x^{\alpha}}\right)
\end{aligned}
$$

As $g^{\alpha \alpha}=\frac{1}{g_{\alpha \alpha}}$ for diagonal $g_{\mu \nu}$, we have for $i=1,2,3$

$$
\begin{aligned}
\frac{1}{c^{2}} \frac{d^{2} x^{i}}{d t^{2}} &=\frac{1}{2\left(1+h_{i i}\right)} \frac{\partial g_{00}}{\partial x^{i}} \\
& \approx \frac{1}{2}\left(1-h_{i i}\right) \frac{\partial h_{00}}{\partial x^{i}},
\end{aligned}
$$

or

$$
\frac{d^{2} x^{i}}{d t^{2}} \approx \frac{c^{2}}{2} \frac{\partial h_{00}}{\partial x^{i}},
$$

retaining only terms of first order in $h_{\mu \nu}$ and their derivatives. In vector form this is

$$
\frac{d^{2} \mathbf{r}}{d t^{2}} \approx \frac{c^{2}}{2} \nabla h_{00},
$$

which is the same as Newton's equation

$$
\frac{d^{2} \mathbf{r}}{d t^{2}}=-\nabla \Phi
$$

if we set

$$
h_{00} \approx-\frac{2 \Phi}{c^{2}}+\text { constant }
$$

\section{9}

(a) An atom, whose center of mass motion is influenced by gravitational forces only, is orbiting at a distance $R$ from a star of mass $M$. According to a certain principle of physics, the spectral lines of the atom - as measured by an observer in orbit with the atom - are (to very high accuracy) the same as they would be in the absence of the star. State and explain this principle.

(b) Suppose that, instead, the atom is at rest on the surface of the star (radius $R_{0}$ ). The spectral lines are observed by an observer orbiting the star at a great distance $R$. What frequencies will be observed in this case (relative to the frequencies that would be observed in the absence of the gravitational field)?

(c) Assume again that the atom is orbiting the star at a distance $R$. Actually, because the size of the atom is not zero, the spectral lines as measured by a co-orbiting observer are shifted slightly relative to what they would be in the absence of the gravitational field. Explain why the fact that the atom does not have zero size is relevant, and estimate roughly the magnitude of this shift in terms of the size $a_{0}$ of the atom and other natural constants.

(d) Suppose now that the star is a black hole and that the atom is on an orbit that will lead to its being captured by the black hole. Eventually, as it approaches the singularity at the center of the hole, the atom will be torn apart and ionized. You may assume that this occurs when the energy shift you estimated in part (c) equals the binding energy of the atom, $E_{0}$. Estimate roughly, in terms of the mass of the black hole and the other parameters that have been given, at what radius from the center of the hole the atom will be ionized.
