where $\mu$ is the reduced mass of the orbiting electron. For the positronium $\mu=\frac{1}{2} m_{\mathrm{e}}$, for hydron atom $\mu \approx m_{\mathrm{e}}$. Hence

$$
f=\frac{E_{\mathrm{p}}}{E_{\mathrm{h}}}=\frac{\frac{1}{2} m_{\mathrm{e}}}{m_{\mathrm{e}}}=\frac{1}{2} .
$$

The correct answer is (b).

(4) The scale height of the atmosphere (change in altitude to cause a pressure drop by a factor of $e$ ) is
a) $4 \mathrm{~km}$,
b) $8 \mathrm{~km}$,
c) $12 \mathrm{~km}$,
d) $16 \mathrm{~km}$,
e) $20 \mathrm{~km}$.

\section{Solution:}

If we consider air near the Earth's surface as an isothermal layer, the pressure change with altitude is given by $p=p_{0} e^{-m g z / k_{\mathrm{B}} T}$. The scale height $z$ of the atmosphere is then given by $\frac{m g z}{k_{B} T}=1$. Thus

$$
z=\frac{k_{\mathrm{B}} T}{m g}=\frac{1.38 \times 10^{-23} \times 300}{\frac{29 \times 10^{-3}}{6.02 \times 10^{23}} \times 9.8}=8.6 \times 10^{3} \mathrm{~m}=8.6 \mathrm{~km} \text {. }
$$

Hence the correct answer is (b).

(5) A system contains 4 non-identical spin 1/2 particles. Disregard any other degrees of freedom. In classifying the wave function in terms of total spin, what statistical weight should be assigned to states with a total spin $=1 ?$
a) 0
b) 3
c) 6
d) 9
e) 12 .

\section{Solution:}

Coupling two non-identical spin- $\frac{1}{2}$ particles results in 2 states of total spins $S$ equal to

$$
\frac{1}{2}+\frac{1}{2}=1, \quad \frac{1}{2}-\frac{1}{2}=0 .
$$

Adding one spin- $\frac{1}{2}$ particle to the above states gives 3 states of total spins $S$ equal to

$$
1+\frac{1}{2}=\frac{3}{2}, \quad 1-\frac{1}{2}=\frac{1}{2} ; \quad 0+\frac{1}{2}=\frac{1}{2} .
$$

Adding another spin- $\frac{1}{2}$ particle to the above states gives 6 states of total spin $S$ equal to

$$
\begin{array}{lll}
\frac{3}{2}+\frac{1}{2}=2, & \frac{3}{2}-\frac{1}{2}=1 ; & \frac{1}{2}+\frac{1}{2}=1, \\
\frac{1}{2}-\frac{1}{2}=0 ; & \frac{1}{2}+\frac{1}{2}=1, & \frac{1}{2}-\frac{1}{2}=0 .
\end{array}
$$

Thus the coupling of four particles has total-spin eigenfunction

$$
2|0\rangle+3|1\rangle+|2\rangle,
$$

and the statistical weight of states with total spin $S=1$ (in terms of probability) is $3 \times 3=9$. Hence the correct answer is (d).

(6) The mean free path of a $5 \mathrm{eV}$ neutron in vacuum is closest to:
a) $1 \mathrm{~km}$,
b) $10 \mathrm{~km}$,
c) $100 \mathrm{~km}$,
d) $1000 \mathrm{~km}$,
e) $10,000 \mathrm{~km}$.

\section{Solution:}

The mean free path in vacuum is the distance the neutron travels in its lifetime, from generation to decay. Lifetime of the neutron is about $10^{3} \mathrm{~s}$. As its energy $5 \mathrm{eV}$ is much less than its rest energy $940 \mathrm{MeV}$, nonrelativistic approximation may be used and its velocity is

$$
v=\sqrt{\frac{2 E}{m}}=c \sqrt{\frac{2 E}{m c^{2}}}=\sqrt{\frac{2 \times 5 \times 10^{-6}}{940}} \times 3 \times 10^{8}=10^{4} \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{s}
$$

Thus $S=v t=10^{4} \mathrm{~km}$. Hence the correct answer is (e).

(7) The Bohr radius of an atom of nuclear charge $Z$ is of order
a) $\frac{\hbar}{Z \alpha m c}$,
b) $\frac{Z \alpha \hbar}{m c}$,
c) $\frac{m c}{Z \alpha \hbar}$.

Note $\alpha$ is the fine structure constant $e^{2} / \hbar c$. 

\section{Solution:}

The Bohr radius for a hydrogen-like atom of nuclear charge $Z$ and one electron outside complete shells is $\frac{\hbar^{2}}{m e^{2} Z}=\frac{\hbar}{Z \alpha m c}$. Thus the correct answer is (a).

(8) The binding energy of the deuteron is of order
a) $10^{6} \mathrm{eV}$
b) $10^{8} \mathrm{eV}$
c) $10^{10} \mathrm{eV}$.

\section{Solution:}

The uncertainty principle gives $2 p r \sim \hbar$, where $p$ is the momentum of each nucleon and $r$ is their separation, or

$$
p \sim \frac{\hbar}{2 r} .
$$

The binding energy is then

$$
\begin{aligned}
\frac{p^{2}}{2 m} \sim \frac{\hbar^{2} c^{2}}{8 r^{2} m c^{2}} &=\frac{\left(6.6 \times 10^{-16} \times 3 \times 10^{10}\right)^{2}}{8 \times\left(1.4 \times 10^{-13}\right)^{2} \times 940 \times 10^{6}} \\
&=2.7 \times 10^{6} \mathrm{eV} .
\end{aligned}
$$

Hence the correct answer is (a).

(9) A piece of organic material contains $N_{\mathrm{e}}$ electrons per gram. $N_{\mathrm{e}}$ is about
a) $3 \times 10^{22}$,
b) $3 \times 10^{23}$
c) $3 \times 10^{24}$
d) $3 \times 10^{25}$
e) $3 \times 10^{26}$.

\section{Solution:}

One mole of a substance contains $6.02 \times 10^{23}$ molecules or atoms. The molecule of organic matter consists of carbon and hydrogen atoms for which the number of electrons is equal to the atomic number. As the molecular weight of organic matter is generally greater than 13 , the number of electrons per gram is

$$
N_{\mathrm{e}} \leq \frac{6.02 \times 10^{23}}{13}=4.6 \times 10^{22} .
$$

Hence the correct answer is (a).

(10) The wavelength of a neutron with energy $1 \mathrm{eV}$ is closest to:
a) $1 \mathrm{~cm}$,
b) $10^{-2} \mathrm{~cm}$
c) $10^{-4} \mathrm{~cm}$
d) $10^{-6} \mathrm{~cm}$,
e) $10^{-8} \mathrm{~cm}$. 

\section{Solution:}

The wavelength $\lambda$ of a particle of momentum $p$ is

$$
\lambda=\frac{h}{p}=\frac{h}{\sqrt{2 m E}}=\frac{h c}{\sqrt{2 m c^{2} E}}=\frac{4.14 \times 10^{-15} \times 3 \times 10^{8}}{\sqrt{2 \times 940 \times 10^{6}}}=2.9 \times 10^{-11} \mathrm{~m} .
$$

Hence the correct answer is (e).

\subsection{MEASUREMENTS, ESTIMATIONS AND ERRORS (3026-3048)}

\section{6}

(a) An observer weighs a body repeatedly with the following results:

$$
10.1, \quad 10.6, \quad 10.2, \quad 10.5, \quad 10.3, \quad 10.5, \quad 10.4,10.6 \text { grams. }
$$

The standard deviation of a single measurement (from the average of a very large number of measurements) is

The standard deviation of a mean of the measurements (from the average of a very large number of measurements) is

If we call this last quantity $\bar{\sigma}$, the probability that the true value of the mass is within $2 \bar{\sigma}$ of the mean value obtained is about . (Make whatever assumptions you consider reasonable)

(b) The mean result of measurements of the mass of the body by observer $A$ is $10.7 \pm 0.1$ grams; by observer $B$ is $10.1 \pm 0.2$ grams.

The weighted average of these measurements is

The standard deviation of the average based upon the quoted errors is

The standard deviation of the average based upon the disagreement between the two determinations is

What does the difference between the two standard deviations indicate about the measurements? (Make whatever assumptions you consider reasonable) 

\section{Solution:}

(a) The mean of the eight measurements is

$$
\begin{aligned}
\bar{m} &=\frac{1}{8}(10.1+10.6+10.2+10.5+10.3+10.5+10.4+10.6) \\
&=10.4 \mathrm{~g} .
\end{aligned}
$$

As

$$
\begin{aligned}
\sigma^{2} &=\frac{1}{8} \sum_{i=1}^{8}\left(m_{i}-\bar{m}\right)^{2} \\
&=\frac{1}{8}\left(0.3^{2}+0.2^{2}+0.2^{2}+0.1^{2}+0.1^{2}+0.1^{2}+0.2^{2}\right) \\
&=0.03 \mathrm{~g}^{2},
\end{aligned}
$$

the standard deviation of a single measurement is

$$
\sigma=0.17 \mathrm{~g},
$$

and the standard deviation of the mean of the measurements is

$$
\bar{\sigma}=\frac{\sigma}{\sqrt{N}}=\frac{0.03}{\sqrt{8}}=0.061 \mathrm{~g} .
$$

If we assume that the measured values, which are continuous, obey the normal distribution, then the mean has distribution function

$$
p(\bar{m})=\frac{1}{(2 \pi)^{1 / 2} \bar{\sigma}} e^{-\left(\bar{m}-m_{0}\right)^{2} / 2 \bar{\sigma}^{2}},
$$

where $m_{0}$ is the true value of the mass. The probability that $\bar{m}$ is within $2 \bar{\sigma}$ of $m_{0}$ is

$$
\int_{m_{0}-2 \bar{\sigma}}^{m_{0}+2 \bar{\sigma}} p(\bar{m}) d \bar{m},
$$

which is the area under the normal curve for $m_{0}=0, \bar{\sigma}=1$, between $x=-2$ and $x=+2$. This is given by standard table as $2 \times 0.4773 \simeq 95 \%$.

(b) The weight of a measurement is inversely proportional to the square of its error. Thus the weighted mean of the two measurements is

$$
\left(\frac{10.7}{0.1^{2}}+\frac{10.1}{0.2^{2}}\right) /\left(\frac{1}{0.1^{2}}+\frac{1}{0.2^{2}}\right)=10.58
$$

and the standard deviation of the weighted average based upon the quoted errors is

$$
\bar{\sigma}=\left(\frac{0.1}{0.1^{2}}+\frac{0.2}{0.2^{2}}\right) /\left(\frac{1}{0.1^{2}}+\frac{1}{0.2^{2}}\right)=0.12 \text {. }
$$

The standard deviation of the average based upon the disagreement between the two determinations is, as

$$
\begin{aligned}
\bar{\sigma}^{\prime 2} &=\frac{1}{2}\left[(10.7-10.58)^{2}+(10.1-10.58)^{2}\right]=0.122, \\
\bar{\sigma}^{\prime} &=0.35 .
\end{aligned}
$$

The disagreement between $\bar{\sigma}$ and $\bar{\sigma}^{\prime}$ indicates that the measurements by the two observers probably involve certain systematic errors.

\section{7}

To within a factor of ten, estimate, calculate roughly, or remember the following (say or show how you got your answer):

(a) The de Broglie wavelength of the Earth

(b) The total binding energy of an Fe nucleus

(c) The magnetic dipole moment of the Earth

(d) The vapor pressure of water at $20^{\circ} \mathrm{C}$

(e) The average speed of a conduction electron in a bulk sample of sodium metal

\section{Solution:}

(a) The Earth revolves around the Sun once a year and its orbit has a mean radius $150 \times 10^{6} \mathrm{~km}$. Hence its average orbital speed is

$$
v=\frac{2 \pi \times 150 \times 10^{9}}{365 \times 24 \times 3600}=3 \times 10^{4} \mathrm{~m} \mathrm{~s}^{-1} \text {. }
$$

As the Earth has mass $6 \times 10^{24} \mathrm{~kg}$, its average momentum is

$$
p=m v=1.8 \times 10^{29} \mathrm{~kg} \mathrm{~m} \mathrm{~s}^{-1} \text {, }
$$

and so its de Broglie wavelength is

if the Sun can be considered stationary.

$$
\lambda=\frac{h}{p}=\frac{6.63 \times 10^{-34}}{1.8 \times 10^{29}}=3.7 \times 10^{-63} \mathrm{~m},
$$

(c) The mean binding energy per nucleon for middle-range elements is about $8.8 \mathrm{MeV}$. Hence the binding energy of ${ }^{56} \mathrm{Fe}$ is

$$
56 \times 8.8=493 \mathrm{MeV} .
$$

(c) Consider the Earth as a magnetic dipole of moment $M$. The horizontal component of the magnetic field at distance $r$ from the geomagnetic axis is

$$
B=\frac{M}{r^{3}} \cos \lambda,
$$

where $\lambda$ is the geomagnetic latitude. At $\lambda=0^{\circ}, B \approx 0.3$ gauss. As $r \approx 6400 \mathrm{~km}$, we have

$$
\begin{aligned}
M & \simeq 0.3 \times\left(6.4 \times 10^{8}\right)^{3} \\
&=8 \times 10^{25} \text { gauss } \mathrm{cm}^{3} .
\end{aligned}
$$

(d) For rough estimation, consider water vapor as an ideal gas. The Clapeyron equation

$$
\frac{d p}{d T}=\frac{L}{T\left(v_{\beta}-v_{\alpha}\right)} \approx \frac{L}{T v},
$$

where $p$ is the vapor pressure , $T$ the temperature, $L$ the molar latent heat in energy units, and $v, v_{l}$ are the specific volumes of the water in the vapor and liquid phases $\left(v \gg v_{l}\right)$ respectively, and the ideal gas equation

$$
p v=R T
$$

give

$$
\frac{d p}{d T}=\frac{L p}{R T^{2}}
$$

or

$$
p=p_{0} e^{-L / R T},
$$

i.e.

$$
\ln \left(\frac{p}{p_{0}}\right)=\frac{L}{R}\left(\frac{1}{T_{0}}-\frac{1}{T}\right),
$$

assuming $L$ to be independent of $T$.

At the triple point of water, $p_{0}=4.581 \mathrm{mmHg}, T_{0}=273.16 \mathrm{~K}, L=$ $590 \mathrm{cal} / \mathrm{g}=590 \times 4.186 \times 18 \mathrm{~J} / \mathrm{mol}, R=8.31 \mathrm{~J} / \mathrm{mol} \cdot \mathrm{K}$.

Thus

$$
\frac{L}{R}\left(\frac{1}{273}-\frac{1}{293}\right)=1.34
$$

and

$$
p=p_{0} e^{1.34}=17.45 \mathrm{mmHg} .
$$

(e) The conduction electrons in a metal at absolute temperature $T$ fill up states to within $\sim k_{\mathrm{B}} T$ of the Fermi energy $E_{\mathrm{F}}(0)$ at absolute temperature zero given by

$$
E_{\mathrm{F}}(0)=\frac{\hbar^{2}}{2 m}\left(3 \pi^{2} n\right)^{\frac{2}{3}},
$$

where $n$ is the number of conduction electrons per unit volume of the metal. For sodium metal, $n=2.5 \times 10^{28} \mathrm{~m}^{-3}$, and

$$
\begin{aligned}
E_{\mathrm{F}}(0) &=\frac{\hbar^{2} c^{2}}{2 m c^{2}}\left(3 \pi^{2} n\right)^{\frac{2}{3}} \\
&=\frac{\left(6.58 \times 10^{-16} \times 3 \times 10^{8}\right)^{2}}{2 \times 0.51 \times 10^{6}} \times\left(3 \pi^{2} \times 2.5 \times 10^{28}\right)^{\frac{2}{3}} \\
&=3.13 \mathrm{eV} .
\end{aligned}
$$

At room temperature, $T \approx 300 \mathrm{~K}$ and we have

$$
k_{\mathrm{B}} T \approx \frac{1}{40} \ll 3.13,
$$

which means that we can treat the problem as if the temperature were $0 \mathrm{~K}$.

At thermal equilibrium, the number of electrons with energies between $E$ and $E+d E$ is

$$
d n \propto f(E) E^{\frac{1}{2}} d E,
$$

where $f(E)$ is the Fermi-Dirac distribution function. At $T=0 \mathrm{~K}, f(E)=1$ for $E \leq E_{\mathrm{F}}(0)$ and $f(E)=0$ for $E>E_{\mathrm{F}}(0)$. As $E=\frac{p^{2}}{2 m}$, the average momentum is

$$
\bar{p}=\frac{\int_{0}^{E_{\mathrm{F}}(0)} p E^{\frac{1}{2}} d E}{\int_{0}^{E_{\mathrm{F}}(0)} E^{\frac{1}{2}} d E}=\frac{\int_{0}^{p_{\mathrm{F}}} p^{3} d p}{\int_{0}^{p_{\mathrm{F}}} p^{2} d p}=\frac{3}{4} p_{\mathrm{F}},
$$

where $p_{\mathrm{F}}=\sqrt{2 m E_{\mathrm{F}}(0)}$. The average speed is then

$$
\begin{aligned}
\bar{v} &=\frac{\bar{p}}{m}=\frac{3 \sqrt{2 m E_{\mathrm{F}}(0)}}{4 m}=\frac{3 c}{2} \sqrt{\frac{E_{\mathrm{F}}(0)}{2 m c^{2}}} \\
&=\frac{3}{2} \sqrt{\frac{3.13}{2 \times 0.51 \times 10^{6}}} \times 3 \times 10^{8}=7.9 \times 10^{5} \mathrm{~m} \mathrm{~s}^{-1} .
\end{aligned}
$$

Note that since

$$
\bar{v}=\frac{3}{2} \sqrt{\frac{E_{\mathrm{F}}(0)}{2 m}}=\frac{3 \hbar}{4 m}\left(3 \pi^{2} n\right)^{\frac{1}{3}}
$$

the average speed is determined solely by the electron concentration $n$.

\section{8}

On a long space voyage, it may be desirable to simulate gravity by rotating the spaceship. Suggest the size and rotation period so that gravity is simulated without Coriolis effects being annoying.

\section{Solution:}

In a frame $\sum^{\prime}$ attached to the rotating spaceship we have

$$
m \mathbf{a}^{\prime}=\mathbf{F}-m \dot{\boldsymbol{\omega}} \times \mathbf{r}+m \omega^{2} \mathbf{r}-2 m \boldsymbol{\omega} \times \mathbf{v},
$$

where $\mathbf{F}=0, \dot{\boldsymbol{\omega}}=0$, assuming the spaceship to be far away from the Earth and any other celestial object and to rotate at a constant rate, and $\mathbf{r}$ is the distance of the point under consideration from the center of rotation, or

$$
\mathbf{a}^{\prime}=\omega^{2} \mathbf{r}-2 \boldsymbol{\omega} \times \mathbf{v}^{\prime} .
$$

The first term on the right is the centrifugal force that provides the simulated gravity and the second term is the Coriolis force. It is required that

$$
\left|\omega^{2} \mathbf{r}\right| \gg 2\left|\boldsymbol{\omega} \times \mathbf{v}^{\prime}\right|, \quad \text { or } \quad \omega^{2} r \gg 2 \omega v^{\prime},
$$

and

$$
a^{\prime}=g \approx \omega^{2} r .
$$

Combining these conditions we have for $v^{\prime} \approx 5 \mathrm{~m} \mathrm{~s}^{-1}$

$$
\omega \approx \sqrt{\frac{10}{r}} \ll 1 \mathrm{~s}^{-1},
$$

as $g \approx 10 \mathrm{~m} \mathrm{~s}^{-2}$. It is reasonable to take the size of the spaceship to be $r \approx 250 \mathrm{~m}$. Then $\omega \approx 0.2 \mathrm{~s}^{-1}$, corresponding to a period of rotation of $10 \pi$ seconds. 

\section{9}

In the Thomson-Lorentz model of an atom, an electron is harmonically bound to a force center, i.e., it is a (3-dimensional) harmonic oscillator of natural frequency $\omega_{0}$. (Short answers, as indicated by the blanks which follow, are sufficient, but you may expand on them if you wish)

(a) Classical statistical mechanics (CSM) states that the mean energy (kinetic plus potential) of each oscillator is in thermal equilibrium.

(b) Quantum statistical mechanics (QSM) states that the mean energy is

(c) For an assembly of oscillators, the specific heat is (CSM) or (QSM). CSM is valid when

(d) A static electric field $\mathbf{E}$ is applied, producing a mean dipole moment $\mathbf{P}=\alpha \mathbf{E}$. Classical mechanics $(\mathrm{CM})$ predicts that $\alpha=$ Quantum mechanics (QM) predicts that $\alpha=$ in the ground state and $\alpha=$ at thermal equilibrium.

\section{Solution:}

(a) The energy of an oscillator can be expressed as $\varepsilon=\frac{p^{2}}{2 m}+\frac{1}{2} \omega^{2} q^{2}$, where there are six squared terms in all. The law of equipartition of energy of classical statistical mechanics gives for each degree of freedom an average energy $\frac{1}{2} k T, k$ being Boltzmann's constant, at thermal equilibrium. Hence the mean energy of an oscillator is $\bar{\varepsilon}_{\mathrm{CSM}}=3 k T$.

(b) The oscillator can be considered a boson and the problem treated, in principle, by using Bose-Einstein statistics. However, this is rather complicated and we shall assume, without loss of generality, that the motion of the oscillator is localized. Then the oscillators are distinguishable, and we may apply the quantum Boltzmann statistics. The partition function for each dimension of the oscillator is

$$
Z=\sum_{n=0}^{\infty} e^{-\beta\left(n+\frac{1}{2}\right) \hbar \omega_{0}}=e^{-\frac{1}{2} \beta \hbar \omega_{0}} /\left(1-e^{-\beta \hbar \omega_{0}}\right),
$$

where $\beta=\frac{1}{k T}$. Hence the average energy is

$$
\bar{\varepsilon}_{\mathrm{QSM}}=-3 \frac{\partial}{\partial \beta} \ln Z=\frac{3 \hbar \omega_{0}}{2}+\frac{3 \hbar \omega_{0}}{e^{\beta \hbar \omega_{0}}-1},
$$

where $\frac{3 \hbar \omega_{0}}{2}$ is the zero point energy of the oscillator. (c) The specific heat per oscillator is

$$
C_{\mathrm{CSM}}=\frac{\partial \bar{\varepsilon}_{\mathrm{CSM}}}{\partial T}=3 k
$$

or

$$
C_{\mathrm{QSM}}=\frac{\partial \bar{\varepsilon}_{\mathrm{QSM}}}{\partial T}=\frac{3 k\left(\beta \hbar \omega_{0}\right)^{2} e^{\beta \hbar \omega_{0}}}{\left(e^{\beta \hbar \omega_{0}}-1\right)^{2}} .
$$

Note that when $\beta \hbar \omega_{0} \ll 1$, i.e., $k T \gg \hbar \omega_{0}, C_{\mathrm{QSM}} \rightarrow 3 k=C_{\mathrm{CSM}}$. In other words, classical statistical mechanics is valid at high temperatures.

(d) For the CM case, two methods will be used to find $\alpha$.

(1) The restoring force on the electron is $-m \omega_{0}^{2} \mathbf{r}$, where $\mathbf{r}$ is the displacement from equilibrium position. Then

$$
-m \omega_{0}^{2} \mathbf{r}-e \mathbf{E}=0,
$$

or

$$
\mathbf{r}=-\frac{e \mathbf{E}}{m \omega_{0}^{2}} .
$$

\section{By definition}

$$
\mathbf{P}=-e \mathbf{r}=\alpha \mathbf{E} .
$$

Hence

$$
\alpha=\frac{e^{2}}{m \omega_{0}^{2}} .
$$

The negative sign indicates that $\mathbf{P}$ is opposite in direction to $\mathbf{E}$.

(2) The second method is based on classical statistical mechanics. Take the $z$-axis along the direction of $\mathbf{E}$. As $E=-\frac{\partial V}{\partial z}, V=-E z$ and the total energy of the oscillator is

$$
\begin{aligned}
\varepsilon &=\frac{p^{2}}{2 m}+\frac{m \omega_{0}^{2}}{2} z^{2}+e E z \\
&=\frac{p^{2}}{2 m}+\frac{m \omega_{0}^{2}}{2}\left(z+\frac{e E}{m \omega_{0}^{2}}\right)^{2}-\frac{e^{2} E^{2}}{2 m \omega_{0}^{2}}
\end{aligned}
$$

By definition $P=-e z$, and Boltzmann statistics gives 

$$
\begin{aligned}
\bar{P} &=\frac{\int(-e z) e^{-\varepsilon / k T} d^{3} \mathbf{p} d^{3} \mathbf{r}}{\int e^{-\frac{\varepsilon}{k T}} d^{3} \mathbf{p} d^{3} \mathbf{r}} \\
&=\frac{\int_{-\infty}^{\infty}(-e z) e^{-m \omega_{0}^{2}\left(z+\frac{e E}{m \omega_{0}^{2}}\right)^{2} / 2 k T} d z}{\int_{-\infty}^{\infty} e^{-m \omega_{0}^{2}\left(z+\frac{e E}{m \omega_{0}^{2}}\right)^{2} / 2 k T} d z} \\
&=\frac{\int_{-\infty}^{\infty}\left(-e z^{\prime}+\frac{e^{2} E}{m \omega_{0}^{2}}\right) e^{-\frac{m \omega_{0}^{2}}{2 k T} z^{\prime 2}} d z^{\prime}}{\int_{-\infty}^{\infty} e^{-\frac{m \omega_{0}^{2}}{2 k T} z^{\prime 2}} d z^{\prime}}=\frac{e^{2} E}{m \omega_{0}^{2}},
\end{aligned}
$$

where we have set $z^{\prime}=z+\frac{e E}{m \omega_{0}^{2}}$. Hence

$$
\alpha=\frac{\bar{P}}{E}=e^{2} / m \omega_{0}^{2} \text {. }
$$

For QM calculations, consider the electron in its ground state. As the ground state is nondegenerate, we use the nondegenerate perturbation method to find the wave function to the first order:

$$
|\psi\rangle=|0\rangle+\sum_{k} \frac{\left\langle k\left|H^{\prime}\right| 0\right\rangle}{\varepsilon_{0}^{(0)}-\varepsilon_{k}^{(0)}}|k\rangle,
$$

where

$$
\begin{aligned}
\hat{H}^{\prime} &=e E z \\
\hat{H}_{0} &=-\frac{\hbar^{2}}{2 m} \nabla^{2}+\frac{1}{2} m \omega_{0}^{2} z^{2}, \quad \varepsilon_{n}^{0}=\left(n+\frac{3}{2}\right) \hbar \omega_{0}, \\
|n\rangle & \equiv\left|n_{x}\right\rangle\left|n_{y}\right\rangle\left|n_{z}\right\rangle, \quad|0\rangle \equiv|0\rangle|0\rangle|0\rangle .
\end{aligned}
$$

As

$$
\begin{aligned}
\hat{H}^{\prime}|0\rangle &=e E|0\rangle|0\rangle \sqrt{\frac{\hbar}{m \omega_{0}}} \quad\left(\sqrt{\frac{1}{2}}|1\rangle\right) \\
&=e E \sqrt{\frac{\hbar}{2 m \omega_{0}}}|0\rangle|0\rangle|1\rangle,
\end{aligned}
$$

we have

$$
\begin{aligned}
|\psi\rangle &=|0\rangle+\frac{e E \sqrt{\frac{\hbar}{2 m \omega_{0}}}}{\varepsilon_{0}^{(0)}-\varepsilon_{1}^{(0)}}|0\rangle|0\rangle|1\rangle \\
&=|0\rangle-\frac{e E}{\hbar \omega_{0}} \sqrt{\frac{\hbar}{2 m \omega_{0}}}|0\rangle|0\rangle|1\rangle,
\end{aligned}
$$

and

$$
P=\langle\psi|-e z| \psi\rangle=2 \frac{e^{2} E}{\hbar \omega_{0}} \sqrt{\frac{\hbar}{2 m \omega_{0}}} \cdot \sqrt{\frac{\hbar}{2 m \omega_{0}}}=\frac{e^{2}}{m \omega_{0}^{2}} E .
$$

Hence $\alpha=e^{2} / m \omega_{0}^{2}$, identical with that from classical mechanics.

Actually this problem is amenable to precise calculation. Even though the precise wave function is not identical with the approximate one given by the perturbation method, $\alpha$ has the same value in both cases. Precise calculation gives the energy levels

$$
\varepsilon_{n}=\left(n+\frac{3}{2}\right) \hbar \omega_{0}-\frac{e^{2} E^{2}}{2 m \omega_{0}}
$$

and eigenfunctions

$$
\psi_{n}^{\prime}(\mathbf{r})=\psi_{n}\left(\mathbf{r}^{\prime}\right),
$$

where $\psi_{n}(\mathbf{r})$ are the eigenfunctions in the absence of the applied electric field and

$$
\mathbf{r}^{\prime}=\mathbf{r}+\frac{e \mathbf{E}}{m \omega_{0}^{2}} .
$$

The average displacement of the electron in any state is

$$
\begin{aligned}
\langle\psi|\mathbf{r}| \psi\rangle &=\left\langle\psi\left|\mathbf{r}^{\prime}-\frac{e E}{m \omega_{0}^{2}}\right| \psi\right\rangle \\
&=-\frac{e E}{m \omega_{0}^{2}}+\left\langle\psi\left|\mathbf{r}^{\prime}\right| \psi\right\rangle=-\frac{e E}{m \omega_{0}^{2}} .
\end{aligned}
$$

Boltzmann statistics then gives

$$
\bar{z}=\frac{\sum_{n=0}^{\infty}-\frac{e E}{m \omega_{0}^{2}} e^{-E_{n} / k T}}{\sum_{n=0}^{\infty} e^{-E_{n} / k T}}=-\frac{e E}{m \omega_{0}^{2}},
$$

whence

$$
\begin{aligned}
&P=-e \bar{z}=\frac{e^{2}}{m \omega_{0}^{2}} E, \\
&\alpha=e^{2} / m \omega_{0}^{2} .
\end{aligned}
$$

Note that the four results obtained are all the same.

\section{0}

1. A beam of electrons is fired in free space over a distance of $10^{4} \mathrm{~km}$. If the transversal width of the initial packet is $1 \mu \mathrm{m}$, what will be the minimum spread upon arrival if the kinetic energy of the electrons is $13.6 \mathrm{eV}$ ?

2. For surface tension waves in shallow water, the relation between frequency and wavelength is

$$
\nu=\left(\frac{2 \pi T}{\rho \lambda^{3}}\right)^{1 / 2},
$$

where $T$ is the surface tension and $\rho$ the density. What is the group velocity of these waves?

3. The resistive force on a very-high-velocity object of area $A$ passing through a gas of density $\rho$ at a velocity $v$ is

$$
\text { Force } \sim A^{r} \rho^{s} v^{t} .
$$

\section{Estimate $r, s, t$.}

4. Which of the following reactions are allowed? If forbidden, state the reason.
(a) $\pi^{-}+\mathrm{P} \rightarrow \mathrm{K}^{-}+\Sigma^{+}$
(b) $\mathrm{d}+\mathrm{d} \rightarrow \mathrm{He}^{4}+\pi^{0}$
(c) $\mathrm{K}^{-}+\mathrm{P} \rightarrow \Xi^{-}+\mathrm{K}^{+}$

What is the ratio of reaction cross sections

$$
\frac{\sigma\left(\mathrm{P}+\mathrm{P} \rightarrow \pi^{+}+\mathrm{d}\right)}{\sigma\left(\mathrm{n}+\mathrm{p} \rightarrow \pi^{0}+\mathrm{d}\right)}
$$

at the same center-of-mass energy?

(5) If the average number of counts in a second from a radioactive source is 4 , what is the probability of recording 8 counts in one second? 

\section{Solution:}

1. The initial wave packet of an electron in the beam has uncertainties $\Delta p_{x}, \Delta x$ in a transverse direction related by the uncertainty relation

$$
\Delta p_{x} \cdot \Delta x \geq \frac{\hbar}{2} .
$$

If it has momentum $p$, the angle of divergence is

$$
\theta=\frac{\Delta p_{x}}{p} \gtrsim \frac{1}{2 \Delta x} \frac{\hbar}{p}=\frac{1}{2 \Delta x} \frac{\hbar c}{p c} .
$$

As

$$
\begin{gathered}
p=\sqrt{2 m T}=\frac{1}{c} \sqrt{2 m c^{2} T}=\frac{1}{c} \sqrt{2 \times 0.51 \times 10^{6} \times 13.6}=3.72 \times 10^{3} \mathrm{eV} / \mathrm{c}, \\
\Delta x \approx 10^{-6} \mathrm{~m},
\end{gathered}
$$

we have

$$
\theta \gtrsim \frac{6.6 \times 10^{-16} \times 3 \times 10^{8}}{2 \times 10^{-6} \times 3.72 \times 10^{3}}=2.66 \times 10^{-5} \mathrm{radian}
$$

and the minimum transverse spread is

$$
L \theta_{\min }=266 \mathrm{~m} .
$$

2. The group velocity is by definition

$$
v_{\mathrm{g}}=\frac{d \omega}{d k}=\frac{d \nu}{d\left(\frac{1}{\lambda}\right)}=\frac{3}{2} \sqrt{\frac{2 \pi T}{\rho \lambda}} .
$$

(3) The dimensions of the quantities are

$$
[F]=\mathrm{MLT}^{-2}, \quad[A]=\mathrm{L}^{2}, \quad[\rho]=\mathrm{ML}^{-3}, \quad[v]=\mathrm{LT}^{-1} .
$$

The equation must be valid for the dimensions also:

$$
[F]=[A]^{r}[\rho]^{s}[v]^{t},
$$

or

$$
\mathrm{MLT}^{-2}=\mathrm{L}^{2 r} \mathrm{M}^{s} \mathrm{~L}^{-3 s} \mathrm{~L}^{t} \mathrm{~T}^{-t}=\mathrm{L}^{2 r+t-3 s} \mathrm{M}^{s} \mathrm{~T}^{-t} .
$$

Equating the powers of each dimension gives

$$
2 r+t-3 s=1, \quad s=1, \quad t=2,
$$

i.e.,

$$
r=1, \quad s=1, \quad t=2 .
$$

(4) To find out if the following reactions are forbidden by the strong force, we first consider the isospin $I$ and its third component $I_{3}$ which must both be conserved.

(a)

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_67926f4524ee79171339g-34.jpg?height=164&width=847&top_left_y=701&top_left_x=631)

As $\Delta I_{3}=\frac{1}{2}-\left(-\frac{1}{2}\right)=1 \neq 0$, the reaction is forbidden.

(b)

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_67926f4524ee79171339g-34.jpg?height=164&width=798&top_left_y=975&top_left_x=658)

As $\Delta I=1-0=1$, the reaction is forbidden.

(c)

$$
\begin{aligned}
& \mathrm{K}^{-}+\mathrm{p} \rightarrow \Xi^{-}+\mathrm{K}^{+} \\
& I_{3}:-\frac{1}{2} \quad \frac{1}{2} \quad \frac{1}{2} \quad \frac{1}{2}
\end{aligned}
$$

As $\Delta I_{3}=1-0=1$, the reaction is forbidden.

Since all the reactions are already forbidden it is not necessary to consider other quantum numbers.

To estimate

$$
\frac{\sigma_{\mathrm{a}}}{\sigma_{\mathrm{b}}} \equiv \frac{\sigma\left(\mathrm{p}+\mathrm{p} \rightarrow \pi^{+}+\mathrm{d}\right)}{\sigma\left(\mathrm{n}+\mathrm{p} \rightarrow \pi^{0}+\mathrm{d}\right)},
$$

we note that, as $m_{n} \approx m_{p}, m_{\pi^{+}} \approx m_{\pi^{0}}$, the two reactions have identical center-of-mass dynamics so that the ratio is independent of energy and momentum. Since no strange particles are involved we consider the isospin states $\left|I I_{3}\right\rangle$ of the initial and final systems.

With

$$
\begin{array}{llllllll}
\mathrm{a}: & \mathrm{p} & + & \mathrm{p} & \rightarrow & \pi^{+} & + & \mathrm{d} \\
I: & \frac{1}{2} & & \frac{1}{2} & & 1 & & 0 \\
I_{3}: & \frac{1}{2} & & \frac{1}{2} & & 1 & & 0 \\
\mathrm{~b}: & \mathrm{n} & + & \mathrm{p} & \rightarrow & \pi^{0} & + & \mathrm{d} \\
I: & \frac{1}{2} & & \frac{1}{2} & & 1 & & 0 \\
I_{3}: & -\frac{1}{2} & & \frac{1}{2} & & 0 & & 0
\end{array}
$$

the initial states are

$$
\begin{aligned}
\text { for } a,(p+p): &|11\rangle \\
\text { for } b,(n+p): & \text { both }|10\rangle \text { and }|00\rangle \text { are possible, so the } \\
& \text { state is } \frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}(|10\rangle+|00\rangle)
\end{aligned}
$$

the final states are

$$
\begin{aligned}
& \text { for } \mathrm{a},\left(\pi^{+}+\mathrm{d}\right):|11\rangle \text {, } \\
& \text { for } b,\left(\pi^{0}+d\right) \text { : }|10\rangle \text {. }
\end{aligned}
$$

Since $I=1$ for the final states, only the $I=1$ combination in the initial states can contribute. Thus the scattering amplitudes are in the ratio

$$
A_{\mathrm{a}}: A_{\mathrm{b}}=1: \frac{1}{\sqrt{2}},
$$

and the cross sections are in the ratio

$$
\sigma_{\mathrm{a}}: \sigma_{\mathrm{b}}=A_{\mathrm{a}}^{2}: A_{\mathrm{b}}^{2}=1: \frac{1}{2} .
$$

Hence

$$
\frac{\sigma_{\mathrm{a}}}{\sigma_{\mathrm{b}}}=2 .
$$

(5) The number of counts follows the Poisson distribution:

$$
P(n, \lambda)=\frac{\lambda^{n} e^{-\lambda}}{n !} .
$$

The mean value is

$$
\begin{aligned}
\bar{n} &=\sum_{n}^{\infty} n P=\sum_{n=0}^{\infty} \frac{n \lambda^{n} e^{-\lambda}}{n !}=\sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \frac{n \lambda^{n} e^{-\lambda}}{n !} \\
&=\lambda e^{-\lambda} \sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \frac{\lambda^{n-1}}{(n-1) !}=\lambda e^{-\lambda} \sum_{k=0}^{\infty} \frac{\lambda^{k}}{k !}=\lambda e^{-\lambda} e^{\lambda}=\lambda=4 .
\end{aligned}
$$

Therefore,

$$
P(8,4)=\frac{4^{8} e^{-4}}{8 !}=0.030
$$



\section{1}

Estimate the surface temperature of the Sun as follows:

(a) Find the most probable energy $E$ for photons emitted by a black body of temperature $T$, assuming that the intensity $I(E) \sim E^{5} e^{-E / k T}$.

(b) The Balmer lines of hydrogen (transitions to $n=2$ ) span the visible frequency range. What are their energies?

(c) If the human eye has evolved to be most sensitive to sunlight, estimate the surface temperature of the Sun.

\section{Solution:}

(a) If $n_{E}$ is the number of photons with energy $E$, then the distribution of $n_{E}$ is given by

$$
P\left(n_{E}\right) \sim \frac{I(E)}{E} \sim E^{4} e^{-E / k T} .
$$

The most probable energy $E_{m}$ of photons satisfies the equation

$$
\left.\frac{d p}{d E}\right|_{E=E_{m}}=4 E_{m}^{3} e^{-E_{m} / k T}-\frac{1}{k T} E_{m}^{4} e^{-E_{m} / k T}=0,
$$

giving

$$
E_{m}=4 k T .
$$

(b) The Balmer lines of hydrogen are emitted when electrons transit from energy levels of $n>3$ to that of $n=2$. Thus the maximum energy of the Balmer line photons is

$$
E_{\max }=E_{\infty}\left(\frac{1}{2^{2}}-\frac{1}{\infty}\right)=\frac{13.6}{4}=3.4 \mathrm{eV}
$$

and the minimum energy is

$$
E_{\min }=E_{\infty}\left(\frac{1}{2^{2}}-\frac{1}{3^{2}}\right)=1.9 \mathrm{eV}
$$

with $E_{\infty}=h c R_{\infty}=13.6 \mathrm{eV}, R_{\infty}$ being the Rydberg constant. Hence the energy of a Balmer line photon is between $1.9 \mathrm{eV}$ and $3.4 \mathrm{eV}$.

(c) If the human eye is most sensitive to sunlight and the visible light is the most probable frequency band of the Sun's light emission, and, furthermore, the visible light corresponds to the frequency range of the Balmer lines, the surface temperature of the Sun is given by

$$
E_{m}=4 k T=(1.9 \sim 3.4) \mathrm{eV},
$$

yielding

$$
T=(0.5 \sim 1) \times 10^{4} \mathrm{~K},
$$

as $1 \mathrm{eV}$ is associated with a "temperature" of $1.16 \times 10^{4} \mathrm{~K}$.

\section{2}

Explain how a breeder reactor can produce more fuel than it consumes. What are the physical reasons for the statement that breeder reactors are less safe than ordinary reactors.

\section{Solution:}

A breeder produces power due to fission by fast neutrons and in the process regenerates more fissionable material than it consumes. For example , ${ }^{232} \mathrm{Th}$ and ${ }^{238} \mathrm{U}$ are not fissionable by thermal neutrons and cannot be used as reactor fuel, but they can absorb fast neutrons produced in thermal fission of their fissionable isotopes and become the fissionable ${ }^{233} \mathrm{U}$ and ${ }^{239} \mathrm{Pu}$ after two $\beta^{-}$decays:

$$
\begin{aligned}
&{ }^{232} \mathrm{Th}+\mathrm{n} \rightarrow{ }^{233} \mathrm{Th} \stackrel{\beta^{-}}{\longrightarrow}{ }^{233} \mathrm{~Pa} \stackrel{\beta^{-}}{\longrightarrow}{ }^{233} \mathrm{U} \\
&{ }^{238} \mathrm{U}+\mathrm{n} \rightarrow{ }^{239} \mathrm{U} \stackrel{\beta^{-}}{\longrightarrow}{ }^{239} \mathrm{~Np} \stackrel{\beta^{-}}{\longrightarrow}{ }^{239} \mathrm{Pu}
\end{aligned}
$$

As each fission produces two to three fast neutrons, more fissionable isotopes can be produced than those consumed. No moderator is used in the core of a breeder as fast neutrons are needed to breed fissionable fuel. It is difficult to control the density of neutrons in the reactor and should the amount of fissionable fuel exceed the critical mass, nuclear explosion may take place. Furthermore, liquid sodium usually has to be used as the coolant in a breeder reactor and it is a less safe material to handle than coolants such as water that are used in a thermal reactor.

\section{3}

Decide whether the following statements can be correct. Explain your answer.

(a) A focused beam of $100 \mathrm{eV}$ electrons is used to probe molecular structure with a resolution of $0.1 \AA\left(10^{-11} \mathrm{~m}\right)$. (b) Two states $10^{-3} \mathrm{eV}$ apart, each with lifetime $10^{-10} \mathrm{sec}$, can be clearly resolved. The two states do not decay into each other.

(c) A new narrow-band pulsed laser system operates at $600 \mathrm{~nm}$ and produces pulses of $2 \times 10^{-10} \mathrm{sec}$ duration with a bandwidth of $10^{-3} \mathrm{~nm}$. $\left(\hbar=6.58 \times 10^{-16} \mathrm{eV}-\mathrm{sec}, m_{\mathrm{e}}=9.11 \times 10^{-31} \mathrm{~kg}, 1 \mathrm{eV}=1.6 \times 10^{-19}\right.$ joules $)$

\section{Solution:}

(a) As the de Broglie wavelength of the electrons is

$$
\begin{aligned}
\lambda &=\frac{2 \pi \hbar}{\sqrt{2 m E}}=\frac{2 \pi \times 6.58 \times 10^{-16} \times 1.6 \times 10^{-19}}{\sqrt{2 \times 9.11 \times 10^{-31} \times 100 \times 1.6 \times 10^{-19}}} \\
&=1.23 \times 10^{-10} \mathrm{~m}=1.23 \AA>0.1 \AA,
\end{aligned}
$$

this is not possible.

(b) Since the lifetime of the energy levels is $10^{-10} \mathrm{~s}$ each will have a width given by the uncertainty principle $\Delta E \cdot \Delta t \approx \frac{\hbar}{2}$ of about $3.3 \times 10^{-6} \mathrm{eV}$. As this width is much smaller than the separation $10^{-3} \mathrm{eV}$, there is no overlapping of the two energy levels and they can be resolved.

(c) The duration and width of a pulse of the laser must satisfy the uncertainty principle $\Delta t \cdot \Delta E \gtrsim \frac{\hbar}{2}$. As

$$
\begin{aligned}
\Delta E=h \Delta \nu & \approx \frac{h c \Delta \lambda}{\lambda^{2}}=\frac{2 \pi \times 3 \times 10^{8}}{600 \times 10^{-9}} \times\left(\frac{10^{-3}}{600}\right) \hbar \\
&=\frac{\pi \hbar}{6} \times 10^{10}, \\
\Delta t & \approx 2 \times 10^{-10} \mathrm{~s}, \\
\Delta E \cdot \Delta t &=\frac{\pi}{3} \hbar>\frac{\hbar}{2} .
\end{aligned}
$$

Hence the required pulse can be produced.

\section{4}

A small perfectly reflecting particle is levitated by the force of a laser beam. The particle has a mass of $10^{-3}$ gram. The laser light is reflected over a wide spread of angles $\theta$ relative to the incoming beam; take the average value of $\cos \theta$ to be 0 . What is the power (in watts) of the laser? 

\section{Solution:}

The weight of the particle $m g$ is balanced by the upward force $f$ exerted by the laser beam. Suppose the beam has power $W$ and is vertically upward. Then it carries an upward vertical momentum $\frac{W}{c}$ per unit time. On reflection through angle $\theta$, its downward vertical momentum is $-\frac{W}{c} \cos \theta$ per unit time. Hence the upward force is

$$
f=\frac{W}{c}-\left(-\frac{W}{c} \cos \theta\right)=\frac{W}{c}(1+\cos \theta)
$$

and so

$$
\frac{W}{c}(1+\overline{\cos \theta})=\frac{W}{c}=m g,
$$

giving

$$
W=m g c=10^{-6} \times 9.8 \times 3 \times 10^{8} \approx 3 \times 10^{3} \mathrm{~W} .
$$

Thus the power of the laser beam is 3 kilowatts.

\section{5}

Consider an electron in an atmospheric gas atom to be a bound harmonic oscillator with natural angular frequency $\omega_{0}$. A monochromatic plane electromagnetic wave of frequency $\omega$ and wavelength much greater than atomic dimensions is incident on the atom. Ignore damping effects.

(a) Find the dependence on $\omega$ of the total cross section for scattering by the atom when $\omega \ll \omega_{0}$.

(b) The atoms are now ionized so that all the electrons are free. Find the dependence on $\omega$ of the total cross section for scattering by the free electrons.

NOTE: In both (a) and (b) you are not required to derive a complete formula. It is sufficient to determine only the dependence upon frequency.

\section{Solution:}

(a) Under the action of the electric field $\mathbf{E}_{0} e^{-i \omega t}$ of the incident plane electromagnetic waves, the oscillator has the equation of motion

$$
\ddot{\mathbf{r}}-\tau \dddot{\mathbf{r}}+\omega_{0}^{2} \mathbf{r}=-\frac{e}{m} \mathbf{E}_{0} e^{-i \omega t},
$$

where $\tau=\frac{e^{2}}{6 \pi \epsilon_{0} m c^{3}}$ is a characteristic time arising from the reaction of the secondary radiation emitted by the oscillator. In the steady state

$$
\mathbf{r}=\mathbf{r}_{0} e^{-i \omega t} .
$$

\section{Substitution gives}

$$
\mathbf{r}=-\frac{e}{m}\left(\frac{1}{\omega_{0}^{2}-\omega^{2}-i \omega^{3} \tau}\right) \mathbf{E}_{0} e^{-i \omega t} .
$$

The oscillator radiates secondary radiation of the same frequency whose energy per unit time per unit solid angle is

$$
\frac{d P}{d \Omega}=\frac{e^{2} \sin ^{2} \theta}{16 \pi^{2} \epsilon_{0} c^{3}} \ddot{\mathbf{r}}^{2} .
$$

Macroscopically the incident waves appear to be scattered by the oscillator into various directions. The differential scattering cross section is defined as

$$
\frac{d \sigma}{d \Omega}=\frac{\text { average radiated power } / \text { unit solid angle }}{\text { incident energy/unit area/unit time }}
$$

The denominator is the magnitude of the Poyting vector $\mathbf{N}=\mathbf{E} \times \mathbf{H}$ of the incident radiation. For plane electromagnetic waves, $\sqrt{\epsilon_{0}}|\mathbf{E}|=\sqrt{\mu_{0}}|\mathbf{H}|$, $\mathbf{E} \perp \mathbf{H}$, so that

$$
N=\epsilon_{0} c|\mathbf{E}|^{2}=\frac{\epsilon_{0}}{2} c \mathbf{E}_{0}^{2},
$$

independent of frequency. Hence, considering the frequency dependence alone, we have

$$
\frac{d \sigma}{d \Omega} \propto\left|\frac{\omega^{2}}{\omega_{0}^{2}-\omega^{2}-i \tau \omega^{3}}\right|^{2}=\frac{\omega^{4}}{\left(\omega_{0}^{2}-\omega^{2}\right)^{2}+\tau^{2} \omega^{6}} .
$$

The radiation reaction term in the above is the square of

$$
\begin{aligned}
\tau \omega^{3} &=\frac{2}{3}\left(\frac{e^{2}}{4 \pi \epsilon_{0} m c^{2}}\right) \frac{\omega^{3}}{c}=\frac{2 \gamma_{0} \omega^{3}}{3 c} \\
&=\frac{2}{3} \times \frac{2.8 \times 10^{-15}}{3 \times 10^{8}} \omega^{3} \\
& \approx 6 \times 10^{-24} \omega^{3} \ll \omega^{2}
\end{aligned}
$$

if $\omega \ll 10^{23}$ s, and can usually be neglected except when $\omega \approx \omega_{0}$. Therefore, if $\omega \ll \omega_{0}$,

$$
\frac{d \sigma}{d \Omega} \propto\left(\frac{\omega}{\omega_{0}}\right)^{4}
$$

from which follows the total cross section

$$
\sigma \propto\left(\frac{\omega}{\omega_{0}}\right)^{4} .
$$

(b) The electrons are now free so that the equation of motion becomes

$$
\ddot{\mathbf{r}}-\tau \dddot{\mathbf{r}}=-\frac{e \mathbf{E}}{m} e^{-i \omega t},
$$

which has the steady state solution

$$
\mathbf{r}=\frac{e}{m} \frac{\mathbf{E}_{0} e^{-i \omega t}}{\omega^{2}+i \omega^{3} \tau} \approx \frac{e}{m \omega^{2}} \mathbf{E}_{0} e^{-i \omega t} .
$$

As

$$
\frac{d P}{d \Omega} \propto|\ddot{\mathbf{r}}|^{2}
$$

the differential cross section is

$$
\frac{d \sigma}{d \Omega} \propto\left(\frac{\omega^{2}}{\omega^{2}}\right)^{2}=\text { constant }
$$

Hence the total cross section for scattering by free electrons is independent of frequency.

\section{6}

The atmosphere shields the Earth from much of the electromagnetic radiation incident from external sources. The following processes are primarily responsible for this shielding in various regions of the electromagnetic spectrum. Give an approximate wavelength or frequency or the corresponding photon energy for each.

1. Photoelectric absorption by nitrogen K-shell electron

2. Reflection from plasma in the ionosphere

3. Photodisintegration of ozone

4. Compton scattering

5. Electron-positron pair production

6. Absorption by $\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ rotational excitations 

\section{Solution:}

1. A photon that can eject an electron from the $K$-shell of a nitrogen atom must have an energy greater than the binding energy of a nitrogen $\mathrm{K}$-shell electron, $402 \mathrm{eV}$. The corresponding angular frequency is $\omega=\frac{E}{\hbar}=$ $\frac{402}{6.6 \times 10^{-16}}=6 \times 10^{17} \mathrm{~s}^{-1}$.

2. An electromagnetic wave incident on a plasma will be reflected if its angular frequency $\omega$ is less than the plasma (angular) frequency

$$
\omega_{\mathrm{p}}=\sqrt{\frac{N e^{2}}{\epsilon_{0} m}},
$$

where $N$ is the number of free electrons per unit volume of the plasma. The maximum $N$ for ionospheric layers is about $10^{12} \mathrm{~m}^{-3}$, giving

$$
\begin{aligned}
\omega_{\mathrm{p}} &=\sqrt{\frac{4 \pi N c^{2} e^{2}}{4 \pi \epsilon_{0} m c^{2}}}=c \sqrt{4 \pi N r_{0}} \\
&=3 \times 10^{8} \sqrt{4 \pi \times 10^{12} \times 2.8 \times 10^{-15}}=5.6 \times 10^{7} \mathrm{~s}^{-1}
\end{aligned}
$$

$r_{0}=\frac{e^{2}}{4 \pi \epsilon_{0} m c^{2}}$ being the classical radius of the electron, $2.8 \times 10^{-15} \mathrm{~m}^{-3}$. Hence we require that $\omega<6 \times 10^{7} \mathrm{~s}^{-1}$.

3. Ozone, $\mathrm{O}_{3}$, is a trace constituent of the upper atmosphere, with a maximum concentration at about $25 \mathrm{~km}$ above Earth's surface. It can photodisintegrate by absorbing a photon:

$$
\mathrm{O}_{3}+h \nu \rightarrow \mathrm{O}+\mathrm{O}_{2} \text {. }
$$

This happens most strongly for radiation of wavelengths $2000-3000 \AA$, and less strongly for radiation of $4500-8000 \AA$.

4. Compton scattering is important for radiation shielding when the wavelength of the incident radiation is comparable with the Compton wavelength of the electron

$$
\lambda_{\mathrm{C}}=\frac{h}{m_{\mathrm{e}} c} \approx 0.024 \AA .
$$

The cross section for Compton scattering decreases and the process of pair production of electrons gains importance with increasing frequency. 5. Electron-positron pair production can take place only when the energy $\varepsilon$ of a photon is greater than the rest energy of the pair:

$$
\varepsilon \geq 2 m_{\mathrm{e}} c^{2}=1.02 \mathrm{MeV} .
$$

The corresponding photon (angular) frequency is $\omega=\frac{1.02 \times 10^{6}}{6.6 \times 10^{-16}}=1.5 \times$ $10^{21} \mathrm{~s}^{-1}$.

6. The molecular rotational spectrum consists of lines

$$
E_{n}=2 n B, \quad(n=0,1,2, \ldots)
$$

with $B=h^{2} / 8 \pi^{2} I, I$ being the moment of inertia of the molecule. As the principal moment of inertia of $\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ is $\sim 10^{-47} \mathrm{~J} \cdot \mathrm{s}^{2}, B \sim 10^{-21} \mathrm{~J}$. The energy of a photon causing a transition $n=0$ to $n=1$ is $\varepsilon=\Delta E_{1,0}=$ $2 B \sim 10^{-21} \mathrm{~J}$, the corresponding angular frequency being

$$
\omega=\frac{10^{-21}}{10^{-34}}=10^{13} \mathrm{~s}^{-1} .
$$

\section{7}

In preparation for a landing on the bright side of the Moon, you are asked to estimate the surface temperature. You may assume that the lunar surface material is a good insulator. Make as quantitative an estimate as you can. For your information, the solar constant is 1353 watts $/ \mathrm{m}^{2}$. What additional information would you need to improve your estimate?

\section{Solution:}

Let the absorptivity of the Moon's surface be $\alpha$. The absorption of heat and radiation from the surface are in equilibrium for a stable moon:

$$
\varepsilon \sigma T^{4}=\alpha I_{0},
$$

where $I_{0}$ is the solar constant (solar energy flux on lunar surface), $\varepsilon \sigma T^{4}$ is the radiation emitted per unit area per second by the lunar surface, $\varepsilon$ being a correction factor introduced to account for the Moon not being a black body, $\sigma$ the Stefan-Boltzmann constant, $5.67 \times 10^{-8} \mathrm{~W} / \mathrm{m}^{2}$, and $T$ the surface temperature. For a rough estimate of $T$, we take $\varepsilon \approx \alpha$. Then 

$$
T=\left(\frac{I_{0}}{\sigma}\right)^{\frac{1}{4}}=\left(\frac{1353}{5.67 \times 10^{-8}}\right)^{\frac{1}{4}}=393 \mathrm{~K}=120^{\circ} \mathrm{C} .
$$

To improve the estimate of $T$, we have to have some knowledge of the ratio $\alpha / \varepsilon$

\section{8}

Describe briefly how to measure 8 of the following quantities. Also make an order of magnitude estimate of the expected value.

(1) Vacuum in a cyclotron

(2) Magnetic field in a spectrometer magnet used for elementary particle experiments

(3) Temperature of a sample of liquid hydrogen

(4) Lifetime of the muon

(5) The Hubble constant

(6) Planck's constant

(7) Distance to nearby stars

(8) Distance to galaxies

(9) Avogadro's number

(10) Size of a nucleus

(11) Size of an atom

(12) Lamb shift

(13) Temperature of the cosmic black body radiation

(14) Nuclear g-factors

(15) Mass of a meson or baryon resonance

\section{Solution:}

(1) Vacuum in a cyclotron

Measurement of the ultra-high vacuum in a cyclotron can be made with a Bayard-Alpert gauge shown schematically in Fig. 3.3. Electrons emitted by the cathode and accelerated by a high voltage ionize the residual gas molecules by collision. The resulting positive ions are collected by the collector electrode. The cathode current $I_{\mathrm{e}}$, collector current $I_{\mathrm{i}}$ and the pressure are related by

$$
\frac{I_{\mathbf{i}}}{I_{\mathrm{e}}}=k p,
$$



![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_67926f4524ee79171339g-45.jpg?height=360&width=1111&top_left_y=384&top_left_x=626)

Fig. 3.3

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_67926f4524ee79171339g-45.jpg?height=536&width=986&top_left_y=911&top_left_x=556)

Fig. $3.4$

where $k$ is a proportional constant. By measuring the currents the pressure is obtained. It is about $10^{-8}-10^{-9}$ atmosphere in a cyclotron.

(2) Magnetic field in a spectrometer magnet

The magnetic field can be measured as follows. A probe consisting of a Hall unit is placed in the magnetic field and a known current is allowed to flow through it, producing a Hall potential $V_{\mathrm{AA}^{\prime}}$, which can be read off a millivoltmeter, as shown in Fig. 3.4. With the known Hall coefficient $K$ the formula

$$
V_{\mathrm{AA}^{\prime}}=K \frac{I B}{d}
$$

gives the magnetic induction $B$ of the field. For a spectrometer for elementary particle experiments, $B \sim 10 \mathrm{kGs}$.

(3) Temperature of liquid hydrogen

The temperature of liquid hydrogen is about $20 \mathrm{~K}$. It can be determined by means of a hydrogen vapor pressure thermometer. 

\section{(4) Lifetime of muon}

\section{A muon decays according to}

$$
\mu^{\pm} \rightarrow e^{\pm}+\nu_{\mathrm{e}}+\nu_{\mu} .
$$

As $\mu^{+}, \mu^{-}$are antiparticles of each other, they have the same mean lifetime, $\tau=2.2 \times 10^{-6} \mathrm{~s}$, which is defined as

$$
\tau=\frac{1}{\Gamma},
$$

where $\Gamma$ is the decay rate, the probability per unit time that any given muon will disintegrate. As

$$
\frac{d N}{d t}=-\Gamma N,
$$

or

$$
N(t)=N(0) e^{-\Gamma t},
$$

$\Gamma$ and hence $\tau$ can be obtained by observing the change of cosmic ray muon flux with altitude or by determining the time distribution of muon decays using a counter arrangement.

(5) The Hubble constant

Hubble's cosmological law relates the distance $R$ and receding velocity $v$ of a distant galaxy: $R=\frac{v}{H}$, where $H$ is Hubble's constant. Using $v$ inferred from the red shift of spectral lines and $R$ from other astronomical observations, $H$ can be determined. $H=100 \mathrm{~km} / \mathrm{s} / \mathrm{Mpc}$.

(6) Planck's constant

The Compton wavelength $\lambda_{\mathrm{C}}=\frac{h}{m c}$ of the electron can be determined from the Compton shift $\Delta \lambda=\lambda_{\mathrm{C}}(1-\cos \theta)$ resulting from Compton scattering. Knowing the electron mass, Planck's constant $h$ can be deduced: $h \sim 6.626 \times 10^{-34} \mathrm{~J} \cdot \mathrm{s}$.

(7) Distance to nearby star

By using the trigonometric parallax method, the distance to a nearby star within about 30 parsecs of the solar system can be determined. The semimajor axis of the Earth's orbit is used as the base line. The angle between the lines drawn from the star to each end of the base line is the angle of parallax from which the distance of the star can be deduced by a geometric calculation. 

\section{(8) Distance to galaxies}

The apparent luminosity (observed optical flux) of a star or galaxy depends on its distance. Distances to galaxies within a few megaparsecs can be measured by using cepheid variable stars, for which there is a relation between the period of light variation and the apparent luminosity, the relation being calibrated using cepheids in our own galaxy. For farther galaxies, the distance is estimated by its red shift using Hubble's relation.

(9) Avogadro's number

The amount of chemical reaction occurring at an electrode during electrolysis can be expressed in moles as $n=Q / z F$, where $Q$ is the total charge supplied, $z$ the charge of an ion and $F$ the faraday. Thus $F$ can be determined accurately by electrochemical means. Then Avogadro's number is obtained as

$$
N_{\mathrm{A}}=\frac{F}{e}=6.023 \times 10^{23} \mathrm{~mol}^{-1}
$$

using the electronic charge which can be accurately determined by Millikan's oil drop method.

(10) Size of a nucleus

In Rutherford's experiment on scattering of $\alpha$-particles by a heavy nucleus it was found that, for moderate $\alpha$-particle energies, the experimental results were consistent with Rutherford's assumption of a simple inverse-square (Coulomb) force of interaction. As the energy was increased, deviation from this assumption appeared, particularly at large angles. The deviation is associated with the fact that the $\alpha$-particle trajectory reaches closer to the nucleus at higher energies at which the inverse-square law fails. This distance at which the interaction changes character may be considered as the nuclear radius. It varies from a few times $10^{-15} \mathrm{~m}$ for light nuclei to about $10^{-14} \mathrm{~m}$ for heavy nuclei. A rough estimate is given by the closest approach of an $\alpha$-particle incident head-on on a nucleus of charge $Z e$ :

$$
T=\frac{1}{2} m v^{2}=\frac{2 Z e^{2}}{4 \pi \epsilon_{0} r},
$$

or

$$
r=\frac{2 Z e^{2}}{4 \pi \epsilon_{0} T}=\frac{2 \times 100 \times\left(1.6 \times 10^{-19}\right)^{2} \times 9 \times 10^{9}}{6 \times 10^{6} \times 1.6 \times 10^{-19}} \simeq 5 \times 10^{-14} \mathrm{~m},
$$

using $T=6 \mathrm{MeV}, Z=100$. (11) Size of an atom

Take an element whose atoms are arranged in a cubic lattice. If it has atomic weight $A$ and the density is measured to be $\rho$, the atomic radius $r$ is given by

$$
\frac{4}{3} \pi r^{3} N_{\mathrm{A}}=A / \rho,
$$

$N_{\mathrm{A}}$ being Avogadro's number, or

$$
r=\left(\frac{3 A}{4 \pi \rho N_{\mathrm{A}}}\right)^{\frac{1}{3}} \sim 10^{-10} \mathrm{~m} .
$$

\section{(12) Lamb shift}

In 1947 Lamb and Rutherford measured the energy interval between the states $E_{2,0,1 / 2}$ and $E_{2,1,1 / 2}$ of the hydrogen atom, $\Delta E=4.37516 \times 10^{-6} \mathrm{eV}$, by using a radio-frequency spectrometer. The correct theoretical expression for the energy difference was first given by Lamb and the effect is known as the Lamb shift.

(13) Temperature of cosmic black body radiation

The spectral distribution of the cosmic background radiation is measured and the results compared with the black body spectrum to find the appropriate temperature. Or the Wien displacement law is used to calculate the temperature from the wavelength $\lambda_{\max }$ corresponding to the maximum intensity. $T \approx 3 \mathrm{~K}$.

\section{(14) Nuclear g-factors}

By measuring the Larmor precession frequency of a nucleus in a magnetic field $H$,

$$
\omega=\frac{\mu H}{I \hbar}=\frac{g \mu_{N} H}{\hbar},
$$

where $\mu_{\mathrm{N}}$ is the nuclear magneton, $H$ is the applied magnetic field and $\mu=g \mu_{\mathrm{N}} I$ is the nuclear magnetic moment, the nuclear g-factor can be deduced. It can be positive or negative and its magnitude falls in the range $0.1-10$.

(15) Mass of a meson or baryon resonance

A resonance state has a lifetime of only about $10^{-23}$ to $10^{-22} \mathrm{~s}$ and its mass is deduced by measuring the energies $E_{1}, E_{2}, E_{3}, \ldots$ and momenta $\mathbf{p}_{1}, \mathbf{p}_{2}, \mathbf{p}_{3}, \ldots$ of the resonant particles and using the relativistic equation 

$$
m^{2} c^{4}=\left(E_{1}+E_{2}+E_{3}+\ldots\right)^{2}-\left(\mathbf{p}_{1}+\mathbf{p}_{2}+\mathbf{p}_{3}+\ldots\right)^{2} c^{2}
$$

For example, in

$$
\begin{aligned}
p+\pi^{-} \rightarrow n+\rho^{0} & \\
\hookrightarrow \pi^{+}+\pi^{-}
\end{aligned}
$$

the mass of $\rho^{0}$ is given by

$$
m c^{2}=\sqrt{\left(E_{1}+E_{2}\right)^{2}-\left(\mathbf{p}_{1}+\mathbf{p}_{2}\right)^{2} c^{2}} .
$$

Experimentally the interaction is observed as

$$
p+\pi^{-} \rightarrow n+\pi^{+}+\pi^{-} .
$$

If the above calculation gives a peak in the distribution of $m$, then the resonance state is confirmed and its mass determined. In this way it is found that $m_{\rho^{0}} \approx 776 \mathrm{MeV} / \mathrm{c}^{2}$.

\section{9}

A. Describe briefly how you could make as accurate as possible a measurement of 4 (four) of the following quantities. In each case, give a description of the apparatus and a careful explanation of the physical principles by which it works.

(1) The relative natural abundance of the two stable isotopes of copper.

(2) The pressure in the vacuum chamber of a cyclotron (approximately $10^{-8}$ atm.)

(3) The magnetic moment of the deuterium nucleus.

(4) The temperature of the surface of the Sun.

(5) The lifetime of the short-lived $\left(\sim 10^{-22} \mathrm{sec}\right)$ nuclear state.

B. Estimate very roughly (i.e., to within an order of magnitude) for the following. Use any method, including intuition, but credit may be given only if some method is shown.

(1) Energy released in a nuclear fission explosion in which $1 \mathrm{~kg}$ of $\mathrm{U}^{235}$ undergoes fission.

(2) Radius of the mumesic atom which is a hydrogen atom with the electron replaced by a muon.

(3) Radius of a $10 \mathrm{TeV}$ proton accelerator $\left(1 \mathrm{TeV}=10^{12} \mathrm{eV}\right)$. 

\section{Solution:}

A. (1) The relative natural abundance of an isotope is its number expressed as a percentage in a natural mixture of the element. First a sample of the naturally occurring element copper, which is a mixture of ${ }_{29}^{63} \mathrm{Cu}$ and ${ }_{29}^{65} \mathrm{Cu}$, is prepared. Then with a mass spectrometer the mass $m$ for each type of isotope is measured from the curvature of its trajectory, $R$, in a magnetic field $B$ using the relation

$$
R=\frac{m v}{q B} .
$$

Suppose the masses of the isotopes measured are $63 u$ and $65 u$, where $u$ is a constant, respectively. Measure also the mass $M$ per mole of the sample by a chemical method. Suppose the mean mass of an atom is found to be $M / N_{\mathrm{A}}=63.54 u$. Letting $x$ denote the relative abundance of ${ }_{29}^{63} \mathrm{Cu}$, and $1-x$ that of ${ }_{29}^{65} \mathrm{Cu}$, we have

$$
63 x+65(1-x)=63.54,
$$

and hence

$$
x=68.94 \%, \quad 1-x=31.06 \% \text {. }
$$

(2) See Problem 3038 (1).

(3) The total angular momentum of a nucleus is the vector sum of the spin momenta of the constituent nucleons and is known as the nuclear spin momentum.

As a nucleus carries charge its spin produces a nuclear magnetic moment $\mu=g \mu_{n} T$, where $\mu_{n}$ is the nuclear magneton and $I$ is the nuclear spin. In an applied magnetic field $H_{0}$ the nucleus will have $2 I+1$ spin energy levels. A radio-frequency alternating electromagnetic field is applied at right angles to the external magnetic field to induce transitions between the neighboring spin energy levels. Let two neighboring energy levels of the deuterium nucleus be $E_{I}$ and $E_{I+1}$. Transition between them will produce a resonance line of angular frequency

$$
\omega=\frac{E_{I}-E_{I+1}}{\hbar}=\frac{g \mu_{n} H_{0}}{\hbar}
$$

since $E_{I}=-g \mu_{n} I H_{0}$. This gives the magnetic moment of the deuterium nucleus as

$$
\mu\left({ }_{1}^{2} \mathrm{H}\right)=0.85742 \mu_{n},
$$

where the nuclear magneton $\mu_{n}$ has the value $5.051 \times 10^{-27} \mathrm{~J} / \mathrm{T}$. Experimentally, the measurements are carried out using a nuclear magnetic resonance apparatus.

(4) The solar-radiation spectrum obtained outside the Earth's atmosphere us very nearly that of a black body. Then from the wavelength found for the maximum intensity, $\lambda_{m} \approx 4900 \AA$, Wien's displacement law $\lambda_{m} T=2.898 \times 10^{7} \AA \cdot \mathrm{K}$ gives $T=5.9 \times 10^{3} \mathrm{~K}$.

(5) A nuclear state of lifetime $\sim 10^{-22} \mathrm{~s}$ is a resonance state whose existence is ascertained only by the appearance of a narrow peak in the frequency distribution of the effective mass calculated from the energies and momenta of the secondary particles presumably produced in its decay (see Problem 3038 (15)). Its lifetime is estimated using the uncertainty principle

$$
\Delta t \cdot \Delta E \gtrsim \frac{\hbar}{2},
$$

where $\Delta E=\Delta m c^{2}, \Delta m$ being the width of the peak in the effective-mass distribution.

B (1) For rough estimation assume

$$
{ }_{92}^{235} \mathrm{U}+{ }_{0}^{1} \mathrm{n} \rightarrow{ }_{92}^{236} \mathrm{U} \rightarrow \mathrm{X}+\mathrm{Y}+2{ }_{0}^{1} \mathrm{n}
$$

where $\mathrm{X}$ and $\mathrm{Y}$ are nuclei with $A=117$ each. The mean binding energy of each nucleon in a nucleus of mass number $A$ is $7.7 \mathrm{MeV}$ for $A \approx 236$ and 8.6 MeV for $A \approx 117$. So the total binding energy for ${ }^{236} \mathrm{U}$ is $236 \times 7.7=$ $1817 \mathrm{MeV}$ and that for $\mathrm{X}$ and $\mathrm{Y}$ is $2 \times 117 \times 8.6=2012 \mathrm{MeV}$, and thus about $195 \mathrm{MeV}$ of energy is released per fission. As $1 \mathrm{~kg}$ of ${ }^{235} \mathrm{U}$ contains

$$
\frac{10^{3} \times 6.02 \times 10^{23}}{235}=2.56 \times 10^{24}
$$

${ }^{235} \mathrm{U}$ atoms, their fission gives rise to

$$
2.56 \times 10^{24} \times 195=5 \times 10^{26} \mathrm{MeV}
$$

of energy.

(2) The first radius $a$ of the Bohr atom is inversely proportional to the mass $m$ of the orbiting particle: 

$$
\frac{a_{\mu}}{a_{\mathrm{e}}}=\frac{m_{\mathrm{e}}}{m_{\mu}} .
$$

As $a_{\mathrm{e}} \approx 0.5 \AA, m_{\mu}=210 m_{\mathrm{e}}$,

$$
a_{\mu}=0.5 \times \frac{1}{210} \approx 0.0023 \AA .
$$

(3) The radius $R$ of the orbit of a proton of mass $m$, charge $e$, velocity $v$, Lorentz factor $\gamma$ in a magnetic field of flux density $B$ is given by

$$
\frac{m \gamma v^{2}}{R}=e v B .
$$

Hence

$$
R=\frac{p c}{e B c},
$$

where $p$ is the momentum of the proton. Taking $B \approx 5 \mathrm{~T}$, as $p c \approx 10 \mathrm{TeV}$ $=10^{13} \mathrm{eV}=1.6 \times 10^{-6} \mathrm{~J}$, we have

$$
R=\frac{1.6 \times 10^{-6}}{1.6 \times 10^{-19} \times 5 \times 3 \times 10^{8}}=6.7 \times 10^{3} \mathrm{~m}=6.7 \mathrm{~km}
$$

\section{0}

A photomultiplier tube is to be used to detect light pulses each of which consists of a small but fixed number of photons. The average photoelectric efficiency is $10 \%$. That is, a photon has a $10 \%$ probability of causing the emission of a detectable photoelectron. Assume the photomultiplier gain is $10^{6}$ and that the output current as a function of time can be approximated as shown in Fig. 3.6.

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_67926f4524ee79171339g-52.jpg?height=309&width=894&top_left_y=2071&top_left_x=648)

Fig. 3.6 (a) $I_{\max }$, when averaged over many pulses, is $80 \mu \mathrm{A}$. What is $N$, the average number of photons in each pulse?

(b) What, in terms of $N$, is the probability that a light pulse will go undetected (i.e., that no photoelectrons are produced)?

(c) What effects are responsible for the fluctuation in the value of $I_{\max }$ ?

\section{Solution:}

(a) The total quantity of charge carried by one pulse of current is

$$
Q=\int I d t
$$

which is the area of the triangle in Fig. 3.6. Thus

$$
Q=\frac{1}{2} \times 20 \times 10^{-9} \times 80 \times 10^{-6}=8 \times 10^{-13} \mathrm{C}
$$

and the number of electrons carried by one pulse is

$$
n=\frac{Q}{e}=\frac{8 \times 10^{-13}}{1.6 \times 10^{-19}}=5 \times 10^{6} .
$$

Then the number of photoelectrons emitted per light pulse is

$$
n^{\prime}=n / 10^{6}=5,
$$

and hence the number of photons in one light pulse is

$$
N=n^{\prime} / 0.1=50 .
$$

(b) The probability that all photons of a light pulse will go undetected is

$$
\left(\frac{90}{100}\right)^{N}=(0.9)^{50}=5.15 \times 10^{-3}=0.52 \% .
$$

(c) In each pulse there is a finite probability for a certain number of photons not being detected. Thus the number of photons detected will vary from pulse to pulse. Consequently the maximum current $I_{\max }$ will fluctuate about a mean value. The greater the number of photons in a pulse, the smaller will be the fluctuation. 

\section{1}

Two parallel conducting plates of area $A$ are separated by distance $L_{0}$ (from the inner surface of one plate to the of the other). They are connected by a conducting spring of relaxed length $L_{0}$ and spring constant $k$. The whole system is neutral. The spring is relaxed. ( $L_{0}$ is small compared with the transverse dimensions of the plates) The system is now subjected to a uniform electric field $E_{0}$ normal to the plates. The equilibrium separation between the plates is $L$. Find $L$ as a function of $E_{0}, L_{0}, k$, and $A$.

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_67926f4524ee79171339g-54.jpg?height=344&width=780&top_left_y=858&top_left_x=800)

Fig. $3.7$

\section{Solution:}

As the gap between the plates is small compared with the transverse dimensions, we can consider shielding as complete and take the internal electric field as $E_{\text {in }}=0$. Apply Guass' flux theorem

$$
\oint_{S} \mathbf{E} \cdot d \mathbf{S}=\frac{Q}{\epsilon_{0}},
$$

taking for $S$ a closed surface closely fitting one plate. Then as $E_{\text {in }}=0$ and any edge effect can be neglected we have

$$
E_{0} A=\frac{Q}{\epsilon_{0}}, \quad \text { or } \quad Q=\epsilon_{0} E_{0} A,
$$

where $Q$ is the magnitude of the charges carried by the plates. The force exerted by the field on each plate is

$$
F=\int E_{0} \sigma d S=E_{0} \sigma A=E_{0}\left(\frac{Q}{A}\right) A=\epsilon_{0} E_{0}^{2} A .
$$

Let $z$ be the extension of the spring caused by this force. Hooke's law gives

$$
F=k z,
$$

or

$$
z=\frac{\epsilon_{0} E_{0}^{2} A}{k}
$$

The equilibrium separation is therefore

$$
L=L_{0}+z=L_{0}+\frac{\epsilon_{0} E_{0}^{2} A}{k} .
$$

\section{2}

The Shiva laser at Livermore delivers $10 \mathrm{~kJ}$ of $1 \mu$ wavelength radiation in $10^{-9}$ seconds onto a focal spot of $10^{-3} \mathrm{~cm}^{2}$ area.

(a) Find the power density, the peak electric field, the energy density in the electromagnetic field, and the radiation pressure, in practical MKS units.

(b) If all the energy were absorbed and converted into thermal energy uniformly distributed over a sphere whose size just matches that of the focal spot, containing $5 \times 10^{18}$ hydrogen atoms (fully ionized), estimate the pressure of this material and show that the radiation pressure of the laser light, although huge, is much too low to hold the cloud together, and therefore permits free expansion.

\section{Solution:}

(a) The mean power density is

$$
\langle S\rangle=\frac{\text { energy }}{A t}=\frac{10 \times 10^{3}}{10^{-3} \times 10^{-4} \times 10^{-9}}=10^{20} \mathrm{~J} / \mathrm{s} \cdot \mathrm{m}^{2}
$$

The electromagnetic radiation has mean energy density $\epsilon_{0}\left\langle E^{2}\right\rangle$, so

$$
\langle S\rangle=\epsilon_{0} c\left\langle E^{2}\right\rangle=\frac{\left\langle E^{2}\right\rangle}{\mu_{0} c} .
$$

Hence

$$
\sqrt{\left\langle E^{2}\right\rangle}=\sqrt{\langle S\rangle \mu_{0} c}=\sqrt{10^{20} \times 4 \pi \times 10^{-7} \times 3 \times 10^{8}}=1.94 \times 10^{11} \mathrm{~V} / \mathrm{m}
$$

and the peak value of the electric field is

$$
E_{0}=\sqrt{2\left\langle E^{2}\right\rangle}=2.75 \times 10^{11} \mathrm{~V} / \mathrm{m}
$$

The mean energy density is

$$
\langle U\rangle=\varepsilon_{0}\left\langle E^{2}\right\rangle=\frac{\langle S\rangle}{c}=3.33 \times 10^{11} \mathrm{~J} / \mathrm{m}^{3},
$$

and the radiation pressure is

$$
P=\frac{\langle U\rangle}{c} \cdot c=\langle U\rangle=3.3 \times 10^{11} \mathrm{~Pa} .
$$

(b) As all the absorbed energy is converted into the kinetic energy of the thermal motion of the hydrogen atoms, we have

$$
W=\frac{3}{2} N k T,
$$

where $N$ is the number of hydrogen atoms involved and $k$ is Boltzmann's constant. Considering the hydrogen atoms as an ideal gas, we have

$$
p v=N k T,
$$

and thus

$$
p=\frac{N k T}{v}=\frac{2 W}{3 v}=\frac{2 W}{3}\left(\frac{4}{3} \pi R^{3}\right)^{-1} .
$$

As the radius $R$ of the sphere is related to the area $A$ of the focal spot by $\pi R^{2}=A$, the above becomes

$$
p=\frac{W}{2 \pi}\left(\frac{\pi}{A}\right)^{\frac{3}{2}} .
$$

With $W=10^{4} \mathrm{~J}, A=10^{-7} \mathrm{~m}^{2}, p=2.80 \times 10^{14} \mathrm{~Pa}$. Since this pressure is much higher than the radiation pressure generated by the laser light, the latter is unable to hold the cloud of atoms together and this permits free expansion.

\section{3}

Light of unknown polarization is examined with a polaroid and found to exhibit maximum transmitted intensity $I_{\max }=I_{0}$ for vertical polarization plane and minimum intensity $I_{\min }=2 I_{0} / 3$ for horizontal polarization plane. (a) Can one, from this information, determine the intensity transmitted through the polaroid at any angle $\theta$ relative to the vertical? If so, state $I(\theta)$ in terms of $I_{0}$ and $\theta$.

(b) The light is passed through a quarter-wave plate with axes vertical and horizontal, and then through the polaroid. After the polaroid the maximum intensity is at an angle of $30^{\circ}$ to the vertical. State as much as you can about the polarization of the incident light (unpolarized fraction, locations of major and minor axes of elliptic polarization, relative intensities along major and minor axes).

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_67926f4524ee79171339g-57.jpg?height=480&width=507&top_left_y=934&top_left_x=907)

Fig. $3.8$

\section{Solution:}

(a) Suppose the incident light is a mixture of unpolarized and elliptically polarized light. An elliptically polarized light can be considered as consisting of two linearly polarized components of mutually perpendicular planes of polarization with difference amplitudes and a phase difference of $90^{\circ}$. Let the intensities of the unpolarized component, the polarized component with plane of polarization parallel to the vertical direction, and that with plane of polarization parallel to the horizontal direction be $I_{1}, I_{2}, I_{3}$ respectively. Then

$$
\begin{aligned}
&I_{1}+I_{2}=I_{0}, \\
&I_{1}+I_{3}=\frac{2 I_{0}}{3} .
\end{aligned}
$$

If the polaroid is now rotated about its axis through an angle $\theta$, as shown in Fig. 3.8, the intensity transmitted is 

$$
\begin{aligned}
I(\theta) &=I_{1}+I_{2} \cos ^{2} \theta+I_{3} \sin ^{2} \theta \\
&=\left(I_{1}+I_{2}\right) \cos ^{2} \theta+\left(I_{1}+I_{3}\right) \sin ^{2} \theta \\
&=I_{0} \cos ^{2} \theta+\frac{2}{3} I_{0} \sin ^{2} \theta \\
&=I_{0}\left(1-\frac{1}{3} \sin ^{2} \theta\right) .
\end{aligned}
$$

(b) After passing through a quarter-wave plate, the two linearly polarized components will undergo a change of phase of $-90^{\circ}$, so that the elliptically polarized light will become linearly polarized, with the plane of polarization making an angle $\theta$ with the vertical given by

$$
\tan \theta=\sqrt{\frac{E_{3}^{2}}{E_{2}^{2}}}=\sqrt{\frac{I_{3}}{I_{2}}} .
$$

With $\theta=30^{\circ}$, we have $I_{2}=3 I_{3}$. Combining the above gives

$$
I_{3}=\frac{1}{6} I_{0}, \quad I_{1}=\frac{1}{2} I_{0}, \quad I_{2}=\frac{1}{2} I_{0} .
$$

The unpolarized fraction of the light is $\frac{I_{1}}{I_{0}}=\frac{1}{2}$ As for the elliptically polarized light, the major axis is along the vertical direction, the minor axis is along the horizontal direction, and the ratio of the intensities along the major and minor axes is

$$
I_{2} / I_{3}=3 .
$$

\section{4}

An experimental result $y$ is computed from the formula $y=\frac{(a-3 b) \sqrt{x}}{g}$. The values for the right-hand side of the equation are measured to be $a \pm \Delta a$, $b \pm \Delta b, x \pm \Delta x, g \pm \Delta g$, where $\Delta a, \Delta b, \Delta x$, and $\Delta g$ are estimates for the root-mean-square values of the random and uncorrelated errors in $a, b, x$, and $g$ respectively.

Find an expression for $\Delta y / y$ in terms of $a, b, x, g, \Delta a, \Delta b, \Delta x$ and $\Delta g$, where $\Delta y$ is the r.m.s. value of the random error in $y$. 

\section{Solution:}

Taking logarithm of both sides of the equation, we have

$$
\ln y=\ln (a-3 b)+\frac{1}{2} \ln x-\ln g .
$$

\section{Differentiating we find}

$$
\frac{\delta y}{y}=\frac{\delta a-3 \delta b}{a-3 b}-\frac{1}{2} \frac{\delta x}{x}-\frac{\delta g}{g} .
$$

Considering root-mean-square deviations, the above becomes

$$
\frac{\Delta y}{y}=\left[\left(\frac{\Delta a}{a-3 b}\right)^{2}+\left(\frac{3 \Delta b}{a-3 b}\right)^{2}+\left(\frac{\Delta x}{2 x}\right)^{2}+\left(\frac{\Delta g}{g}\right)^{2}\right]^{\frac{1}{2}}
$$

\section{5}

A long, narrow (right circular) cylinder stands on its end on a flat horizontal surface in a gravitational field as shown in Fig. 3.9. The cylinder has density $\rho$, height $H$ and radius $R$. This configuration is not absolutely stable if $H / 2>R$. For given $\rho$ and $H$ use the uncertainty principle to give an order of magnitude estimate of how large $R$ must be for the cylinder to remain upright for an appreciable time.

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_67926f4524ee79171339g-59.jpg?height=510&width=376&top_left_y=1878&top_left_x=880)

Fig. $3.9$ 

\section{Solution:}

When the cylinder lies flat on the horizontal surface, it is in a stable state of gravitational potential energy

$$
E_{0}=m g R=\pi \rho g R^{3} H .
$$

When it stands on an end surface, it is in a state of gravitational potential energy

$$
E_{1}=m g \frac{H}{2}=\frac{\pi}{2} \rho g R^{2} H^{2} .
$$

The difference in energy between the two states is

$$
\Delta E=E_{1}-E_{0}=\pi \rho g R^{2} H\left(\frac{H}{2}-R\right) .
$$

If $\Delta E>0$, or $\frac{H}{2}>R$, the standing state $E_{1}$ is not absolutely stable as the cylinder can transit from this state to the lying state. The uncertainty principle

$$
\Delta E \Delta t \sim \hbar,
$$

where $\Delta t$ is the time scale of transition from standing state to lying state, gives

$$
\pi \rho g R^{2} H\left(\frac{H}{2}-R\right) \Delta t \sim \hbar .
$$

For $\Delta t$ large, $\Delta E \sim 0$, giving $R \sim \frac{H}{2}$. A better estimate is

$$
R \sim \frac{H}{2}-\frac{\hbar}{\pi \rho g R^{2} H \Delta t} \sim \frac{H}{2}-\frac{4 \hbar}{\pi \rho g H^{3} \Delta t} .
$$

\section{6}

Suppose that the ionosphere is in thermal equilibrium at temperature $T$. It consists of electrons (mass $m$ ) and singly charged ions (mass $M$ ). If there were no large-scale electric field, the electrons and ions would have very different scale heights, $k T / m g$ and $k T / M g$ respectively. This would produce a large electric field, thereby modifying the density vs. height relation.

Taking into account the electrostatic field, find

(a) the density-height relation, (b) the magnitude (and direction) of the self-consistent electric field $\mathbf{E}$,

(c) the numerical value of $E$ if the ions are protons. (You may ignore the curvature of the Earth's surface. it may be helpful to know that the electric field turns out to be uniform.)

\section{Solution:}

(a) Use a coordinate system with origin at the Earth's surface and the $z$-axis vertically upward. The self-consistent electric field $\mathbf{E}$ is along the $z$ direction:

$$
E=-\frac{\partial V}{\partial z},
$$

or

$$
V=-E z
$$

assuming $E$ to be uniform. Then an electron and an ion at height $z$ will respectively have energies

$$
\begin{aligned}
&\varepsilon_{\mathrm{e}}=m g z+e E z \\
&\varepsilon_{\mathrm{i}}=M g z-e E z
\end{aligned}
$$

and number densities

$$
\begin{aligned}
&n_{\mathrm{e}}=n_{\mathrm{e} 0} e^{-\varepsilon_{\mathrm{e}} / k T}=n_{\mathrm{e} 0} e^{-(m g+e E) z / k T}, \\
&n_{\mathrm{i}}=n_{\mathrm{i} 0} e^{-\varepsilon_{\mathrm{i}} / k T}=n_{\mathrm{i} 0} e^{-(M g-e E) z / k T}
\end{aligned}
$$

as functions of height.

(b) Since

$$
\Delta \cdot \mathbf{E}=\frac{\rho}{\epsilon_{0}}=0
$$

for a uniform field, the net charge density $\rho=0$ everywhere. This means that

$$
-e n_{\mathrm{e}}+e n_{\mathrm{i}}=0,
$$

or

$$
n_{\mathrm{e} 0}=n_{\mathrm{i} 0}, \quad m g+e E=M g-e E .
$$

The last equation gives

$$
E=\frac{(M-m) g}{2 e} \approx \frac{M g}{2 e}
$$

as $M \gg m$. (c) If the ions are protons, we have $M=1.672 \times 10^{-27} \mathrm{~kg}$, $e=$ $1.602 \times 10^{-19} \mathrm{C}$, and

$$
E=\frac{1.672 \times 10^{-27} \times 9.807}{2 \times 1.602 \times 10^{-19}}=5.12 \times 10^{-8} \mathrm{~V} \mathrm{~m}^{-2} .
$$

\section{7}

Consider a more or less ordinary type of photomultiplier that you might find in the laboratory. It has for example five stages, and the manufacturer recommends a maximum supply voltage of 700 volts. Suppose the sensitivity versus wavelength is as shown in Fig. 3.10.

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_67926f4524ee79171339g-62.jpg?height=366&width=507&top_left_y=1151&top_left_x=771)

Fig. 3.10

(a) Why is the phototube not sensitive for wavelengths less than $\lambda_{1}$ ?

(b) Why is the phototube not sensitive for wavelengths greater than $\lambda_{2}$ ?

(c) Sketch the electrical circuit you would use with this tube to observe light pulses of 20 nanosecond duration with am oscilloscope. Your sketch should include the electrical connections to the cathode, dynodes, anode, and oscilloscope.

\section{Solution:}

(a) For shorter wavelengths, or higher frequencies, because the cross section for the interaction of the electrons in the cathode of the photomultiplier with photons is very small, very few electrons are emitted. In fact, when the wavelength is smaller than a certain value $\lambda_{1}$, the number of electrons emitted is practically zero, i.e., the photomultiplier has zero sensitivity. (b) For longer wavelengths, or lower frequencies, the window of the photomultiplier strongly absorbs the incident photons. Also, if the wavelength is greater than a certain value $\lambda_{2}$, the energy $h \nu$ of the photon is too small to knock off electrons from the cathode, again there is no sensitivity.

(c) A possible circuit diagram is shown in Fig. 3.11, where $R_{L} C$ is in the range $\tau$ to $5 \tau$ with $\tau=20$ ns.

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_67926f4524ee79171339g-63.jpg?height=952&width=1309&top_left_y=749&top_left_x=381)

Fig. 3.11

\section{8}

Consider a vertical film of soapy water across a vertical wire loop. The gravitational acceleration is $g$, the fluid density is $\rho$, the surface tension is $\sigma$. Find equations for the fluid pressure and film width as functions of height in the film.

\section{Solution:}

It is easier to consider the three-dimensional case of a vertical rectangular wire loop with sides very long along the $y$-direction but short along 

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_67926f4524ee79171339g-64.jpg?height=326&width=404&top_left_y=409&top_left_x=446)

(a)

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_67926f4524ee79171339g-64.jpg?height=331&width=203&top_left_y=412&top_left_x=988)

(b)

Fig. 3.12

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_67926f4524ee79171339g-64.jpg?height=347&width=436&top_left_y=439&top_left_x=1300)

(c)

the $z$-direction as shown in Fig. 3.12(a). The shape of the film for any $x z$ cross-section, as shown in Fig. 3.12(b), can then be regarded as the same for all values of $y$.

Suppose the atmospheric pressure is $p_{0}$ and the fluid pressure near any point $Q$ on the film surface is $p$. The boundary condition along $x$-direction is then

$$
p=p_{0}-\sigma / R,
$$

$R$ being the radius of curvature of the film.

This can be briefly proved as follows. Consider a section of the film parallel to the $x z$ plane of unit thickness (along the $y$ direction). Its cross section in the $x z$ plane is bounded by two straight lines parallel to the $x$-axis and two arcs as shown in Fig. 3.12(b). Consider an element of arc AB centered at Q. As shown in Fig. 3.12(c), the resultant surface tension force is along $\mathrm{QO}^{\prime}$ with magnitude $2 \sigma \sin \theta$, and is balanced by a force due to the excess pressure on the element of arc. Thus

$$
2 R \theta\left(p-p_{0}\right)+2 \sigma \sin \theta=0 .
$$

Letting $\theta \rightarrow 0$, we have

$$
p-p_{0}=-\frac{\sigma}{R},
$$

as stated above.

Let the thickness of the film be $t=2 f(z)$. Then the curvature of the $\operatorname{arc}$ is

$$
R^{-1}=f^{\prime \prime} /\left(1+f^{\prime 2}\right)^{3 / 2}
$$

where $f^{\prime}=\frac{d f}{d z}$, and thus

$$
p=p_{0}-\sigma f^{\prime \prime} /\left(1+f^{2}\right)^{3 / 2} .
$$

But as the vertical film is in static equilibrium, the pressure satisfies the equation

$$
p(z)=p(z=0)-\rho g z .
$$

Hence

$$
p(z=0)-\rho g z=p_{0}-\sigma f^{\prime \prime} /\left(1+f^{\prime 2}\right)^{3 / 2} .
$$

Note that $p_{0}$ and $p(z=0)$ are constants and

$$
\Delta p=p_{0}-p(z=0)
$$

is also a constant, in terms of which

$$
\left(1+f^{\prime 2}\right)^{-\frac{3}{2}} \frac{d f^{\prime}}{d z}=\frac{\Delta p+\rho g z}{\sigma} .
$$

Integration yields

$$
f^{\prime} /\left(1+f^{2}\right)^{1 / 2}=\frac{1}{\sigma}\left(\Delta p z+\frac{1}{2} \rho g z^{2}\right)+C_{1},
$$

where $C_{1}$ is a constant. At $z=0$ the tangent to the arc is parallel to the $z$-axis and so $f^{\prime}=0$, giving $C_{1}=0$. Hence

$$
f^{\prime} /\left(1+f^{\prime 2}\right)^{1 / 2}=\frac{1}{\sigma}\left(\Delta p z+\frac{1}{2} \rho g z^{2}\right) \approx f^{\prime},
$$

as $f^{\prime} \ll 1$ on account of the film being very thin. Integrating again gives

$$
f(z)=\frac{1}{\sigma}\left(\frac{\Delta p}{2} z^{2}+\frac{1}{6} \rho g z^{3}\right)+C_{2} .
$$

If the film thickness at $z=0$ is $2 f_{0}, C_{2}=f_{0}$. Hence

$$
p(z)=p(z=0)-\rho g z=p_{0}-\Delta p-\rho g z
$$

and the thickness is

$$
t=2 f(z)=\frac{2}{\sigma}\left(\frac{\Delta p}{2}+\frac{1}{6} \rho g z\right) z^{2}+2 f_{0} .
$$



\subsection{MATHEMATICAL TECHNIQUES (3049-3056)}

\section{9}

Consider the inhomogeneous differential equation

$$
f^{\prime \prime}(x)+2 z f^{\prime}(x)+k^{2} f(x)=\delta\left(x-x_{0}\right)
$$

where $k$ and $z>0$ are real constants and $\delta(x)$ is the Dirac $\delta$-function. Find the general solution for $k^{2}>z^{2}$.

\section{Solution:}

We first find the general solution for the corresponding homogeneous differential equation

$$
f^{\prime \prime}(x)+2 z f^{\prime}(x)+k^{2} f(x)=0 .
$$

Let $f(x)=e^{\lambda x}$. Substitution gives

$$
\lambda^{2}+2 z \lambda+k^{2}=0,
$$

which has solutions

$$
\lambda=-z \pm i \sqrt{k^{2}-z^{2}} .
$$

Thus the general solution is

$$
f(x)=A_{1} e^{\left(-z+i \sqrt{k^{2}-z^{2}}\right) x}+A_{2} e^{\left(-z-i \sqrt{k^{2}-z^{2}}\right) x},
$$

where $A_{1}$ and $A_{2}$ are constants.

We next find a special solution of the original equation. Expressed as Fourier transforms,

$$
