\begin{aligned}
f(x) &=\frac{1}{2 \pi} \int_{-\infty}^{\infty} g(p) e^{i p\left(x-x_{0}\right)} d p, \\
\delta\left(x-x_{0}\right) &=\frac{1}{2 \pi} \int_{-\infty}^{\infty} e^{i p\left(x-x_{0}\right)} d p .
\end{aligned}
$$

Substitution in the inhomogeneous differential equation gives

$$
g(p)=\frac{1}{k^{2}+2 i z p-p^{2}} .
$$

The special solution is therefore

$$
f(x)=-\frac{1}{2 \pi} \int_{-\infty}^{\infty} \frac{e^{i p\left(x-x_{0}\right)} d p}{p^{2}-2 i z p-k^{2}} .
$$

As $p^{2}-2 i z p-k^{2}=\left(p-p_{1}\right)\left(p-p_{2}\right)$, where

$$
p_{1}=i z+\sqrt{k^{2}-z^{2}}, \quad p_{2}=i z-\sqrt{k^{2}-z^{2}},
$$

with $z>0, k^{2}>z^{2}$, there are two singularities $p_{1}, p_{2}$ in the upper half of the complex plane.

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_67926f4524ee79171339g-67.jpg?height=509&width=552&top_left_y=941&top_left_x=838)

Fig. 3.13

For $x>x_{0}$, take for the contour of integration $C$ the upper half-circle in the complex $p$-plane shown in Fig. 3.13, for which we find (cf. Problem 3050 (c))

$$
\begin{aligned}
f(x) &=\lim _{R \rightarrow \infty}\left[-\frac{1}{2 \pi} \oint_{C} \frac{e^{i p\left(x-x_{0}\right)} d p}{p^{2}-2 i z p-k^{2}}\right] \\
&=-\frac{1}{2 \pi} \cdot 2 \pi i\left[\frac{e^{i\left(x-x_{0}\right)\left(i z+\sqrt{k^{2}-z^{2}}\right)}}{2 \sqrt{k^{2}-z^{2}}}-\frac{e^{i\left(x-x_{0}\right)\left(i z-\sqrt{k^{2}-z^{2}}\right)}}{2 \sqrt{k^{2}-z^{2}}}\right] \\
&=\frac{\sin \left[\left(x-x_{0}\right) \sqrt{k^{2}-z^{2}}\right]}{\sqrt{k^{2}-z^{2}}} e^{-\left(x-x_{0}\right) z} .
\end{aligned}
$$

For $x<x_{0}$, we have to employ for the contour of integration the lower half-curve shown in Fig. 3.13, as use of the upper half-circle would give rise to a factor

$$
e^{-i(\operatorname{Re} p+i \operatorname{Im} p)\left|x-x_{0}\right|}=e^{\operatorname{Im} p\left|x-x_{0}\right|} e^{-i \operatorname{Re} p\left|x-x_{0}\right|}
$$

which goes to infinity as $R \rightarrow \infty$. However, since the lower half-circle encloses no singularity, $f(x)=0$ for $x<x_{0}$.

Hence the general solution is

$$
\begin{aligned}
f(x)=& A_{1} e^{\left(-z+i \sqrt{k^{2}-z^{2}}\right) x}+A_{2} e^{\left(-z-i \sqrt{k^{2}-z^{2}}\right) x} \\
&+\Theta\left(x-x_{0}\right) \frac{\sin \left[\left(x-x_{0}\right) \sqrt{k^{2}-z^{2}}\right]}{\sqrt{k^{2}-z^{2}}} e^{-\left(x-x_{0}\right) z},
\end{aligned}
$$

where

$$
\Theta\left(x-x_{0}\right)= \begin{cases}1 & \text { if } x>x_{0} \\ 0 & \text { if } x \leq x_{0}\end{cases}
$$

\section{0}

(a) Sum the series $y=1+2 x+3 x^{2}+4 x^{3}+\ldots$ ( $\left.|x|<1\right)$

(b) If $f(x)=x e^{-x / \lambda}$ over the interval $0<x<\infty$, find the mean and most probable values of $x$.

(c) Evaluate

$$
I=\int_{0}^{\infty} \frac{d x}{4+x^{4}} .
$$

(d) Find the eigenvalues and normalized eigenvectors of the matrix

$$
\left(\begin{array}{lll}
1 & 2 & 4 \\
2 & 3 & 0 \\
5 & 0 & 3
\end{array}\right) \text {. }
$$

Are the eigenvectors orthogonal? Comment on this.

\section{Solution:}

(a) As

$$
y=1+2 x+3 x^{2}+4 x^{3}+\ldots+n x^{n-1}+\ldots
$$

we have

$$
x y=x+2 x^{2}+3 x^{3}+4 x^{4}+\ldots+n x^{n}+\ldots
$$

and thus

$$
y-x y=1+x+x^{2}+x^{3}+\ldots+x^{n-1}+\ldots=\frac{1}{1-x}
$$

since $|x|<1$. Hence

$$
y=\frac{1}{(1-x)^{2}} .
$$

(b) The mean value of $x$ is

$$
\bar{x}=\frac{\int_{0}^{\infty} x f(x) d x}{\int_{0}^{\infty} f(x) d x}=\frac{\int_{0}^{\infty} x^{2} e^{-x / \lambda} d x}{\int_{0}^{\infty} x e^{-x / \lambda} d x}=\frac{2 \lambda \int_{0}^{\infty} x e^{-x / \lambda} d x}{\int_{0}^{\infty} x e^{-x / \lambda} d x}=2 \lambda,
$$

where integration by parts has been used for the numerator and $\lambda>0$ has been assumed . The most probable value of $x, x_{m}$, is given by

$$
\left(\frac{d t}{d x}\right)_{x_{m}}=0,
$$

whence $x_{m}=\lambda$.

Note that if $\lambda<0, f(x)$ is infinite at $\infty$ and the mean and most probable values are also infinite.

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_67926f4524ee79171339g-69.jpg?height=463&width=556&top_left_y=1357&top_left_x=817)

Fig. $3.14$

(c) Write

$$
\begin{aligned}
I &=\frac{1}{2} \int_{0}^{\infty} \frac{d x}{x^{4}+4}+\frac{1}{2} \int_{0}^{\infty} \frac{d x}{x^{4}+4} \\
&=\frac{1}{2} \int_{-\infty}^{0} \frac{d x}{x^{4}+4}+\frac{1}{2} \int_{0}^{\infty} \frac{d x}{x^{4}+4}=\frac{1}{2} \int_{\infty}^{\infty} \frac{d x}{x^{4}+4}
\end{aligned}
$$

and consider

$$
\oint_{C} \frac{d z}{z^{4}+4}
$$

where $C$ is the closed contour of Fig. $3.14$ consisting of the line from $-R$ to $R$ and the semicircle $\Gamma$, traversed in anticlockwise sense, in the complex plane. As

$$
\frac{1}{z^{4}+4}=\frac{1}{\left(z-z_{1}\right)\left(z-z_{2}\right)\left(z-z_{3}\right)\left(z-z_{4}\right)},
$$

where

$$
z_{1}=1+i, \quad z_{2}=-1+i, \quad z_{3}=-1-i, \quad z_{4}=1-i
$$

the residue theorem gives

$$
\begin{aligned}
&\oint_{C} \frac{d z}{z^{4}+4} \\
&=2 \pi i\left[\frac{1}{\left(z_{1}-z_{2}\right)\left(z_{1}-z_{3}\right)\left(z_{1}-z_{4}\right)}+\frac{1}{\left(z_{2}-z_{1}\right)\left(z_{2}-z_{3}\right)\left(z_{2}-z_{4}\right)}\right] \\
&=2 \pi i\left[\frac{1}{2(2+2 i) \cdot 2 i}-\frac{1}{2 \cdot 2 i(-2+2 i)}\right]=\frac{\pi}{4} .
\end{aligned}
$$

But

$$
\oint_{C} \frac{d z}{z^{4}+4}=\int_{-R}^{R} \frac{d x}{x^{4}+4}+\int_{\Gamma} \frac{d z}{z^{4}+4} \rightarrow \int_{-\infty}^{\infty} \frac{d x}{x^{4}+4}
$$

as $R \rightarrow \infty$. Hence

$$
I=\frac{1}{2} \cdot \frac{\pi}{4}=\frac{\pi}{8} .
$$

(d) The characteristic equation of the matrix

$$
\left|\begin{array}{ccc}
1-\lambda & 2 & 4 \\
2 & 3-\lambda & 0 \\
5 & 0 & 3-\lambda
\end{array}\right|=0,
$$

or $(\lambda-3)(\lambda+3)(\lambda-7)=0$, has roots $\lambda_{1}=3, \lambda_{2}=-3$ and $\lambda_{3}=7$. These are the eigenvalues of the matrix. For $\lambda_{1}=3$, the matrix equation

$$
\left(\begin{array}{ccc}
-2 & 2 & 4 \\
2 & 0 & 0 \\
5 & 0 & 0
\end{array}\right)\left(\begin{array}{l}
x_{1} \\
x_{2} \\
x_{3}
\end{array}\right)=0
$$

has solution

$$
\left\{\begin{array}{l}
x_{1}=0 \\
x_{2}=-2 x_{3}
\end{array} .\right.
$$

Hence the corresponding eigenvector is

$$
\left(\begin{array}{c}
0 \\
-2 x_{3} \\
x_{3}
\end{array}\right)
$$

and the normalized eigenvector is

$$
\frac{1}{\sqrt{5}}\left(\begin{array}{c}
0 \\
-2 \\
1
\end{array}\right) \text {. }
$$

For $\lambda_{2}=-3$, the matrix equation

$$
\left(\begin{array}{lll}
4 & 2 & 4 \\
2 & 6 & 0 \\
5 & 0 & 6
\end{array}\right)\left(\begin{array}{l}
x_{1} \\
x_{2} \\
x_{3}
\end{array}\right)=0
$$

has solution

$$
\left\{\begin{array}{l}
x_{2}=-\frac{1}{3} x_{1}, \\
x_{3}=-\frac{5}{6} x_{1},
\end{array}\right.
$$

which gives the normalized eigenvector

$$
\frac{6}{\sqrt{65}}\left(\begin{array}{c}
1 \\
-\frac{1}{3} \\
-\frac{5}{6}
\end{array}\right) .
$$

For $\lambda_{3}=7$, the matrix equation

$$
\left(\begin{array}{ccc}
-6 & 2 & 4 \\
2 & -4 & 0 \\
5 & 0 & -4
\end{array}\right)\left(\begin{array}{l}
x_{1} \\
x_{2} \\
x_{3}
\end{array}\right)=0
$$

has solution

$$
\left\{\begin{array}{l}
x_{2}=\frac{1}{2} x_{1}, \\
x_{3}=\frac{5}{4} x_{1},
\end{array}\right.
$$

which gives the normalized eigenvector

$$
\frac{4}{3 \sqrt{5}}\left(\begin{array}{c}
1 \\
\frac{1}{2} \\
\frac{5}{4}
\end{array}\right) \text {. }
$$

These three eigenvectors are not orthogonal, since the matrix

$$
\left(\begin{array}{lll}
1 & 2 & 4 \\
2 & 3 & 0 \\
5 & 0 & 3
\end{array}\right)
$$

is not Hermitian, which is obvious as $a_{i j} \neq a_{j i}$ generally.

\section{1}

Evaluate

$$
\int_{0}^{2 \pi} \frac{d x}{1-2 p \cos x+p^{2}} \quad \text { for } 0<p<1
$$

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_67926f4524ee79171339g-72.jpg?height=431&width=520&top_left_y=1156&top_left_x=846)

Fig. $3.15$

\section{Solution:}

Let $z=e^{i x}$. Then $d x=d z / i z, \cos x=\frac{1}{2}\left(z+z^{-1}\right)$, and

$$
\begin{aligned}
I &=\int_{0}^{2 \pi} \frac{d x}{1-2 p \cos x+p^{2}} \\
&=\oint_{C} \frac{d z}{i z\left[1-p\left(z+z^{-1}\right)+p^{2}\right]}=-\frac{1}{i p} \oint_{C} \frac{d z}{\left(z-p^{-1}\right)(z-p)},
\end{aligned}
$$

where $C$ is the circle with unit radius shown in Fig. 3.15. There is only one pole, $p$, lying inside the circle. The residue at this pole is

$$
\frac{1}{p-p^{-1}}
$$

Hence by the residue theorem we have

$$
I=-\frac{1}{i p} \cdot \frac{2 \pi i}{p-p^{-1}}=\frac{2 \pi}{1-p^{2}} .
$$

\section{2}

Evaluate the infinite sum $S(x)=1+2 x^{2}+3 x^{3}+\ldots$ for $|x|<1$.

\section{Solution:}

Rewriting the sum, we have

$$
\begin{aligned}
S(x) &=\left(x+2 x^{2}+3 x^{3}+\ldots\right)+(1-x) \\
&=x\left(1+2 x+3 x^{2}+\ldots\right)+(1-x) \\
&=x \frac{d}{d x}\left(x+x^{2}+x^{3}+\ldots\right)+(1-x) \\
&=x \frac{d}{d x}\left(\frac{x}{1-x}\right)+(1-x) \\
&=\frac{x}{1-x}+\frac{x^{2}}{(1-x)^{2}}+(1-x) \\
&=\frac{x}{(1-x)^{2}}+(1-x) .
\end{aligned}
$$

\section{3}

Find a solution that is spherically symmetric and goes to zero at infinity for each of the following partial differential equations:
(1) $\nabla^{2} U(\mathbf{r})=-4 \pi \delta(\mathbf{r})$
(2) $\left(\nabla^{2}+k^{2}\right) U(\mathbf{r})=-4 \pi \delta(\mathbf{r})$
(3) $\left(\nabla^{2}-\frac{1}{c^{2}} \frac{\partial^{2}}{\partial t^{2}}\right) U(\mathbf{r}, t)=-4 \pi f(t) \delta(\mathbf{r})$ 

\section{Solution:}

(1) As

$$
\nabla^{2}\left(\frac{1}{r}\right)=-4 \pi \delta(\mathbf{r}),
$$

a spherically symmetric solution of the differential equation is

$$
U(\mathbf{r})=a+\frac{b}{r},
$$

where $a, b$ are constants. For $U \rightarrow 0$ as $r \rightarrow \infty$, we required $a=0$.

Consider next

$$
\int_{V} \nabla^{2} U(\mathbf{r}) d V=-4 \pi \int_{V} \delta(\mathbf{r}) d V=-4 \pi
$$

As by Gauss' theorem

$$
\begin{aligned}
\int_{V} \nabla^{2} U(\mathbf{r}) d V &=\oint_{S} \nabla U(\mathbf{r}) \cdot d S \\
&=-\frac{b}{r^{2}} \cdot 4 \pi r^{2}=-4 \pi b,
\end{aligned}
$$

where we have taken for $V$ a sphere of radius $r$ centered at the origin, $b=1$. Hence the required solution is

$$
U(\mathbf{r})=\frac{1}{r} .
$$

(2) If $r \neq 0$, the differential equation becomes

$$
\frac{1}{r} \frac{d^{2}}{d r^{2}}(r U)+k^{2} U=0,
$$

or

$$
\frac{d^{2}}{d r^{2}}(r U)+k^{2}(r U)=0,
$$

whose solution is $r U=e^{\pm i k r}$. Hence

$$
U=\frac{1}{r}\left(c_{1} e^{i k r}+c_{2} e^{-i k r}\right),
$$

where $c_{1}, c_{2}$ are constants.

For $r \rightarrow 0$,

$$
\frac{1}{r} \frac{d^{2}}{d r^{2}}(r U) \gg k^{2} U
$$

and the differential equation becomes

$$
\nabla^{2} U \approx-4 \pi \delta(\mathbf{r}),
$$

whose spherically symmetric solution is given by (1) to be

$$
U=\frac{1}{r} \text {. }
$$

Thus for $r \rightarrow 0$

$$
\frac{c_{1}+c_{2}}{r}=\frac{1}{r},
$$

or

$$
c_{1}+c_{2}=1,
$$

and the required solution is therefore

$$
U=\frac{1}{r}\left[e^{i k r}+c_{2}\left(e^{-i k r}-e^{i k r}\right)\right] .
$$

(3) For $U(\mathbf{r})$ spherically symmetric, we have

$$
\frac{1}{r} \frac{\partial^{2}}{\partial r^{2}}(r U)-\frac{1}{c^{2}} \frac{\partial^{2} U}{\partial t^{2}}=-4 \pi f(t) \delta(\mathbf{r}) .
$$

If $\mathbf{r} \neq 0$, this becomes the wave equation

$$
\frac{\partial^{2}}{\partial r^{2}}(r U)-\frac{1}{c^{2}} \frac{\partial^{2}}{\partial t^{2}}(r U)=0
$$

with general solution

$$
r U=F_{1}\left(t-\frac{r}{c}\right)+F_{2}\left(t+\frac{r}{c}\right),
$$

where $F_{1}, F_{2}$ are arbitrary functions. The solution $F_{2}$ implies that effect precedes cause and is usually omitted. Hence

$$
U=\frac{1}{r} F_{1}\left(t-\frac{r}{c}\right) .
$$

For $r \rightarrow 0$

$$
\frac{1}{r} \frac{\partial^{2}}{\partial r^{2}}(r U) \gg \frac{1}{c^{2}} \frac{\partial^{2} U}{\partial t^{2}}
$$

and the equation becomes

$$
\nabla^{2} U \approx-4 \pi f(t) \delta(\mathbf{r}),
$$

whose solution is given by $(1)$ as $(b=f(t))$

$$
U=\frac{f(t)}{r} .
$$

A comparison of the above gives $F_{1}(t)=f(t)$ for $r \rightarrow 0$. Hence we have to take, in general,

$$
F_{1}\left(t-\frac{r}{c}\right)=f\left(t-\frac{r}{c}\right),
$$

and the solution required is

$$
U=\frac{1}{r} f\left(t-\frac{r}{c}\right) .
$$

\section{4}

The series $\sum_{n=0}^{\infty}(-1)^{n} n ! z^{n}$ is divergent. Nevertheless, it can be "summed" in the sense that one can find a function $F(z)$ which has the property

$$
\lim _{|z| \rightarrow 0} \frac{1}{n !} \frac{d^{n} F(z)}{d z^{n}}=(-1)^{n} n !
$$

for $|\arg z| \leq \pi-\varepsilon, \varepsilon>0$, and any given $n=0,1,2, \ldots$.

(a) Use the formula $\Gamma(n+1)=n !=\int_{0}^{\infty} d \lambda e^{-\lambda} \lambda^{n}$ in order to obtain $F(z)$ in the form of an integral representation.

(b) Write $F(z)$ as a Hilbert representation

$$
F(z)=\int d x \frac{\rho(x)}{x-z} .
$$

(c) Consider functions of the form $f(z)=c \exp \left(\frac{-b}{z^{a}}\right)$, with $b$ real and positive. Under certain conditions, $f(z)$ may be added to $F(z)$ without affecting the limits (1) for any given finite value of $n=0,1,2, \ldots$ and any $\varepsilon>0$. What are these condition? (Assume $a$ is real)

\section{Solution:}

(a) For a given $n$, suppose for $|z| \rightarrow 0$

$$
\frac{d^{n} F_{n}}{d z^{n}}=(-1)^{n}\left(n !^{2}\right) .
$$

Integrating once gives

$$
\frac{d^{n-1} F_{n}}{d z^{n-1}}=(-1)^{n}(n !)^{2} z+A,
$$

or

$$
(-1)^{n-1}[(n-1) !]^{2}=(-1)^{n}(n !)^{2} z+A \text {, }
$$

where the constant of integration $A$ is obtained by putting $z=0$. Continuing the process we have

$$
F_{n}(z)=\sum_{0}^{n}(-1)^{r} r ! z^{r} .
$$

In particular, for $n \rightarrow \infty$ we have

$$
\begin{aligned}
F(z) &=\sum_{0}^{\infty}(-1)^{n} n ! z^{n} \\
&=\int_{0}^{\infty} d_{\lambda} e^{-\lambda} \sum_{n=0}^{\infty}(-1)^{n} \lambda^{n} z^{n} .
\end{aligned}
$$

With $|z| \rightarrow 0$ we can always take $|\lambda z|<1$, for which

$$
\sum_{n=0}^{\infty}(-1)^{n} \lambda^{n} z^{n}=\sum_{n=0}^{\infty}(-\lambda z)^{n}=\frac{1}{1+\lambda z} .
$$

Hence

$$
F(z)=\int_{0}^{\infty} \frac{e^{-\lambda} d \lambda}{1+\lambda z} .
$$

(b) Let $\lambda=-\frac{1}{x}$. The above becomes

$$
F(z)=\int_{-\infty}^{0} \frac{\left(\frac{1}{x} e^{\frac{1}{x}}\right) d x}{x-z},
$$

which is a Hilbert representation.

(c) The requirement is that, as $|z| \rightarrow 0,|f(z)|$ goes to 0 faster than $|F(z)|$, and hence faster than any power of $z$. As

$$
\begin{aligned}
f(z) &=c \exp \left[-b\left(|z| e^{i \theta}\right)^{-a}\right] \\
&=c \exp \left\{-b|z|^{-a}[\cos (a \theta)-i \sin (a \theta)]\right\} \\
|f(z)| &=|c| \exp \left[-b|z|^{-a} \cos (a \theta)\right]
\end{aligned}
$$

Then $|f(z)| \rightarrow 0$ as $|z| \rightarrow 0$ if

$$
a \geq \delta \quad \text { with } \quad \delta>0
$$

and

$$
\cos (a \theta)>0, \quad \text { or } \quad a|\theta| \leq \frac{\pi}{2}-\delta^{\prime} \quad \text { with } \quad \delta^{\prime}>0 .
$$

We are given that

$$
|\theta| \leq \pi-\varepsilon \quad \text { with } \varepsilon>0,
$$

where $\theta=\arg z$, or

$$
a|\theta| \leq a \pi-a \varepsilon .
$$

All the above conditions can be satisfied if

$$
0<\delta \leq a \leq \frac{1}{2}, \quad \delta^{\prime}=\varepsilon .
$$

\section{5}

(a) Solve the differential equation

$$
\left(\frac{d}{d t}+2\right)\left(\frac{d}{d t}+1\right) y=1
$$

with initial conditions $\left.\frac{d y}{d t}\right|_{t=0}=\left.y\right|_{t=0}=0$.

(b) Calculate the definite integral

$$
\int_{-\infty}^{\infty} d x \frac{1}{\left(a^{2}+x^{2}\right)^{2}}
$$

with " $a$ " real.

(c) What are the eigenvalues of the matrix

$$
M=\left(\begin{array}{ccc}
1 & 0 & -i \\
0 & 2 & 0 \\
i & 0 & -1
\end{array}\right) ?
$$

\section{Solution:}

(a) The equation is usually written as

$$
\frac{d^{2} y}{d t^{2}}+3 \frac{d y}{d t}+2 y=1,
$$

whose eigenequation

$$
\lambda^{2}+3 \lambda+2=0
$$

has solutions

$$
\lambda_{1}=-2, \quad \lambda_{2}=-1 .
$$

Hence the general solution of the homogeneous equation is

$$
y(t)=A e^{-2 t}+B e^{-t} .
$$

A special solution is $y=\frac{1}{2}$, giving the general solution of the differential equation as

$$
y=A e^{-2 t}+B e^{-t}+\frac{1}{2} .
$$

The initial conditions then give

$$
A+B+\frac{1}{2}=0, \quad-2 A-B=0,
$$

which have solution

$$
A=\frac{1}{2}, \quad B=-1 .
$$

Therefore

$$
y(t)=\frac{1}{2} e^{-2 t}-e^{-t}+\frac{1}{2} .
$$

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_67926f4524ee79171339g-79.jpg?height=441&width=563&top_left_y=1731&top_left_x=800)

Fig. 3.16

(b) Consider

$$
\oint_{C} \frac{d z}{\left(z^{2}+a^{2}\right)^{2}}
$$

where $C$ is the semicircle shown in Fig. 3.16.

As

$$
\left(z^{2}+a^{2}\right)^{-2}=(z-i|a|)^{-2}(z+i|a|)^{-2},
$$

the integrand has a pole of order 2 enclosed by $C$ at $z_{1}=i|a|$. The residue at $z_{1}$ is

$$
\lim _{z \rightarrow i|a|} \frac{1}{(2-1) !} \frac{d^{2-1}}{d z^{2-1}}\left[\frac{(z-i|a|)^{2}}{\left(z^{2}+a^{2}\right)^{2}}\right]=\left.\frac{-2}{(z+i|a|)^{3}}\right|_{z=i|a|}=\frac{1}{4 i|a|^{3}} .
$$

Hence

$$
\oint_{C} \frac{d z}{\left(z^{2}+a^{2}\right)^{2}}=\frac{2 \pi i}{4 i|a|^{3}}=\frac{\pi}{2|a|^{3}} .
$$

On the other hand,

$$
\oint_{C}=\int_{-R}^{R} \frac{d x}{\left(x^{2}+a^{2}\right)^{2}}+\int_{\Gamma} \frac{d z}{\left(z^{2}+a^{2}\right)^{2}} \rightarrow \int_{-\infty}^{\infty} \frac{d x}{\left(x^{2}+a^{2}\right)^{2}}+0
$$

as $R \rightarrow \infty$. Therefore

$$
\int_{-\infty}^{\infty} \frac{d x}{\left(x^{2}+a^{2}\right)^{2}}=\frac{\pi}{2|a|^{3}} .
$$

(c) The eigenequation

$$
\left|\begin{array}{ccc}
1-\lambda & 0 & -i \\
0 & 2-\lambda & 0 \\
i & 0 & -1-\lambda
\end{array}\right|=-(\lambda-2)\left(\lambda^{2}-2\right)=0
$$

has solutions

$$
\lambda_{1}=2, \quad \lambda_{2}=\sqrt{2}, \quad \lambda_{3}=-\sqrt{2} .
$$

These are the eigenvalues of the matrix $M$.

\section{6}

Consider a linear system in one dimension whose displacement $y(t)$ is governed by the equation

$$
c_{1} \ddot{y}+c_{2} \dot{y}+c_{3} y=F(t),
$$

where $c_{1}, c_{2}, c_{3}$ are time independent but unknown. At $t=0$, the system is at rest at $y=0$.

You are given the response of the system for all times to a constant driving force of magnitude $F_{0}$ and lasting between $t=0$ and $t=10 T: y_{0}(t)$.

Find, in terms of what is given,

(a) the displacement at $t=50 T$ to a driving force of magnitude $F_{0} / 5$ acting between $t=0$ and $t=50 T$,

(b) the displacement at $t=6 T$ to a driving force of magnitude $F_{0}$ acting between $t=0$ and $t=T$.

(c) Consider now an arbitrary driving force $F(t)$ acting between $t=0$ and $t=T$, where $F(0)=F(T)=0$. Show that the displacement at $t=6 T$ can in fact be found and that it can be written in the form

$$
y(6 T)=A \int_{0}^{T} y_{0}(6 T-t) \dot{F}(t) d t
$$

where $A$ is a constant.

\section{Solution:}

(a) We are given

$$
c_{1} \ddot{y}_{0}(t)+c_{2} \dot{y}_{0}(t)+c_{3} y_{0}(t)=F(0),
$$

or

$$
c_{1} \frac{\ddot{y}_{0}(t)}{5}+c_{2} \frac{\dot{y}_{0}(t)}{5}+c_{3} \frac{y_{0}(t)}{5}=\frac{F_{0}}{5} \quad \text { for } 0 \leq t \leq 10 T
$$

and

$$
c_{1} \ddot{y}_{0}(t)+c_{2} \dot{y}_{0}(t)+c_{3} y_{0}(t)=0 \quad \text { for } 10 T<t \text {, }
$$

and are required to find $y(t)$ which satisfies

$$
\left\{\begin{array}{lll}
c_{1} \ddot{y}(t)+c_{2} \dot{y}(t)+c_{3} y(t)=\frac{F_{0}}{5}, & & 0 \leq t \leq 50 T, \\
c_{1} \ddot{y}(t)+c_{2} \dot{y}(t)+c_{3} y(t)=0, & & 50 T<t .
\end{array}\right.
$$

Since the system is linear, its response $y(t)$ to a constant driving force $\frac{F_{0}}{5}$ which acts for the interval $0-50 T$ can be considered as a linear superposition of the responses to five constant forces each of magnitude $\frac{F_{0}}{5}$ acting in turn for the intervals $0-10 T, 10 T-20 T, 20 T-30 T, 30 T-40 T, 40 T-50 T$. Thus 
(2) $\begin{cases}c_{1} \frac{\ddot{y}_{0}(t)}{5}+c_{2} \frac{\dot{y}_{0}(t)}{5}+c_{3} \frac{y_{0}(t)}{5}=\frac{F_{0}}{5}, & 0 \leq t \leq 10 T, \\ c_{1} \frac{\ddot{y}_{0}(t)}{5}+c_{2} \frac{\dot{y}_{0}(t)}{5}+c_{3} \frac{y_{0}(t)}{5}=0, & t>10 T\end{cases}$

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_67926f4524ee79171339g-82.jpg?height=181&width=1334&top_left_y=506&top_left_x=360)
(4) $\begin{cases}c_{1} \frac{\ddot{y}_{0}(t-20 T)}{5}+c_{2} \frac{\dot{y}_{0}(t-20 T)}{5}+c_{3} \frac{y_{0}(t-20 T)}{5}=\frac{F_{0}}{5}, & 20 T \leq t \leq 30 T, \\ c_{1} \frac{\ddot{y}_{0}(t-20 T)}{5}+c_{2} \frac{\dot{y}_{0}(t-20 T)}{5}+c_{3} \frac{y_{0}(t-20 T)}{5}=0, & t>30 T ;\end{cases}$
(5) $\begin{cases}c_{1} \frac{\ddot{y}_{0}(t-30 T)}{5}+c_{2} \frac{\dot{y}_{0}(t-30 T)}{5}+c_{3} \frac{y_{0}(t-30 T)}{5}=\frac{F_{0}}{5}, & 30 T \leq t \leq 40 T, \\ c_{1} \frac{\ddot{y}_{0}(t-30 T)}{5}+c_{2} \frac{\dot{y}_{0}(t-30 T)}{5}+c_{3} \frac{y_{0}(t-30 T)}{5}=0, & t>40 T ;\end{cases}$
(6) $\begin{cases}c_{1} \frac{\ddot{y}_{0}(t-40 T)}{5}+c_{2} \frac{\dot{y}_{0}(t-40 T)}{5}+c_{3} \frac{y_{0}(t-40 T)}{5}=\frac{F_{0}}{5}, & 40 T \leq t \leq 50 T, \\ c_{1} \frac{\ddot{y}_{0}(t-40 T)}{5}+c_{2} \frac{\dot{y}_{0}(t-40 T)}{5}+c_{3} \frac{y_{0}(t-40 T)}{5}=0, & t>50 T .\end{cases}$

Combining (2)-(6) we have (1) with

$$
\begin{aligned}
y(t)=& \frac{1}{5}\left[y_{0}(t)+y_{0}(t-10 T) \theta(t-10 T)+y_{0}(t-20 T) \theta(t-20 T)\right.\\
&\left.+y_{0}(t-30 T) \theta(t-30 T)+y_{0}(t-40 T) \theta(t-40 T)\right], \quad t \geq 0,
\end{aligned}
$$

where

$$
\theta\left(t-t_{0}\right)= \begin{cases}1, & t \geq t_{0}, \\ 0 & t<t_{0} .\end{cases}
$$

The required displacement is $y(50 T)$ as given by the above formula with $t=50 T$.

(b) Considering the effect of $F_{0}$ acting in the interval $0 \leq t \leq 10 T$ and the effect of $F_{0}$ acting in the interval $T \leq t \leq 11 T$, we have

$$
\begin{cases}c_{1} \ddot{y}_{0}(t)+c_{2} \dot{y}_{0}(t)+c_{3} y_{0}(t)=F_{0}, & 0 \leq t \leq 10 T, \\ c_{1} \ddot{y}_{0}(t)+c_{2} \dot{y}_{0}(t)+c_{3} y_{0}(t)=0, & t>10 T .\end{cases}
$$

and

$$
\begin{cases}c_{1} \ddot{y}_{0}(t-T)+c_{2} \dot{y}_{0}(t-T)+c_{3} y_{0}(t-T)=F_{0}, & T \leq t \leq 11 T, \\ c_{1} \ddot{y}_{0}(t-T)+c_{2} \dot{y}_{0}(t-T)+c_{3} y_{0}(t-T)=0, & t>11 T .\end{cases}
$$

\section{Subtracting we obtain}



$$
\begin{aligned}
&c_{1}\left[\ddot{y}_{0}(t)-\ddot{y}_{0}(t-T) \theta(t-T)\right]+c_{2}\left[\dot{y}_{0}(t)-\dot{y}_{0}(t-T) \theta(t-T)\right] \\
&+c_{3}\left[y_{0}(t)-y_{0}(t-T) \theta(t-T)\right]= \begin{cases}F_{0}, & 0 \leq t \leq T, \\
0, & T<t \leq 10 T, \\
-F_{0}, & 10 T<t \leq 11 T, \\
0, & t>11 T\end{cases}
\end{aligned}
$$

Since an earlier motion is not affected by conditions applied at a later time, the first two of the last set of equations are valid for $0 \leq t \leq 10 T$. Hence

$$
y(t)=y_{0}(t)-y_{0}(t-T), \quad 0 \leq t \leq 10 T,
$$

and thus

$$
y(6 T)=y_{0}(6 T)-y_{0}(5 T) .
$$

(c) In a similar way, consider the effects of $F_{0}$ acting in $0 \leq t \leq 10 T$ and in $T \leq t \leq(10+\tau) T$, where $\tau>0$, and obtain

$$
\begin{aligned}
& \text { (1) } c_{1} \ddot{y}_{0}(t)+c_{2} \dot{y}_{0}(t)+c_{3} y_{0}(t)=F_{0}, \quad 0 \leq t \leq 10 T \text {, } \\
& \text { (2) } c_{1} \ddot{y}_{0}(t-\tau)+c_{2} \dot{y}_{0}(t-\tau)+c_{3} y_{0}(t-\tau)=F_{0}, \quad \tau \leq t \leq(10+\tau) T \text {. } \\
& \text { (1) - (2) gives } \\
& c_{1} \frac{d^{2}}{d t^{2}}\left[y_{0}(t)-y_{0}(t-\tau) \theta(t-\tau)\right]+c_{2} \frac{d}{d t}\left[y_{0}(t)-y_{0}(t-\tau) \theta(t-\tau)\right] \\
& +c_{3}\left[y_{0}(t)-y_{0}(t-\tau) \theta(t-\tau)\right]= \begin{cases}F_{0}, & 0 \leq t \leq \tau, \\ 0, & \tau<t \leq 10 T .\end{cases}
\end{aligned}
$$

Using the definition of time derivative

$$
\begin{aligned}
y_{0}(t)-y_{0}(t-\tau) \theta(t-\tau) &= \begin{cases}\dot{y}_{0}(t) t, & 0 \leq t \leq \tau, \\
\dot{y}_{0}(t) \tau, & \tau<t\end{cases} \\
& \approx \dot{y}_{0}(t) \tau \quad \text { for } \tau \rightarrow 0,
\end{aligned}
$$

we can write the above as

$$
c_{1} \frac{d^{2}}{d t^{2}} \dot{y}_{0}(t)+c_{2} \frac{d}{d t} \dot{y}_{0}(t)+c_{3} \dot{y}_{0}(t)= \begin{cases}\frac{F}{\tau}, & 0 \leq t \leq \tau \\ 0, & t>\tau .\end{cases}
$$

With $\tau \rightarrow 0$, this becomes

$$
c_{1} \frac{d^{2}}{d t^{2}} \dot{y}_{0}(t)+c_{2} \frac{d}{d t} \dot{y}_{0}(t)+c_{3} \dot{y}_{0}(t)=B \delta(t), \quad 0 \leq t \leq 10 T,
$$

where $B$ is a constant. Replacing $t$ by $t-\tau$, multiplying both sides by $F(\tau) d \tau$ and integrating over $\tau$ from 0 to $T$, we obtain

$$
\begin{aligned}
&c_{1} \frac{d^{2}}{d t^{2}}\left[\int_{0}^{T} \dot{y}_{0}(t-\tau) F(\tau) d \tau\right]+c_{2} \frac{d}{d t}\left[\int_{0}^{T} \dot{y}_{0}(t-\tau) F(\tau) d \tau\right] \\
&+c_{3} \int_{0}^{T} \dot{y}_{0}(t-\tau) F(\tau) d \tau=B \int_{0}^{T} \delta(t-\tau) F(\tau) d \tau .
\end{aligned}
$$

We are required to find $y(t)$ given that

$$
\begin{aligned}
c_{1} \ddot{y}(t)+c_{2} \dot{y}(t)+c_{3} y(t) &= \begin{cases}F(t), & 0 \leq t \leq T, \\
0, & t>T,\end{cases} \\
&=\int_{0}^{T} \delta(t-\tau) F(\tau) d \tau .
\end{aligned}
$$

Comparing the last two differential equations, we have for $t \leq 10 T$

$$
\begin{aligned}
y(t) &=B^{-1} \int_{0}^{T} \dot{y}_{0}(t-\tau) F(\tau) d \tau \\
&=-\left.B^{-1} F(\tau) y_{0}(t-\tau)\right|_{0} ^{T}+B^{-1} \int_{0}^{T} y_{0}(t-\tau) \dot{F}(\tau) d \tau \\
&=A \int_{0}^{T} y_{0}(t-\tau) \dot{F}(\tau) d \tau
\end{aligned}
$$

as $F(0)=F(T)=0$. Note that the constant $B^{-1}$ has been replaced by $A$. Hence

$$
y(6 T)=A \int_{0}^{T} y_{0}(6 T-\tau) \dot{F}(\tau) d \tau .
$$



![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_67926f4524ee79171339g-85.jpg?height=347&width=411&top_left_y=1190&top_left_x=848)

Qualifying Questions and Solutions

\section{PROBLEMS AND SOLUTIONS ON SOLID STATE PHYSICS, RELATIVITY AND MISCELLANEOUS TOPICS}

The material for this series was selected from the examination questions for graduate students at the University of California (Berkeley), Columbia University, the University of Chicago, MIT, the State University of New York at Buffalo, Princeton University and the University of Wisconsin.

This volume comprises 165 problems. The section on Solid State Physics includes crystal structures and properties, electron theory, energy bands and semiconductors. The Relativity section covers both the special and general theories. Topics that were not appropriate for the other 6 volumes in this series appear here under the heading of Miscellaneous Topics.
